## Acknowledgements 

As this revised and expanded second edition goes to print, I wish to express my gratitude to many individuals and institutions. Like this book, the list of those who have in some way contributed to the research, writing and improvement of this book is long, and I am blessed by the fact that the number of colleagues and friends continues to grow. None of them, however, are responsible for any errors or inaccuracies, which are my personal fault. 

I fondly recall my graduate training at the University of Vienna and those who inspired my work, including Oliver Schmitt. On-site in Zadar, my thanks go to Marko Trogrlić and Laris Borić. A special thanks also goes to both Suzana Martinović and Karolina Ražov, whose swift attendance to my requests makes every visit to the Croatian State Archive in Zadar (Državni arhiv u Zadru) much more than a research trip. I wish to furthermore acknowledge my two dissertation advisors at the University of Graz, the late Karl Kaser and Harald Heppner. Bernd Roeck at the University of Zurich and Gherardo Ortalli at Ca’ Foscari contributed to the funding of the first edition, which shall also be duly recognised. 

I am honoured and very grateful that the University of Zurich’s PrivatdozentenStiftung resolved to fund the open-access version of this book. Special thanks go to my colleagues and friends Rebecca Darley (now at the University of Leeds) and David Starr-Glass, and to Sascha Attia, for their careful editing and commentary on the first edition. Similarly, my parents Karl and Ursula Sander, as well as my grand-aunt Wilhelmine Bauer, lived with many of the urban elites for years, only to be joined by my parents-in-law Rosina and Helmut Faes in due course. They have been accompanied by a large and growing number of colleagues with whom I have had the privilege to continue my research-based dialogue on the Adriatic past, including Ana Plosnić, Ines Ivić, Filip Malesević, Bratislav Lučin, Bruno Crevato-Selvaggi and Rita Tolomei, Katalin Prajda, Andrea Fara, Holly Hurlburt, Monique O’Connell, Eric Dursteler, Georg Michels, Erin Maglaque, Luca Zenobi, Nicolò Villanti, Michele Santoro and others. Throughout the past almost two decades, I have relied on their efforts and companionship, and I sincerely hope that I have represented all cited works accurately and duly acknowledged them in the appropriate places. If I have failed anyone in this regard, I offer my unreserved apologies.

Even more faithful and enduring has been my wife, Dorothea Faes, to whom I am enormously indebted for all her generous care, unending patience and unyielding support over the years. My love: I hope you will enjoy this revised and enlarged version, too, and it is to you and to our two beautiful daughters that it is dedicated.

Note on Text, Spelling and Translation 

Given the linguistic characteristics of the Renaissance Adriatic and the purview of this book, I have chosen the following method of naming individuals and places: when referring to the entirety of territories ruled by Venice, I use the term “Venetian Republic” or “commonwealth”. I decided against the term “empire” or “state” to avoid terminological confusion, anachronistic or otherwise. As regards the lands situated along the shores of the Adriatic, I have used names like Dalmatia, Istria, Albania or Veneto to relate to historical-geographical regions. Specifics are given when referring to clearly identifiable polities, such as the Kingdom of Naples or the Duchy of Milan. None of these designations shall infer the absence of much more complicated and variegated realities both “on the ground” and those that tied these places to their wider environments. Similarly, no territorial claim or other (value) judgement shall be inferred. 

The overwhelming majority of sources were written in Latin with 16[th] -century characteristics. To enhance the linguistic consistency, I have endeavoured to provide English translations of all direct quotations, with their original spelling, including punctuation, cited in the footnotes. This practice implies two major considerations: first, a modernisation of all homonyms and toponyms, even though I wish to add that I have tried to stick as close as possible to the punctuation and meaning of the original sources. Second, to facilitate locating the sometimes-obscure placenames on modern maps, I have used the present-day toponyms, i.e., Croatian names in Dalmatia or Italian names in Italy; exceptions are places that are generally familiar, such as Rome, Venice or Istanbul. Nothing in this usage shall infer taking sides with competing claims, historical, nationalistic or otherwise. For all places within the vicinity of Zadar, additional information is provided in the appendix, including their original spelling in the primary sources, present-day Croatian toponyms and, if available, their historical versions. 

The main denominations used were ducats, libras ( _lira_ , _lire_ ) and soldi ( _solidi_ , _solada_ ). All sums that appear in this volume are interchangeable in the following ways: 1 ducat equalled 6 libras and 4 soldi, and 1 libra equalled 20 soldi or 240 denarii. All other currencies (Mocenigo, Scudo, Ungaro, Zecchino) are similarly convertible into libras and soldi. Heavy golden coins (esp. ducats) were rarely,

<!-- page 11 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

10 

if ever, used in everyday life, but they were typically used in notarised deeds to indicate prices. For statistical reasons, I have converted all such sums into their libra and/or denarii value, and all seeming inconsistencies are a result of these practices. 

The Venetian year, which began in March, is referenced with the abbreviation “m.v.” ( _more veneto_ ). Calendrical norms in the rest of the Adriatic are less clear; unless indicated otherwise all dates are reproduced as they appear in the cited sources.

## Glossary of Select Key Terms 

- lease or rental contract leaseholder or tenant 

- _affictuarius bolletta_ , _contralettere_ clearance certificate or customs receipt _breviarium_ accounts current, (abridged) statement of assets and liabilities 

- _capitulum_ , _capitolo_ chapters, subsections in legal texts, as in the _Statuta Iadertina_ 

- _chyrographum_ promissory note, obligation or debt security _colonus_ holder of a concession or land grant _concessio_ concession or land grant _conductio_ lease or tenancy contract _conductor_ leaseholder or tenant _contrados_ counter-dowry, also known as _donatio propter nuptias district_ ( _districtus_ , _distrikt_ ) administrative subdivisions of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana governed by castellans but subject to the overall jurisdiction of Zadar’s count 

- _dos_ dowry _emphytheosis_ lease of property on the condition of taking care of it during the tenure 

- _emptio_ contract of purchase/sale _fideiussio_ co-signing, guarantee of payment of a loan, personal security 

- _honorantia_ special gift _incantum_ , _incanto_ process by which public property was auctioned off to the highest bidder 

- _indictio_ indiction, 15-year cycle used in dating mediaeval documents 

- _instrumentum pacis_ extrajudicial arbitration settlement of a legal feud _invocatio_ formal beginning of a legal document _jurisdiction_ ( _contado_ , _county_ , _distrikt_ ) the territory controlled by an urban centre 

- _licentia comitis_ ducal licence or permission _livellatio_ document certifying a lawful contract, bill of exchange

<!-- page 13 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

12 

_locator_ landlord _pizochara_ , _bizzoche_ beguine or lay nuns, member of a lay sisterhood _primicerius_ first or senior of the lower clergy _procura_ legally binding authorisation or mandate of representation _procurator_ legal agent or representative, proxy _territory_ ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ) public lands in the vicinity of an urban centre

Preface to the 1[st] Edition 

New Perspectives for an Important Adriatic Centre 

The path of knowledge proposed by Stephan Sander-Faes in his thorough and well-documented study merits attention for a series of reasons, beginning with the selection of the title of the book and the reference to a Venetian commonwealth. This is not merely a figure of speech but proposes a new interpretation of the Republic of St Mark as a reality marked by particular relationships and connections between Venice the metropolis and the many components of her composite state. Because of these highly-varied relationships, Venice was able to acquire, institutionalise and maintain her positions in Italy and the Mediterranean over the long term: in the _Terraferma_ and her maritime dominions via informal colonies of citizens in foreign lands, formal delegations throughout the Mediterranean and elsewhere and in regions where Venetian prerogatives and de facto-dependence substituted direct control. 

Sander-Faes authoritatively articulates this new interpretation of Venetian historiography and the characteristics of the fragmented and varied components of Venice’s rule. His book offers an original, well-researched and at times surprising contribution. The history of the Serenissima and her commonwealth has always been and continues to be the object of an extensive and growing body of scholarship by a large number of scholars from all over the world and from very different spheres. In addition to certain well-studied regions and epochs, there are others that, while not entirely neglected, offer ample room for further examination. Within this context, Sander-Faes’ book is distinctive for the period under survey, in the perspective it offers and in the articulation of the investigative methods it employs. 

The years on which the book focuses constitute a period in which the heyday of the Most Serene Republic had passed. The first two decades of the 16[th ] century mark a decisive shift in Venetian fortunes. The ignominious defeat by the troops of the League of Cambrai at Agnedello in 1509 heralded a dramatic change to Venice’s position and her waning role on the international chessboard. The ensuing decades witnessed the indisputable decline of the Serenissima. The area and period under survey in this volume, which centres on Zara, is circumscribed by two traumatic events in Venetian history. In 1540, after thirty years of continuous clashes with the Ottoman Empire, conflict temporarily ceased. Venice’s separate peace imposed by Suleiman II the Magnificent brought about the loss of important centres in the Aegean, the Peloponnese and Dalmatia, where, in Zara’s jurisdiction, the two strategic castles of Vrana and Nadin came under the control of the Ottomans.

<!-- page 17 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

16 

If 1540 was the year of the unfavourable peace treaty with the Most Sublime Porte, 1569 marked the advent of renewed Ottoman-Venetian conflict. It began in 1570 and came to a close in 1573 with Venice’s loss of the large island kingdom of Cyprus. The great battle of Lepanto, fought in the interim, had renewed hope, pride and illusions but did little to change reality or Ottoman attitudes. This was reflected in the comment made by the Grand Vizier Sokullu Mehmed Pasha: Lepanto was to Cyprus what a beard trim was to the amputation of an arm. Venice’s time as a great power had ended, and after an initial phase of shock and bewilderment, and conscious of her limits, the _respublica_ acquiesced to the logic of military and political neutrality in order to survive. 

Within this general context, Sander-Faes’ book examines in depth the history of Venetian Zara. He positions the city as a centre of decisive importance for Venice’s commercial, maritime and political interests in the Adriatic. Zara was the key to Venetian presence in the Adriatic, continuing a centuries-old interest dating as far back as the expedition of Doge Pietro II Orseolo around the turn of the first millennium. Control over this important harbour town had long been troubled, especially due to the rival interests of the kingdom of Hungary. From the early 15[th] century Venice was able to settle the issue, and Zara remained under Venetian control until the fall of the Republic in 1797. 

victories, defeats or international affairs. The historical contextualisation begins with the peace of Zara (1358), in which Venice was forced to cede her claims over Dalmatia to King Louis the Great of Hungary. Then Zara’s urban life in all its diverse expressions is fully illuminated, providing the volume with robust substance that transcends the scope suggested by the title. There are the economic practices and networks that connected Zara’s urban elites with elites of other Adriatic cities, the detailed activities, variations of conjunctures, the lifestyle of the privileged strata and the quiet workings of quotidian life. All the while, Venice is regarded as the guarantor of security and stability for the inhabitants of Dalmatia during an era of growing dependence in the peripheries on the fortunes of the centre. 

The systematic study of more than 3,500 unpublished documents opens important windows onto diverse social aspects: the secular and ecclesiastical protagonists, relationships between Venetians and locals, family relationships, the presence of foreigners, marriage, immigration structures, Jewish and Croat communities, the property market, the allocation of urban spaces, behavioural traits of the Dalmatian nobility, artisans, farmers, shepherds and the role of transhumance. Its depth illuminates the smallest events but never minimises perception of the complexities of the whole system, as so often occurs in the treatment of microhistory. And in the background is felt the weight of Ottoman proximity, which at once gave rise to tensions and fears and reinforced loyalty to Venice. 

In sum, we can say frankly that Stephan Sander-Faes’ work is fundamental to making Zara, among the Adriatic centres that made up part of the Venetian commonwealth, one of the best-understood societies in all its diverse characteristics in the tumultuous middle decades of the 16[th] century. 

Gherardo Ortalli, Bernd Roeck, penned in 2013

<!-- page 18 -->

Preface to the 2[nd] Edition 

Almost two decades have passed since my study of the urban elites of Zadar began during my time as a Ph.D.-level researcher, first at the University of Vienna and then at the University of Graz, both in Austria. Much of what is new in this revised and expanded edition was originally tucked away in footnotes of either my underlying Ph.D. dissertation or this book’s first edition; a secondary source of new inputs stemmed from my own evolving interests and sustained enquiries into one or the other direction, departing from discussions at conferences and workshops, as well conversations in lecture halls across Europe and the United States. A selection of these included presentations at the Renaissance Society of America’s conventions in New York City (2014), Berlin (2015), Boston (2016) and Dublin (2022), at workshops at the Central European University (2017) and the University of California at Riverside (2018), and, to my great honour, at the Marko Marulić conference in Split, Croatia (2019). While the WHO-declared pandemic temporarily brought the invaluable in-person exchange to a halt, it also afforded me the time to transform these papers – as well as a substantial amount of previously unpublished and thus original research – into this book. 

Compared to the first edition, beyond the changes – really: additions – to Chapters 2 through 4 and 6, this second edition contains significant expanded material in the thematic introduction (Chapter 1), which now includes a brief introductory overview to the most relevant dedicated studies on the subject matter. Chapter 5, entitled “Crime, Punishment, and Arbitration Settlements”, is entirely new and offers a notable contribution to the study of crime history in Renaissance Dalmatia that goes well beyond the elite-heavy existing literature. It is based on the extant extrajudicial settlements in the notarial protocol books, which are analysed both qualitatively and quantitatively; in addition to presenting several case studies, I introduce the conceptual approaches of “extra-justice” and “para-justice” pioneered by Benoît Garnot to the study of the Renaissance Adriatic. Chapter 7 (Chapter 6 in the first edition) underwent slightly less massive changes, although I substituted several new examples drawn from the rich archival documentation. I also expanded its last segment from the original book (“The Cityscape”) and rendered it into a separate chapter. The result, Chapter 8 in the present second edition, retains the brief quantitative discussion, yet its emphases now rests on new detailed case studies of two of Zadar’s most renowned “elite

<!-- page 19 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

18 

citizens” (James Grubb), Simon Budineus, or Šime Budinić, and Hieronymus Cortesius, and thus offers a wealth of new original research as a point of departure for future studies. 

These new segments do not disavow my original interpretations but rather seek to augment, broaden and deepen the substance of my book’s argument by pursuing paths I did not include previously for several reasons. The underlying ideas – to spread the word about the wonderful resources of Renaissance Dalmatia in an interesting and fruitful manner – remain, and I am convinced that the original and expanded editions contribute to the ongoing “mainstreaming” of an area whose history used to be confined to a handful of specialists. This book, then, is both a revised piece of scholarship and a contribution to the lingering debate about the content and extent of _European_ history. 

Turning, for a moment, to the proverbial bigger picture, I am honoured by the impact my first book had, as evidenced by the very positive reviews by Nenad Fejić in _Annales_ (“a plea for the epistemological renewal of the study of the entirety of the _Stato da mar_ ”), Tea Mayhew in _The Renaissance Quarterly The Mediterranean_ (“an essential contribution to the field”) and Egidio Ivetić in _History Review_ , with the latter honouring my work by noting that “nothing significant” had been written “since the books by Grga Novak on Split (1961) and Tomislav Raukar on Zadar (1977)” and calling my book “a welcome addition to the literature”, which “to some degrees fills the gap”. Further vindication arrived in the guise of the equally warm reception by Croatian, Slovenian and Southeast European scholars, to all of whom I wish to express my gratitude for welcoming me warmly into their circles. In addition, I wish to expressly honour the memory of my Ph.D. supervisor, the late Karl Kaser, who, while unwavering in his support over the decade of temporary penury, always had an open ear. I am very grateful for Karl’s companionship and encouragement while I note, with lasting sadness, that his untimely passing two years ago prevented him from seeing this second edition appear in print. This edition, then, also serves as a testament to a kindred spirit who, until his death, kept up his curiosity, and it is his memory that I wish to acknowledge here. Godspeed, my friend. 

I mention this to offer a few words of encouragement to current Ph.D. candidates and post-doctoral researchers alike who, by following their very own interests in whatever subject they focus on, may at some point or another question the wisdom of having done so: will my research have the desired impact beyond the admittedly very narrow circle of interlocutors and discussants in any given (niche) field? I deeply cherish, and am forever grateful for, the camaraderie and friendships my enquiries into the Renaissance Adriatic brought forth. Apart from relating humanist values, such as integrity and companionship, I remain convinced that, in one way or another, the present second edition will serve as encouragement to all those who engage in the seemingly thankless task of historicising, thus redefining, and ultimately reinvigorating what seems to most endless and barren fields of research. Moreover, I am equally convinced that, all other things being equal, hard work, dedication and merit will continue to leave their mark on the scholarship. In this sense, to paraphrase Friedrich Nietzsche,

<!-- page 20 -->

Preface to the 2[nd] Edition 

19 

my own academic career, which once began with the study of Zadar’s urban elites in the 16[th] century, is both quite “untimely” (orig. _unzeitgemäß_ ) and also right on time, hopefully offering motivation to go the proverbial extra mile(s). Ultimately, I wish to acknowledge the comments, support and encouragement I received from my wife Dorothea over the years. This revised and expanded second edition grew in no small part from your companionship, and I shall hope that you, my dear, will enjoy this book even a tad more than you liked the first edition. After all, this second edition is substantially longer and heftier. 

Bergen, summer 2025

<!-- page 22 -->

## Introduction 

Thanks to a combination of painstaking diplomacy and good luck, Venice was able to settle down to one of the longest periods of peace she could remember – a period in which, in the words of one of her principal French historians, “the history of the Venetians flows on without being marked by any events worthy of the attention of posterity”.[1] 

John Norwich, 1983, and Pierre Daru, 1821 

Venice was not only one of the greatest cities of medieval and early modern Europe, it was also one of Europe’s most enduring republics, an expansive empire and, from the fifteenth century on, an imposing regional state.[2] 

John Martin and Dennis Romano, 2000 

Over the course of the 16[th] century, a period to which John Norwich, citing Pierre Daru, ascribed the absence of memorable events, Venice stood at a crossroads.[3] With the benefit of hindsight, historians have concluded that its days as an expanding imperial republic had drawn to a close during the decade after the Battle of Zonchio (1499) and the near-catastrophic War of the League of Cambrai, which culminated in the ignominious defeat at Agnadello (1509).[4] While the naval defeat off Zonchio marked the end of major acquisitions in the eastern Mediterranean, the Serenissima’s struggle against the League of Cambrai brought about the cessation of her designs on the Italian mainland. These changes occurred contemporaneously with equally momentous geopolitical shifts. Unlike earlier eras, the Republic of St Mark now found herself sandwiched in-between 

1. Norwich, _History of Venice_ , pp. 459-460. In the original, it was, “l’histoire des Vénetiens s’écoule sans être marquée par des évènements dignes d’occuper la postérité”. See Daru, _Histoire de la république de Venise_ , vol. IV, p. 118. 

2. Martin, Romano, “Reconsidering Venice”, p. 1. 

3. Dursteler, “Histories of Venice”; Povolo, “Creation of Venetian Historiography”. It has been a long time since comprehensive histories of the Republic were published. The most recent additions are by Horodowich, _Venice_ , and Karsten, _Geschichte Venedigs_ , both of which focus virtually exclusively on Venice proper, to the detriment of both the _terraferma_ and _stato da mar_ . The most important anthologies therefore remain _La repubblica di Venezia nell’età moderna_ , ed. by Cozzi, Knapton and Scarabello; and the 12-volume _Storia di Venezia_ . 

4. Gullino, “Le frontiere navali”, pp. 90-95; Gilbert, “Venice in the Crisis”; Finlay, _Venice Besieged_ .

<!-- page 23 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

22 

the two self-styled universal monarchies of the Habsburg and Ottoman dynasties and thus hard-pressed from the Occident and Orient alike.[5] This perilous situation was compounded by the “impious alliance” between the Sultan and the Most Catholic King.[6] Faced with these winds of change, and to survive in this hostile environment, the Venetian body politic shifted from assertive policies toward a more defensive stance. New policies were devised to hold on to far-flung possessions extending from Cyprus in the eastern Mediterranean to the gates of Milan in Lombardy, overseen by the Serenissima’s “men of empire”.[7] Changes in Venetian society during the 16[th] century, though not visible on the surface, extended beyond affairs of state. What patrician diarist Girolamo Priuli (14761547) called the “flight from the sea” amounted to nothing less than a sea change in the most literal sense of the term: the shift from a Mediterranean to a nascent Atlantic world economy and the encroachment of French, English and later Dutch merchants upon previously Venetian-controlled commerce in the Levant.[8] This book, in short, is a detailed enquiry into these subcutaneous changes during the 16[th] century and therefore a (still) long overdue contribution to the study of the Renaissance Adriatic. 

In the wake of these developments, the writings of Marino Sanuto (14661536), Gasparo Contarini (1483-1542) and others celebrated the glories of the aristocratic republic and cemented what eventually became known as the “myth[s] of Venice”.[9] For centuries these myths defined how historians approached the history of the Republic of St Mark.[10] Two major issues, however, remained unresolved: one spatial and the other conceptual. As regards the former, there remains what John Martin and Dennis Romano called the “central paradox in Venetian history”, that is, 

5. This notion was introduced as early as 1857 by Leopold von Ranke and has stayed with historians since (at least among those cognisant of events and developments in East-Central and South-Eastern Europe). See, for instance, Braudel, _The Mediterranean_ , vol. I, p. 476. More recently, the Ottomans have been “returning” to European history, as intimated by Goffman, _The Ottoman Empire_ , pp. 1-20. 

6. Garnier, _L’alliance impie_ . 

7. O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ . While I personally disagree with the reified use of “empire” (I think a realm or republic may _have_ an empire), see also her “Individuals, Families, and the State”, as well as the more critical essays in _Cultures of Empire_ , ed. by Christ and Morche. For a different approach, see Maglaque, _Venice’s Intimate Empire_ .

This shift contributed to the demise of the Venetian long-distance convoy system ( _mude_ ), on which see Judde de Larivière, _Naviguer, commercer, gouverner_ . Introduction 

23 

The sharp contrast between the tendency of Venetians both to represent and to think about themselves in terms of fixed categories and the underlying reality [ _sic_ ] of economic, social, and geographic fluidity…which was constantly in motion.[11] 

As regards the latter aspect, we note, in passing, that scholarship still focuses overwhelmingly on the lagoon metropolis, with both the _terraferma_ and _stato da mar_ continuing their existence as the city’s proverbial poor relations (although the former to a somewhat lesser degree); in this regard, “Venice Reconsidered” does not stray from well-established scholarship.[12] We note, therefore, that while myth-centred scholarship has declined over the past generation, even more attention than before has been showered on Venice proper. In this regard, the end of the Cold War (1947-1992) brought with it an increase of new and fine-grained approaches to Venetian sotiety, revealing many new insights into her diverse, multicultural and poly-confessional population.[13] By and large, however, these trends in the scholarship have not yet reached Venice’s possessions in the eastern Mediterranean.[14] 

The history – rather, histories – of Venice’s overseas possessions along the shores of the Aegean and Adriatic seas was mostly left to Greek, Yugoslav and later Croatian scholars.[15] Dalmatia’s eventful past was first studied from an Italocentric point of view by scholars like Vitaliano Brunelli, Angelo de Benvenuti and Giuseppe Praga.[16] The same events and developments were studied by South-Slavic scholars, who reached rather different conclusions; moreover, anachronistically and perhaps even ironically so, most materialist Yugoslav scholarship after the

24 

These selective Second World War tended to focus on specific ethnic groups.[17] interpretations and perceptions of Venetian history served the purposes of national movements in the second half of the 19[th] century and nationalistic ends during the first half of the 20[th] .[18] Around the turn of early 21[st] century, and their biases notwithstanding, their function shifted yet again: as the historiography of Venice’s maritime state is still _in statu nascendi_ , a spatially, chronologically and thematically very uneven body of scholarship – that, in a way, mirrors the pluri-national, polyconfessional and trans-imperial characteristics of the historical _stato da mar_ – has since emerged.[19] In other words, this distinct branch of scholarship is still quite young, highly dynamic and, in telling contrast to more established fields (e.g., the history of Venice proper), its practitioners enjoy a bit more leeway in terms of approaches, concepts, sources and themes.[20] 

In the aftermath of the Second World War, Venetian historiography was largely characterised by two models. First, due to rapid decolonisation and Cold War rhetoric, the _stato da mar_ entered in the ideologically-charged debates of the era, most notably in the writings of Freddy Thiriet.[21] While materialist considerations have receded, the imperial dimension (interpretation) of Venetian rule persists to this day, although what that “empire” was is rarely discussed.[22] Second, American scholars Frederic Lane and William Bouwsma celebrated the ideals of Renaissance republicanism, placing Venice within a long-standing Western tradition.[23] With few exceptions – most notably, essays by Robert Finlay and Edward Muir come to mind – this emphasis on republican virtues has all but disappeared from scholarly debates.[24]

Introduction 

25 

At the same time, more diverse, nuanced approaches to late mediaeval and early modern colonialism have (re)emerged that emphasise the continuities between the Mediterranean and Atlantic.[25] For the Venetian maritime state, the works of Benjamin Arbel, Michel Balard, Alain Ducellier, David Jacoby, Chryssa Maltezou and others are seminal.[26] While it can be said that these recent trends have added considerable nuance to the discussion of the Venetian _stato da mar_ , significant blind spots persist. Much in line with earlier materialist interpretations, the role of ecclesiastical actors and institutions continues its existence as the proverbial poor relation of the renewed push, in Oliver Schmitt’s formulation, to “integrate Venice into the framework of the comparative study of empires”. At the same time, this emphasis on political institutions and the ensuing discussions of the perceived “true” nature of Venetian rule comes to the detriment of social and economic history, a fact that even the slight increase in microhistorical case studies cannot obscure.[27] 

With scholarly attention trained firmly on the lagoon metropolis, there are but a handful of recent case studies that live up to the oft-repeated calls by Tomislav Raukar, first voiced over forty years ago, for more primary sourcesbased investigations into social and economic history.[28] There exists, of course, a relatively large number of edited (mostly normative) sources;[29] and there is the fairly large body of literature detailing the history of Dalmatia before the advent of the second Venetian dominion in 1409/1420.[30] Much less attention, by

Introduction 

27 

abundance of the known research possibilities in the various Adriatic branches of the Croatian State Archive, there has been little effort directed at the history of everyday life in Zadar. While its urban history in the late mediaeval period has been explored in great detail by Tomislav Raukar, very little new research has been published about central Dalmatia and its “key and metropolis” in the past decade, with Ivan Majnarić’s recent enquiry being perhaps the proverbial exception that proves the rule.[37] 

## 1. _Approach, sources, methods and chapter outline_ 

This study offers a detailed enquiry into the life, activities and times of Zadar’s “urban elites”, by which I mean analytical groupings that cut across unidimensional characterisations along secular vs ecclesiastical and functional vs status groups.[38] Hence, and depending on the context, these are not understood as “fixed” or unchanging groups; for instance, non-noble humanists such as Simon Budineus (Šime Budinić) might be associated with the ecclesiastical elite due to his functions within the Archdiocese of Zadar.[39] Another example would be the elevated status of Venetian patricians and citizens alike, which derived from their geographical and social provenance. In the following pages, the focus rests firmly on the city known in the 16[th] century as Iader (in Latin) and Zara (in Italian), then the nominal capital of Venice’s dual province of Dalmatia and Albania.[40] The city’s jurisdiction was relatively large and encompassed the incorporated districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana during the three interwar decades from 1540 to the outbreak of the Cyprus War in 1570. These years mark the apogee of Islamic dominance and the corresponding nadir of Christian naval power in the wider Mediterranean basin, whose repercussions could especially be felt along the frontiers of Venice’s maritime state. The repercussions of this situation were clearly visible along the outskirts of cities like Zadar, with Antonio Diedo, sent on an inspection tour in 1553, noting that “in some places the city walls…are the borders of [Venetian rule in] Dalmatia”.[41]

Urban Elites of Zadar 

28 

This book presents a multivariate framework that considers the geographical locations, economic developments and socio-functional relations of the various ecclesiastical and secular elites along the diffuse and ambiguous borders of the Republic of St Mark and the Ottoman Empire. While scholarly debates “about how to characterise Venice’s rule over both the _Terraferma_ and the _Stato da mar_ ” continue, and will continue, I am using the term “commonwealth”.[42] Making recourse to its mediaeval and early modern usage – _res publica_ , rendered into English as “common weal” or “commonwealth” – allows the historian to avoid the anachronistic connotations of empire, republic or “composite state” with reference to the Republic of St Mark.[43] A commonwealth of this type is the manifestation of ancient and mediaeval continuities that do not preclude the existence of a domestic, republican body politic and the pursuit of aggressive policies abroad.[44] 

Within this overall framework, at least two forms of authority existed in the _stato da mar_ that, every so often, overlapped to such a degree that drawing distinctions is an exercise in futility. Power and (legal) authority at the local level was concentrated in the hands of Venice’s governors, legates and military commanders, _and_ it included the participation of Dalmatia’s indigenous elites, secular and ecclesiastical.[45] In exchange for their loyalty, the inhabitants of the coastal cities and hinterlands continued to live under Christian rule, even though this often meant cross-border raids, enslavement by marauders from beyond the borders and the attempted subordination of Dalmatia’s economic and political life under Venetian prerogatives.[46] Over time, after the restoration of Venetian rule in 1409/1420, this combination gradually marginalised and impoverished the coastal communities.[47] The proximity of the neighbouring Ottoman Empire kept the local

Introduction 

29 

populace firmly loyal to Venice but also increased insecurity in the continental hinterlands of the various coastal towns; in this regard, Zadar’s jurisdiction was no different.[48] At the same time, contacts with the peoples in the neighbouring regions under Ottoman suzerainty and further afar continued, albeit on lower levels than especially during the period between the Peace of Zadar (1358) and the return of Venetian rule after 1409.[49] 

This study examines how the urban populations of Zadar interacted with each other and with their contemporaries beyond the city’s jurisdiction, including Ottoman subjects, transhumance peoples from across the frontiers and Venetians. The latter group included both the Serenissima’s officials (governors, legates), military personnel stationed in Dalmatia and aboard passing naval vessels, artisans and merchants, among others. Typically, Venice’s representatives – many of whom where patricians of lower rank and wealth[50] – found themselves in an environment strikingly familiar to them.[51] Often, they were required to mitigate the conflicts between commoners and noblemen while, first and foremost, safeguarding Venice’s overarching economic, military, political and strategic interests.[52] 

This study is divided into eight chapters. Chapter 1, entitled “The Setting”, serves a dual purpose. First, it offers a concise introduction to Venice’s overseas possessions from the aftermath of the Chioggia War (1378-1381) all the way to the outbreak of the Ottoman-Venetian war of 1537-1540. Second, these pages detail the most important qualities of the second Venetian dominion over Dalmatia, focusing specifically on its economic, legal, political and social implications leading up to the period under survey here. The main objective of these pages is to identify the structural characteristics of Venetian rule in the Adriatic. 

The approach to the various urban groups follows the concept of “elite citizens”, which refers to considerations by James Grubb applied to nascent non-

patrician civic families whose scions staffed the various offices of the Republic of St Mark. While both the scale and complexity found in Zadar – and, by extension, across Venice’s _stato da mar_ – were smaller due to population differentials, the role of this _noblesse de robe à la vénetienne_ and their local counterparts was just as essential for the functioning of society in Dalmatia as it was in Venice.[53] Their study across several intertwined themes is organised as follows. First, Chapter 2 identifies the principal actors of Zadar’s society and their pan-Adriatic connections. Expanding on Grubb, and with reference to Budak’s considerations, I introduce the category of “intellectual elites”,[54] whose activities are surveyed in detail by studying a mostly overlooked type of documentary source – _procura_ contracts – to quantitatively analyse the economic, occupational, geographical and social origins of both contracting parties, combining them with the geographical destinations of the appointees. A total of 930 individual notarial contracts from 1540 to 1569 have been analysed, and they provide quantitative and qualitative data to supplement more traditional methods measuring “communication”.[55] Moreover, these documents allow for a survey and categorisation of the motivations underwriting these appointments (economic, legal or social), and they reveal useful distinctions for the further study of the contracting parties both on an individual level as well as in relation to their group identities (ecclesiastical versus secular, social stratum, etc.).

Introduction 

31 

include the Venetian patricians, Dalmatian nobilities[56] and members of the clergy, which, for practical purposes, include both natural and legal persons, such as Zadar’s archbishopric, Nin’s bishopric and the city’s monastic congregations. These pages also contain a section on the nobility and their interactions with the Church based on an analysis of testaments and codicils. Consideration is furthermore directed at how the city’s three main congregations – the Benedictines, Dominicans and Franciscans – interacted with these individuals of privileged descent[57] and the commercially active elites, which consisted of merchants, property owners and spice traders. Based on selected examples, the various actors and commodities are analysed and discussed. 

Chapter 4, entitled “Zadar’s Interwar Property Markets”, examines the developments of real estate transactions between 1540 and 1569. Scholars have identified a general downward trend of Zadar’s transactions following the reestablishment of Venetian rule in 1409. This study calls into question that uniform downward trend. Following a brief introduction based on Tomislav Raukar’s extensive work on late mediaeval Dalmatia,[58] this segment analyses real estate transactions based on 1,772 individual notarial deeds detailing sales, (planting) concessions, and rental or leasehold transactions. The results show an uptick in activity especially during the latter half of the three decades between 1540 and 1569, hence calling any blanket assessments into question. Given the near-total absence of comparative data for the decades before and after this 30-year period, however, the conclusions must be viewed with caution due to a lack of comparative data for earlier and subsequent decades. That said, such empirical analyses go a long way toward addressing these particular lacunae, as well as the more general desideratum – rather, a gaping (black) hole – of economic history.[59] 

Shifting gears, Chapter 5 reverts the focus back to the intersections of social and crime history, with particular attention paid to the role and function of the city’s

Urban Elites of Zadar 

32 

intellectual elites. While the available literature on both topics has established the general rules that governed Venice’s Dalmatian possessions, I wish to highlight in particular the exemplary studies by Lena Sadovski and Fabian Kümmeler. There exist few long-form enquiries into the ways and means, as well as the nooks and crannies, of the administration of law under the conditions of Venetian rule.[60] These pages seek to address these lacunae by pursuing three distinct objectives: most existing studies are little more than a listing of case studies; hence the point of departure here is to outline several ways to move beyond any one individual. In a second step, quantitative and qualitative analyses of extrajudicial settlements show yet another hitherto un-researched aspect of Venetian rule in the Adriatic, which connects their study to what Benoît Garnot and others called “infra-justice, parajustice, and extra-justice”.[61] Two detailed case studies of legal disputes – one revolving around the construction of a latrine along a shared wall, and the other detailing an inheritance quarrel among cousins whose documented case extended from the late 15[th] century all the way to the mid-1560s – break new ground in the field and round off this chapter. 

The final three chapters focus on Zadar’s social and societal fabric at the micro level. Chapter 6 addresses the geographical and social mobility of Zadar’s foreigners, examining how they integrated into noble circles. The city’s nobles preferred marriage alliances with families of aristocratic descent from elsewhere in Dalmatia, but they also considered partners from further away. This section is based on analysis of 656 marriage-related contracts (i.e., matrimony contracts, dowry settlements and pertinent quitclaims) to gain a detailed understanding of what Neven Budak termed the “self-identity of the Dalmatian urban nobility” regarding their willingness to let their sons and daughters marry into other aristocratic circles.[62] A brief glance at the material culture of the upper social stratum concludes the section, thereby extending the research carried out by Zoran Ladić, Valerija Turk-Presečki and especially Branka Grbavac from the Dalmatian Middle Ages to the Renaissance.[63]

Introduction 

33 

Chapter 7 discusses “other” elite groups – identified by their geographical and social descent, educational attainment[64] or religious affiliation – and their interactions with, and integration into, Zadar’s society around the mid-16[th] century. These specifically refer to Venetians, the city’s “elite citizens” (Grubb) and the Croat and Jewish communities, which were small but important.[65] These forays demonstrate that, while in the mid-16[th] century the legal and social ties between the western Balkan hinterlands and the coastal cities were weaker than in earlier centuries, they did not cease altogether. 

Shifting gears and focus, Chapter 8 discusses the notion of “topographies of everyday life” and seeks to cross-relate and correlate personal, geographical and social information.[66] Using the quantitative information derived from several thousand individual notarial deeds and from over 1,000 testaments and codicils, I undertake a detailed, if first of its kind, look at individual connections, their relation to Zadar’s urban landscape, explicitly including its intramural (sub) divisions, demographic distribution (e.g., merchant quarters, artisans’ parishes and the locations where nobles dwelled) and use of public and private spaces. 

The seven research-based chapters of this book – two of which present new research undertaken after the publication of the first edition of this study – document life in the 30 years between two Ottoman-Venetian wars as they were experienced by Zadar’s inhabitants. They investigate economic developments, geography and social relations on five levels. The first examines the geographical ranges and ties of Zadar’s 16[th] -century inhabitants, which extended over both shores of the Adriatic and included the hinterlands of the Western Balkans. The second level offers an economic case study of the city’s jurisdiction (Zadar proper and its subject territories and subdivisions). Third, the intersections of crime and social history, as well as of Venetian authority and rule, is approached via the study of extrajudicial settlements to break new ground, while, on a fourth plane, this study also enters the street level by way of analysing official reports and notarial deeds within the same framework. While the former, in Sally McKee’s words, “provide a moving image”, the latter “furnish the soundtrack of the city’s bustle, thus bringing the scene closer to life than either set of sources would do on their own”.[67] On a fifth plane, data compiled from all the extent notarial deeds preserved in Croatian State Archive’s Zadar branch offers a strong spatialgeographical element to the economic, legal, political and social history aspects,

Urban Elites of Zadar 

34 

thus permitting the historian to not merely “look” at and “listen” to this past society but also to “retrace” the steps of individuals and groups through the streets of Zadar and across the salty waters of the Adriatic. 

Even so, the fact that the present study is based on archival material from the Croatian State Archive in Zadar and the editions by Simeon Ljubić and Grga Novak[68] means that its scope is confined to certain areas of central Dalmatia.[69] While I have worked consciously to overcome these limits – mainly by adding dense sections on spatial aspects – future research into the activities and interactions of Zadar’s urban elites should, ideally, work comparatively and include evidence from both shores of the Renaissance Adriatic, as well as make good use of the vast amounts of material preserved in the various other Dalmatian archives.[70] For too long the rich histories of both occidental and oriental shores have been subject to closely delimited and limiting interpretations that artificially divide a past _linked_ , not separated, by the salty waters of the Adriatic, if not the Mediterranean. In this sense, this book constitutes my contribution to the overcoming of decades of artificially separated historiographies that all speak to centuries of an essentially shared history.

## 1. The Setting 

The central paradox in Venetian history lies in the sharp contrast between the tendency of Venetians both to represent and to think about themselves in terms of fixed categories and the underlying realit[ies] of economic, social, and geographic fluidity.[1] 

John Martin, Dennis Romano 

Since the appearance of the first edition of the present study a little over than ten years ago, at least some advances in terms of primary source-rich analyses of the Venetian Adriatic have been made. Most efforts have gone into the study of the city and society of Split (Spalato), of which the studies by Ermanno Orlando, Lena Sadovski, Mario Nepo Kuzmanić, Marina Marasović-Alujević and Tonia Andrić deserve notice.[2] Comparatively less attention has been paid to smaller places, hence the importance of the research by Oliver Schmitt and Fabian Kümmeler on Korčula, by Lena Sadovski on Poljica (Poglizza), Omiš (Almissa) and Klis (Clissa), and by Ana Plosnić on Trogir (Traù).[3] Related to Renaissance Dalmatia are the equally important studies by Luca Zenobi on the _terraferma_ , by Erin Maglaque on Istria and the Aegean,[4] the studies by Meri Kunčić on mediaeval Rab as well as those by Mirna Zeman on 18[th] -century German travelogues talking about “the Illyrians”.[5] Then and now, Dubrovnik and its very rich history virtually co-exists with the study of Venice’s _stato da mar_ .[6] 

As regards overviews and synthetic accounts, there is much less novelty to acknowledge, with the renewed push to “imperialise” the Venetian commonwealth

once more at the heart of these developments, as Benjamin Arbel and Monique O’Connell in particular have proposed; both did so in 2013 and have remained thus beyond the first edition of my book.[7] Note, however, that while I include them here, no significant changes to the subsequent pages have been necessary, in particular due to their emphases on political and cultural aspects to the detriment of social and economic history, although the wonderful collection of essays in Viella’s new series _Storia delle Venezie_ constitutes a more than welcome – and hopefully turnaround – in these regards.[8] Moreover, and unlike Cold War-era usages of term and concept, “empire” nowadays comes without the arguably bad reputation and ideological baggage deriving from Marxist scholarly practice (pun intended); as it happens, I tend to agree with Georg Christ and Franz Julius Morche, who notably consider the “concept of Venice as an empire [to be] of rather limited use Whatever the reasons for in assessing the specificities of Venetian overseas rule”.[9] the sustained use of the term and concept, there is simply too little substance in it to warrant a change on my part in this regard. Put succinctly, the Venetians exercised _imperium_ over social formations and in territorial contexts that were subordinate to the Republic of St Mark. If in doubt, the briefest glimpse across the contested and ill-defined borders with the Ottomans or Habsburgs should suffice to demonstrate that, whatever Renaissance Venice _was_ , the authority wielded by its ruling elites was different from that of its neighbours.[10] 

## 1. _Venice’s maritime state, 1358-1570_ 

The Republic of St Mark emerged as a major Adriatic power around the turn of the first millennium, and her success and wealth soon became the envy of her neighbours.[11] Due to the events of the Fourth Crusade, the subsequent conquest of Constantinople in 1204-1205 and the establishment of the Latin Empire, Venice ended up in control of vast expanses of the eastern Mediterranean.[12] For the next

8. Barbiera, Dalla Zuanna, Zannini, _Popolazioni e società_ ; Bianchi, Panciera, _Governi e forme della politica_ .

<!-- page 38 -->

The Setting 

37 

century and a half the Republic of St Mark enjoyed her position of pre-eminence – dubbed her “imperial age” by Chambers – and managed to hold her foes at bay.[13] Her first overseas acquisitions along the eastern shore of the  Adriatic, however, led to a severe setback in the mid-14[th] century. In the wake of the Black Death, an exhausted Venetian Republic faced an array of enemies and in 1358 was forced to cede her Dalmatian territories to Louis I of Hungary. Signed in the Franciscan monastery of Zadar, the treaty provided for the independence of Dubrovnik and placed the other Venetian possessions under the suzerainty of the victorious king.[14] 

The second main adversary was Genoa, against which the Venetians repeatedly fought in the first half of the 1350s and whose attempts to achieve commercial supremacy in the eastern Mediterranean led to another war two decades later.[15] The Genoese, well-established in the resurrected Byzantine Empire and in control of the lucrative Black Sea trade, renewed hostilities in 1379.[16] Fighting initially arose over control of the tiny island of Tenedos (today Bozcaada, Bozdja-Ada) – strategic because of its location at the southern entrance of the Dardanelles – and soon spread across vast stretches of the Mediterranean.[17] In the ensuing war, and in a strategic departure from previous conflicts, the Genoese fleet advanced into the Adriatic. 

Seeking to prevent Venice’s access to vital food and supplies, the Genoese landed an expeditionary force near the town of Chioggia, which controlled access to the Venetian lagoon. In this perilous hour, the Venetians were able to hold their ground, rally their forces and fight back by virtue of a statewide mobilisation of manpower, supplies and morale. The gruesome fight for Chioggia continued for almost a year, but by the summer of 1380 the mortal threat to Venice’s lifelines had receded, and the tide began to turn. Although the war raged on until both parties were exhausted, a settlement was negotiated in Turin in 1381. Unlike her Genoese rival, whose society descended into fractional strife and internecine conflict, Venice was able to quickly reconstitute her economy and finances. At this point, the Republic of St Mark controlled only the large island of Crete and some minor footholds in the Aegean.[18]

On Crete, see McKee, _Uncommon Dominion_ .

<!-- page 39 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

38 

The decade after the peace of Turin witnessed two major events in the history of the Adriatic. Within a few years, Venice re-emerged as a major power by taking over Corfu and several minor possessions close by. She added Nafplio (Nauplia), the Cyclades and the large island of Euboea (Negroponte) to her maritime state around 1390. The second development had an even greater impact on the eastern Mediterranean. As the Venetians renewed expansion, the Ottomans advanced deep into the Balkans, reached the fields of Kosovo and defeated the Serbian host mustered against them.[19] In the aftermath of the battle, many realms in southeastern Europe and the neighbouring Aegean felt the Ottoman pressure mounting, requiring a reorientation of their foreign affairs. In addition, many communities and regions along both shores of the Adriatic became involved in the struggle for the Crown of St Stephen.[20] 

In combination, these events contributed to the expansion of Venice into areas previously out of her reach. In the last decade of the 14[th] century the Republic of St Mark acquired a few cities along the Adriatic’s southeastern coast, securing the towns of Durrës (Durazzo), Lëzhe (Alessio), Shkodër (Scutari) and Drisht (Drivasto). These acquisitions led to the organisation of the Venetian province of Albania after 1392,[21] which would likely been quite impossible without the convergence of these developments.[22] 

The 15[th] century began under even more promising auspices. As Tamerlane led his armies westward, his forces were met by the Ottoman host outside Ankara in 1402. The Mongols soundly defeated their enemy and took prisoner Sultan Bayezid I, who died in captivity shortly thereafter. This plunged the Ottoman realm into a tumultuous succession crisis that lasted for more than a decade.[23] Against this background, Venice, for a time unperturbed by the  Ottomans, continued her expansion in the Adriatic and Aegean. The cities of Bar (Antivari), Budva (Budua) and Ulcinj (Dulcigno) were added to her Albanian province in 1405, followed by Naupactus (Nafpaktos) and Patras (Pátra) in 1408 and 1409, respectively. The next large territorial acquisitions occurred in the same year, although the focus of Venetian expansionism had shifted towards the Adriatic’s eastern coast.

I, pp. 28-40; Werner, _Geburt einer Großmacht_ , pp. 116-179. See also Kreiser, _Der Osmanische Staat_ ; Goffman, _Ottoman Empire_ . 20. Engel, _Realm of St Stephen_ , pp. 195-243. I, pp. 28-40; Werner, _Geburt einer Großmacht_ , pp. 180-218. For a detailed discussion of Venice and the Ottoman advance, see Setton, _The Papacy and the Levant_ , vol. II, pp. 1-38.

<!-- page 40 -->

The Setting 

39 

Half a century earlier Louis I of Hungary had driven the Venetians from the Dalmatian cities and forced them to recognise his claims, enlarging his vast domains even further.[24] Soon after his death in 1382, however, his patrimony disintegrated and descended into a long and bloody succession conflict among the various contenders for the Crown of St Stephen.[25] This conflict had not been resolved by 1409, the year in which Ladislaus of Naples, the last male of the senior Angevin line and titular king of Hungary since 1390, sold his hereditary claims to Dalmatia and his remaining Dalmatian possessions to Venice for the sum of 100,000 ducats.[26] The Republic of St Mark accepted, eager to reassert her influence over the eastern shores of the Adriatic in order to provide additional security to her mercantile shipping. Thus, the major islands in the Kvarner Gulf (Quarnero), as well as the cities of Zadar and Nin (Nona), came under Venetian rule again.[27] Over the following decades Venice extended her hold over the Adriatic by acquiring Šibenik (Sebenico) in 1412, followed by Pag (Pago), Vrana (Aurana), Trogir (Traù), Split (Spalato), Omiš (Almissa), Kotor (Cattaro) and the islands of Brač (Brazza), Vis (Lissa) and Korčula (Curzola) by 1420. Hvar (Lesina) was added the following year. The Serbian clan of the Paštrovići, ruling between Budva and Bar, accepted Venetian suzerainty in 1423.[28] These developments coincided with Venice’s brief occupation of Thessaloniki and her expansion into the Italian mainland.[29] 

The ensuing half-century until the first long Ottoman-Venetian war can be described as a period of consolidation of Venice’s previous gains, though these gains were not as numerous as before. Contemporaneously, other important events occurred that would shape the centuries to come.[20] After the end of the decade-long succession crisis following the battle of Ankara, Murad II consolidated Ottoman power and continued his predecessor’s expansive policies.

40 

His successor, Mehmed II, earned, in Franz Babinger’s words, the epithet “the Conqueror” – at least in Western historiography as among Ottomanists, he is also known as “the Lawgiver”[30] – because on 29 May 1453 his troops breached the walls of Constantinople and put an end to the Byzantine Empire.[31] The fall of “Christendom’s bulwark”[32] and the subsequent relocation of the Ottoman capital from Edirne to Istanbul had a profound, if not long-lasting, impact on Christian rulers.[33] The first to bear the brunt of the Ottoman onslaught were the minor realms in the western Balkans and the successor states of the Byzantine Empire in Trebizond and the Peloponnese.[34] 

The decade after the fall of Constantinople witnessed Ottoman expansion not only in the Aegean and Black Sea but also in the Balkan peninsula, where the conquerors subdued Serbia in 1459 and Bosnia in 1463, eventually reaching Albania.[35] In the midst of the Ottoman-Venetian antagonism (1463-1479) stood George Kastrioti Skanderbeg. At first, he opposed both the Ottomans and Venetians. From the late 1440s on, he allied with the Republic of St Mark and, subsequently, also with the Kingdom of Naples, commanding a loose alliance of local warlords defending their homelands.[36] In addition to her losses in Albania, Venice lost Euboea in this first, long war. But she was able to expand her maritime state elsewhere. Shortly after the outbreak of hostilities, she acquired Monemvasia (Malvasia) in the Peloponnese in 1464.[37] And well into the second

decade of the war the Senate managed to install Caterina Corner (Cornaro) as queen of Cyprus (1473), paving the way for the eventual formal acquisition of the large island kingdom in 1489.[38] 

In the aftermath of the long Ottoman-Venetian war Venice was able to round off her possessions in Dalmatia by incorporating the large island of Krk in the Kvarner Gulf in 1480 and Zakynthos (Jacinto, Zante) two years later. In addition to these gains, Venice temporarily occupied positions in Apulia and the Aegean.[39] 

Peace between the Porte and the Republic of St Mark lasted merely two decades. War broke out anew before the end of the 15[th] century. This caused further Venetian territorial losses, but also minor gains.[40] Apart from Methoni and Koroni in the southern Peloponnese, dubbed the “eyes” of Venice,[41] the first large-scale naval encounter with the Ottomans, the Battle of Zonchio (1499, also known as the Battle of Sapienza or the First Battle of Lepanto) ended in defeat. The next year Venice took over Kefalonia and neighbouring Ithaca, successfully replacing the lost ports in the Peloponnese with the Ionian islands. The war dragged on for another two years without any other major events, and when the Sultan’s raiding parties reached Friuli over land routes, the Senate was ready to sue for peace. After a century of expansion, the conclusion of the war left Venice in possession of extensive territories but also weakened and with a stained reputation. Attempting to take advantage of the situation, Venice’s enemies, led by France and blessed by Pope Julius II, forged an alliance at Cambrai. The ensuing conflict almost destroyed the Republic of St Mark. But in the aftermath of her ignominious defeat at Agnadello (1509), the Venetians were again able to stem the tide and slowly fought back.[42] 

By the early 16[th] century the _stato da mar_ had reached its maximum territorial extent, coinciding with relative peace and stability in the eastern Mediterranean until the 1530s. In the meantime, decisive events occurred elsewhere. While the Portuguese established direct maritime trade routes with India by circumnavigating Africa, the Ottomans continued their expansion, most notably by conquering Syria and Egypt in 1516-1517, a “first glimpse of the future greatness” of the Porte.[43] Selim’s successor, Suleiman, acceded to the throne in in 1520, and at first

the new sultan’s ambitions focused on areas other than Venice’s maritime state,[44] before the larger contest between Emperor Charles V and French King François I (by virtue of the latter’s “impious alliance” with Suleiman) forced the Republic of St Mark to choose sides again.[45] 

The next two rounds of fighting between the Ottoman Empire and Venice – wars of “Holy Leagues” (1537-1540, 1570-1573) – resulted in significant losses to the _stato da mar_ . In the first conflict, Venice lost the Battle of Preveza (28 September 1538), and with it Monemvasia and Naupactus in the Peloponnese and some islands in the Aegean and Ionian seas.[46] Furthermore, while the Ottoman Empire reached its apogee over the ensuing decades, the Republic of Venice also lost the next war. Despite Christendom’s victory off of Lepanto on 7 October 1571, Venice could save neither the island kingdom of Cyprus, her largest and richest overseas territory, nor the Albanian cities of Bar and Ulcinj.[47] In addition to these losses, the borders close to Zadar were redrawn twice (in 1573 and 1576), eventually reducing the Venetian possessions in the city’s hinterlands to a mere strip of land “extending only three miles from the city”.[48] 

Venice and her overseas expansion from the 1380s onward cannot be considered outside of the context of the Ottoman Empire, especially after the end of the interregnum in the aftermath of the Battle of Ankara (1402). Both states quickly expanded into the power vacuum left by the moribund Byzantine Empire

and its even weaker neighbours in Anatolia and South-Eastern Europe. From the first Ottoman-Venetian war over Thessaloniki in the 1420s, the growing menace of the Sultans’ armies became painfully visible – at least to Venice’s subjects along the borders.[49] Nevertheless, the protracted Ottoman-Venetian war between 1463 and 1479 was fought only once the two powers had gobbled up all of the buffer realms between them.[50] 

Notwithstanding earlier developments, both Ottoman and Venetian expansion increased significantly in the early decades of the 15[th] century, taking advantage of developments elsewhere. In the aftermath of the peace of Turin, the Ottomans advanced over land and relied heavily on the military. Venice extended her dominions via the Mediterranean and employed a combination of diplomatic and military tactics. While in some cases these overall strategic circumstances aided Venetian expansionism, the enlargement of her maritime possessions cannot be attributed to them exclusively. In general, the Republic of St Mark pursued “extremely limited territorial ambitions”, preferring commercial over territorial expansion.[51] By exerting considerable cultural, economic, religious and social pressures, Venice eventually recreated many aspects of her domestic society abroad, a development sometimes called “Venetianisation” (Gaetano Cozzi, Michael Knapton) or “symbiosis” (Viktor Novak, Bariša Krekić), which contradicts the simplistic notion of “empire” and “colonies”.[52] We note, in passing, that mainstream Croatian scholarship – exemplified by the mediaevalist Tomislav Raukar and the (early) modernist Egidio Ivetić – does not use that kind of heavily connotated terminology, with the latter adding significantly to the debate by discussing “Venice as a civilization”.[53] In some cases, circumstance aided

Venetian expansion; there was the extinction of local ruling dynasties for instance, as when Venice annexed Zakynthos in 1482 after the local populace disposed of its ruler.[54] A number of Aegean islands, on the other hand, were already under indirect Venetian rule and more easily incorporated into her maritime state.[55] 

By taking advantage of the confusing circumstances in the western Balkans during the last quarter of the 14[th] century, the Republic of St Mark capitalised on the disintegration of the local and regional realms in Albania, Bosnia and Serbia.[56] Several communities along the southeastern coast of the Adriatic were acquired by military means,[57] and some came under Venetian suzerainty via marriage or inheritance.[58] By far the greatest prize was the large island of Cyprus, taken over by the _respublica_ after declaring Caterina Cornaro a “daughter of St Mark” and forcing her to abdicate in 1489. However, the Republic of St Mark did not immediately incorporate every commune that invited Venetian dominion to avoid conquest by the Ottoman Empire. This was seen in the years leading up to 1409 when Venice’s representatives managed to decrease the price for Ladislaus’ claims on Dalmatia by two-thirds.[59] 

Under all circumstances, the advantages and disadvantages were deliberated in the Senate before any action was taken. The rationales for the acquisition of new dependencies were mainly economic-strategic: potential acquisitions were considered for their commercial, diplomatic and military advantages. The consequences of occupying a city, its defensive needs once annexed and its possible integration into long-distance trading networks were of prime interest to the Venetians, as indicated by referrals to both “honour and profit” (although misquoted by Arbel and O’Connell) in some of the 15[th] - and 16[th] - century reports from Zadar, Trogir, Korčula and Šibenik.[60] While some places

immediately attracted Venice’s interest, others did not (but some of these were incorporated later).[61] In each case, once the decision was taken to expand the _respublica_ ’s commitments, formal treaties were drawn up. Usually their clauses ( _capituli_ ) codified the legal basis for Venetian rule but reaffirmed most of the existing privileges and rights of the local population. However, while the new suzerain generally adopted the preexisting normative and social order, amendments ( _reformationes_ ) were introduced to the various bodies of communal law. This provided for the smooth execution of Venice’s power and successful administrative by her representatives.[62] At times the costs of her expansionist mercantile ventures were assigned to individual patricians by means of concessions or feudal investiture subject to Venetian “protection”.[63] 

Venetian expansion into mainland Italy and the eastern Mediterranean followed similar patterns. Once a city had come under Venetian dominion, the new rulers introduced amendments to the existing mediaeval legal systems.[64] On a practical level, the means of acquisition employed in the Aegean Sea and in Friuli were essentially identical. Administrative, economic and fiscal differences were negligible, prompting Benjamin Arbel to posit that Venetian rule was unidimensionally inclined towards the centre, an assessment he repeated, if not reinforced, in his 2013 survey.[65] Venice’s representatives were invested with their

new suzerainty by the local authorities, and in return the _respublica_ guaranteed (with primary consideration of her own interests) most of the privileges and rights of the local elites and the established social order. On the organisational level, the widespread Venetian possessions formed a large entity in which, wherever possible, the hinterlands provided the agricultural basis for the coastal communities. Commercially, Corfu – from whose harbour the trading convoys ( _mude_ ) spread out into the Mediterranean and beyond – was of the utmost importance.[66] The enactment of additional legislation to cement Venetian rule was a routine practice after the assumption of administrative, economic and military control in the _stato da mar_ . 

## 2. _Administration_ 

Venice’s commercial and territorial expansion into mainland Italy and in the eastern Mediterranean after 1381 precipitated several administrative and institutional changes. By the 1440s the nominal boundaries between the _terraferma_ and the maritime state were drawn up, demarcating the two entities as possessions northwest and southeast of Istria.[67] Local or regional differences notwithstanding, the underlying structural principles of government applied throughout all of her dependencies. Within the Venetian Signoria the territorial expansion resulted in the enlargement of existing offices and the creation of new ones to accommodate economic, fiscal and legal developments. Over the 15[th] century, the power of the ruling patrician merchant elite of Venice became more and more confined to a small circle of wealthy and politically influential families. Contemporaneously, the need to provide public offices for the increasing number of impoverished patricians grew larger. The newly acquired territories in the _terraferma_ and her maritime state provided Venice with the opportunity to employ her less successful nobles. Around the turn of the 16[th] century a range of new offices in public administration was created. These served the dual purpose of alleviating the situation of the 

pp. 22-33. While esp. Arbel’s terminology remained virtually identical ( _impero_ /empire, _colonie_ /colonies) from his 1996 essay to the 2013 update, the latter contains several telling terms and formulations, e.g., there is little, if any, qualitative or otherwise judgemental jargon in his “Colonie d’oltremare”, esp. pp. 954-955, which contrasts strongly with his “Venice’s Maritime Empire”, p. 127 (my emphasis), which highlights the “cultural and physical milieus” found overseas “that were considerably different from the Venetian one” and which are said to have caused “ _estrangement_ …experienced by Venetians”. This laden term also appears a second time in the same context, for this ideologically-laden terminology (found also in “The Communist Manifesto”) cannot do with but one side. Hence, it seems, his invocation of “a sense of estrangement among both subjects and governors in the _stato da mar_ ”.

Arbel, “Colonie d’oltremare”, p. 954; Cozzi, “Ambiente veneziano, ambiente veneto”, p. 302; Arbel, “Venice’s Maritime Empire”, p. 148.

<!-- page 48 -->

The Setting 

47 

poorer patricians and preventing the most disillusioned nobles from becoming too rebellious, thus further cementing the oligarchic rule of the Signoria.[68] 

The _stato da mar_ consisted of various sub-regions: the two large islands of Crete and Cyprus; the dominions in the Aegean and the Peloponnese; the Ionian possessions; and the Adriatic components, organised in the dual province of Dalmatia and Albania. These entities were bound to Venice by several factors: defence against the Ottoman Empire, the integration of local legal institutions and nobles into the Venetian administration and economy, and the Church. 

Occasional raids by bandits, corsairs and pirates threatened the security of the _stato da mar_ .[69] Outlooks, manned watch posts and fortified towers along the coasts were commissioned to alarm the naval forces to fend off potential marauders.[70] Most cities and towns under Venetian rule had to muster one or more warships. However, the mainstay against these incursions was the light cavalry. From the 14[th] century onward, these so-called _stratioti_ – recruited mostly among the Albanians and Greeks – were highly mobile and their members over time integrated themselves into the societies of the territories they were defending.[71] 

Except for the War of the League of Cambrai, however, the Ottoman Empire was by far the gravest threat to Venice’s security – and especially to her overseas possessions. This is particularly evident in the sheer number of reports written by her overseas representatives, which almost exclusively described external threats.[72] Fearing situations analogous to the temporary territorial losses in the _terraferma_ during the War of the League of Cambrai, Venice invested increasing amounts of money, supplies and personnel into gigantic fortifications throughout her possessions abroad.[73] For instance, between January of 1568 and July of 1569 Zadar alone received 27,000 ducats to be invested in strengthening the

city’s defences.[74] These fortifications were even bigger than before in order to accommodate large numbers of the hinterland’s population in the event of an emergency.[75] Usually manned by the local militia,[76] in times of war reinforcements of mercenaries became additional burdens on the cities.[77] Venetian galleys were often manned with sailors and oarsmen – so-called _galeotti_ of Albanian, Dalmatian, and Greek origin – adding further financial strain to the overseas possessions.[78] 

In addition to providing this substantial defensive buffer, the maritime state was important for Venice, and by extension the rest of Christendom, because of the information the overseas possessions and consulates provided about the Ottoman Empire and its advances. At the first sign of the mobilisation of the sultan’s armies or fleets, the merchants and public officials sent word to Venice. Once the information network picked up speed, the Signoria found itself flooded with news, true or false.[79] 

In religious matters, the Republic of St Mark employed a policy of relative freedom of worship, partially because most of her subjects in the _stato da mar_ did not adhere to Catholicism.[80] The largest group of non-Catholics was of Orthodox faith – mostly Greeks – though their clergy was subject to the Catholic dioceses. The Venetian dominions were the only territories in which the attempted

In the lower strata and among the population of the hinterlands, Albanian and Slavic culture prevailed.[82] Despite this policy of relative freedom of worship, the Signoria was forced at times to intervene to prevent too much religious zealotry on the part of the Catholic clergy.[83] 

## 3. _Economy_ 

In the decades after the peace of Turin, Venice came to dominate the first “world economy” (Braudel), roughly circumscribed by Lisbon, Fez, Damascus, Azov and the Hanseatic city of Bruges.[84] The major advantages enjoyed by the Republic of St Mark in comparison to her Genoese rivals were found in the greater coherence and resilience of her society, and particularly the patriciate. Civil unrest and factional strife for domestic supremacy described Genoa’s late mediaeval experience. Conversely, such descriptions were much rarer in the Venetian Republic. In addition, the latter had a second crucial commercial tool at her disposal: a reliable, state-run convoy system.[85] These so-called _mude_ had fixed dates to call at the ports along their routes and sailed to the most important harbours in the eastern and western Mediterranean and to the centres of trade in southern England and Flanders.[86]

<!-- page 51 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

50 

A combination of underlying maritime structures and improvements enabled Venice to earn most of her riches via maritime commerce until the turn of the 16[th] century. Moreover, cogs and galleys increased steadily in size and cargo volume over time, with medium-sized vessels usually employed in regional transportation. Further improvements in the state-owned galleys, the mainstay of Venice’s merchant and military marine power, guaranteed the _respublica_ ’s competitive edge, at least until the turn of the 16[th] century.[87] The nexus between Venice’s enterprising elites and the Serenissima’s political-territorial ambitions in the eastern Mediterranean is evident from the geographical extent of her long-distance trading network. The coastal cities of the _stato da mar_ did not just provide safe harbours, supplies, fresh water and food;[88] places of supra-regional importance like Crete, Cyprus, Zakynthos and Zadar served as homeports for large numbers of local seafarers who constituted a readily available reserve pool of experienced sailors eligible for conscription in wartime.[89] 

One of the cornerstones of Venetian wealth and power was the salt trade, which had been subject to continuous monopolisation efforts by the state since the Middle Ages. Already contributing to the _respublica_ ’s opulence during her “Imperial Age” (Chambers), Venice’s expansion into mainland Italy significantly increased the salt income over the late 14[th] and 15[th] centuries.[90] Economically and financially, Venice aimed for the self-sufficiency of her possessions and dependencies. If excess income was available, it was sometimes sent to another dominion as a subsidy.[91] From the War of Chioggia to the Cyprus War, the _stato da mar_ was highly profitable, offering secure ports of call, trading posts with familiar structures and ample reservoirs of revenues and manpower. The 

87. Cogs were used in transport en route, as well as end-point delivery. Many privatelyowned vessels transported bulk goods like corn from the Black Sea, cotton and sugar from Cyprus, or wine from Crete.
