51 

enormous sums of money offered to the Ottoman Empire after the losses of Euboea in 1470 (250,000 ducats) and Cyprus in 1573 (an annual tribute of 100,000) testify to the economic importance of Venice’s maritime state.[92] Thus, Venetian investment into overseas expansion paid off in that it generated “payments in excess of the cost”, at least until the end of the Cyprus War,[93] although one of the core weaknesses of the literature on these matters should be noted, too: while mention is typically made of economic developments, transfer payments and even the evaluation of taxes and wealth, as the recent work by Alfani and Di Tullio shows, no recent study exists that ties together Venice proper, the republic’s financing of foreign adventures, and the massive investments and transfers of money, matériel and people required to expand and construct fortifications to fend off the Ottomans.[94] We further note, in passing, that these developments occurred _after_ the “flight from the sea” precipitated a drastic shift within the Venetian economy away from overseas mercantile endeavours and towards production on the _terraferma_ , as shown by the massive construction boom of villas and landed estates on the mainland between 1500 and 1800, which is another aspect that is often overlooked.[95] 

Throughout the Renaissance, the protagonists of Venice’s commercialexpansionist endeavours were the city’s patricians, in whose hands the organisation of the government and the most lucrative trading ventures were concentrated. The cargo bays of the state-owned galleys were auctioned off to the highest bidder, and the successful bidder became the patron of the vessel, obliged to finance the journey in advance. But he was also able to choose his merchant companions, who usually consisted of close relatives. Eventually, this fed into the aggregation of Venice’s mercantile capital, wealth and political power in the hands of a small number of patrician families, especially during the later decades of the 15[th] and beginning of the 16[th] centuries. A domestic consequence was that what had once

Urban Elites of Zadar 

52 

been a broad-based aristocratic enterprise gave way to a de facto monopolisation of maritime commerce.[96] In combination with the more challenging foreign environment after the 1450s, these factors contributed to the decline of Venetian mercantile shipping. Even the reorganisation of the Arsenal in the first decades of the 16[th] century[97] could not prevent the slow, inexorable shift of the commercial centre of gravity from the Mediterranean to the Atlantic. This same period saw the end of an epoch in which Venice’s patricians acted as a unified body politic on behalf of their commercial interests and that of the state, which led Ugo Tucci to conclude that “the personal interests of the merchant no longer coincided with that of the public good”.[98] 

By the dawn of the 16[th] century, Venetian society and the state-run convoy system faced new realities. Yet, despite repeated armed conflicts and economic woes, the domestic cohesion of the Republic of St Mark did not falter, even during the difficult months in the aftermath of her ignominious defeat at Agnadello (1509). By the time the crisis of the War of the League of Cambrai had passed, however, Venice had changed. Maritime trade no longer exclusively dominated her fortunes, and the Mediterranean steadily lost ground as the prime source of Venice’s opulence. A combination of geographical, political and technical disadvantages contributed to the demise of this region’s economic position and the increasing marginalisation of Venice’s maritime state over the course of the 16[th] century as manufacturing and the _terraferma_ state became more relevant.[99] But the Signoria addressed the manifold changes in its environs with continuous innovations. Though by the middle of the 16[th] century her once-favoured trading position with the Ottoman Empire had eroded significantly, Venice was able to compensate for the decline of her maritime fortunes. The rise in the manufacturing of luxury goods and improvements in her mainland possessions became increasingly important economic and political factors. 

96. In today’s terminology, such a construct can be labelled a “holding”. During the 15[th] century, it was known as _fraterna_ . It described an economic unit based on kinship ties comprising at least two brothers who were equal legal partners, even without preestablished notarial paperwork. Consequently, this construction enabled individual wealthy families to acquire most of the available cargo volumes, eventually concentrating much of Venice’s economic power in the hands of a limited number of patrician families. Chojnacki, “Kinship Ties and Young Patricians”, p. 246; Doumerc, “Dominio del mare”, pp. 151-154. See also Lane, “Family Partnerships and Joint Ventures”.

98. Tucci, _Mercanti, navi e monete_ , p. 58.

On societal and political changes, see Chojnacki, “Identity and Ideology”; Finlay, _Politics in Renaissance Venice_ , pp. 203-204; Muir, _Civic Ritual_ , pp. 167172; Murano, “La festa Veneziana”; O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ , pp. 57-74; Queller, _Venetian Patriciate_ , pp. 51-112.

## 4. _The Adriatic context_ 

The aftermath of the Chioggia War witnessed a renewed wave of Venetian expansion into the eastern Mediterranean. The Republic of St Mark intervened in the Hungarian succession crisis to reestablish her authority over most Dalmatian and Albanian harbours from the late 14[th] century onward. This secured the vital shipping routes along the oriental seaboard of the Adriatic.[100] The jurisdictions of the absorbed cities included the smaller towns and villages on the mainland and most of the coastal islands. Venice’s Adriatic dominion covered most communities from Butrint (Butrinto) in present-day southern Albania to the large islands in the Kvarner Gulf.[101] Despite several superficial differences among the cities and towns along the oriental littoral of the Adriatic, their underlying structures were similar. All communities exerted layered forms of authority over their surroundings, both on the mainland and the coastal islands. Many of the cities were also sees of Catholic (arch)bishoprics. Upon their (sometimes involuntary) incorporation into the _stato da mar_ , the coastal communes were reorganised according to Venetian interests and eventually consolidated into the dual province of Dalmatia-Albania. Its nominal capital, the city of Zadar, commanded a comparatively large hinterland that included the minor fortified towns of Nadin and Vrana and the minor districts of Nin and Novigrad.[102] After the initial purchase of the rights to Dalmatia in 1409, Venetian expansion progressed gradually; Šibenik was incorporated in 1412, Split was absorbed in 1420, Omiš followed two decades later, and with the takeover of Cres in the Kvarner Gulf in 1480, the reestablishment of Venetian rule over Dalmatia – with the exception of Dubrovnik – was accomplished.[103] 

These developments cannot be separated from Venetian rule elsewhere on the Apennine peninsula and in the eastern Mediterranean, and the maritime dimensions of the _stato da mar_ in its entirety should always be kept in mind. The driving force of the merchant aristocracy of the Rialto was the security of the vital navigation routes along the eastern Adriatic coastline.[104] Venice’s “extremely limited territorial ambitions” signified no particular interest in continued expansion into the hinterlands of the western Balkans.[105] While the reestablishment of her authority over the coastal areas of Dalmatia and Albania progressed without much trouble,

from the 1410s onward a new opponent emerged from the Balkans: the Ottomans.[106] The low-lying hinterland of Zadar enabled frequent cross-border raids that, after the first incursions into the district of Nin in the early 1430s, placed considerable strains on agricultural production.[107] At the same time, the waning capabilities of Hungary-Croatia to defend the western Balkans against Ottoman advances led to While the Adriatic remained at recurring conflict between the Porte and Venice.[108] the periphery of the Ottoman-Venetian conflicts during most of the 15[th] century, the coastal cities of Dalmatia were nonetheless important, especially considering Venice’s supply lines and trade routes:[109] “he who does not preserve the city of Zara”, the returning governor Marc’Antonio da Mula wrote in his _relazione_ in the 1540s, “loses not only great authority over the sea but control over the entire Gulf [i.e., the Adriatic]”.[110] 

After the first Ottoman-Venetian war over Thessaloniki in the 1420s, Mehmed II renewed the war in 1463, seeking to conquer and pacify parts of Albania. In September of 1468 his troops raided the jurisdictions of Split, Šibenik and Zadar. While the cities were not immediately threatened, the raids led to increased investments in their fortifications.[111] The subsequent wars between the Ottoman Empire and Venice witnessed further reductions in the agriculturally important hinterlands of many Dalmatians cities. Fighting was mainly concentrated around strategic positions, many of which were established during Hungarian rule.[112] One of these, Klis (Clissa), annexed by the Ottoman Empire in 1537, became the centre of regional administration for the eponymous district ( _sanjak_ ) after the conclusion of the Cyprus War.[113] Only through her maritime power was Venice able to prevent all-out Ottoman assaults on the coastal cities.[114]

<!-- page 56 -->

The Setting 

55 

These conflicts constituted an inescapable part of everyday life, the hardships of which were compounded by the ambiguous and ill-respected borders.[115] In the aftermath of the Cyprus War, these borders were (re)drawn, at times even in the absence of Venetian representatives.[116] Given Venice’s primary interest in keeping the sea lanes open, those who were most disadvantaged were the urban communities and their inhabitants. The local nobles lost the greater part of their incomes deriving from landed property, and the rural population and their livestock either fled the hinterlands or were captured, resettled somewhere across the borders or sold into slavery.[117] One of Zadar’s former captains, Zaccaria Vallaresso, wrote in 1527 that “every day the Turks are outside the gates of Zara” and that continuous agriculture had become impossible without armed guards.[118] Those who remained continued to cultivate their fields, causing additional ongoing friction between the inhabitants on both sides of the borders.[119] As the borders moved closer to the city walls during the 1570s – almost within shouting distance of the ramparts[120] – many inhabitants All chose to emigrate or to move to the security of the fortified urban centres.[121] these changes had a profound impact on agricultural production within Zadar’s jurisdiction. While livestock farming prevailed on the coastal islands, many villages were forced to abandon their fields, irrigation networks and vineyards, resulting in the disruption of agriculture.[122] By the mid-16[th] century the only

This in turn caused retaliatory attacks by Venetian subjects and vice-versa. The Uskoks, who continuously harassed the Ottoman subjects, thus further compounding the problematic situation, presented another problem. This resulted, as Mayhew noted, in situations eerily familiar to later centuries along the similarly disputed Habsburg-Ottoman borders in Hungary: without the deployment of artillery, and with less than 5,000 soldiers, incursions were not considered a _casus belli_ either. Urban Elites of Zadar 

56 

agricultural export left was wine, even though the cultivation of grapes was considerably more labour-intensive.[123] 

After the Cyprus War, Venice continued her policy of neutrality regarding the Ottoman Empire and enacted legislation to prevent her subjects from settling too close to the borders.[124] But these efforts and another round of border revisions (1626) did not resolve the underlying problems caused by insufficient arable land close to the Dalmatian cities. Another factor was the massive influx of funds used for the enlargement of the fortifications, and consequently the expansion of their garrisons, which resulted in additional costs to the fiscal chambers of Dalmatia’s cities. While the Venetians had to import biscuits ( _biscotti_ ), foodstuffs, grains and hay, the Ottomans were able to employ the much greater resources of the coastal hinterlands.[125] Despite the considerable territorial contraction of the _stato da mar_ over the course of the 16[th] century, the maritime state grew ever more demanding in human and other resources, placing increasing pressure on Venice’s finances.[126] Since these expenditures had to be financed at least partially by the coastal communities themselves, surplus wealth was often transferred within the _stato da mar_ , depriving the economically viable communes of available capital.[127] 

## 5. _“Zara is the metropolis and key”_[128] 

The territorial jurisdiction of Zadar was reconstituted by Venice in the years after 1409. This jurisdiction encompassed the city proper and its suburban settlements ( _burgus_ , _borgo_ , _suburbs_ ), part of the continental mainland including the fortified towns of Nin and Novigrad, and several coastal islands. By the mid16[th] century, despite the territorial losses sustained in the Ottoman-Venetian war 

123. Giovanni Battista Giustiniano wrote that wine was sold “to the foreigners in good quantities”. He also noted that much potential for export-oriented olive oil production existed in the vicinity of Zadar – but also that the olive trees, cut down during the Ottoman-Venetian war of 1537-1540, had not been replanted. Consequently, “there is little oil production here, and much of it is obtained from Apulia”, thus allegedly depriving the fiscal chamber of Zadar of 25,000 ducats per year. _Commissiones_ , vol. Arbel, “Colonie d’oltremare”, p. 960; Bacchion, _Dominio veneto_ , pp. 53-54; Malz, “Dalmatinische Städtewelt”, pp. 108-111; and the report by Venetian syndics Leonardo Venier and Hieronymo Contarini from 1525 via _Commissiones_ , vol. II, pp. 9-34, with notes on Zadar at pp. 11-13.

<!-- page 58 -->

The Setting 

57 

of 1537-1540, Zadar’s jurisdiction comprised 37 islands and 85 minor villages.[129] Moreover, despite the additional losses that resulted from the Cyprus War, the basic administrative structures dating back to the Middle Ages were preserved.[130] 

The natural borders of the mainland possessions were the Adriatic, the Krka (Cherca) river basin to the southeast and the Velebit-Dinara ranges (Morlachia, Montagna della Morlacca) to the northeast.[131] The geophysical properties of Zadar’s jurisdiction consist mostly of karst, forming the Bukovica plateau, an elevation averaging between 250 and 300 metres above sea level. Below the southern slopes of the Velebit massif, between the Bay of Karin and the river Krka, lies the flat valley of Ravni kotari. The coastal areas along the Velebitski kanal, composed mostly of limestone and karst, belonged to Zadar’s mediaeval jurisdiction too. These conditions impacted habitation and land use, in combination with the availability of fresh water and arable lands.[132] Red soil ( _terra rossa_ ) was one of the defining characteristics of Zadar’s continental hinterlands. The lowest areas surrounded the town of Nin and its eponymous bay, which was – and still is – used to produce salt. 

These differing qualities of the mainland territory formed the basis for two types of agriculture. Red soil was exploited for the cultivation of Mediterranean crops like grains, grapevines and rye. The karst areas, with their vegetation of low shrub ( _macchia_ ), were used for animal husbandry, mostly by the transhumance peoples (seasonal shepherds).[133] These structural characteristics had shaped the lives and livelihoods of the inhabitants of the area since antiquity. Fertile soil and fresh water meant that most settlements were concentrated in one area. As in other regions of the Mediterranean, many creeks were only seasonal; the longer and permanent streams were used to power mills.[134]

133. Šarić, “Lika and Krbava”, p. 248.

The agriculturally productive regions with their fertile soil are located around Karin, Nin, Novigrad and Posedarje. The area of Ravni kotari, as Mayhew pointed out, attractive to immigrants since antiquity, was reestablished as a rich agriculturally productive region in the 20[th] century – with the war in former Yugoslavia in the 1990s having the same effect as conflicts in previous centuries. Archaeological evidence suggests that many places in the area were inhabited before the Roman era. Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 94-96; Suić, _Antički grad_ , pp. 95; Suić, _Zadar u starom vijeku_ , pp. 18, 95.

<!-- page 59 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

58 

While human settlements in Zadar’s hinterlands date back 5,000 years, the Romans were the first to systematically change the landscape. By turning the fertile areas into plantations of mostly grapes and olives, and by constructing aqueducts and irrigation canals, they developed the existing villages.[135] In the Middle Ages there was a renewed use of the remnants of Roman castles, roads and villages around the Catholic bishoprics of Nin, Skradin and Zadar.[136] It was only after Venice regained control of the area that a first cadastre was compiled in 1420, surveying and enlarging the jurisdiction with the incorporation of the minor districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana.[137] 

With the ensuing string of wars between the Ottoman Empire and Venice, the former’s marauders continuously exerted sustained pressure on the coastal communities. At first these incursions were temporary, as was migration to the perceived safety of the Dalmatian islands. As the raids occurred on a more regular basis, the continuous Ottoman-induced insecurity effected significant changes.[138] Large numbers of inhabitants were killed, robbed of their livestock or sold into slavery. Many left the hinterlands, abandoning their villages and maintenance of the roads and irrigation systems.[139] The places that were not abandoned – Nadin, Novigrad, Tinj, Vrana and Zemunik – were reinforced, quickly repopulated and adapted for military purposes. This further changed the cultural landscape of Zadar’s surroundings.[140] 

The losses of Nadin and Vrana during the Ottoman-Venetian war from 1537-1540 triggered a new wave of emigration, even though parts of the rural

The Setting 

59 

population chose to live under the sultan’s rule.[141] Under these circumstances, survival became the defining factor of everyday life as continuous agriculture became all but impossible. This precarious situation in the rural hinterlands of Zadar was further compounded by the outcome of the Cyprus War (1570-1573): the Venetians razed the suburban dwellings to make way for new earthworks, fortifications and ramparts.[142] And two rounds of border demarcations during the 1570s left the Ottomans in control of most of the agriculturally productive areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction. By the time the new frontiers were agreed upon in 1576, only those settlements close to fortified places or guard towers were still inhabited. Those who still engaged in agriculture did so within a couple hundred metres of the city walls, causing additional problems like erosion, loss of topsoil and an increase in real estate prices.[143] 

## 6. _Zadar under Venetian rule (1409-1570)_ 

Venetian rule from the late 14[th] century was, in principle, based on additions and amendments to existing administrative and legal institutions. As Gaetano Cozzi and others have argued, this was the case for newly-acquired possessions both in mainland Italy and the eastern Mediterranean.[144] The mediaeval Byzantine organisation and adherence to Orthodoxy increased the complexities of Latin rule in the eastern Mediterranean. 

In central Dalmatia, the territory of Zadar’s jurisdiction had previously belonged to three subdivisions: the central and northeastern parts to the county of Luka, the area extending from Biograd na moru to the southeast toward Šibenik to Sidraga county, and the territory between here and the county of Nin.[145] After 1409 the Venetians decided to keep these mediaeval divisions and focused on amending the legal framework to suit their authority.[146] The main changes concerned the 

141. Those who remained under Venetian rule stayed in their villages, if they could be defended. Examples included Ljubač, Posedarje and Radovin. In the case of Ražanac, the inhabitants moved their village to a more defensible location nearby. On real estate prices in the 15[th] century, see Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 151-196; and Chapter 4.

On Istria, Ivetić, _Oltremare_ , pp. 21-47; and Viggiano, “Amministrazione veneziana”. On Dalmatia, see Maštrović, _Razvoj sudstva_ , pp. 11-17.

<!-- page 61 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

60 

districts of Ljubač, Novigrad and Vrana. They were incorporated into the overall jurisdiction of Zadar, which by then also included Nin and its district. The subject territories were considered state property under the control of Zadar’s fiscal chamber. Its officials publicly auctioned their use to the highest bidder to raise revenues ( _incantum_ , _incanto_ ), which was the main reason Venice kept these mediaeval structures in place.[147] The border areas with the Hungarian-Croatian kingdom were demarcated by a number of fortified places, usually commanded by Croat nobles.[148] Yet, despite the increasing Ottoman threat, no dedicated frontier zone was established until the late 16[th] century.[149] 

The three minor districts and several coastal islands were subject to Zadar’s count ( _comes_ , _conte_ ), who embodied the supreme civilian-judicial authority. He was assisted by the city’s military captain ( _capiteneus_ , _capitano_ ). These two Venetian officials, usually patricians of lower status and wealth, nominally outranked the governors and military commanders in Dalmatia and Albania.[150] Together they administered an area that exceeded the city’s territory ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ).[151] Control over the minor districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana was in the hands of their respective castellans. The following discussion of territorial divisions applies a different terminology than the one established by Tea Mayhew. _Contado_ , a term “with slightly indefinable meaning”, as Mayhew admitted, has been replaced by “jurisdiction”. This is to reflect the fact that after 1409 the authority of Zadar’s count exceeded its mediaeval dimension. “Territory” refers to the immediate surroundings of the urban centre, which in the Middle Ages were known as _ager publicus_ or _territorium_ . “District” is used for the minor territorial entities subject to Zadar’s authority, such as Nin, Novigrad and Vrana.[152] 

Over the course of the 15[th] century Venice made efforts to unify most of Dalmatia’s cities to facilitate their defence and establish a streamlined administration, although no efforts were made to consolidate the various units and Dalmatia, which, until the French and subsequent Habsburg take-over around 1800, “in reality never constituted a unit […], nor were they territorially connected”.[153] After the Republic of St Mark regained control, it established offices to safeguard its strategic interests. The safety and security of the vital navigational

As regards the minor districts, these appear as _dictio_ or _districtus_ throughout the sources, and for them the English translation “district” has been chosen. 

153. Starting with reciprocal guarantees of assistance in the event of armed conflict, Venice gradually bound together the various cities along the Adriatic’s eastern coast. For the quote, Krekić, “Developed Autonomy”, p. 185. See also Pederin, _Mletačka uprava_ , p. 17.

61 

routes were of the utmost importance.[154] The two supra-regional authorities were the Overseer-General (Provveditore generale in Dalmazia e Albania) and the Captain-General (Capitano generale), who spent most of their tours of duty aboard military vessels supervising the various communities.[155] The other functions of these provincial officials included enforcing Venetian rule: upholding the law, conducting low-level negotiations with the Ottoman administrators across the borders and providing sufficient foodstuffs. 

The next administrative level consisted of the governors of the cities and towns. Depending on the importance and size of the possession, they were assisted by military personnel.[156] These posts, reserved for Venetian patricians of lower rank and wealth, provided the officeholders with a certain amount of social stature in the Dalmatian towns.[157] In general, the new suzerain respected the cities’ local autonomy, but the inhabitants of the subject communities had no influence on the election of these officials. Zadar’s count was considered the first among his peers to govern Dalmatia’s cities. By virtue of his office, he also filled in for the provincial _Provveditore_ during the latter’s absence and led diplomatic negotiations with neighbouring provincial officials or Ottoman legates from the other side of the borders.[158] 

Outside the walls, the authority of Zadar’s count extended over the city’s entire jurisdiction, including the subdivisions of the minor fortified towns of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana (although these places were commanded by military personnel, not civilian officials).[159] Apart from the count, Zadar proper hosted the

Urban Elites of Zadar 

62 

captain ( _capitaneus_ , _capitano_ ) and his administrative apparatus, thus separating civilian legislative-judicial administration from matters related to military security.[160] In addition to these two offices, possibilities existed for the political participation of local urban elites (although not with any significant executive authority). The office of Zadar’s count employed a gastald ( _gastaldus_ ) and four public heralds ( _praecones_ ), paid out of the communal fiscal chamber. Their duties included the execution of the count’s orders, public announcements, overseeing public auctions and ecclesiastical obligations.[161] The count’s chancellery ( _cancellaria comitis_ ) organised the day-to-day paperwork, including the officeholder’s correspondence with Venice. Despite the geographical distances involved, provincial governors enjoyed only limited autonomy from the Signoria.[162] 

The subject cities also enjoyed limited autonomy within Venice’s maritime state (and limited economic power to back up such ambitions).[163] By taking advantage of the social conflicts between the cities’ nobilities and commoners, the Republic of St Mark was able to take on the role of “honest broker”, further strengthening her position. In the case of Zadar, Venice was sympathetic toward the commoners.[164] The count or captain presided over gatherings of local inhabitants

164. Especially given the city’s seven prior rebellions against Venice in the 13[th] and 14[th] centuries, concurred Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , p. 163; Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ ,

The Setting 

63 

consisting of nobles, citizens ( _cives_ ) and residents ( _habitatores_ ). The commoners, united in their stance against the nobles, constantly disagreed with them.[165] These gatherings, though lacking political clout, offered the most prestigious positions available to Zadar’s urban nobility under Venetian rule.[166] Comprising around 70 individuals around the mid-16[th] century, the council was the body from which four councillors ( _consiliarii comitis_ ) were elected every three months.[167] These councillors were permitted to offer advice in civil proceedings to the presiding count or captain, but the officeholder was in no way bound to follow their advice.[168] 

In criminal proceedings, cases involving Venetians or her subjects from other parts of her dominions, or cases of a particularly grave nature – Venice’s syndics explicitly mention “extortion, grand theft, rape, sedition and other atrocious instances” – the count “does as he pleases, without consideration of their [the councillors] opinion [orig. fa, quanto gli piace, senza tor il parere d’essi]”.[169] The legal system was founded on communal statutes, common law and case precedent.[170] If none of these were applicable or were not in the best interests of the Republic of St Mark, the count judged according to his conscience.[171] In case of disagreement with the court’s findings, there existed the possibility of petitioning the Court of Appeals (Quarantia) in Venice.[172] In these cases, the appeals had to be considered in light of local law, not Venetian

Urban Elites of Zadar 

64 

legal norms and practices.[173] Appeals of lower institutions, such as brotherhoods or guilds ( _schola_ , _fratalea_ ),[174] were handled by the count personally.[175] In combination with the necessary centralisation of power with the officeholders during wartime, these factors detached the officeholders further from the urban citizenry.[176] 

Given Venice’s lack of interest in extending her rule over the coastal hinterlands, the rural organisation of Zadar’s jurisdiction was able to retain comparatively more autonomy within the _stato da mar_ .[177] The office of judge ( _iudex_ ) in the villages was unpaid, although some territorial privileges could be obtained.[178] After 1537, these officials could be obliged to unpaid public works but were exempted from military service.[179] From the mid-15[th] century onwards, it was these judges who first dealt with the waves of migration, such as the large numbers of people fleeing the Ottoman advances that made their first appearance in Venetian reports in the 1520s. These movements resulted in increased pressure on the towns and villages along the Dalmatian coast.[180] As far as possible, those newcomers who stayed were integrated into the economic and social framework of Zadar’s jurisdiction. Many of them settled in or around the coastal cities and were employed to work in the fields, producing vegetables, fruits and olives close to Zadar while grain was harvested further inland.[181] 

The legal basis of agricultural production continued to be rents ( _livellum_ ), land grants ( _livellatio_ ) and planting concessions ( _concessio_ , _pastinatio_ ) to colonists.[182] Legally binding contracts were drawn up by the city’s notaries according to the customary law, and, like all other deeds, they were validated by communal public officials in the presence of at least two witnesses. These documents granted the farmer-labourer use-rights to property for a certain amount of cash ( _affictus_ , _locatio_ ) or payment in kind of produce ( _concessio_ , _pastinatio_ ). In addition, the statutes provided the option to re-rent or grant

livestock, real estate or other property to a third party ( _conductio_ ).[183] They also contained detailed provisions for harvesting and transporting the produce,[184] as well as other complimentary clauses.[185] Over the course of the 16[th] century, there emerged a tendency toward the cultivation of one half of a field, leaving the other half for pastureland; ploughed and fallow halves were swapped every year.[186] In addition to the stipulated share of the harvest belonging to the landlord, special gifts ( _honorantiae_ ) had to be consigned to the proprietor.[187] If the landlord altered the provisions (excess of duties, change of transport location, etc.), the colonist – meant here in the sense of the Latin term _colonus_ , i.e., a tenant – could resell, re-rent or leave his or her obligations in accordance with the provisions of the communal statutes.[188] 

Viewed together, the changes introduced by Venice over the course of the 15[th] century were considerable. While Dalmatia’s cities enjoyed a relatively high degree of autonomy under the previous Hungarian suzerainty, the Republic of St Mark incorporated her new subject societies into her more centralised and monopolised economic system. While not without benefits,[189] the obligation to recognise Venice’s staple rights from the 1420s onward constituted a continuous drag on the local economy.[190] Efforts to monopolise the lucrative salt trade were especially grave,[191] given the Dalmatian cities’ reliance on it to balance

Urban Elites of Zadar 

66 

their budgets.[192] Although the artisans were less affected, commerce and trade also declined after 1409. The hinterlands’ produce – fabrics, honey, raisins, wax and wool[193] – continued to arrive in Zadar and the other coastal cities, but in decreasing quantities. While migration originating in the western Balkans subsided, Ottoman expansion pushed the borders close to the city walls, depriving the urban communities of their agriculturally important hinterland and eventually culminated in the crisis of the 16[th] century. Even though Zadar’s port remained the most important centre of exchange in central Dalmatia, around 1500 the entire region had become “economically insignificant”.[194] 

In combination with the deteriorating situation in the coastal cities’ rural hinterlands, these factors negatively impacted Dalmatian everyday life in a variety of ways, most importantly in terms of cattle theft, robberies and enslavement. These facets of living in Dalmatia had long existed but had become much more serious in the decades leading up to the Cyprus War.[195] Consequently, organised cross-border theft – whose frequency testifies to the weak governance of both the Ottomans and Venice in their respective peripheries – was punished more severely, usually with death.[196] Capture and enslavement became a common experience for inhabitants of the Mediterranean.[197] If contacted by the recently enslaved, family members, relatives or business partners attempted to raise ransom money, which was at least partially refundable by the Venetian government.[198] 

After 1409, Zadar under Venetian rule continued to exist as a typical Dalmatian coastal community with mediaeval commercial, economic and social organisation. These, however, were amended by the new suzerain’s administrative and cultural influences. The fortified towns of Ljubač, Nin, Novigrad and Vrana on the mainland were subject to the city’s jurisdiction, as were numerous islands off the coast. As the Ottoman Empire continued its expansion, life along the frontiers of Venice’s Adriatic dominions became increasingly difficult. But the common enemy held the social strata together and made it easier for the _respublica_ ’s representatives to provide a certain amount of stability during the eventful 16[th] century.

## 2. Zadar’s Society: Spatial, Social and Occupational Fault Lines 

If the government’s records of its deliberations, proclamations, and court records provide a moving image of [Zadar], notarial records furnish the soundtrack of the city’s bustle, thus bringing the scene closer to life than either set of sources would do on their own.[1] 

Sally McKee 

Over the half-century following the peace of Zadar (1358), Dalmatia’s coastal communities and their jurisdictions were integrated into the larger economic and political framework of the domains of Louis I of Hungary (r. 1342-1382).[2] Louis’s rule encouraged business with Florentine bankers and merchants, resulting in increased trans-Adriatic commerce and investment.[3] Given Dalmatia’s geographic location between Florence and Hungary, nobles and commoners alike invested in real estate and trade. Italian merchants and financiers cooperated with local businesses in salt production, trade and the brisk transfer of raw materials.[4] 

By the beginning of the 15[th] century, Zadar had gained prominence through its salt production and textile trade. Its economy was rivalled only by Dubrovnik.[5] In accordance with local practices, and for security reasons, parts of the profits were invested in real estate.[6] The decades following Louis’s death in 1382 were marked by succession conflicts, contributing to the weakness of the HungaroCroatian realm, first felt at its periphery. As the fight between the Houses of Anjou

<!-- page 69 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

68 

and Luxembourg over the Crown of St Stephen intensified, Venice was among the external powers to take advantage of the situation. Eager to regain control over the important coastal cities along the eastern Adriatic, the _respublica_ incorporated the communes into her more centralised system of government.[7] 

A continuous slowdown in economic activities occurred during the period leading up to the first long Ottoman-Venetian war (1463-1479). Venice’s attempt to enforce her staple rights from the 1420s onwards, specifically for salt, set the stage for this escalation.[8] A second main cause of the economic downturn was external. Ever since the armies of Mehmed II had stormed the ramparts of Constantinople in 1453, the Ottoman Empire had continued its seemingly inexorable westward expansion. Because of their sustained pressure, agricultural production of the coastal hinterlands of the Balkans declined, and so did transAdriatic maritime trade. Thus, due to both external and internal factors, Dalmatia had become “economically insignificant” by the turn of the 16[th] century.[9] 

Recent urban historiography of the region has reached beyond analysis of societal microcosms to emphasise the bigger picture of interactions between urban centres and their subject territories (but note that the early modern period is clearly underresearched compared to its ancient, mediaeval and modern iterations).[10] Peregrine Horden and Nicolas Purcell stressed that maritime dominion was in fact “a network of communications” and that the corresponding trade routes formed an “essential aspect of Mediterranean power at every period”.[11] This presents possibilities for focusing, for another moment, less on traditional indicators of socioeconomic change and more on local, regional and supra-regional interconnectedness.[12] By this is meant the connections between an urban centre and its jurisdiction (local), interactions among a group of neighbouring cities (regional) and networks of regions within the emerging European world economy (supra-regional).[13]

I hold that neither commercial nor economic connections alone could account for “modernisation”, especially in the early modern period and considering the indicators of “modern societies” listed by Malz: “democracy, [a] market economy, and an open society”. See also Ringrose, “Urbanization and Modernization”, who argues for more research to be directed at cities within the context of their environs (including roads, migration patterns and banking), which relates to “the underlying reality [ _sic_ ] of economic, social, and geographical fluidity” of Venetian society noted by Martin, Romano, “Venice Reconsidered”, p. 21.

<!-- page 70 -->

Zadar’s Society 

69 

At the local level, the number of inhabitants and the size of its jurisdiction determined a city’s economic importance. In the 14[th] century, Zadar proper encompassed around 28 hectares. The adjacent suburban settlement extended over roughly 18 hectares.[14] In the subsequent two centuries, continuous immigration, originating in the hinterlands of the western Balkans, contributed significantly to the population growth of Dalmatia’s coastal cities and necessitated the enlargement of the suburbs.[15] This tendency was initially reinforced by Ottoman expansion into the western Balkans, causing additional incentives to flee to the more secure coastal areas or beyond the sea. Over time, the continuous influx of newcomers from the Bosnian and Croatian hinterlands markedly altered the social fabric along both shores of the Adriatic. As early as the first decades of the 15[th] century, parts of Zadar’s elites had left for cities along the eastern littoral of the Apennine peninsula.[16] The continuous integration of immigrants into Dalmatia’s communes and their jurisdictions resulted in cities that exhibited a more agrarian character.[17] 

During the century-long crisis from the Ottoman-Venetian war over Albania (1463-1479) to the conflict over Cyprus (1570-1573), the _respublica_ ’s control in (over) the eastern Mediterranean decreased significantly. The latter conflict, while ostensibly over the large island kingdom, drastically altered this situation elsewhere, too, as most of Venice’s possessions along the eastern coast of the Adriatic were reduced to small strips of land. In this period Venetian officials wrote about abandoned villages, banditry, pestilence and the Ottoman threat as major factors responsible for the continued depopulation of vast stretches of coastal hinterland.[18] As a consequence of these conditions, Dalmatia’s cities remained relatively small, economically constrained and demographically static through the late mediaeval and early modern periods, especially compared to wider European trends.[19] 

## 1. _Zadar and trans-Adriatic communication_ 

Speaking very broadly, Zadar’s fortunes followed these trends. According to the detailed report by Zaccaria Vallaresso from 1527, around 8,000 people lived within the city walls and the suburban settlements. Zadar’s jurisdiction on the mainland comprised roughly another 9,000 inhabitants, and the islands were home

to some 7,000 people.[20] The war from 1537-1540 and the ensuing territorial losses exacerbated an already problematic security situation, rendering the inhabitants of Zadar’s jurisdiction unable to remain agriculturally self-sufficient from the 1530s onward.[21] As the 16[th] century came to a close, the urban population had dropped to some 5,200 people and to around 9,200 on the islands, mostly concentrated on the two larger islands of Pašman (Pasmano) and Ugljan (Ugliano).[22] Those who still migrated toward the coastal areas continued to alter demographic and economic patterns. Most newcomers after the Cyprus War were Catholic and Orthodox refugees from the Bosnian and Croatian hinterlands who reinforced The urban the shift away from agriculture to livestock breeding and fishing.[23] populations, therefore, became increasingly dependent on imported foodstuffs, further straining the little surplus capital available.[24] 

Most of these developments were exogenous, but significant changes occurred within the Republic of St Mark. To secure her position in the long term, Venice began expanded existing and constructing new fortifications around the turn of the 16[th] century.[25] Zadar’s mediaeval castle was located in the southern part of the city to facilitate naval support or evacuation in an emergency.[26] As the 15[th] century gave way to the 16[th] , advances in military technology and weaponry necessitated renewed investment. Renowned architect Michele Sanmicheli, who oversaw the fortification efforts on the northeastern defences guarding the harbour, started the first new projects in Zadar in 1537.[27] These works are still clearly visible within Zadar’s urban landscape today, yet the associated demographic shifts and economic impact, such as the influx of military personnel and artisans (carpenters, masons, stonecutters, etc.), are less well documented.[28] A third wave of fortification

efforts was precipitated by the outbreak of the Cyprus War, leading to the razing of the suburban settlements to enable the construction of new, gigantic defensive structures under the supervision of Sforza Pallavicino.[29] 

For most of their early mediaeval past, Dalmatia’s cities had been (at least nominally) under Byzantine suzerainty. As that empire’s might began to wane over the course of the 11[th] century, the cities along the eastern shore of the Adriatic reoriented themselves. Consequently, the previously interconnected coastal areas and hinterlands of the western Balkans became fragmented.[30] During the Angevin dominion (1358-1409), central Dalmatia, owing to its advantageous location between Hungary and the southern parts of Italy, functioned as a turnover hub for commercial and financial transactions. Certainly exchange – mercantile or otherwise – between the coastal areas and their hinterlands continued after the advent of the second Venetian dominion, but on a considerably smaller scale.[31] Shortly after 1409, Venice amended Zadar’s statutes to enforce the former’s staple rights, only to repeal the legislation not long afterward.[32] However, when it was reinstated about a century later (in 1519), Venetian prerogatives were extended and (theoretically) covered the entire _stato da mar_ .[33] The increase of export duties

The most important river systems in the western Balkans are the Krka (Cherca) and Neretva (Narenta) in Dalmatia and the Bojana (Boiana) and Drin (Drina) in Albania. See also Lampe, “Redefining Balkan Backwardness”, pp. 179-181.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

72 

on certain goods[34] and the favouring of Šibenik during the first half of the 16[th] century caused the redirection of trade routes.[35] When Venice followed Daniel Rodriga’s suggestions and sought to enlarge Split’s role as a free harbour after the Cyprus War, commodity flows were redirected again.[36] 

Venetian policy, though a considerable constraint on the economic development of Adriatic commerce after 1409, never stopped commercial exchange between the coastal communities within and without the borders of the _stato da mar_ . Qualitative analysis of customs receipts ( _contralittere_ ) by Novak and Raukar has demonstrated, to the extent possible due to the (un)availability of source materials, the range and scale of these interactions.[37] This proven approach notwithstanding, export licenses describe only _economic_ connections between certain places. If we are to further our understanding of the “remarkable geographical mobility” so characteristic of Venetian society, other sources must be considered.[38] 

## 2. Procura _networks in central Dalmatia_ 

To gain a more nuanced picture of Zadar’s urban elites, it is necessary to investigate their economic, geographic and social activities. The incentives for, and ranges of, interconnectedness in the early modern Adriatic can be quantitatively discerned in two forms of documentation: export licenses and procuratorial appointments ( _procurae_ ).[39] Analysis of the export licenses, if

Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 253-257.

39. A _procura_ describes the unilateral conferment of legal powers, with or without explicit mandate as to the duties of the executing party, the so-called _procurator_ . These powers, if not assigned for a specific period or task, typically lasted until the death of one or both contracting

<!-- page 74 -->

Zadar’s Society 

73 

available in reasonable numbers, is convenient because they are accessible and enable us to reconstruct the documented elements of certain economic activities. But their limited scope and fragmentary survival renders their study methodically tricky and delimits their analytical value to certain spaces and periods.[40] Procuratorial appointments, by contrast, are more inclusive in terms of information about the contracting parties’ economic and social markers, their geographical provenance and destinations, and often specify the reasons for drawing up the contract. 

The following analysis is based on 930 individual procuratorial contracts, written between 1540 and 1569. The analysis is designed to further our understanding of the mechanisms of communication between the local Dalmatian elites, foreign dignitaries and the rest of the populace.[41] While the acts are not a “silver bullet” source, they permit the historian to study, in a more inclusive manner, early modern mobility in terms of the economic, educational, geographical, occupational and social diversity of the contracting parties. In this sense, they are eminently suited for analysis of communication activities across several social and/or functional groups. 

Individuals, groups and institutions bestowed legal powers upon their agents via formal contractual obligations drawn up by a notary. Between standardised formulas at the beginning and end, the contracts contain information about the social and geographical provenance of both stipulating parties. In addition, around three-fourths of the procuratorial appointments include descriptions of the assigned duties.[42] When combined, these clues allow for the reconstruction of mechanisms of communication within the _stato da mar_ and its Adriatic components, in particular. Given the adherence of Dalmatia’s cities to common economic, legal, monetary and social structures, the findings support the designation of the _stato da mar_ as a commonwealth.[43] The

43. Both Saint-Guillain and Schmitt speak of a “sphere of communication” ( _Kommunikationsraum_ ), defined as a geographical entity characterised by the exchange of ideas, individuals and goods occurring in an order of magnitude distinctively larger if compared to adjacent (theoretical) entities. As this at best implicitly includes a shared cultural-legal foundation, it is a partially helpful proxy. See Schmitt, “Südosteuropa als Kommunikationsraum”, pp. 78-79; Saint-Guillain and Schmitt, “Die Ägäis als Kommunikationsraum”, p. 217. Note that

<!-- page 75 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

74 

following pages contain a discussion of the conclusions drawn from analysis of the 930 procuratorial documents written in Zadar between 1 January 1540 and 31 December 1569.[44] In addition to possibilities for quantitative analysis, their abundance and uniformity enable the reconstruction and description of communication means in more intimate ways.[45] 

Qualitative analysis of the procuratorial assignments suggests three categories of individual contractual obligations: economic, legal and social. Any task referring to business, commercial or mercantile endeavours is considered an _economic_ assignment. These include orders to buy, rent or sell goods or property, and mandates to collect or invest money somewhere outside of Zadar and its jurisdiction. Involvement in judicial processes or legal proceedings (e.g., the execution of a testament, representation in a court of law or the appointment of a lawyer) denotes a _legal_ field of activity. Finally, since many assignments concern a variety of issues occurring outside of Venice’s maritime state, certain _social_ characteristics complete the analytical framework. It must be stressed, however, that these three aspects constitute a tentative model for the qualitative assessment of the interactions of early modern urban elites since in many cases distinctions among economic, legal and social intents of the contracting parties cannot be clearly separated from each other. 

Another advantage of these documents is that they allow the reconstruction of the essential communication networks underlying such assignments.[46] According to the model proposed by Arié Malz, these networks shall be assessed on three levels: local, regional and supra-regional.[47] As summarised above, _local_ describes activities occurring within Zadar’s jurisdiction, i.e. the city proper, the minor subject districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana, and the islands and mainland territories. _Regional_ denotes activities within Venice’s Adriatic dual province. _Supra-regional_ relates to connections within the wider Mediterranean (see also Table 2.2). 

a clear-cut academic definition of such a “sphere of communication” exists neither in German nor in English.

The most immediate revelation of the documentation is that the total number of procuratorial contracts increased by roughly two-thirds over the course of the three decades between 1540 and the outbreak of the Cyprus War in 1570.[48] The average number of procuratorial acts stipulated per decade was 310, and women ordered a quarter of these.[49] Despite these numbers, only a meagre 2% of the procuratorial appointees were female.

Ancona, Alta Badia (alla Badia), Bari, Bologna, Castel Sant’Angelo, Ferrara, Genoa, Vasto (Guasto), Manfredonia, the Marche region, Milan, Parma, Pescara, Pisauro, Pontremoli, Rimini, Rivellino, Rome, Tarvisio and Vicenza; (d) gives the no. of contracting parties originating in Venice proper and the _terraferma_ , specifically Bergamo, Chioggia, Cividale del Friuli, Crema, Murano, San Grande del Friuli, Trecenta, Udine and the City of Venice; (e) gives the no. of contracting parties originating elsewhere, unspecific, unlisted, infrequently-mentioned (e.g., Rijeka, Hungary) or otherwise non-specific locations (e.g., Croatia, Crete [Candia] and Cyprus). 

As Table 2.2 indicates, four out of five contracting parties originated within Zadar’s jurisdiction. Since this territorial entity included several subdivisions, it is worth taking a closer look at the origins of these individuals, institutions and groups from an administrative-juridical perspective. As Table 2.3 demonstrates, the number of constituent parties residing within the city walls of Zadar increased by more than 10% from the 1540s to the 1560s. Since the procuratorial records initiated at the request of individuals originating from outside the fortified city centre declined correspondingly, this phenomenon can be explained by increasing depopulation and insecurity. It is likely that this trend continued well past the conclusion of hostilities in 1573, and it did so for two main reasons: first, the Ottoman gains during the conflict, affirmed by the border revisions in its aftermath, were significant. Apart from Cyprus (the immediate cause of the war), large swaths of territory formerly subject to Zadar’s jurisdiction now belonged to the Ottomans.[50] Second, the city’s suburban settlements were razed in 1570 to allow for the construction of additional massive fortifications, which further reduced the number of individuals residing outside of Zadar’s city walls (Table 2.3).

Before focusing on the individuals and their assignments, we shall investigate the geographical destinations of the procuratorial appointees. The data suggest that large parts of the economic, legal and social activities originating in the _stato da mar_ were directed towards Venice proper. This reflects the changes in policies pursued by the Republic of St Mark, most clearly visible in economic matters. Economically, Venice was the most important city in the Adriatic during the 16[th] century.[51]

As Table 2.4 demonstrates, neither the flow of goods and commodities nor analysis of procuratorial data alone offers a full picture of communication networks.[52] There (may) have been unofficial voyages not recorded or, more simply, undertaken illegally. Notwithstanding the fragmentary state of research, however, it is safe to state that Venice made up roughly a quarter of all known procuratorial destinations out of Zadar between 1540 and 1569. When viewed from Venice, the dual province constituted one and the same entity. When viewed considering the numbers in Table 2.5, this clear-cut picture changes significantly.[53] 

Despite their joint organisation within the _stato da mar_ , procuratorial ties between Zadar and, say, the referenced cities in Albania were virtually nonexistent. When based on procuratorial data, communication on the regional level, by contrast, occurred in all but nine instances _within_ Dalmatia itself. This therefore necessitates a more detailed discussion. 

As indicated by Table 2.5, the available procuratorial data suggest the existence of three sub-regional areas of increased interactions. By far the greatest number of appointees was destined for action within Zadar’s jurisdiction. This accounts for slightly more than half of all procurators (205 instances or c. 54%). If combined with the directly neighbouring towns of Pag and Šibenik, the share of central Dalmatia increases to more than seven out of ten (272 instances or c. 71%) of all appointments. This suggests that these three places were more intensely intertwined than the other areas of Venice’s Adriatic possessions. This is reinforced 

connections and judicial exchange within Venice’s maritime state into a larger framework, especially since “communication” cannot be described by the sum of economic data alone. Also, since these data are usually incomplete, the term “sum” must be treated with care; “sample” is typically a more accurate description. 

53. Schmitt assumes the existence of two areas of denser, regional communication hubs in the Adriatic, extending roughly from Zadar via Šibenik to Split, as well as another further south between Kotor and Durrës (in the Ionian Sea, he proposes that another communication hub existed in the triangle between Corfu, Naupactus and Patras). Schmitt, “Südosteuropa als Kommunikationsraum”, p. 85.

<!-- page 80 -->

Zadar’s Society 

79 

by the fact that the areas to the northwest and southeast attracted approximately the same share of procuratorial appointees operating out of Zadar. The large island communities in the Kvarner Gulf – Krk, Cres, Osor and Rab – attracted 44 appointees or a combined share of c. 12% of the procuratorial appointees. All places to Šibenik’s southeast were the destination of 66 procurators or c. 17% (this comprises Trogir, Split, Omiš, the island communities of Brač, Hvar, Vis and Korčula, and the three Albanian cities of Bar, Kotor and Perast). These numbers suggest the existence of an area of increased _procura_ -related connectivity, as well as associated communication and exchange in central Dalmatia extending roughly from Pag via Zadar to Šibenik.[54]

## 3. _Economic, legal and social incentives_ 

Moving beyond quantitative assessments, new questions about the qualitative aspects of the procuratorial appointments are discussed here. As illustrated above, roughly three out of four notarial acts include the reason for their stipulation, categorised according to the stated aims of the constituent parties. Many acts describe economic activities unrelated to the social strata or geographical origins or destinations of the contracting parties. The most ubiquitous assignment was the recovery of outstanding money, as the following examples amply demonstrate. We learn of artisans who relocated and required the appointee to rent or sell real estate property in the constituents’ former city of residence. For instance, when “master-caulker Simon Grubissich son of the late Antonius de Jadra” moved to Chioggia, he appointed “master-cobbler Martino” to sell his house of stone and wood located in the southern parts of Zadar proper.[55] An identical request, although in the opposite direction, occurred when “master-oarsmaker [ _marangonus_ ] Johannes Galeacij of Venice, a resident [ _habitator_ ] of Zadar”, tasked “Franciscus son of the late Demetrius Eugenico of Naupactus” to rent out the constituent’s house “found in the nourishing [ _alma_ , add “mother”] city of the Venetians in St Anthony’s parish”.[56] 

Another interesting aspect of the _procurae_ is that virtually all appointments involving military personnel, their spouses or offspring as constituent parties have a common economic incentive: the collection of outstanding payment for military service.[57] Testifying to Venetian payment practices (and her questionable reliability), this motivation transcended all geographical origins and destinations, military ranks and social boundaries. Consider the following examples: in mid- 

> 55. Simon’s house was located “in St John’s parish, also known as blacksmiths’ quarter [in contrata fabrorum], on territory that belongs to the benefice of the venerable master [ _domini_ ] priest Grisogonus Cedulinus, canon of Zadar”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 8, s.p., 28 May 1541. 

> 56. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 4, f. 83r, 6 January 1556 (my addition). Note that this profession was so important that the Signoria restricted its performance to her expatriate citizens, notes Čoralić, “Ragusans in Venice”, p. 17. 

> 57. I have discussed military migration specifically in my article, “Soldaten der Serenissima”.

<!-- page 82 -->

Zadar’s Society 

81 

October of 1557, “Johannes Durcich of Osor”, a discharged oarsman, appointed “the reverend master [ _dominus_ ] Georgius Matassouich, archpriest of the rural parts of the diocese of Nin [ _archipresbyter ruralis_ ]” to recover outstanding payments for the former’s service – which had ended more than a year-anda-half before – on the “bireme commanded by the magnificent master Pietro Pisano”.[58] And then there was “mistress [ _dona_ ] Catherine daughter of the late magnificent knight [ _eques_ ] master George Rhenesi, and wife of the magnificent knight master Thomas Luxi”, who around the same time commanded a cavalry squadron. On behalf of her husband, and with his explicit license, she appointed an absent Venetian citizen, “the distinguished master Bartholomew Nigrus”, to finally collect Thomasius’ outstanding payments of 25 ducats per annum from the fiscal chamber of Crete.[59] 

In most cases unrelated to the military, the reasons for appointing a procurator to collect outstanding money were probably as mundane as the following case from autumn of 1556: “Stephanus Goycich, a butcher [ _macellator_ ] and resident” of Zadar had sold a quantity of pork, which had not been paid for. He therefore needed a procurator “to follow-up and obtain from Andrea dicto del Conte, a citizen of Krk, and _ser_ Francesco Badoer [Baduario] the sum of 29 lire and 8 soldi”. Evidently, Stephanus’ customers had not paid up, so the butcher assigned the task of collecting the outstanding money owed “for the remainder of the price of pork meat sold and appointed the Rev. Father Stephanus, minister of the province of the Third Order Regulars of St Francis”. The friar was already on his way to the island of Krk, and perhaps the constituent hoped his customers might be more willing to pay their debts if a man of the cloth came to collect the money.[60] 

Reasons for _legal_ appointments included custody duties after the death of one or both parents, succession disputes among siblings, neighbours or otherwise related individuals and representation in a court of law. In the first case, the appointment of one or more legal guardians for children not yet of legal age was usually stipulated in the constituent’s testament,[61] though the city’s statutes

HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 2, c. 78v, 15 October 1557. 59. 61. Zadar’s statutes provided distinctions between a variety of age categories: infants were minors up to the age of seven; girls under the age of twelve and boys under fourteen were required to have at least one legal guardian. The necessity of guardianship expired once the adolescents of both sexes completed their twentieth year. Book II, tit. IV, cap. 26: “Per quas personas infantes, ed est minores septem annis, debeant se tueri et in iudiciis placitare”;

<!-- page 83 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

82 

provided for the possibility of substituting the guardian with someone else.[62] Keeping this substitution provision open was important[63] since differences that might arise over who inherited what could lead to prolonged legal conflicts decades later.[64] Cases like these were rather uncommon in 16[th] -century Zadar, however, because the population was not very wealthy. Consequently, legal representation in a court of law and extrajudicial settlements – not inheritance concerns – were the predominant reasons for assigning procuratorial powers. 

The rationale behind these lawsuits ranged from (admittedly few) highprofile homicide cases[65] to much more common causes, most notably money. For 

cap. 27: “Per quas personas masculus maior quattuordecim annis et femina maior duodecim annis, minores tamen viginti annis debeant se tueri et in iudiciis placitare”: _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 146-148. Moreover, testamentary executors were required by law to transact on behalf of underage heirs, as per Book II, tit. IV, cap. 30: “Quod fideicommissarius generalis vel specialis relictus per testatorem vel creatus per Curiam alicui minori annis potest agere et in placitis respondere”. _Statuta Iadertina_ , p. 150. 

62. See Ref. 110: “De testamentis, tutoribus et commissariis”, _ibid_ ., p. 594. Some cases involved procurators who were appointed posthumously by the legal guardians of underage children to collect outstanding payments for military services rendered by the children’s respective father. This happened, for instance, when “Mattheus, Catherina, et Hellena filij quondam Simonis Panoeuich quondam Matthei seu Mathuli de Insula Exo [Iž] districtus Jadre facientes nominibus suis proprijs et vice ac nomine Georgij et Michaelis fratrium suorum in minori aetate existentium” appointed “celeberrimum dominum Johannem Franciscum Salomono quondam celeberrimi domini Laurentii patritium venetum”. The latter was to travel to Venice and collect the outstanding payments for the constituents’ late father’s service as oarsman “super Trireme supracomito Magnifico domino Manfredo Justiniano”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 7, s.p., 28 October 1565. 

63. All legal documents in Zadar had to be copied into the stipulating notary’s books in case of legal procedures or loss of the original instrument. If a notary left the city for periods exceeding two months, his instrument books had to be placed in the communal chancellery. Book II, tit. XVII: “De fide instrumentorum”, which contains 17 chapters. See also Ref. 156, “Quod iudices examinatores subscribere acta notariorum. De examinatione notariorum. Quod notarii absentes extra civitatem per duos menses debeant relinquere in cancellaria sua acta et prothocolla notarilia”. _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 206-218, 670. In the event of the outbreak of plague or any other contagious disease, exceptions were possible, as per Ref. 141, “Quod contractus confici possint tempore testis sine examinatoribus”, via _ibid_ ., pp. 642-644. 

64. In late 1561 a member of Zadar’s Tetrico family left a patch of land with a quantity of livestock on the island of Iž to his heirs whose legal rights to dispose of this property was promptly challenged by one of his cousins of the related Grisogono family. At the root of this conflict was a marriage contract from the late 15[th] century. The contract caused the feud between next-of-kin some 60 years later and lasted from the spring into the autumn of 1561. One of the city’s notaries, Simon Mazzarellus, filled two entire books with these proceedings. The proceedings include transcripts of the adversaries’ lawyers and copies of the relevant clauses from the statutes, as well as all other documentation pertaining to the case. See HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, books 3 and 4, and note that this case is discussed at length in Chapter 5.

E.g., “Spectabilis dominus Georgius ab Aquila nobilis brachiensis” (of Brač) appointed “spectabilem ser Hieronymum de Laurentijs Civem Jadram” to take the case of the constituent’s matters in the proceedings against “Hieronymum de Negroponte”, who allegedly killed Georgius’s next-of-kin, “quondam domini Stephani ab Aquila”. Prior to the procuratorial appointment, the captain-general had apprehended the suspect in Split, but the trial was to take

instance, over the course of ten months “dominus Hieronymus de Gallellis of the late dominus Simon, nobleman of Zadar” thrice appointed “dominus Joannes Franciscus de Dominis, nobleman of Zadar and Rab [arbensis]” to obtain the 15 ducats still missing from his wife’s dowry.[66] These extrajudicial settlements, as well as other crime-related facets, are discussed in detail in Chapter 5. 

What these examples and comparable cases, such as people petitioning the count for the redress of grievances,[67] have in common is that the appointees were individuals from Zadar or elsewhere in Dalmatia. This changed once the destination shifted from local or regional environs to business “in the mother city of Venice”.[68] Usually, this led to the appointment of individuals of elevated social status. In almost all such cases, procuratorial duties were assigned to Venetian patricians, Dalmatian

67. Usually, labourers sought redress if landlords attempted to levy excess duties. In the mid-1560s, “Slade Panoeuich…judex possobe ville Sancti Philippi et Jacobi” (Sv Filip i Jakov), representing himself and the other inhabitants of the village, stated that the rental contract stipulating 12 kvart of corn (1 kvart = c. 250 litres) as duties to be delivered “in die Sancti Jacobi de mense Julij” (29 July) could not be complied with. This issue was raised at the beginning of April either because the upcoming harvest would not allow for such large duties or because the landlord had increased the charges unilaterally. Consequently, the inhabitants of Sv Filip i Jakov appointed “Excellens dominus Vincentius Merula”, a doctor of canon and civil law, to act on their behalf and “deffendere omnes causas dicti…communis”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 3, f. 50v, 2 April 1565. The term “judex possobe” refers to the rural organisation in Zadar’s hinterland. The claimant was the judge or governor of a village assembly ( _posoba_ ) and its surrounding lands. After 1409, Venice kept many of the existing mediaeval institutions of rural autonomy. While the office of the judge itself was unpaid, in some cases territorial privileges could be obtained and its holder was exempt from military service. After the outbreak of the Ottoman-Venetian war in 1537, these judges were required to be “uno di piu vecchi della ditta villa”, at least 40 years old, and could be obliged to undertake unpaid public works. _Posoba_ meant one village assembly, and _liga_ referred to several village assemblies, which were granted special privileges by Doge Francesco Foscari in 1455. See Ref. 137, “Forma privilegiorum ligarum”; Ref. 142, “Provisio contra bannitos”; and Ref. 153, “De aetate iudicum villarum”, all via _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 626-632, 644-646, 664-666. See also Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 164-183; Pederin, _Mletačka uprava_ , p. 21; Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , p. 49. Both O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ , pp. 81-84, and Arbel, “Venice’s Maritime Empire”, pp. 156-164, place these Dalmatian aspects in the context of the _stato da mar_ as a whole. For a recent detailed case study, see Kümmeler, _Korčula_ , pp. 215-314. 

68. In the protocols of Zadar’s notaries, the term “alma” – literally nourishing, like a mother – is attributed to only two cities, Rome and Venice. Additional semantic differentiation was bestowed by the words used for the cities themselves. Venice was described as either “civitas” or body-politic in the sense of a self- governing, free city-state, or as “urbs” (city), the substantive typically reserved for the Eternal City. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 7, s.p., 1 January 1540; Johannes a Morea, I, 2, 3, ff. 18r-19r, 6 July 1542.

<!-- page 85 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

84 

nobles, individuals with judicial knowledge (attorneys, lawyers, solicitors) or a combination thereof.[69] These cases included various undefined legal proceedings in Venice or, more rarely, investigations before the court of appeals, the so-called Quarantia.[70] There appears one unifying characteristic in these latter contexts. Individuals tasked with legal representation, especially proceedings taking place in Venice proper, were explicitly referred to as trained professionals: advocate or barrister ( _advocatus_ ), attorney ( _causidicus_ ), doctor of both canon and civil law ( _leges utriusque doctor_ ) or solicitor ( _solicitator_ ). 

The _social_ motivations behind the appointment of procurators were broad and sometimes overlapped with economic and/or legal motivations as the boundaries between them were (are) often blurry. Subject to the available documentation, late mediaeval and early modern communication in general may be categorised along its economic, legal and social dimensions, yet we note that assignments pertaining to clientele or family relations fall into this third category. Examples thereof include, for instance, the appointment of procurators with a high social position to attend the baptism of a Venetian patrician’s child in Venice. In February of 1558, “the renowned master Franciscus Thomaseus, citizen of Zadar” and one of the city’s public notaries, sought representatives who would act on his behalf “at the holy baptism of the son or daughter to be born to the most acclaimed master Marc’ Antonio Corner [orig. Marci Antonij Cornelij], patrician of Venice, and his most acclaimed wife”. The appointees had to be of commensurate social standing and descent appropriate to the occasion, thus the appointment of “superb lord Michele Foscari [orig. dominu(m) Michaelem Fuscareno] son of the splendid master Gerolamo [orig. Hieronymi], a nobleman [orig. nobilem – _sic_ ] of Venice and lord Christophorus de Nassis”, a renowned nobleman of Zadar.[71] 

Other examples involved the return of the remains of relatives who had died elsewhere, although these occasions did not arise with great frequency. A notable case is the posthumous voyage of “the late nobleman, the master Theodosius”, brother of the “valiant and noble man of Zadar, the master Simon de Begna son of the late nobleman Christopher”.[72] A descendant of one of Zadar’s aristocratic

Four of the ship’s sailors appointed two of their fellow seamen to represent them in a Venetian court of law. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 9, s.p., 12 June 1562.

families, the late Theodosius was “serving with the most illustrious master Johannes Baptista, gastald [steward] of the former captain-general of the army of the most serene king of Hungary, in whose domains his life had ended”. As heir to his deceased brother, Simon appointed no less a figure than “the noble lord Baptista Besalius of Porto Buffaleto [Portobuffolè], a member of the household of the most acclaimed lord Paolo Tiepolo [orig. Paulj Theupuli], the honourable ambassador [ _orator_ ] of the most serene duke of Venice to the king of the Romans [Ferdinand of Habsburg]”. The bodily remains were to be brought back to Dalmatia, along with whatever goods and money the late Theodosius possessed.[73] Instances like these occasionally led to legal (and other) disputes among the living relatives over how to divide the movable and immovable possessions of the deceased, in turn necessitating the appointment of representatives for legal proceedings, as discussed in Chapter 5. 

## 4. _Secular and ecclesiastical elites_ 

Having defined the origins and destinations of the contracting parties, the next task is to survey the integration of these parties within the social fabric of 16[th] -century Dalmatia. This section deals with the men, women, groups and institutions behind geographical indicators and contracting motivations discussed in the previous section. 

In general, the numbers deriving from the procuratorial appointments provide a clear indication of the proportion of ecclesiastical versus secular elites. In total 124 (on average c. 13%) of all constituent parties between 1540 and 1569 were members of the clergy. I use the term “clergy” to describe both individuals and institutions. The ecclesiastical members of Zadar’s society were identified by looking for specific terms, such as adjectives “reverend” (reverendus) or “venerable” ( _venerabilis_ ), which typically preceded the naming of their role, education or office, described as “canon” ( _canonicus_ ), “cleric” ( _clericus_ ), “deacon” ( _diaconus_ ), “arch/bishop” ( _archi_ / _episcopus_ ), “priest” ( _presbyter_ ), “parish priest” ( _parochianus_ ), “father” ( _pater_ ), “brother” or ”sister” ( _fra_ , _sor_ ) or “vicar” ( _vicarius_ ). Institutions include abbeys ( _abbatia_ ), convents ( _conventus_ ), churches ( _ecclesia_ ), monasteries ( _monasterium_ ) and hospitals ( _lazarettum_ ). 

As Table 2.6 indicates, Zadar’s nobility made up the single largest social group appointing representatives. It is interesting to note that the city’s noblemen played a relatively prominent ecclesiastical role only during the first decade of the period under survey. During the remaining twenty years, an increase of ecclesiastical activities can be observed while the number of aristocratic members of the clergy drops considerably. The percentages of the other constituent groups – artisans, 

73. Baptista was appointed “specialiter et expresse ad nominem ipsius domini Constituentis exigendum, percipiendum et recuperandum omnes, et quascumque Sumas, et quantitates denariorum tantum, quas dictus quondam dominus Theodosius eius frater vocatus Joannes Croatus ut supra debebat, et nunc ipse dominus Constituens uti eius frater, et heres habere debet a quibuscumque personis tam publicis tamquam privatis…”. _Ibid_ .

<!-- page 87 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

86 

merchants and other presumably literate individuals – remained stable over the entire period. It is also worth noting that the share of noblewomen who appointed procurators increased significantly over time, almost tripling in absolute numbers from the 1540s to the 1560s. 

Women of aristocratic descent were not the only group whose importance grew over time. Members of the armed forces too appear as frequent constituent parties in the procuratorial documents. This can be explained by the reports by Venice’s civilian and military officials, which document the militarisation of Zadar, along with the rest of the peripheral regions of the _stato da mar_ .[74] Virtually every dispatch from the period under consideration enumerates the additional defence requirements for the continuation of Venetian rule in Dalmatia in the face of constantly increasing pressure (whether perceived or experienced) from beyond the borders of the _respublica_ .[75]

74. Lost territories and increased insecurity in the hinterlands compounded the existing problems caused by Ottoman pressure on the coastal communities. This forced the inhabitants of the rural parts of Venice’s Dalmatian possessions to adapt to the “Ottoman way of small war, typical for the frontier areas, based on skirmishes, raids and similar guerrilla actions… creating specific frontiers [sic] societies in the hinterland of the Dalmatian coastal towns”, writes Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 13-14. On the Uskoks of Senj as an exemplary frontier society, see Knapton, “Stato da mar”, pp. 329-331. See also Stanojević, _Jugoslovenske zemlje_ , pp. 75101; Tallett, _War and Society_ , p. 60. 

75. To cite but two examples, Marc’Antonio da Mula, count of Zadar from 1540-1543, wrote that “[i]l territorio è bellissimo e grasso, alquanto ristretto per la perdita di Laurana [Vrana] e Nadin…non si habitava in alcun luogho in terra ferma” and called for additional troops and funding to defend the immediate surroundings of the city proper. This was virtually verbatim repeated by Giovanni Battista Giustiniano, syndic in 1553, who lamented the dilapidated state of the fortifications: “le munizioni…sono mal all’ordine” and the biscuits “non sono tenuti, come si doveriano governare”. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, pp. 172, 195. Giacomo Pisani, count of Zadar 1564-1566, mentioned negotiations with, and “presents” for, Ottoman officials across the border; he also wrote that most able-bodied inhabitants of the hinterlands carried weapons all the time “ma la maggior parte sono banditi”. _Commissiones_ , vol.
