nos. in parentheses identify nobles; (e) total no. of constituents per decade engaged in commerce and trade, as identified by their descriptor as a spice trader ( _aromatarius_ ), small retailer ( _bazariotus_ ), merchant ( _mercator_ ); (f) total no. of constituents per decade who belonged to the intellectual elite, as defined by Budak, “Urban élites”, 188, and identified by the descriptors lawyer ( _advocatus_ / _causidicus_ / _solicitator_ ), chancellor ( _cancellarius_ ), salt tax collector ( _gabellotus_ ), doctor of canon and civil law ( _leges utriusque doctor_ ), notary ( _notarius_ ), scribe ( _scriba_ ), medical doctor ( _artium et medicinae doctor_ / _medicus physicus_ ); nos. in parentheses refer to those in this category who were of noble descent; (g) total no. of constituents per decade belonging to none of the above groups. 

The same data further indicate that the share of urban residents of Zadar increased from roughly 60% in the 1540s to more than 70% in the 1560s. As insecurity in the rural areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction increased, more inhabitants moved to the perceived safety behind the city walls. These numbers provide the empirical evidence to verify both the reports by Venice’s officials and the ensuing militarisation discussed above. 

In addition, we note that neither Zadar’s artisans nor its mercantile community contributed significantly to the procuratorial appointments. Those who did need representation in a court of law[76] or abroad mostly did so to obtain (relatively small) amounts of money invested in the Venetian _Monte nuovo_ ,[77] to administer[78]

The place names at times also yield information about the provenance of the constituent parties. For instance, there were artisans from virtually everywhere in the Adriatic parts of Venice’s maritime state: “de Venetiis”,[81] “de Bergomo” (Bergamo),[82] “de Sebenico” (Šibenik)[83] and “de Castro Nigra de Curcula” (Korčula).[84] Another interesting fact is that while only 53 contracts list craftsmen as constituents, 21 of these were ordered by their daughters, mothers or wives.

<!-- page 91 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

90 

Postner de Gliubgliana”, a merchant-turned-citizen of Zadar who operated out of central Dalmatia from the 1550s onward.[93] 

These examples suggest high degrees of geographical mobility. These activities were closely tied to the flow of goods, money, services and human labour in the form of employment opportunities, with the need for artisans skilled in carpentry, masonry, metalworking and other crafts being self-evident. These were required to improve fortifications or serve Venice’s increasing military needs from the mid-1560s onward.[94] However, when Zadar’s communal loggia partially collapsed in 1564, it also had to be rebuilt. In October of the same year, “seeing the loggia of this city in such state that one could but take swift action, the most acclaimed messer Antonio Cacco, most dignified captain of Zadar” contracted “master Hieronymo of the late Zuane Boccanich de Pucischie [Pučišća], a village on the island of La Brazza [Brač], and master Piero of the late Zuan Ueloxa da Cherso [Cres], a resident of Curzola [Korčula], both masterstonecutters [Taiapiere]” to take swift action. Zadar’s captain therefore obliged them to obtain all the required stones for the loggia’s reconstruction from the quarry near Kamenjani, a village within Zadar’s jurisdiction. The two artisans received 60 ducats for their work up front, which was vouched for by one of the city’s nobles, “messer Gregorio Ciualelli, stipulating for himself and his heirs”. In addition to this payment for the labour, the price of 25 soldi per four-foot stone block was agreed upon, paid for by the communal fiscal chamber. In the document’s concluding clauses, the two artisans agreed to start working within 15 days of the notarial act’s ratification.[95] These examples demonstrate that the high degree of geographical mobility noted by John Martin and Dennis Romano was not restricted to the upper strata of Venetian society or the inhabitants of the lagoon metropolis.[96] 

Let us now turn to the individuals upon whom procuratorial duties were bestowed (Table 2.7). Significant shifts can be identified among the appointees. On average, the proportion of appointees of noble stock increased by a quarter. About 52% of all individuals entrusted with procuratorial assignments were of privileged descent. However, the most profound changes in the stipulating pattern concern a different social and occupational group: the “intellectual elites” (see also below and Chapter 6). Defined as literate individuals of both noble and non-noble

descent,[97] and identified in the documents by references to their education, these appointees represent almost a threefold greater percentage than constituent parties from this group; about 21% of appointees derive from this category (compared to their share of c. 7% among the constituent parties). 

Another obvious conclusion is that while on average women made up a quarter of all constituent parties (c. 25%), their corresponding percentage among the procuratorial appointees was a meagre 2%. The fact that all but three female constituents appointed male representatives indicates that the women showed no bias toward their own sex in making the appointments. One of these women was “dona Clara, daughter of the late ser Dominici de Petrogna de Justinopolis” (Koper), who was referred to as “mulier sui juris” – she stipulated as a woman in her own right – and who tasked “dona Marieta, her sister and wife of ser Nicolai Mirogogno de Justinopolj”, with the collection of outstanding payments from otherwise unnamed individuals in Labin and Koper. The fact that the appointee, herself residing in the Istrian town, was geographically much closer to these cities than the constituent helps explain one of the main reasons for the appointment: geographical proximity. In addition to this consideration, Marieta was to be trusted because she was Clara’s sister, presumably possessed of connections within Venetian Istria and potentially enjoying the support of her husband.[98]

The second case involved “Lucretia, daughter of the respectable [spectabilis] lord Federico de Grisogonis”, a noblewoman of Zadar, who appointed “Marchetta de Bartholatijs” to regain her mother’s dowry. As the surname suggests, the two contracting parties in this case were related by kinship.[99] No further details were specified, which suggests that the contracting parties – and notary Johannes a Morea – knew each other, allowing the details of the agreement to be left unwritten.[100] 

The third and last instance involved “dona Margarita, wife of the late ser Joannis Rachouich, once a citizen and resident of Zadar”. Again, the constituent acted on her own behalf and appointed “the magnificent Zanetta, wife of the late magnificent master Francisco Dandolo”, a Venetian patrician and resident of Zadar, to collect all outstanding payments “from the war office [ab officio camerae Armamenti] of the most illustrious Doge of Venice” (see also Chapter 3). Evidently, Margarita’s late husband had served in the military, and Venice’s failure to pay him was the reason for the appointment. The appointee was tasked with obtaining “all and whatever quantity [of money] said late ser Johannes was entitled to receive due to his services rendered and done aboard the brigantine commanded by ser Nicolao Novello”. The naming of the office alone appears sufficient for the assignee to know what to do since the notary omitted any further information.[101] 

## 5. _Intellectual elites_ 

The fact that the percentage of “intellectual elites”[102] among the procuratorial appointees was on average three times higher than that of the constituent parties requires further elaboration. Despite the shifting absolute numbers of the appointees (especially the difference between the 1540s and the subsequent decades), their 

99. Federicus Grisogonus – “alias de Bartholatijs” – also known as Federik Grisogono (1472-1538), was a Zadar-born academic, medical doctor and professor at the prestigious University of Padua, working in fields as diverse as astrology, cosmography, mathematics and musicology. One of his writings, a treatise on fever pathology entitled _De modo collegiandi, pronosticandi, et curandi febres, nec non de humana felicitate ac denique de fluxu maris lucubrationes_ , was first published in Venice in 1528. Federicus’ social status was very high in comparison to his fellow noblemen, an impression reinforced by the fact that his name is mentioned in virtually all relevant documents pertaining to the de Bartholatijs branch of the Grisogono clan. Even though these two families are listed separately by Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , pp. 310-311, the notarial records are quite clear about their ties, e.g., HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, ff. 18r-18v, 9 May 1546. On Federicus’ academic achievements, see Dadić, “Natural Sciences”, pp. 743 749; Girardi-Karšulin, “Federicus Chrysogonus”, p. 290. See also Grmek, “Federik Grisogon”; Jakić-Čestarić, “Etnički odnosi”. 

> 100. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, f. 90v, 25 August 1555.

<!-- page 94 -->

Zadar’s Society 

93 

relative share remained constant. Besides these tendencies, the average share of appointees of aristocratic descent in this socio-occupational group increased from slightly less than a third during the 1540s to approximately 45% during the decade prior to the Cyprus War (see Table 2.7). 

In this context, two other issues shall be discussed. First, these numbers represent individual contracts, not individual persons. In absolute numbers, the intellectual elites were never as numerous as their three-decade average of about 21% suggests. In the 930 notarial acts, only 29 individuals out of a population of 6,000-6,500 are identified.[103] In spite of their small numbers, they were responsible for 196 appointments. 

Second, subjecting the procuratorial instruments to a correlational analysis of class affiliation and profession reveals additional details. Public notaries, while enjoying elevated authority, social status, education and literacy rates, were usually not the first choice of the constituent parties. This comes as no surprise since notaries were of such essential importance to the functioning of urban societies in Venice’s _stato da mar_ that their absence would have left a hole in the organisational fabric of their communities of residence. This assertion is supported by the fact that public notaries living in Zadar between 1540 and 1569 were rarely appointed to carry out procuratorial duties. The unique exception was attorney Marcus Aurelius Sonzonius, who was the only notary whose appointments reached the double figures (the fact that he left only 15 individual acts may have had something to do with these assignments, but that is speculative).[104] No other public notary was appointed this often (e.g., neither Augustinus Martius, Gabriel Cernotta, Franciscus Thomaseus, Johannes and Simon Mazzarellus, Nicolaus Canali, Petrus de Bassano nor Nicolaus Drasmileus), and their procuratorial activities are summarised in Table 2.8.

Urban Elites of Zadar

(a) names of all notaries who assumed procuratorial duties; (b) legal status of the named individuals, with citizen ( _civis_ ) relating to citizenship rights in Zadar; (c) provenance of the named individuals; (d) occupation of the listed individuals, including the overseer of the powder magazines ( _supramassarius munitionum_ )[105] and communal chancellor ( _cancellarius communitatis_ or _jadrensis_ );[106] (e) total no. of appointments per notary over the three-decade period. 

As I detailed in an essay that appeared after the first edition of this book, the surviving notarial records are distributed quite unevenly.[107] All but two notaries were laymen, yet individuals and institutions pertaining to the category “clergy” were constituents in approximately 16% of all deeds (1,027 out of 6,422 acts). More interestingly, these actors preferred a handful of notaries to conduct their business: Simon Budineus, a canon, of whom four out of ten customers fell into this category (346 out of 827 acts); cleric Paulus de Sanctis (10 out of 43 acts); lawyers Marcus Aurelius Sonzonius (5 out of 16 acts) and Petrus de Bassano (146 out of 820 acts); and communal chancellor Simon Mazzarellus, son of Johannes Michael, a nobleman of Trogir and citizen of Zadar (130 out of 438 acts). Together, these five notaries attracted slightly less than two-thirds of all business required by the “clergy” (637 out of 1,027 acts); by contrast, all ten other notaries active in Zadar between 1540 and 1569 attracted ecclesiastical customers in 390 instances. 

There is little, if any, substantial difference between the stipulating behaviour of these members of the “clergy” that might set them apart from their secular counterparts, except perhaps the statutory ban on the alienation of church property.[108] This means that ecclesiastical legal persons – such as the city’s convents and parish churches – typically used intermediaries (procurators) to rent, lease or otherwise grant their property to third parties (discussed in detail in Chapter 4). Frequently, these ecclesiastical institutions conducted their business by making use of notaries related to the former’s members, such as Franciscus Thomaseus (whose brother Johannes was a member of the cathedral chapter of St Anastasia),[109] Horatius de Marchettis (kinsman of canon Matthaeus 

105. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Canali, I, 1, C, s.p., 2 January 1541. 

106. The Mazzarelli were one of Trogir’s noble families mentioned in Giovanni Battista Giustiniano’s itinerary, via _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 208. Both Johannes and his son Simon were communal chancellors in Zadar and, at one point in time, they jointly appointed “ser Jacobum de Leonardis Civem et habitatorem Tragurij” to represent them in all causes in their hometown. This must be considered in conjunction with a rental contract on the same folio in which Jacobus rented “omnes, et singulos fructus redditus, et proventus possessionum, et terrenorum ipsius Locatoris [Johannes stipulated in his and Simon’s name but technically alone] positorum in comitatu Tragurij”. <!-- page 96 -->

Zadar’s Society 

95 

de Marchettis)[110] and notary Paulus de Sanctis, who was related to the canon and member of the metropolitan chapter Sanctus de Sanctis (the former wrote four contracts involving members of the metropolitan chapter, one including the archbishop himself).[111] The most prominent case was Simon Budineus who was approached by cathedral chapter members on ten separate occasions and whose customers included the archbishop of Zadar and the bishop of Nin.[112] Needless to say, there is much more to Budineus’ life and times, and I have discussed his activities and interactions in a separate publication and shall return to this subject matter in Chapter 8, too.[113] 

The second subgroup of appointees now enters into focus. These were individuals of elevated social status or education who were _not_ public notaries. Apart from some prominent individuals, like Johannes de Rosa and Bernardinus Carnarutus, most of these individuals tend to be overlooked by scholarship since they did not go on to enjoy enduring fame as authors, military commanders, public notaries or scholars (see also Chapter 6).[114] One way to quantitatively assess the educated individuals of a Mediterranean urban community, as the present study emphasises, is to include procuratorial instruments. 

The predominance of the legal professions among the appointees is immediately apparent: attorneys, barristers and other university-educated legal professionals made up two-thirds of all appointees during the period under consideration. By comparison, the nobles’ percentage among the educated appointees corresponds roughly to their overall average.[115] While a high number of instruments conferred duties to “ser Franciscum Petrouich”[116] and “dominum Hieronymum de Bassano”,[117] both referred to as attorneys and citizens of Zadar,

[1552]; and I, 1, f. 28r, 16 October 1551.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

96 

the recurrence of their appointments cannot be considered representative. The former was well-established before 1 January 1540, and his last appearance in the notarial records dates from the end of March 1543.[118] 

In the case of Hieronymus de Bassano, it is known that he was the brother of Petrus de Bassano, one of Zadar’s public notaries.[119] Their father, Marcus Antonius, who had died before autumn 1541, was also an attorney.[120] Hieronymus continued to appear in the notarial instruments throughout the 1550s[121] and likely remained active until the end of the period under survey (and probably beyond). For instance, “Bianca et Julia daughters and heirs of the late master Franciscus de Rossettis de Pontremulo, a citizen of Zadar”, jointly appointed him to represent them as their new general procurator in May 1569, specifically referring to him to as an attorney (see Table 2.9).[122] 

Based on these procuratorial appointments, there does not appear to be a clear-cut bias toward one social or professional group. Both Tables 2.8 and 2.9 instead point toward two other factors: first, the two categories of nobles and “intellectual elites” were by no means mutually exclusive (as demonstrated by the number of educated individuals in the examples above). On the contrary, educated noblemen were quite a common sight in 16[th] -century Dalmatian towns.[123] Second, as exemplified by the two attorneys, no bias toward procuratorial appointees of privileged descent can be ascertained. 

Let us now focus on the remaining 66 instances and apply a similar methodology examining the status of the appointees and correlation between spatial/social provenance and their destinations. The first thing that is apparent is the heterogeneity of this subgroup: 66 assignments were given to 55 individuals. In some cases, more than one recipient is named; thus, those recipients who were named twice or more times stand out. 

One such case involved “Camillus de Pechiaro, son of the late Johannes, a nobleman of Zadar, who acts for his share, as he asserted, of the patrimony of his late brother Darius”. To secure his rightful portion of the inheritance, he appointed

The notary’s quarrel with the archbishop revolved around a named but otherwise unspecified decision issued “in excellentissimo consilio Rogatoruam diej xxv. Junij 1557”. “Aloysius Cesarius de Opitergio [Oderzo], a solicitor in the high city of Venice”.[124] The other half of the late Darius’ possessions was to be inherited by his other brother, “Franciscus de Pechiaro”. Both he and Camillus appointed the same procurator to represent them in a court of law in Venice to sort out all other differences related to their inheritance.[125] Only a couple of months later, a third instrument was drawn up by the two brothers because “Catherina, daughter of the most excellent and late Federicus de Grisogonis”, second wife of the testator (Darius), decided to appeal to the courts to assert her rights. Again, Aloysius Cesarius was tasked with representing Camillus in court.[126] While these procuratorial instruments are silent on the outcome of these proceedings, the root cause of the legal suit is identified in a quitclaim stipulated three years earlier: the restitution of Catherina’s dowry of 490 ducats.[127] Matters become more complicated from here since Catherina had been married three times. She appears in the sources first as “wife of Johannes de Nassis of the late Nicolaus”, then as wife of Darius de Pechiaro and finally as “wife of the nobleman Johannes de Begna”.[128] Obviously, an amount well in excess of the average yearly income of Zadar’s nobility was worth a fight, “even” – especially – between nextof-kin.[129]

The two heirs, Camillus and Franciscus, not only inherited their deceased brother’s possessions but also his legal feud against the sailor. Catherina was not too able to reacquire her dowry: a couple of years later she appointed a procurator of her own, “dominum Bernardinum de Begna”, her third husband’s brother to “expresse ad exigendum, percipiendum et recuperandum a domina Slaua ipsius dominae constituentis et dicti quondam domini Joannis filia, nunc uxore domini Gregorij de Calcina herede dicti quondam patris sui…omnia et quecumque dona eidem dominae Catherinae constituenti factam Tempore nuptiarum quando nupsit dicto quondam domini Joanni”. Zadar’s Society 

99 

A quite comparable story can be reconstructed for “Johannes de Soppe of the late respectable Simon”. A descendant of the eponymous noble family, he worked as the communal chancellor in Kotor around 1540.[130] By 1542 he had returned to his native town and was assigned procuratorial duties by “Catherina daughter and testamentary heiress, as is said, of the late Johannes Margitich of the village Bibigne [Bibinje], and wife of Johannes Ostoych son of Vitus of Bibigne”. Johannes de Soppe was to nullify the actions taken by “Michael Margitich, her uncle [ _patruo_ ]” and divide her father’s inheritance equally between Catherina and her paternal uncle.[131] On a third occasion, the former chancellor of Kotor appeared in the procuratorial sources when he was appointed the general representative of “Jacobus Clarich of the village of Podi [Podi] in Zadar’s jurisdiction”.[132] 

Another case involved the Venetian patrician “Julius Trivisano [Trevisan], citizen of and attorney in Venice”, who was tasked twice by residents of Zadar. In the first instance, “Francischina, wife of the late Bertus Charanina, former citizen and merchant of Zadar”, and her two daughters “Paulina and Helysabeth”, appointed Julius to resolve their problems “with ser Dominico Uambirascosi, merchant of Venice at the sign of the dragon [ad insignum draconis]”. Unfortunately, no reason is given in the procuratorial act; however, since both the late Bertus and Dominicus were merchants, it is likely that the conflict arose over a business deal gone awry.[133] In the second instance, the above-mentioned daughters, both married to merchants residing in Zadar (Paulina to “Julius Toninus”, Helysabeth to “Bernardinus Tirabuschi”) and heirs of their late father, appointed Julius Trevisan and “Vivianus Barlendi, merchant of Venice”, to ratify the agreement reached between them as Bertus’ heirs and “Dominicus de Gamberarijs”.[134] 

130. Returning to his hometown in late 1541 or early 1542, “[c]ircumspectus dominus Joannes de Soppe quondam spectabili domini Simonis nobilis Jadre Cancellarius magnifici communitatis Cathari” had not been paid for his service in Kotor. Consequently, he appointed his brother, “discretum Juvenem dominum Hieronymum de Soppe…se transferendum ad Civitatem Cathari” to obtain all outstanding payments “a Camera fiscali Cathari”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 1, E, s.p., 21 June 1542. This indicates that Venice’s bad payment practices affected not just soldiers, and further evidence is found in early 1543 when Johannes re-appointed his brother Hieronymus to finally get hold of “omnes pecunias salarij sui Tam Temporis elapsi tamquam futuri”, naming specifically the office of Kotor’s communal chancellor as one of the past assignments for which Johannes had not been paid for until then. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 1, E, s.p., 10 February 1543. 

131. Michael, on the other hand, had already commissioned Johannes Mazzarellus, Zadar’s communal chancellor, to divide the inheritance, apparently not to the liking of Catherina. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 1, E, s.p., 12 November 1542. 

132. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 1, E, s.p., 19 December 1542. 

133. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 2, ff. 31r-31v, 25 April 1564. 

134. In early 1565, Paulina and Elisabeth appointed Julius Trevisan “ad nominem ipsarum Constituentium, et pro eis, ac quaque earum Laudandum, approbandum, Confirmandum, et ratificandum quoddam Instrumentum compositionis, Concordij, et transactionis factum, ut dixerunt, Inter eumdam dominum Julium Trivisanum, et dominum Vivianum Barlendi mercatorem venetum…ex una ac dominum Dominicum de Gamberarijs, et filios mercatores venetos ex alia”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 3, f. 31v, 5 February 1565.

<!-- page 101 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

100 

In general, the cases in which a non-resident was tasked with procuratorial duties were related to business in the places of residence of the appointees. This allows an impression of the various interactions across cultural, geographical, religious and social dimensions.[135] Consequently, it becomes possible to reimagine parts of the life and times of “magnificent master Andrea Zane of the late magnificent Johannes Aloysius”, a Venetian patrician who once served as the chamberlain of Bar.[136] Likewise, once can speculate about the exact nature of the relations between “valiant master Johannes Ulani of Neapolj [Naples or Nafplio], head of stratiotti in Zadar”, and his procuratorial appointee, “the excellent Johannes Euretopolo, physician of Korkyra [Corcirensis]”. This becomes immediately apparent considering the latter’s task: he was to obtain all outstanding money owed to the constituent on the island of Corfu, “from Jacobo de Aurani of Korkyra” and from the heirs of “the late ser Damiano Androminda of Neapolj [Naples or Nafplio]”.[137] 

For a variety of reasons, procurators from abroad were assigned with specific tasks involving individuals personally unknown to the constituent parties. While the relationship between the contracting parties remains in many cases subject to speculation, the existence of foreign appointees highlights the one underlying commonality that unites these seemingly diverse appointments: the relevance of knowledge of the procuratorial appointees of the destination area. This was true of both patricians and commoners sent to Venice and elsewhere. 

## 6. _Ecclesiastical activities_ 

Inhabitants of Zadar’s subject dominion were eligible for prebendaries of less than 60 ducats of value, but after 1423 Zadar’s archbishopric and the bishopric

Having paid back the money by mid-July of 1566, the instrument cites the ducal letters accepting Maria’s late husband into Venetian military service (dated 21 January 1541). When, in turn, Venice had still not paid the constituent sixteen years later, Andrea Zane was appointed by Maria and sent to Venice “ad comparendum coram quibuscumque cleberrimis dominis Judicibus, officij, et Magistratibus civitatis Venetiarum et ad pedes Serenissimi Principis ad agendum, petendum et omnia ac singula Jura sua procurandum etiam si Talia forent quo mandatum exigerent magis spetiale, promittens dicta dona Maria Se esse creditricem dicti officij de eius provisione”. <!-- page 102 -->

Zadar’s Society 

101 

of Nin were reserved exclusively for members of the Venetian patriciate.[138] Like their secular counterparts (count, captain), these two high-ranking dignitaries rarely appear in the instruments, with exceptions occurring usually in the case of newly appointed clergymen. For instance, on such an occasion in late 1556 “priest Sanctus de Sanctis, canon of Zadar, and Johannes Raimundinus, citizen of Zadar”, conferred procuratorial powers upon “the Reverend Father Mutius Calino”, then archbishop-designate of Zadar. While on his way to his new see, Calino was to acquire outstanding payments “from the accountants of the revered Apostolic Chamber” in Rome.[139] 

A second example involved the bishopric of Nin. Like most newly appointed dignitaries travelling to their assigned posts, he too left his former prebendary behind. This transpired, for instance, in the case of “the Reverend Father Marcus Lauredanus [Loredan], by the Grace of God and the Apostolic See bishop of Zadar”. Prior to his designation, Marcus was appointed procurator “by the Reverend Francisco Superantio, abbot of Saint Michael on the Mountain”, on 5 October 1554. Due to circumstance, Franciscus then transferred Marcus’ procuratorial duties to “Hieronymus de Bellis, a cleric from the diocese of Verona”, and tasked the latter with taking care of the abbey’s problems with its temporal possessions.[140] 

Beyond these two high-profile examples, the notarial protocol books contain little evidence that these dignitaries engaged in mundane or secular activities – with one exception: both sees possessed significant amounts of landed property. This land was auctioned off by the officeholders’ procurators to the highest bidder for tax farming purposes.[141] These legal matters involving ecclesiastical landholders underline the need for further study of the representatives’ essential role as mediators along the often-blurry lines between families and institutions (see also Chapter 3). 

138. Arbel, “Colonie d’oltremare”, p. 975; Pederin, “Venezianische Verwaltung”, p. 143. After the annexation of Cyprus, this policy was extended to the large island kingdom. Hill, _History of Cyprus_ , vol. III, p. 873. 

139. The terminology used is “Mutium Calino ellectum archiepiscopum Jadre dignissimum”, who was absent at the time of the instrument’s stipulation, suggesting that he was either on his way to Rome or still in its vicinity. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 4, f. 128v, 3 November 1556. 

140. Originally, Loredan had been tasked “ad capiendum, et intrandam possessionem specialem, et temporalem dicta Abbatia et propterea praesentandum celeberrimo domino Rectori Jadra cui diriguntur litteras Ducales super eodem possessum”, most likely because the mentioned property was located within Zadar’s jurisdiction. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 6, ff. 488r-488v, 12 February 1565. 

141. Here the term refers to the practice of conceding or renting property (which was also transmissible to third parties) for a fixed annual sum of money or payment in kind. The landlord transferred his or her rights to the income of the property to the renting party (or the renting party to the third party). The latter could keep all income gained from the rented property minus the agreed-upon rental fees and the Church’s tithes. See Doumerc, “Dominio del mare”, pp. 113-138; Lane, _Venice_ , pp. 137-152. For Dalmatian specifics, see Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , p. 64; Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , p. 143. Property transfers are discussed in detail in Chapter 4.

Urban Elites of Zadar

Another, quite different field of ecclesiastical activity concerns the various congregations in Zadar, which were run by the Benedictines, Dominicans and Franciscans. They too appear in the procuratorial instruments.[142] There were nine monastic institutions within the city walls, two run by the Benedictines and three each by the Dominicans and the Franciscans.[143]

(a) monastic congregations; (b) affiliation of the listed congregations; note that both convents of the Poor Clares (St Nicholas, St Marcella) and the Third-Order Regulars (St Francis) also belong to the Franciscan family of ecclesiastical orders; (c) male/female-only convent; (d) notes the social status of the given convents’ residents; (e) provides the names of the provosts ( _abbas_ / _abbatissa_ , _prior_ , _guardianus_ , _vicarius_ ); (f) Hieronymus was joined by the provincial deacon ( _minister provinciae_ ), Father Augustinus Pollicianus, and the convent’s vicar, Father Augustinus of Hvar. 

All monastic congregations – as well as the chapters of the archbishopric and the parish churches – regularly appointed procurators, and they did so for a variety of reasons. There was but one unifying characteristic: whenever possible 

142. It has only been “exceptional”, as Budak noted over a quarter-century ago, that the Church and its various activities have received much scholarly attention. The oldest and, traditionally, largest community were the Benedictines, established in Zadar in the early Middle Ages. Their presence included the monastery of St Chrysogonus and the noble nunnery of St Mary. And while the 14[th] century witnessed a decline of their influence relative to the contemporaneous ascent of the Dominicans and Franciscans, both abbot and abbess of the Benedictine communities continued to command considerable influence in Zadar. Budak, “Urban élites”, p. 188. 

143. Not counting the Third Order Regulars (Tertius Ordo Sancti Francisci), organised in the community of St Francis (sv Frane) and open to both sexes.

<!-- page 104 -->

Zadar’s Society 

103 

and feasible, appointees’ duties were bestowed upon individuals belonging to Zadar’s aristocracy and/or legally trained individuals. The tasks of these representatives included the renting or conceding of landed property to tenants or colonists, and this is discussed in detail in Chapter 4, yet it is important to note that the activities and behaviour of ecclesiastical constituents did not differ markedly from those of their secular peers. As I have detailed in a case study focusing on the cathedral chapter, many notarial deeds offer a wealth of clues with respect to the embedding of ecclesiastical actors in an urban society, and it is in these details that we find that the individuals tasked to represent institutions of the church frequently crossed the boundaries separating kinship and/or personal relations from their functions, duties and/or roles within the institutions with which they were affiliated.[144] This is most pronounced in the case of five out of the six following examples of procuratorial appointments commissioned by the Dominican convent of St Demetrius and the Benedictines of St Mary that overwhelmingly appointed (fellow) noblemen from Zadar.[145] In the sixth instance, the appointee was “ser Hieronymus Bassano”, brother of notary Petrus de Bassano and a renowned attorney. Though not of privileged birth, his family’s social status was evidently high enough to represent the convents appropriately.[146] All three members of the de Bassano family are referenced in the sources as “lord” ( _dominus_ ), not “sir” ( _ser_ ), the more common title for citizens, underscoring the family’s prominence.[147] 

These two monasteries were reserved exclusively for noblewomen; thus, the appointment of fellow aristocrats can be explained by personal acquaintance, kinship ties[148] or social norms. The bestowal of procuratorial duties upon

Hieronymus de Bassano, on the other hand, may be explained by his family’s prominence, legal expertise and by the fact that he was not the only but the second procurator to represent the monastery.[149] 

By contrast, the non-noble Franciscan convent of St Francis appointed both aristocrats and commoners. In 1560 it appointed two nobles, including “il spettabile meser Zoilo de Ferra nobile, facendo per nome suo et del spettabile et eccelente dottore meser Pietro Fanfogna similmente nobile assente”.[150] Five years later the convent appointed “lord [orig. dominus] Franciscus de Ventura, citizen of Zadar” (see also Chapter 6).[151] In addition, appointees were also responsible for ensuring that any bestowals of property by recently deceased individuals reached their rightful heirs. These bequests could be money, movable or immovable goods or a combination thereof. 

In most cases, however, the procuratorial instruments do not provide specifics on inheritance issues. The appointees had only to ensure that all bequeathed goods to which the respective congregation was entitled be collected. Usually, these bequests consisted of charitable donations, annual stipends for cloistered relatives, income from or usufruct rights to immovable property or requiem money.[152] Other tasks included the securing of worldly goods from a

deceased bishop[153] or nobleman[154] and the collection of any outstanding sums of money owed.[155] 

Speaking generally, the communication mechanisms of ecclesiastical and secular elites followed the same pattern. While the percentage of female constituents was roughly a quarter of the total, only 2% of the appointees were women. Of all constituents, four out of five contracting individuals originated in Zadar’s jurisdiction. This comprised entities as diverse as the city’s hinterlands on the mainland, the inhabited coastal islands and the smaller subject districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana. While the numbers for the constituents remained relatively constant over the period under survey, the picture changes slightly for the procuratorial appointees. On average, some 60% still originated from the same geographical areas as the constituents; however, there is considerable variation between the first and last decades in question. During the 1540s almost two-thirds of procurators originated within Zadar’s jurisdiction. While the last decade (1560-1569) witnessed an overall increase in the number of individual appointments compared to the first decade, the number of appointees from within Zadar’s jurisdiction dropped to just over 50%. This coincided with the doubling of individuals tasked with procuratorial duties in Venice

<!-- page 107 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

106 

proper and a slight increase in missions destined to other parts of Venetian Dalmatia-Albania. 

Based on the data examined above, the following conclusions and trends can be summarised for the three decades under survey: first, the relative importance of Zadar and its jurisdiction decreased by roughly the same amount as Venice, and her _terraferma_ possessions gained in importance with respect to procuratorial appointments. At the same time, second, Zadar’s ties with the rest of the Venetian Adriatic remained more or less constant, although neighbouring Šibenik attracted more procuratorial assignments than any other area in the Venetian dual province (except for Venice proper).[156] These findings, third, suggest that overall network density within Zadar’s jurisdiction was high in the decade after the conclusion of the Ottoman-Venetian war in 1540 but decreased in importance in the subsequent period leading up to the Cyprus War. The “winners” of this phenomenon were Venice proper and her _terraferma_ possessions, which eclipsed all other destinations in the Adriatic by the middle of the 16[th] century. As suggested by Arié Malz and subsequently never addressed by Oliver Schmitt (despite the title of his essay), communication on the local level decreased and gave rise to the growing importance of regional and supra-regional destinations.[157] Of course, these changes occurred gradually and unequally, with the neighbouring city of Šibenik and Venice proper attracting more procuratorial missions than any other part of the _stato da mar_ or mainland Italy. 

*** 

Reality was, of course, more complex than the tripartite economic/legal/ social model used in these pages. Still, this approach allows for a working analytical framework that lends itself to the following main deductions: for most business within or without the _stato da mar_ (excluding Venice proper), any nobleman or commoner with sufficient social status or education could fulfil virtually any task within the range of procuratorial assignments. However, for mandates destined for Venice proper, the appointees were more often Venetian patricians or citizens. 

Ecclesiastical activities mirrored those of secular protagonists. If an assignment led the appointees to high-profile counterparts in Rome or Venice, the best and most renowned procurators available were tasked with the execution of the mandates. More common, however, were tasks such as collecting inheritances or administering real estate property. All of Zadar’s nine monastic communities regularly appointed general representatives to deal with these and other day-to-day matters. 

156. Which is consistent with the assertion of Zadar’s decline and Šibenik’s ascent over the first two-thirds of the 16[th] century. Malz, “Die Dalmatinische Städtewelt”, p. 103. 

157. Compare _ibid_ ., pp. 104-113, with Saint-Guillain, Schmitt, “Die Ägäis”, p. 217 (which gives away the focus on a somewhat reified “maritime communication”); Schmitt, “Südosteuropa als Kommunikationsraum”, pp. 78-79 (adding “human connections” to maritime goods trade). See also note 43.

<!-- page 108 -->

Zadar’s Society 

107 

The terminology used in the documents reveals clear distinctions between the social and occupational groups. At the top of the hierarchy stood the doge, whose invocation set the tone for the naming of other Venetian patricians. Usually, the notarial acts were written “at the time of the most serene Prince and most excellent lord Petrus Lando, by the grace of God the most illustrious Duke [Doge] of the Venetians etc”. The doge’s representative administering an urban community was referred to in a like manner, for instance, “during the governorship of the acclaimed Jacopo Antonio Mauro, count of Zadar”.[158] Other members of the Republic of St Mark were commonly accorded the adjectives “acclaimed” ( _celeberrimus_ ), “magnificent” ( _magnificus_ ) or “noble” ( _generosus_ ). Further distinction was conferred by the epithet “Venetian patrician” ( _patritius venetus_ ), in clear contrast with the terminology used for all other nobles ( _nobilis_ ).[159] Contrary to Venetian patricians, adjectives were rarely, if ever, added to the names of Dalmatian _nobles_ who were typically identified by their origin, e.g., “a nobleman of [insert place]” instead of the afore-cited choice of words. While the honorific “sir” ( _ser_ ) did not convey a particular distinction between aristocrats and commoners in Venice proper, the word may have carried more social status in her maritime state.[160] Too much importance must not be attached to these details, but it is useful to observe that there were clear terminological distinctions between Venetian patriciate and their Adriatic (near) peers.[161] This is borne out by recent research by Fabian Kümmeler on Korčula and Lena Sadovski on Omiš, Poljica and Split (but note that the former uses the term “patrician” for Dalmatian nobles, which the latter does not).[162] 

Identical patterns of background can be observed for ecclesiastical dignitaries, especially in the higher echelons of the Church hierarchy.[163] The lower ranks of the clergy were referred to by their titles, such as

military personnel, if an individual was an officer, the term “valiant” ( _strenuus_ ) was usually written down next to his name and rank.[172] Lower ranks and enlisted men appeared only with additional references to their rank (where applicable) or function before their names.[173] For women who acted on their own behalf, an individual was explicitly noted as “mulier sui iuris”, even though in most instances the stipulating women were acting with the licence, or in the presence, of their husband (“cum licentia, in presentia maritis”).[174] 

Apart from the fundamental aspects of educational qualification and social status, the most important factors in selecting a procurator were, unsurprisingly, local knowledge and/or other business at the destination, kinship ties and/or patronage relations. In combination, these aspects lent the procuratorial networks a seemingly familiar, pragmatic and solution-oriented appearance whose characteristics, to a certain extent, transcend the five centuries elapsed since.

<!-- page 112 -->

## 3. Actors: Political, Ecclesiastical and Economic Elites 

The economic elite consisted of both patricians and commoners. Although in general the patricians were wealthier than their non-noble counterparts, many of the latter possessed wealth much higher than that of the average patrician. Both groups were involved in trade and agriculture, while manufacturing was mostly in the hands of the non-noble population.[1] 

Neven Budak 

It is challenging to discuss the inhabitants of central Dalmatia around the mid-16[th] century because no census data exists prior to Zaccaria Vallaresso’s 1527 report.[2] And while the account of his tenure as Zadar’s captain is very detailed, subsequent indications about the populace are way less detailed and shall be treated with commensurate caution.[3] It is even harder to describe in detail the activities in which the population engaged. However, in this chapter and the next, the available documentation is analysed to gain a picture of the demographics and economic activities of Zadar’s urban elites. The approach is two-fold: Chapter 3 distinguishes these elites by their political, ecclesiastical and economic roles through examples of their activities and interactions with each other and the wider society. Chapter 4 details their involvement in Zadar’s real estate markets between 1540 and 1569 to quantitatively survey their economic footprint and identify shifting trends. 

## 1. _Political elites: Venetians and the local nobility_ 

At the top of the local hierarchy stood Zadar’s count, the highest-ranking civilian authority. The city’s military commander, the captain, assisted him.[4] Both 

1. Budak, “Urban élites”, p. 188. 

2. In his report, he gave the number of 6,903 souls (anime) living in Zadar proper, with another 1,148 souls dwelling in the suburbs. _Commissiones_ , vol. I, pp. 194-223, with a detailed appendix on pp. 203-223. 

3. There are two helpful surveys with demographic indications by Mocellin, “Città forttificata”, pp. 43-44, 60-61, and Malz, “Dalmatinische Städtewelte”, p. 106, both offering graphs based on published literature. For the mid-16[th] century, Zadar’s urban population numbered between 6,538 (1553) and 5,826 (1554) souls. See also Peričić, _Dalmacija_ , pp. 1922, 57-60; _Razvitak gospodarstva Zadra_ , pp. 20-24. 

_stato da mar_ , see Arbel, “Venice’s Maritime Empire”, pp. 144155, although with an emphasis on Venice proper. Detailed studies of overseas administration

<!-- page 113 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

112 

civil and military competences. This structure was also in place in the other major urban centres of Venice’s maritime state.[5] This division between civil and military authority can be seen in the locations in which these officials resided: the Ducal Palace (across from St Simeon’s church) and the Captain’s Palace (in the vicinity of the city’s main gate).[6] The authority of both count and captain extended well beyond the city walls and by the middle of the 16[th] century comprised the entirety of Zadar’s jurisdiction, including the minor fortified villages of Nin, Novigrad, Turanj and Zemunik.[7] To serve under the Venetian officials, civilian and military personnel were imported to central Dalmatia from both shores of the Adriatic or even beyond the Straits of Otranto.[8] The city itself was guarded by two garrisoned forts,[9] while a detachment of light cavalry ( _stratioti_ ) provided security in the hinterlands.[10] They consisted mainly of Croats and Levantines. These mounted soldiers were called upon in case of hostile incursions by Morlachs, Ottoman subjects or Uskoks.[11] 

are rare, but see O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ , esp. pp. 39-56. For two detailed case studies, see Maglaque, _Venice’s Intimate Empire_ , pp. 85-123. 

5. The separation of civil and military authority was instituted by the Venetian Senate during the Middle Ages. This appears mainly to have been a response to the fact that Venice relied heavily on mercenary troops, and the appointment of civilian overseers may have provided a certain amount of control over the “foreign” _condottieri_ . In principle, this policy extended to the _stato da mar_ , although such dual civil-military administration existed “only” in the largest and most important possessions: in Zadar, on Crete, and in Cyprus. Furthermore, in Venice’s maritime state, Venetian patricians filled both positions, a policy that also applied to the higher positions in the Church. Arbel, “Colonie d’oltremare”, pp. 964-966. 

6. The separation of civil and military authority found its expressed in the separate locations of the two offices (though these were not far from each other). The seat of civil authority, today called the Palace of the Duke/Rector and Provveditore (Kneževa i Providurova palača), is situated in Simeon Budineus Square (Poljana Šime Budinića), named after the Zadar-born cleric, grammarian, poet, writer and public notary quoted extensively throughout this book (see Chapter 7). The Captain’s Palace was strategically located next to the Land Gate (Porta Terraferma). While no longer extant, the pentagonal Captain’s Tower in the city’s Five Wells Square (Trg pet bunara) testifies to its former use.

8. Arbel, “Colonie d’oltremare”, pp. 967-968; Lane, _Venice_ , pp. 355-370. 

9. Zadar’s defences consisted of two castles: the mediaeval citadel in the southern corner of the city and a second fort ( _arx_ , _castrum_ , _castro_ ) situated in the northern corner near the entrance of the city’s harbour. The remnants of the former are partially visible today. It functions as home to the University of Zadar, Croatia’s oldest university, founded in 1396 as a school by the Dominicans and reopened in 2003. See Raukar, “Croatia within Europe”, pp. 20-21. The harbour fort has been demolished and today hosts the docks for trans-Adriatic ferry services. The only reminder of its past use are found in Three Wells Square (Trg tri bunara) and the nearby Arsenal (today an upscale food court). See _Commissiones_ , vol. enjoyed an eminent social position, even if they are represented minimally in the notarial records. There are only a handful of instances where a situation was evidently important or grave enough for the count or the captain to appear as acting parties. One of these cases occurred in the summer of 1549 when Zadar’s count sold off goods collected from a shipwrecked vessel off the island of Sali. The sunken ship’s cargo, “thirty-three goat hides [petias…Zimbilotorum] recovered from the sea”, once washed ashore was considered communal property and thus listed in the fiscal chamber’s books.[12] Even though the city’s statutes are silent on wrecking ( _ius litoris_ ),[13] the count paid the money to the owner of either the ship or the cargo, “Vellj de Anguri”. The count, “the magnificent Joanne Dominico Ciconia”, was represented by notary Petrus de Bassano who acted on behalf of the absent count “in his function as public official [uti persona publica]”. The meeting took place in the large audience chamber of the ducal palace.[14] On the following day Vellj issued a quitclaim, which formally confirmed the receipt of the 33 goat hides, 11 of which he had already “sent to Venice with the venerable Laurentius a Puteo” (possibly Pozzuoli), a citizen of Zadar who then lived as a merchant in Venice. The remaining 22 pieces had been handed over to Vellj “by the respectable Simon de Pasinis, Zadar’s deputy accountant” ( _vice collaterale_ ), who acted on behalf of the commune by virtue of his office.[15]

total of 536 star[16] of grain from “Hieronymus Dilza and Joannis Antonij Paiari”, both absent merchants and citizens of Ferrara.[17] They were represented by “ser Gaudentius de Chiavena, resident of Ferrara, acting as [their] legitimate representative”. Jacobus had sailed his ship directly to Barletta and, by allegedly circumventing Venice’s staple rights, stood accused of “having committed smuggling”.[18] He and his ship were captured and brought to Zadar where and sold authorities promptly sentenced Jacobus to pay a fine of 80 ducats[19] off the grain via two intermediaries, the merchant Lazarus de Pontremulo and the nobleman Zoylo de Ferra.[20] In the interim, the grain had been stored in three magazines – all of them owned by Lazarus – as the ship’s cargo bay was considered ill-suited to preserve the corn. As it happened, 353 star of grain were already in rather bad condition, forcing a lower price of only 5 libras and 4 soldi per unit; the remainder, some 151 star, could be sold at the higher price of 6 libras and 10 soldi per unit.[21] The difference between the number of star sold and the initial cargo is explained by the loss of two star prior to the sale, a commission of 26 star for Lazarus and an additional fee of 4 star for the storage of the grain. Gaudentius then formally confirmed the receipt of the money, exonerating the count of the debt. In the instrument’s valediction, the captain’s palace is noted as the location in which the transaction took place in the presence of “ser Antonius de Antibaro [Antivari], a mercenary of the city guard” and “Mattheo Cuitcouich, a herald”.[22] All transactions were meticulously documented and are summarised in Table 3.1.

Both count and captain were routinely assigned procuratorial duties by members of the upper social strata, mostly involving high-profile individuals or magistrates elsewhere. For instance, in June of 1558 the Venetian patrician “Gerolamo Foscarinus [Foscari], son of the late, acclaimed Michael” appointed Zadar’s captain Marc’Antonio Priuli to serve as his procurator. The task was to settle all outstanding financial issues of the constituent’s late brother Pietro who had served as galley commander ( _sopracomes_ )[23] before his death. The procuratorial duties thus included dealings with the captain-general, another Venetian patrician of considerable influence and prestige. They were drawn up on 28 June 1558 in the presence of public notary Franciscus Thomaseus and “ser Francisco de Venetijs”, the captain on the late Pietro’s galley.[24] 

In another instance the count “Aloysij Cornelio [Corner]”[25] intervened more directly in local affairs by granting “Magdalena de Sanctis wife of Joannes Tubicina” a ducal license to sell parts of the couple’s property in her husband’s absence. Magdalena intended to sell one of her houses to 

23. Galley commanders were required to have at least four years of experience and were responsible for the recruitment of sailors, scribes, soldiers, etc. The latter especially amounted to a financial burden since the expenditure had to be borne by the galley commanders. Lane, _Venice_ , p. 365. 

24. Gerolamo is also referred to as “successionarius bonorum et in bonis quondam domini Petri fratris sui dum viveret dignissimi supracomitis Triremis hijs proximis lapsis diebus in hac civitate vita functi…”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 3, c. 137r, 28 June 1558.

116 

“master-cobbler Antonio Balcich” to reacquire a building “from ser Martino Lucatello”, a citizen and merchant of Zadar. These legal requirements are even reflected in the protocol book of Simon Mazzarellus, who wrote down the two individual contracts on the same folio. The first act conferred the ducal license to Magdalena; the second was a sales contract, which explicitly stated that the transaction took place “pursuant to the above-related ducal licence”.[26] A notable aspect of these types of dealings is that a woman was required to have her husband present at the signing or to have his permission to sell her own property. This was a restriction unknown to Roman Law, which had previously applied in Venice and her dominions.[27] 

Among the more common issues facing count and captain during their tenure of office were disagreements between landlord and tenant, often over excise duties or other forms of payment in cash or kind.[28] For instance, in early 1560 labourers on property near Tkon, situated on the island of Pašman and owned by the abbey of Sts Cosmas and Damian of Rogovo, were upset by the excess payments demanded by the landlord. Since this was clearly against the contract, they petitioned Zadar’s count, Benedictus Contarenus (Contarini). The ensuing ducal sentence was appealed by the convent’s representative, the “Reverend Father Montemerlus de Montemerlo, provost of the Abbey of Sts Cosmas and Damian”, further prolonging the dispute. It was only in mid-March of 1562 that an arbitration settlement between the abbey and its labourers was agreed upon, establishing the rent as a quarter of the harvest.[29] 

The various other members of the administration wielded the next layer of political power. These individuals and their families lived within the walled perimetre of the city and ensured the continuous functioning of the bureaucracy. Their main tasks were to oversee the harbour and issue export licenses, to collect

27. The practice of Roman Law in Venice’s dominions curtailed the role of most women to being “daughters or wives of men who were their legal guardians”, with their marital status also defining the degrees of their personal and economic freedoms and opportunities, noted McKee, “Women”, p. 41. See also Grbavac, “Testamentary Bequests”, pp. 68-69. 

28. Agriculture, farming and livestock breeding were the backbone of Dalmatia’s economy, which was left unchanged by the Venetians after 1409. Legal documents, therefore, were based on land grants ( _concessiones_ ) and rental contracts ( _locationes_ ) to colonists, farmers and labourers. Land grants involved payments in kind, usually a quarter of the harvest, which was to be handed over to the landlord. For rental contracts, payment in cash was agreed upon. These contracts were legally binding and negotiated prior to the tenants’ leasehold. Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 228-230; Peričić, “Prilog poznavanju agranih odnosa”, p. 138.

taxes from the inhabitants living within Zadar’s jurisdiction and to command the military detachments. Some also engaged in commercial activities.[30] 

These Venetians regularly appear in the notarial protocols, as demonstrated by one instance from the early 1550s. Two military officers, “the valiant Xacman Gleglieuaz and Petrus Naycinouich, captains of the Morlachs”, were paid the money owed, confirmed the receipt and exonerated “Pietro Vallaresso, son of the late Zaccaria”.[31] Both soldiers were “contracted by the grace of the illustrious Doge of Venice” – mercenaries – who served the Republic of St Mark and commanded troops levied among the Morlachs. On at least two previous occasions[32] and one later occasion, the same constituent parties tasked Petrus Vallaresso again to obtain the money owed “by the office of the magnificent chamberlains of the commune of the Venetians”.[33] In the end “Antonio de Venturino, citizen of Zadar”, paid the three soldiers on behalf of Pietro Vallaresso.[34] While the exact nature of the latter’s role within the Venetian administration in Zadar is unclear, he performed the same procuratorial duties for several other mercenaries, too.[35]

More details were given by Giovanni Battista Giustiniano in 1553 when who wrote of the fiscal chamber disposing of roughly 7,000 to 8,000 ducats per annum but lamented the destruction of most olive trees during the war of 1537-1540 (and the end of which oil sales were worth about 25,000 ducats per year). Other Venetian citizens worked in the administration of Zadar and occupied positions like the principal gastald of the ducal office ( _gastaldus principalis offitium comitis_ ),[36] chamberlain ( _camerarius_ ),[37] communal broker ( _scontrus_ )[38] or salt tax collector ( _gabellotus_ ).[39] In all, the protocol books suggest that the number of Venetians living in Zadar was relatively small, evidenced by the fact that they appear in only 120 instances (out of 6,436 notarial acts stipulated between 1540 and 1569). Also, many of these individuals were mentioned more than once. 

Beneath this small Venetian presence, several other individuals worked as chancellors ( _cancellarius_ ), heralds ( _praecor_ ) or scribes ( _scriba_ ).[40] These positions, while few, were open to qualified educated or literate individuals from the Venetian possessions or elsewhere. For instance, Zadar employed two chancellors, one responsible for communal affairs ( _cancellarius communitatis_ ) and the other for the upkeep of the legal system and its processes ( _cancellarius ad criminalium_ ).[41] _stato_ On many occasions these offices were held by nobles from other areas of the

The latter’s service was to begin on 1 October 1554. Zadar’s fiscal chamber in turn paid 25 libras every three months to the office-holder, plus some allowances for board (23 libras and 14 soldi) and lodging (19 libras and eight soldi). _da mar_ or beyond its borders.[42] Most individuals from the western Balkans were Croat nobles engaged in the defence of the Dalmatian hinterlands. 

Before addressing the ecclesiastical and economic elites, we must consider the size of Zadar’s nobility in relation to the rest of the population. As Giovanni Battista Giustiniano mentioned in his itinerary, seventeen aristocratic families existed in Zadar around the mid-16[th] century (a number disputed by Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec and Peričić, who suggested the existence of twenty families comprising roughly 600 individuals). The adult male nobles formed the city’s council, which in 1553 consisted of 70 individuals.[43] The combination of these numbers allows for the tentative conclusion that Zadar’s nobility made up approximately a tenth of the total population (which is also reflected in the share of existing testaments and codicils: those of noble provenance make up approximately the same 10% of the total).[44] 

## 2. _Ecclesiastical elites: convents, hospitals and parish churches_ 

The following section further explores the various layers of Zadar’s Church hierarchy and examines clerical interactions with the larger society. In the existing literature on the subject, the Dalmatian “ecclesiastical elite” has been defined as the higher echelons of the Church, including the archbishops of Dubrovnik, Split and Zadar; the bishops of Hvar, Korčula, Kotor, Krk, Nin, Osor, Rab, Ston and Trogir, as well as the members of their respective chapters; and the heads and chapters of the monasteries. While the provosts of the large and wealthy convents continued to wield considerable influence, their integration into, and interactions with, society at-large have “only exceptionally been a matter of interest to scholars of the Dalmatian Middle Ages”.[45] Many of these dignitaries were, in fact, “elite citizens” (Grubb) or aristocrats well-integrated into the economic and social fabric of society, irrespective of their geographical provenance. Consequently, both individual members of the clergy and the institutions to which they belonged are here regarded as part of the same socio-occupational group of the “clergy”. Ecclesiastical dignitaries, convents, hospitals and parish churches – as well as individuals representing these people, offices or prebendaries – were members of this category. 

At the top of the ecclesiastical hierarchy stood Zadar’s archbishopric and its chapter. While these offices and the lower-ranking bishoprics were reserved

Urban Elites of Zadar 

120 

for Venetian patricians only,[46] the chapters usually included non-nobles.[47] The second layer within the ecclesiastical hierarchy consisted of the bishop of Nin, forced to reside in Zadar after the devastation caused by the Ottoman-Venetian war of 1537-1540.[48] Next in the hierarchy were the convents. As Table 2.10 outlined (Chapter 2), these were affiliated with the Benedictines, Dominicans and Franciscans. Further down the hierarchy were the parish churches within the city proper, the hospitals of St Jacob and St Mark and the parish churches found throughout Zadar’s jurisdiction. 

Generally, the archbishop and the bishop of Nin and their adjunct institutions are absent from the notarial records. There are, however, some exceptions, usually occurring when new office holders were appointed or when other matters of importance prompted high-level intervention. One of these instances occurred in the mid-1550s when “Johannes Thomaseus, canon and vicar of Zadar, as procurator and in the name of, as he claims, the Reverend Father Cornelio Pisauro, dignified archbishop of Zadar”, exchanged a quarter of a stone house in the city’s St Catherine’s parish to the heirs of “the late Franciscus de Magistris de Pisauro, …Dionore et Priuigna”. The heirs, in turn, gave away half a house constructed of stone and wood in Zadar’s St Vitus’ parish.[49] Other activities of the archbishop or his representatives included the lease of possessions in the city’s hinterlands (sometimes on the condition of

obtaining the consent of the pope)[50] and the receipt of pledges of allegiance from lower-ranking ecclesiastical dignitaries.[51] 

In some cases, new appointees were tasked with the acquisition of outstanding payments. In 1556 two of Zadar’s citizens, “priest Sanctus de Sanctis, canon of Zadar, and Johannes Raymundinus, citizen of Zadar”, appointed “the Reverend Father in Christ Mutius Calino”, the absent archbishop-elect, to retrieve all money owed “by the Apostolic See, specifically from the High Chambers of Apostolic Auditors”. At the time of stipulation, the incoming dignitary was not present – Calino was probably still in Rome or its vicinity – so the two constituents sought to take advantage of the procurator’s social status and presumed geographical proximity to the Vatican.[52] 

A second example involved the designated bishop of Nin. Upon appointment, the new dignitary left behind his former prebendaries to travel to his see, often with little knowledge of the new diocese. This happened to “the Reverend Father in Christ Marcus Lauredanus [Loredan], by the Grace of God and the Apostolic See bishop of Nin and abbot of San Michele in Monte in the city of Pula [Pola]”.[53] After his designation, he was required to relocate to central Dalmatia where, upon arrival, one of Marcus’ first actions was to appoint “the Reverend Dominicus Armanus, primicerius of Nin”, with taking care of the administrative business in Istria.[54] Beyond these two examples there exists little evidence that these high ecclesiastical dignitaries engaged in mundane activities, with perhaps one notable exception: sees commanded significant amounts of landed property, and to 

50. In March 1555, Cornelio Pisauro leased a patch of land belonging to the archbishopric close to Kali, Pašman, “a spettabili domino Doymo, et domino Petro fratribus de Cedulinis nobilibus Jadre”. The contracting parties agreed on annual payments of four ducats for the duration of 29 years (i.e. an “emphytheotica concessio”), “cum hac expressa condictione, et declaratione per Ipsas partes in presenti Instrumento apposite…nec dicti fratres, neque eorum heredes et Successores possint ullo modo cogi, neque compelli ad Solutionem in toto, ut in parte dicti livelli seu pensionis donec et quousque huiusmodi datio et livellaria ac emphytheotica concessio non fuerit confirmata per sanctissimam in Christo patrem et dominum Nostrum dominum Julium divina providentia Papam tertium, seu per eius sancti sedem apostolicam, qua Sic inter partes Ipsas per pactum expressum conventum et statutum fuit”. Urban Elites of Zadar 

122 

monetise these resources, procurators had to be regularly appointed. For instance, Zadar’s archbishopric possessed estates within the city’s jurisdiction as well as on the neighbouring island of Pag. In both jurisdictions, property was auctioned off to the highest bidder for tax farming purposes.[55] 

The following discussion and accompanying tables analyse Zadar’s nobles and their ties with ecclesiastical institutions. Three of the convents within the city walls were exclusively reserved for nuns of aristocratic descent. Each of these nunneries was affiliated with one of three large monastic orders. The Benedictines ran St Mary’s nunnery, the Franciscan Poor Clares the nunnery of St Nicholas and the Dominicans the nunnery of St Demetrius (see also Table 2.10). 

As Table 3.2 indicates, several of Zadar’s aristocratic families showed preferences for one or another of the monastic orders. This snapshot in time (1559) is supported by evidence deriving from the testaments and codicils of the city’s nobles. In addition to the relative popularity of the three congregations, these documents further reveal which families tended to prefer which order. 

To that effect, I have analysed the surviving testaments and codicils of Zadar’s nobles for the period from 1540 through 1569, and these 111 documents provide additional interesting correlations between the noble families and specific monastic orders. Summarised in Table 3.3, the results indicate that the churches of the Benedictine and Franciscan orders were the preferred burial places of Zadar’s nobility. Combined, the two congregations housed two-thirds of their gravesites. Table 3.2, above, shows that the Grisogonus-de Bartholatijs clan[56] had many relatives in each of the three convents. A closer look at the testaments furthermore reveals that six of the twenty-one individuals requesting burial in a Benedictine church were members of the Grisogonus-de Bartholatijs families (related by either blood or marriage). This and the fact that only two family members requested burial elsewhere suggests a clear preference for the Order of St Benedict. On the other hand, the Begna and Nassis families preferred the Dominicans while the Fanfoneus, Ferra and Pechiaro families were rather associated with the Franciscans (Table 3.4, below). 

55. As for the sources, the archbishop rented out “omnes, et quoscumque fructus, redditus, et proventus, affictus Jurisdictionis…Tam Terre firmae tamquam Insularum, diocesis Jadrensis, et decimam Insule Pagi” for the annual payment of 620 ducats. In a contract from the mid1540s, the archbishop himself rented his sees’ income to Johannes Mazzarellus, a noble of Trogir, notary public and Zadar’s communal chancellor, and Johannes Thomaseus, a citizen, canon, priest and member of the metropolitan chapter. Two weeks later, the two accepted “dominum presbytrum Joannem Barbiricich” as the third co-tenant, slightly augmenting the latter’s price from 620 ducats to 635 per annum. Relatedly, an instrument in Simon Budineus’s records demonstrates that the non-noble congregation of the Poor Clares (under the patronage of St Marcella) appointed the same procurator that their aristocratic counterparts did (“Nobilem Virum Jadrensis Joannem Chrisogonum quondam domini Andreae”) for the same task of the liberation of the tithes (see also Table 3.2).[57] In addition, some of the surnames of the nuns listed indicate that they were among the most prestigious non-noble, or elite, citizens of Zadar.[58]

<!-- page 127 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

126 

The number of noble nuns in autumn of 1562 amounted to 56. If this number is put in the context of Zadar’s overall aristocratic population of around 600,[59] it looks like around 20% of Zadar’s noble women lived in convents (based on the assumption that half of these 600 individuals were female; note further that the tables above are not based upon monastic records but notarial deeds). These numbers are corroborated, to a certain degree, by the fact that a high percentage of noble women in Venice and elsewhere in Italy lived in convents, a trend likely mirrored by other urban societies in the _stato da mar_ , including Dalmatia, as suggested by Jutta Sperling and Mary Laven.[60] 

The third ecclesiastical group of actors consisted of the parish churches and hospitals in Zadar’s old town and its surroundings. While the appearance of these institutions in the notary protocols ranges from relative prominence to insignificance, their activity patterns were like the two other groups discussed above. They too appointed procurators for a wide variety of reasons, from leasing parts of their prebends to the highest bidder to the election of new chaplains.[61] 

For instance, around 1550 the church of St Simeon, dedicated to one of Zadar’s two patron saints,[62] had two procurators: “the respectable and excellent doctor of both laws Petrus Fanfoneus and the respectable Doymus Cedulinus”. Both were prominent nobles whose appointments testify to the importance of the parish and its collegium (consistent with the church’s function as the resting place of St Simeon’s relics). Together they appointed another nobleman and family member of Doymus to represent the church in Venice, “Petrus Cedulinum”. 

59. Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , pp. 261-262. 

60. Sperling, _Convents and the Body Politic_ , pp. 18-71: 18-19, suggests monachisation rates among noble women may have exceeded 50% around 1580. See also Laven, _Virgins of Venice_ . 

61. Elections of chaplains rarely appear in the surveyed notarial instruments; however, they bestowed property upon the new dignitary. Usually, the deceased benefactor of a prebend would leave indications in his testament, either willing them directly to his successor or providing for the election of a new holder. The latter instance occurred in the mid-1550s involving the two noble Grisogonus and Tetricus families. In October of 1555 “Reverendus Franciscus de Bartholatijs Nobilis et Archipresbyter Jadrensis ex una, et Magnifici ac Nobiles Viri dominus Joannes Baptista, ac Aloysius Tetrici, Nec non ac nomine et vice Magnificorum dominorum Lombardini et Nicolai Nec Non Magnifica dominae Corneliae Tetricae, ac praefatus dominus Aloysius uti Donatarius et cessionarius noti Venerabilae dominae Perinae de Fumatis monialis In monasterio Sanctae Mariae de Jadra…ex altera”, in accordance with a notarial instrument stipulated by Johannes Mazzarellus on 12 April 1554, convened in Zadar’s communal chancellory. Referring to several other documents from as far back as 10 August 1497 and 17 January 1551, Franciscus de Bartholatijs “fuerit et sit electus et institutus in cappellanum ad altarem seu capellam Sanctae Mariae virginis in dicta ecclesia moniales Sanctae Mariae…vigore Testamenti quondam Magnifici domini Donati Ciualelli Nobili Jadre facti Venetijs”. Usually, elections such as this were followed by the ratification of instruments at the behest of all involved parties. In this case, however, the approbation was followed by Franciscus de Bartholatijs ceding the prebendary to “Reverendum ac excellentem dominum Presbytrum Blasium Sidineum Jadrensis”, an absent doctor of canon and civil law represented by his procurator and uncle, “Reverendus dominus Presbyter Simon Tutofeus”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 1, s.p., 23 October 1555 (two individual instruments). 

62. The other is St Anastasia, to whom Zadar’s cathedral is dedicated.

<!-- page 128 -->

Actors 

127 

Petrus was sent “to negotiate, and in humility present himself at the feet of the illustrious Doge of the Venetians and approach anyone or any other acclaimed magistrate in said high city… to obtain, by grace, assistance for the necessary renovations of the church [of St Simeon’s]”. In cases of such importance, the constituent party sought to appoint high-ranking members of society to increase the odds of success.[63] 

Similarly, parish churches rented out parts of their prebendaries for revenues. In autumn of 1554, “the magnificent and excellent doctor of both laws and knight [ _eques_ ] Johannes Rosa, together with Simon Britanicus, acting as procurators of the church of Our Glorious Lady of Peace in the suburbs of Zadar”, two of Zadar’s most renowned individuals, appear in the sources (see also Chapter 6). By virtue of their appointment as representatives, they leased the income of six morgen (c. 1.4 hectares) located “in the vicinity of the village of Cerno [Crno]” to “Stephano Goycich, a butcher, citizen and resident of Zadar”. For three years starting in March of 1555, Stephano agreed to an annual payment of 10 ducats, transferable “at the end of each month of February”.[64] 

characteristics were quite like the instances discussed so far. Two hospitals existed within the walls of Zadar, while two more lazarettos were situated further away.[65] Usually, the provosts simply appointed general procurators to represent their institutions.[66] Every now and then, the administration of a charitable institution

By the mid-16[th] century, the situation was as follows: the “lazzaretto di San Marco”, situated in today’s Kolovare area to the southeast of the city centre, served the leprous; a second hospital, the “lazaretj pestiferorum Jadre”, cared for those who contracted the plague (or, presumably, any other visible, highly contagious disease). Another lazaretto appeared in the notarial records. Apparently no longer in use by the mid-16[th] century, its name carried on as a toponym. Urban Elites of Zadar 

128 

was bequeathed at least a part of an inheritance. Once the testator or testatrix had made his or her intentions known, a notary was needed to stipulate legally binding documentation. For instance, in 1550 “Martinus de Lucadellis _guardianus_ [provost], ser Simon Britanicus, and Joannes Raymundinus acting in their names and on behalf of Nicolaus de Ventura”, all citizens of Zadar and procurators of the Hospital of St Jacob, intervened to secure such a bequest. Among the female inhabitants of the institution was “Simonella who offered and promised, upon her death, to leave to the hospital all her possessions found in her dwellings”. She did so “freely and deliberately, neither seduced nor coerced” but on the condition “that said hospital is obliged to arrange for her funeral after her death”, probably because her husband, “the late ser Franciscus Britanicus”, could no longer do so.[67] 

As for ecclesiastical individuals, one of the most interesting cases was without a doubt the cleric Simon Budineus or “Budinich”,[68] probably better known under his Slavicised name Šime Budinić. He was born in Zadar where he also worked as a notary public and was most renowned for his literary skills.[69] In the early 1580s he moved to Rome,[70] where he published at least two books in his native tongue. In these books he pioneered the use of certain diacritic signs (č, ž).[71] After spending a couple of years in Italy he returned to his hometown, where he died on 13 December 1600.[72] 

Much less is known about his earlier years, except that he was born in the early 1530s. He appeared in the instruments of Zadar’s notaries more than 20 years later, referred to as “cleric and provost [ _mansionarius_ ] of the cathedral church of Zadar”.[73] He left six books filled with 827 individual notarial acts, of which the earliest is dated 3 October 1556; the last entry was written on 3 July 1565.[74] In the decade he spent in Zadar, Simon Budineus lived and worked in both

3.75 morgen (c. 8,887.5 m[2] ) next to Vrsevac along the road to Nin for the price of 140 libras. In this contract the buyer is specifically referenced as “clericus et notarius jadrensis”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 1, 5, cc. 15r-15v, 1 June 1557.
