129 

the spiritual and temporal worlds, although he only rarely participated in mundane business. Only two contracts hint at economic activities. The first, written in January 1560, mentions that he rented “all fruit of this year brought forth from his canonry that is his [i.e., tithes]” from his uncle, “the Reverend Sanctus de Sanctis, canon of Zadar”. The two contracting parties agreed upon the price of 24 ducats, of which Simon had paid 23 libras and 2 soldi up front and another 76 libras and 18 soldi in cash. The rest, 24 libras, were promised to be paid within the following three weeks. Kinship ties between the two parties notwithstanding, business was business: “Anastasia…de Sale [Sali]”, a resident of Zadar, “vouched for, and offering surety for said tenant [Simon Budineus] and his heirs”. The document’s valediction also reveals the approximate location of Sanctus’s house: “Done in Zadar in the house of residence of said leaseholder ”.[75] _in confinio castri_ 

In the second instrument, drawn up in late November 1558, Simon Budineus conceded three morgen (c. 7,110 m[2] ) in the vicinity of St Peter of Iž to “the renowned doctor of both laws Petrus Fanfoneus, a nobleman of Zadar”, for the period of five years. Simon is referred to as “parishioner [ _plebanus_ ], as he affirms, of said church of St. Peter of Iž”, rendering it likely that he transferred a part of the church’s prebend. The contract further details the obligation to cultivate the parcel of land in its entirety over the next three years and hand over a quarter of the grapes and a third of the olives from 1563 onward. In addition, Petrus was assured a bonus of 4 soldi for each newly planted olive tree.[76] As suggested by his marginal appearance in the instrument books during his documented presence in Zadar, Simon was busy with the other aspects of his existence. His duties as the metropolitan sacristan and the administration of the prebend of St Peter’s of Iž were complemented by his literary and linguistic studies. In comparison with his notarial peers, his activities considerably elevated his social standing within Zadar’s society, and they are explored further in Chapter 7.[77] 

75. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 2A, cc. 46v-47r, 9 January 1560. 

76. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Canali, I, 1, 2, cc. 2r-3r, 28 November 1558. This particular contract is not the only one in which bonuses for the planting of new olive trees are apportioned. At the root of this clause was the decline of olive tree cultivation due to the increasing insecurity, a development further reinforced by the Ottoman-Venetian war of 15371540. In his contemporary report, Giacomo Pisano, count of Zadar 1564-1566, who returned to Venice in early 1566, wrote that “fu fatto un proclama, che alcuno non potesse pinatar vignie, se non piantasse per ogni gugnial [gonjaj] 12 piedi de olivari, con pene a quelli fussero inobbedienti; per il che fino al partir mio n’erano stà piantadi 5,100. Et essendo, come è, beneffitio delli suoi sudditi, continueranno al piantarne ogn’anno. Et si facea nel contado et isole avanti 1537 miera 600 [1 mjera = c. 80 litres, via _Statuta Iadertina_ , p. 759]; ma per li disturbi hora se ne fanno 100 miera, essendo secati et brusati li olivari; il qual con molto apresso che vien dalla Puglia si consuma nella città, contado et isole”. Not only did the overall production of olives and olive drop to about one-sixth of pre-1537 levels, but it also even necessitated the import of olives and olive oil from Apulia. _Commissiones_ , vol. Urban Elites of Zadar

## 3. _Economic elites: actors, merchants and commodities_ 

activities, divided into the categories of mercantile endeavours and real estate markets. During the Middle Ages, banking, commerce and trade were mostly dominated by foreign individuals and institutions.[78] While this started to change with the advent of the second Venetian dominion in Dalmatia after 1409, the emphasis here rests on the accessible information provided by the notaries of Zadar in the mid-16[th] century. 

The reports by Venice’s governors, legates and military commanders paint a consistent picture of Zadar and its populace as living “on little income, mostly from commerce and tradecraft”. The city’s nobles, Giovanni Battista Giustiniano wrote in 1553, “are not wealthy, and the majority of their income among them ranges from 400 or 500 to at most 700 ducats”, with only three families – the Tetrico, Rosa and Civallello clans – disposing of up to 700 ducats annually.[79] Tomislav Raukar wrote of an economically declining nobility after 1409, with even fewer wealthy commoners. The decline in salt production and the rising incidence of Ottoman incursions, which laid the agriculturally important hinterland to waste, exacerbated Dalmatia’s economic decline.[80] The absence of many commercial activities in the notary books underscores Giustiniano’s comment about the city’s declining wealth. With a considerable shift away from commerce and trade, economic activity was increasingly directed toward real estate, the selling and renting of livestock,[81] the

78. Esp. by the business practices of Venetian patricians, at times even anachronistically called a “ _holding_ ”, via Doumerc, “Dominio del mare”, pp. 150-154: 151 (emphasis in the original). See also Lane, “Family Partnerships”, p. 37; Chojnacki, “Kinship Ties”, p. 246.

Raukar pointed to both foreign (Ottoman) and de facto domestic (Venetian) factors that, in combination with the economic and political _conjuncture_ in the wider Mediterranean, caused the Dalmatian cities to first stagnate during the 15[th] and continued to decline in the 16[th] century. Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 214-219, 281-297. 

81. Over the three surveyed decades, only 25 contracts document the sale of livestock. In addition, there are 11 contracts in which livestock was leased or rented to someone else by the owner. In all 36 instances, the number of owners remained limited, suggesting that the livestock was firmly concentrated in the hands of a few families: the Nassis (named in 11 out of the 36 instances), Soppe (4 deeds), Begna (3 deeds) and Ferra and Rosa families (one each). In addition, the archbishopric is named twice as the owner of the livestock. All other owners were listed only once. This concentration is mirrored on the buyer/tenant side, with the merchants of the de Pontremolo (both Johannes Antonius and Lazarus, 9 deeds), Ventura families (Franciscus and Hieronymus, 3 deeds) and Soppe (7 deeds) ranking supreme among recipients. All other owners of noble (e.g., the Begna or Gallellus families) and non-noble (Toninus, de Hermolais) descent were listed only once. In the livestock sales, the turnover amounted to c. 2,370 ducats, with prices ranging from one ducat per cow to three to four goats per ducat. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 8, f. 15v, 15 September 1541; Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, f. 53r, 10 March, 1555; Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 4, f. 170v, 3 October 1558. The turnover in the 11 rental contracts ( _locationes_ ) was smaller, totalling only c. 1,017 ducats (92 ducats on average). As for the number of livestock involved, most documents do

<!-- page 132 -->

Actors 

131 

leasing of (admittedly few) salt pans on the neighbouring island of Pag[82] and the sale of (even fewer) ships.[83] 

This brings us to the problem of identifying the important economic _actors_ . Judging from the notarial records, the involvement of Venetians in the local markets of Zadar was all but non-existent. The local aristocracy and clergy dominated its economy and property markets. Since property markets are discussed in more detail in the following chapter, let us first examine the economic aspects unrelated to real estate transactions. 

With few exceptions, the livestock market was controlled by Zadar’s nobility, which provided those who owned cattle ( _pecudina_ ), goats ( _caprina_ , _pecora_ ) and small domestic animals ( _animalia minuta_ ) with a steady stream of income. As might be expected, the actual number of livestock owners was very small. Among the city’s nobility, the Begna and Nassis families stand out, and they were not the only possessors of livestock.[84] The Nassis family sold livestock on at least seven occasions, and in four instances the buyers came from the de Pontremulo merchant family. In all, 414 small domestic animals changed hands, netting the Nassis clan a total of 408 ducats.[85] Interestingly, this relationship between the contracting parties was kept up in two rental instruments. In both 

not list them, but here and there we find references to herds of 400-800 animals. Even though these contracts remain silent on the species of animals, a third contract listing 600 goats and sheep has been found. Most likely, these were goats or sheep as the existence of comparable numbers of cattle in the agriculturally marginal lands of central Dalmatia is highly unlikely. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, f. 78v, 27 June 1555; Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, ff. 80r-80v, 4 July 1555; Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 11, s.p., 1 February 1556. Based upon 25 livestock sales ( _emptiones_ ) and 11 livestock leases ( _locationes_ ), in 20 out of these 36 transactions, most livestock was located on islands off Zadar but still within the city’s jurisdiction.

instances, “Catherina daughter of the late Simon de Nassis known as il Mesco, a nobleman of Zadar, and wife of Lucius Georgio of Venice”, leased 255 goats and sheep to “Johannes de Pontremulo, citizen and merchant of Zadar”. Included in the contracts, which grossed another 199 ducats, was the provision that the renting party also be granted usufruct rights to the meadows in which the animals were kept.[86] In short, most proprietors of livestock came from the ranks of Zadar’s nobility, while the buyers or leaseholders were either fellow nobles or merchants. Only 40 notarial instruments deal with the transactions of livestock, which, due to the insecurity across the Ottoman-Venetian border, were mostly kept on the coastal islands. 

The second major non-real estate commodity were salt pans. Production of this vital good, which during the Hungarian suzerainty was the backbone of Zadar’s economy, declined significantly after 1409.[87] By the middle of the 16[th] century, only very few members of the local clergy and aristocracy were still engaged in this trade. Of the latter group, the family of “Francischina, wife of the late Nicolaus de Rosa, nobleman of Zadar”, sold, on four different occasions, a total of 25 salt pans “in Pag valley in the parish of St John Incangerich” for the sum of 433 ducats. On all four occasions, the buyer was the same person, “Georgio Mirchouich, a nobleman of Pag”. Payment for these transactions was conferred via an intermediary, the well-known Lazarus de Gnochis de Pontremulo, explicitly referred to as “depositarius”, or broker.[88] Another noble family of Zadar in possession of salt pans on Pag was the Fanfoneus clan. Its most prominent members, “the renowned and excellent doctor of both laws Petrus and the knight Franciscus Fanfoneus”, rented 18 salt pans each to “ser Antonius Romucich of Pag”, and they did so for the duration of six years in exchange for the sum of 312 ducats.[89] Both the nobility and the clergy were involved, and it 

86. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 6, cc. 313r-313v, 12 May 1561, Cornelius Constantius, I, 1, 1, cc. 15r-15v, 16 July 1567. 

87. The number of salt pans in use in Zadar’s jurisdiction declined from 169 (104 run by nobles, 65 by commoners) during the 14[th] century to a mere 42 (26 run by nobles, 16 by commoners) between 1409 and 1500. This decline of approximately 75% was mirrored by the decline of salt production on the neighbouring island of Pag during the same period. While there were 942 (825 run by nobles, 117 by commoners) salt pans in use during the 14[th] century, only 345 (288 run by nobles, 57 by commoners) were in use after the advent of the second Venetian dominion. These developments were brought about by Venetian attempts to monopolise the salt trade, rendering it increasingly difficult for local investors to profit, which further contributed to the overall decline of economic activities after 1409. Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 206-219, 281-297.

is to the latter we now turn. For instance, the Benedictine noble nunnery of St Mary’s of Zadar leased the income of its salt pans on three different occasions between 1540 and 1569: once to Antonio Ramorich de Pago, once to Georgius Mirchouich and once to “Matheo Migauzich, a nobleman of Pag”. While the total earned sum of 12 ducats and 175 libras appears comparatively low, deals like these nonetheless provided the ecclesiastical institutions with a secure and steady flow of income.[90] 

The third area of commerce was maritime trade. Apart from Franciscus Dandolo, this segment of the local economy existed virtually beyond the activities of domestic and foreign nobilities. The contracting parties originated from all over the Adriatic, from Malamocco (Malamocho)[91] and Chioggia (Clodia)[92] in the Venetian lagoon to Piran in Istria,[93] extending southward to Zadar, Šibenik and beyond.[94] The number of ships that were sold was very low, however, as documented in the notarial records. 

Only exceptionally are resident Venetian patricians mentioned between 1540 and 1570 in connection to commercial activity. One of them was “the magnificent Francisco Dandulo [Dandolo], son of the late acclaimed Johannes”, a presumably lower-ranking member of the prestigious Venetian family residing in Zadar.[95] Compared to his peers, he was in many ways the exception to the rule as he quite actively participated in the local real estate market: he bought two

In summer or autumn 1551 Franciscus was crossing the Adriatic to trade in Apulia when the ship carrying him capsized and sank. This incident caused his nephew, “the magnificent Marinus Dandulo, son of the equally magnificent Marcus Antonius, a patrician of Venice”, to travel to Zadar. He and his absent brothers, “Petrus and Andreas”, were the heirs of their deceased uncle. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 9, f. 27r, 9 August 1543. A grippo was a small, single-masted sailing boat, up to 17 metres in length and 3-4 metres in breadth employed in commerce and fishing and could, if needed, also be rowed and converted for fighting purposes. Lane, _Ships and Shipbuilders_ , p. 53.

This brief account of the life and death of Franciscus Dandolo serves as an example of those individuals who, by virtue of descent, education and commercial abilities, were part of the economic, political and social elite of Zadar. A comparable group of individuals was the branch of the Venier family that resided in Zadar and that was also integrated into the wider society (see also Chapter 6).[103]

The local Venier branch also appears comparatively prominent in the reports of the Venetian officials where they were recorded as in charge of the fortified outpost in Zemunik in the early 1540s. ## 4. Zadar’s Property Markets, 1540-1569 

The sixteenth century was an era of profound crisis for Zadar. The Turks conquered not only…most of the Croatian hinterlands but also the majority of Zadar’s [continental] jurisdiction. Whereas in 1527 the city, including the suburbs, counted a little more than 8,000 inhabitants plus another 16,000 in the jurisdiction, these numbers sank to around 5,200 and 9,000 respectively until 1598, although most of the latter lived on the islands (Pašman, Ugljan, etc.) off the coast.[1] 

Neven Budak 

The decades following Venice’s renewed control over most of Dalmatia witnessed many changes. One of these changes was the slow but steady decline of opportunities in maritime commerce, due to a variety of factors ranging from lack of capital to increased Ottoman naval activities. These contributed to the decreasing significance of the entire region. Less investment in long-distance trade over the course of the 15[th] century precipitated the flow of money into what appeared to be safer assets as explored magisterially by Tomislav Raukar fifty years ago.[2] This chapter examines trends in the subsequent century. It offers new insights into the size and turnover of Zadar’s real estate transactions between 1540 and 1569 through investigation of the following three segments of the markets: sales ( _emptiones_ ), planting concessions ( _concessiones_ , _pastinationes_ ) and rental contracts ( _locationes_ ), and it does so including for the first time property transactions on the islands off the coast. 

1. Budak, “Drei Zentralstädte”, p. 116. 

2. This was well-established for 15[th] -century Zadar by Raukar, who determined that real estate, however, held a continuous importance in the local economy even after 1409. Consequently, increasing trade restrictions imposed by Venice, he claimed, resulted in less available surplus capital available for commerce, thereby further reinforcing Dalmatia’s economic decline, manifested by the contemporaneous rise of whatever funds available into real estate. Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 71-196, esp. 151-196 on economic development.

## 1. _Property sales (_ emptiones _)_ 

The sale of immovable property was by far the largest segment of the property markets in terms of individual transactions, exceeding the number of rental contracts almost fivefold.[3] Table 4.2 indicates that despite the somewhat lopsided distribution across Zadar’s notaries, three main observations can be made. First, while the apportionment of the data into three decades is arbitrary, the total number of property sales per decade remained virtually constant.[4] Second, in almost all instances, the language the notaries used for their instrument books was Latin. At least in this regard, property sales constitute a slight deviation from the _procurae_ (of which about 3% were drawn up in Venetian). A more important difference between the property sales and procuratorial instruments is that, as contracting parties, women made up a smaller share of the former than the latter. Whereas in antiquity women enjoyed rights comparable to those of men 

> 3. An exemplary transcript of such a contract is provided in the appendix. 

4. The average number of notarised deeds for all the three decades is 355.67. The median is 356.

<!-- page 140 -->

Zadar’s Property Markets 

139 

the Middle Ages. It was virtually impossible for women to buy or sell their own immovable property without the explicit consent of their father, husband, brother or other male relative.[5] Table 4.2 shows that slightly fewer women participated in property sales than appointed procurators (compared to the data summarised in Table 2.1). On average, slightly more than a quarter of all contracting parties who appointed procurators were female compared to only around 16% who sold real estate. An even bigger contrast is seen in the percentage of procurators who were women (a mere 2%) compared to the percentage of acquiring parties who were women (around 8%, about four times higher).

The following analysis of Zadar’s property market details the occupational, geographical and social provenances of the contracting parties, the size of the real estate in question and the relationship between its location and price. 

Unsurprisingly, slightly fewer than 90% of all sellers originated from within the jurisdiction of Zadar. Roughly a tenth could not be identified, and the remaining 2% originated elsewhere in the Adriatic basin.[6] By further breaking down the

6. A total of 941 out of 1,067 (or c. 88%) of constituents originated within Zadar’s jurisdiction. In 103 instances, the provenance was not mentioned (c. 10%); the remaining

<!-- page 141 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

140 

numbers to include the subdivisions of the city’s jurisdiction, the picture changes. Of these 90%, two-thirds lived within the city walls or suburban settlements. Less than a fifth came from one of the coastal islands, and the remaining stipulating parties originated from the other areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction, i.e. the mediaeval urban territory ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ), the minor districts of Nin, Novigrad and Vrana, and any other remaining area.[7] 

The share of buyers from within Zadar’s jurisdiction was slightly higher. On average, almost 91% of individuals who acquired real estate came from Zadar or one of the jurisdiction’s subdivisions. This marginal increase over the sellers corresponded with a small decrease in the number of unidentifiable buyers whose share decreased to some 8%. Additionally, the share of people who originated from elsewhere is negligible, totalling less than 1%. Again, broken down into identical subdivisions, of these nine buyers seven lived in Zadar or its suburbs, islanders accounted for one of the acquiring parties, and all remaining areas “shared” the last buyer.[8] 

In terms of social provenance, the picture is less monolithic. The nobility (local and non-local) made up slightly more than 8% of the sellers and some 11% of the buyers. Their shares roughly correspond with estimates of the nobility’s proportion of Zadar’s population around the middle of the 16[th] century.[9] Interesting changes occurred in the shares of the other sellers and buyers: on average, artisans, members of the intellectual elite and merchants made up some 12% of the sellers. Their corresponding share among the acquiring parties was about 30%. Broken down further, it emerges that the artisanal group increased their share by only slightly more than 40%, with differences between sellers and buyers pertaining to the intellectual elites and merchant groups being more dramatic. The buying share of intellectual elites _quadrupled_ compared to its activities as sellers (61 vs. 15 occasions). The increase in the merchants’ activities was even greater. Merchants, tradesmen and small retailers sold property in only sixteen instances, but they bought property on 120 occasions.[10]

While these changes are impressive, they must be put in perspective. Only a quarter of all sellers belonged to the intellectual elite or merchant groups, while the other three-quarters did not. Their share of the purchasing market was slightly higher than 40%. In contrast to their share among the sellers, members of the urban elites made up more than half of the buyers. Property sales remained more or less stable in terms of individual contracts per decade, which the upper strata of society continued to dominate by virtue of their economic muscle.

Table 4.3 highlights two facts. First, while the total number of sales remained virtually constant over the period under survey, acreage transferred decreased by a tenth from 368 hectares sold in the 1540s to 334 hectares the 1550s. But the big change occurred in the decade leading up to the Cyprus War. When compared with the preceding decade, the total number of morgens sold more than doubled, even though this massive change is not reflected by any significant increase in notarised contracts (Table 4.2). This sudden jump in activity most certainly exerted pressure on property prices, which we shall now investigate.

and on the four minor islands of Iž (Eso, Exo), Rava (Raua), Silba (Selve) and Vrgada (Vergata). 

As detailed in Table 4.4, average property prices in the outlying areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction did not change uniformly. While it is true that increased insecurity in the rural areas contributed significantly to falling prices, this may not have been the only cause. Another explanation may be that the total transferred acreage increased more than fifteen times from the 1540s to the 1560s, putting downward pressure on prices. These numbers must be treated cautiously; due to data loss, the sample size for this category is very small, especially in comparison with the other two categories of territory and the coastal islands. 

In addition to an increased desire for property closer to the safety of the city walls, there were forces at work in land-holding trends, as revealed by data from the territory ( _Astareja_ , _ager publicus_ ). Even a slightly receding number of morgens sold had a disproportionately large impact on the average price per unit sold. The factor by which the total acreage increased from the 1540s and 1550s to the decade prior to the Cyprus War is not as high as in the jurisdiction category, but the number of morgens sold almost doubled. This should have exerted downward pressure on prices; however, average prices for a morgen of land in sight of Zadar’s city walls _rose_ in lockstep with the acreage transferred. This development is even more pronounced once we consider the relatively consistent value of the ducat and its fixed conversion rate of 6 libras and 4 soldi per ducat. While prices doubled, the monetary denominations kept their relative

<!-- page 144 -->

Zadar’s Property Markets 

143 

value, suggesting that these developments were not the result of the invisible hand of the market alone.[11] 

The coastal islands are the best-documented category – or control group – since the total number of contracts involving the transaction of a parcel of land remained virtually constant over the entire period under survey. The total acreage sold increased considerably less than in the two other examples discussed, by a factor of 170%. Whatever the exact reasons, the average price for a morgen of real estate on the islands remained equally stable at the (virtual) value of about 5.5 ducats per unit sold. This is even more remarkable considering the changes in the overall number of contracts per decade. 

The total number of ducats transferred by all real estate transactions during the three decades amounted to c. 20,529 ducats. Over this timespan the various developments corresponded with the turnover per decade. During the 1540s the transaction volume amounted to c. 5,072 ducats. The 1550s witnessed a modest increase since this sum totalled c. 6,105.5 ducats. In the 1560s, the total turnover was c. 9,351.5 ducats, almost twice as high as the first decade and roughly in line with the numbers discussed above. 

were drawn up. Generally, the communal square with the loggia and the jurists’ bench was the most important place to conduct any business requiring notarial validation. However, analysis of these 1,067 contracts presents a more nuanced picture. The communal square was noted as the location of stipulation in but a third of all instances. The second-largest share of instruments (slightly more than a quarter) were written in the houses of one of the contracting parties.[12] Around 15% of the acts were written in business or storage facilities ( _apotheca_ ), usually located in close proximity to the communal square.[13] Another 13.5% were drawn up in one of Zadar’s chancelleries, and less than 3% were made on ecclesiastical property.[14] The

In the 1540s, 33 contracts transferred c. 552 morgen (c. 131 hectares) for a total of c. 641 ducats, while in the 1560s c. 366 morgen (c. 87 hectares) changed their proprietors for c. 621.5 ducats. The total amount of money remained virtually constant, but the number of individual property transactions and the number of morgens transferred decreased by roughly the same factor as the price went up.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

144 

remaining deeds were notarised in a variety of locations, including the suburbs,[15] ships in port[16] and places as seemingly unlikely “atop the Porta Terraferma”.[17] 

The sales market was not entirely dependent on the economic muscle of the urban elites. This is underscored by the fact that elite groups made up only a quarter of vendors, and their share among the buyers was only slightly higher than 50%. Zadar’s urban elites invested a considerable share of their income in real estate, probably because of its perceived security. The overall volume of the property sales almost doubled during the three interwar decades, mirrored by the number of morgens transferred and the turnover of ducats. This put pressure on property prices, but it did so in a quite nuanced manner. While the number of contracts transferring real estate within sight of Zadar and their respective prices doubled over the three decades, other areas were less affected. Increasing demand in the territory and rising insecurity in the more remote areas of the city’s jurisdiction on the mainland caused a drop of property prices located further away from the city walls. These developments are also corroborated by the price for a morgen of land on one of the islands off the coast, which remained stable over the entire period under survey. 

## 2. _Land grants and planting concessions (_ concessiones _)_ 

The second important sector of the real estate market were land grants ( _concessio_ ) and planting concessions ( _pastinatio_ ). In exchange for a fixed share of the harvest, the landlord conceded the right to cultivate his property to a single individual or group.[18] In addition to certain standardised information (location of the plot, duration of the grant, leaseholder), these contracts furthermore included clauses providing for special gifts ( _honorantias_ ), fixed dates at which the duties and special gifts were to be delivered to the landlord and action in case of natural disasters or Ottoman incursions.[19] 

15. On one occasion, the contract was written “extra Suburbem Jadrensis penes domum capitanei Suburbii”. In it “magister Franciscus Nunchouich”, a master-furrier, citizen and resident of Zadar, sold a parcel of land to “Joanni Voychouich, ligonizatori”, a day labourer and resident. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 9, f. 39v, 2 March 1543.

The contract was written “in una barca fuori del porto”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Cornelius Constantius, I, 1, 2, c. 22r, 18 October 1568. 

Plauocamcich alias Marcouich cognominato Xuvina de valle Sancti Stephani” sold one morgen to “Martino Chissauich de dicta valle Sancti Stephanj”. The property was located near that village and changed hands for the sum of 35 libras while the contract was written “apud portas terre firme”. 19. Typically, small livestock, such as piglets, chickens, roosters or young lambs, had to be consigned to the landlord at Christmas, prior to Lent ( _carnis privum_ ), Easter or any other

Table 4.5 provides an overview of the concessions market during the three decades under survey. Two points stand out. First, this segment of real-estate transactions mirrored the developments of the sales market discussed above, even though, at about 55%, the changes from the 1540s to the 1560s were considerably smaller. Second, the number of female contracting parties was negligible, in stark contrast to the sales market.

It is interesting, however, that while both strata started out owning roughly equal shares of the conceded lands during the 1540s, this changed significantly over the two ensuing decades. By the 1560s the nobility conceded property twice as often as the clergy.

the numbers in parentheses indicating group members of noble descent; (g) no. of landlords belonging to neither of the afore-listed categories. 

It shall be kept in mind that the clergy included institutions – such as churches, congregations and hospitals – and individuals who, on average, made up less than a quarter in this category. Ecclesiastical institutions needed natural persons to represent them, hence the connection between the property markets and previous analysis of the _procurae_ . In all cases where a church, congregation or hospital was the landowner, a procurator was present, a practice not restricted to the clergy. In more general terms, almost half of all landlords appear in the notarial records only via their legal representatives.[20] 

Most conceded possessions were in the hands of the nobility or the clergy, an observation supported by the geographical provenance of the propertied contracting parties. Comparable to the share of sellers, nine out of ten landowners lived within the city walls of Zadar.[21] Data for the recipient parties mirror these numbers: nobility and clergy were almost non-existent in the records. Equally absent were the other socio-functional groups. Also, only slightly more than half of all recipient parties resided in Zadar or its suburbs. The city’s agricultural workforce around the mid-16[th] century consisted in equal numbers of city-dwellers and inhabitants of Zadar’s hinterlands.[22]

20. Over the entire three-decade period, a representative (procurator) was present in 227 or c. 47.5% of all concession deals. These numbers rise further in the rental market (discussed below) in which 140 landowners or c. 62% out of 226 instances were represented by a procurator. However, in many cases property was owned not by a single individual, hence the following caveat must be considered: if a parcel of land was owned by two or more individuals or institutions, only one representative for all constituent parties was present. Under such circumstances, the notary duly noted that the present representative also possessed the legal authority to act on behalf of the absent party or parties.

on Ugljan (Ugliano): Kukljica (Chuchgliza), Lukoran (Lucorano) and Ugljan (Ugliano); and on the minor islands Iž (Eso, Exo), Molat (Melada) and Rava (Raua); (g) no. of tenants per decade originating in unidentified, unlisted or infrequently listed places (such as, Murter, Krk, Castro Novo [probably Herceg novi], Raico/Rainu/Raiuo, Poscaglina, Vegliana and Zahum). 

As time progressed less property was conceded to labourers from Zadar proper and its suburban settlement. Consequently, the number of tenants who originated from elsewhere under the city’s jurisdiction increased. Most likely this was the result of the cumulative effect of the truce between Venice and the Ottoman Empire, which prompted the return of the hinterland’s inhabitants, who, having sought refuge within the city walls during the war, went back to their hamlets and villages in the countryside after the ratification of the peace treaty. One more factor requires explanation: the threefold increase in the column labelled “rest, n/a”. This is at least partly due to the fourfold increase in unidentified toponyms appearing in the documents from the 1540s and the 1560s. This can be partially explained by the fact that, after fighting had ceased, Venetian governors attempted to repopulate the rural hinterlands with colonists from elsewhere.[23] These endeavours both changed the social fabric of the inhabited parts of Zadar’s jurisdiction and contributed to the increase in the appearance in the records of unidentified toponyms relating to 

23. For instance, during Alvise Badoer’s tour of duty as “provveditor generale in Dalmazia…con sede fissa a Zara” in the late 1530s, he invited Morlachs from Istria in an effort to repopulate the abandoned parts of Zadar’s jurisdiction on the mainland, temporarily reaching “circa 1,000 fochi”. The Venetian policy of pressganging the newcomers to serve as oarsmen on war galleys, however, caused the Morlachs to cross the border to escape military service. This, as commented on by Paolo Giustiniano, who later served as Zadar’s captain, rendered these repopulation attempts all but fruitless. Cf. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, pp. 136-144; vol. III, 51-52.

the colonists’ origins. This is evidenced, for instance, by the origin of “Radichio Muhich de Zahum” (presumably Zahumlje) in present-day Herzegovina (Table 4.8).[24] The data compiled below allows two conclusions. First, while the conceded surface area increased more than sixfold from the 1540s to the 1560s, the average number of morgens transferred per transaction increased only by a factor of 2.5. Second, the importance of real estate situated within Zadar’s territory increased markedly over the entire period under survey.

<!-- page 150 -->

Zadar’s Property Markets 

149 

Another interesting fact is that the average concession period increased from 38.5 months during the 1540s to 42.5 months during the 1550s to 45.5 months during the 1560s.[25] The minimum duration of such a land grant could range from as little as one[26] year to up to two[27] or three decades.[28] Usually the landowners decided which crops – mainly corn, grapes or olives – were to be planted by the tenants ( _colonus_ , _sozalis_ ).[29] Zadar’s statutes provided the normative framework for the harvest season; tenants were to give the landlord three-day’s notice of the imminent harvest.[30] The remaining obligations were contained in the notarial instruments, including instructions on where to deliver the landlord’s share of the harvest[31]

HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, f. 37v, 13 January 1555.

The property was in the incorporated district of Nin. The tenant was obliged not to confer a quarter of the harvest per annum, but a fifth. Li principali son…Hierolimo de Lorenzi et altri simili”. _Commissiones_ , vol. III, p. 52.

In exchange for a sixth of the annual harvest, the inhabitants of Tinj were granted the right to cultivate and profit from these lands “ad annos Triginta proxime futuros”, with the sole requirement that the tenants brought the landlords’ share of the harvest “ad marinam” at their own expenses. Urban Elites of Zadar 

150 

and who was to pay for the transport of the crops.[32] In addition to the landlord’s quarter of the harvest (if not stipulated otherwise), the tenant was obliged to hand over an additional tenth to the Church.[33] In case the land had to be prepared prior to cultivation, the tenants were usually required to consign to the landlord partial payments on empty property.[34] 

There were exceptions to these rules, such as the landlord’s share of the harvest could either be fixed or rising incrementally. When, for instance, Franciscus Thomaseus and his brother, then jointly “leaseholders of the income of the archbishopric of Zadar for three years”, conceded parts of said income near Podi to “Andrea Stoymilouich, Petro Stoymilouich, Simoni Tergliaeuich, Paulo Bratussich, Petro Boglielouich, Thomasio Hlapcich and Vucas Sissatouich”, the contracting parties agreed upon the following dues: “in the first year, dues amounted to a sixth, in the second year to a fifth, and in the third year to a quarter” of the harvest.[35] 

On a number of occasions the landlord agreed to bonus payments for the tenants in exchange for the performance of additional tasks, such as planting a certain number of olive trees,[36] a fixed bonus per planted morgen[37] or the waiving 

arable land near Petrčane to “Gregorio Marijch Nautj habitatori Jadre” in autumn of 1551, the tenant was required to transport the landlord’s share of the harvest “ad marina [sic]”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 7, s.p., 16 May 1566. 

32. Typically, tenants were responsible for both the transport of the landlord’s share of the harvest and associated costs, e.g., “conducendum Jadram domum dicti patroni sumptibus, et expensis ipsius sozalis”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Horatius de Marchettis, I, 1, 1, cc. 15v-16r, 3 May 1567.

E.g., “Reverendus dominus presbyter Joannes Thomaseus canonicus Jadrensis” conceded 11 morgen (c. 2.6 hectares) of farmland in the Kolovare area to “ser Marco Grubacich stipendiato ad custodiam portae Terrae firmae, Georgio Messodilich, et Matthaeo ac Andree Babcich fratribus de Suburbio Jadrae” for the duration of five years, with the deed explicitly holding that dues included “quintumdecimum pro decima omnium bladorum”. The landlord’s share was to be brought to Zadar and, in order to boost his tenants’ motivation, the property-owner obliged himself to subsidy payments of 44 soldi per planted morgen per year. XVII, cap. 82 holds that “Quod nullus det terram ad pastinandum minmus quam ad quartam partem; et de poena dantis”; with cap. 83 adding that “Quod pastinator teneatur in circuitu vineae quae sit ultra quattuor gognay plantare arbores olivarum et quattuor arbores mororum”; and cap. 88 explaining “Quibus modis pastinator dara debeat terraticum domino in uvis vel in vino postulanti”. _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 324-326, 330-332.

37. E.g., “dominus Paulus de Pasinis Civis, ac mercator Jadre” offered his four tenants, “domino presbytro Joanni Matulcich, Nicolau Cherstulich, Matheo sive Matulo Susich, Michaelj Michocich”, extra payments for the planting of “pedes quindecim olinariorum pro quoque gognale” in exchange for 12 soldi “pro quaque arbore” on the landlord’s 10 morgens (c. 2.4 hectares) on Ugljan. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 9, ff. 36v-37r, 28 January 1543.

<!-- page 152 -->

Zadar’s Property Markets 

151 

of dues for a period of time.[38] Granted property could, via another visit to a notary, be reassigned to a third party.[39] All instruments also included exemptions from contractual obligations of either party under certain circumstances, such as death, severe illness, military service, Ottoman incursions, pestilence or unpaid public work obligations.[40] 

As to the locations in which the land grants were written, almost half (on average about 44%) were written in the communal main square, the loggia and the jurists’ bench. An additional fifth was drawn up in the houses of one of the individuals involved. Some 18% were written in one of Zadar’s chancelleries. Commercial storage or retail facilities ( _apotheca_ ) were used in roughly 7% of instances, and some 3% of all contracts were agreed upon on ecclesiastical property.[41] 

In sum, land grants and concessions were the second-largest segment of the property market, totalling slightly less than half the number of the sales contracts. This fact notwithstanding, the difference in the total acreage transferred is negligible. During the period under survey, owners sold circa 5,901 morgens, compared to the approximately 6,141 morgens conceded. However, the differences between the two segments of the property market are more apparent once one considers that the number of morgens in question increased sixfold from the 1540s to the 1560s. Geographically, an absolute majority of the parcels of land conceded to tenants was located within sight of Zadar proper, with slightly more than half the contracts involving property situated in the city’s territory ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ). In terms of social provenance/occupation, the nobility and the clergy continued to predominate, and their combined share remained constant at about two-thirds of all landowners across the entire period under survey. 

## 3. _Rental and leasehold arrangements (_ locationes _)_ 

This category is by far the smallest of the three segments of property transaction recorded by the city’s notaries. It totals 226 individual contracts in

For the first six years, the tenants were exempted from delivering any dues to the landlord. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 1, 1, cc. 25v-26r, 23 October 1552.

152 

which the landowner leased his or her proprietary usufruct rights to a plot of land to one or more individuals in exchange for a defined amount of money per rental term. In addition to the relevant data on the contracting parties, the property and the various clauses, the notarial acts include provisions such as the date of remittance and special gifts.

In comparison to the sales market, the number of rental contracts amounted to only about a fifth. An analysis of the rental contracts requires the consideration of three factors: the value of the leased property compared to the sold land, the parties who benefited from landed property (traditionally, landownership was concentrated among the nobility and the church)[42] and the nature of female landownership (given the lesser legal and social status of women in property matters, this topic is of particular interest).[43] 

Geographically, while eight out of ten landlords originated from Zadar proper (including the three constituents from its suburbs), the remaining fifth was more widely distributed, ranging from elsewhere within Zadar’s jurisdiction to other parts of Dalmatia-Albania to Istria and Venice.[44] Leasing property thus involved a geographically more diverse group of landowners than land grants. 

Like with the concessions, clergy and nobility, with a combined share of almost three quarters, dominated the rental market. All other social or occupational groups were found in the remainder, although their numbers were so small as to render their impact economically insignificant. The dominance of

A second observation is that during the 1540s the clergy enjoyed a slight edge over the nobility. By the 1560s, however, that situation had reversed. Nevertheless, the “political importance of the abbot of St Chrysogonus convent” was still considerable.[45] In all, the congregation appeared nine times as landowner. The Benedictine noble nunnery of St Mary appeared twelve times. By comparison, all other groups of landowners were of limited importance to the rental market. 

Compared to their dominant role among the landowners, the nobility and clergy played a smaller role among the tenants. Their three-decade averages decreased significantly to less than half for the nobles and less than a third for members or church institutions, respectively. In the latter case the situation is even more nuanced: while most ecclesiastical landowners were legal persons – archbishoprics, congregations, churches and hospitals – _all_ the tenants were individuals, or natural persons. 

The crucial fact to extrapolate from Table 4.11 is that the single largest category is “rest, n/a”, indicating that most tenants did not belong to any of the elite groups. Compared to the concessions discussed above, Zadar’s mercantile community invested large amounts of money in real estate. On only one occasion was a merchant designated the landowner (Table 4.10). The mercantile community’s share

Together, the merchants, shopkeepers and traders of Zadar spent about 3,110 ducats or almost 15% of all investments in the rental market over the entire period under consideration. Given that the total number of the merchants within the city’s society can be estimated at 200 to 250, or 3-4% of Zadar’s urban population around the mid-16[th] century, their economic muscle far exceeded their small numbers.[46]

In terms of geographical provenance of the tenants, there was a predictable and significant shift in comparison with the origins of the landlords. While nine out of ten tenants originated from within Zadar’s jurisdiction, the city’s relative share declined to slightly less than two-thirds (in contrast to almost 80% among the property-owners). Only three-quarters of all tenants came from the city or its suburbs. The remaining tenth could not be identified.[47] 

The turnover of the 226 rental contracts amounted to about 20,997 ducats, an enormous total when compared with the much larger sales market, both in terms of individual contracts and turnover per notarial act. Summarised above, Table 4.12 gives both total and average turnover per decade in ducats for the three categories of jurisdiction, territory and the islands. In absolute terms, investments in the geographically more remote areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction increased significantly from the 1540s to the 1560s. In relative terms, however, this change had less of an impact than expected since the average turnover per rental contract decreased slightly.

<!-- page 157 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

156 

Like the above-discussed sales and concessions segments of the property markets, the city’s territory was the most dynamic area. While the share of rented property within sight of the city walls increased by only 5% from the 1540s to the 1560s, this seemingly small shift caused a significant change in prices as the number of rental contracts increased fivefold. More importantly, average prices rose by about the same factor (plus 485% from the first to the last decade under survey), which exerted corresponding pressure on rental dues. Once again, data for the islands off the coast serve as a control group. Unlike on the mainland, rents there remained stable. Movement of average prices exhibits virtually no change over the entire period under survey. This is significant given the high fluctuation of the number of contracts and their respective market shares during each of the three surveyed decades. 

since less than half of the contracts list precise information about the size of the parcels of land involved. During the preceding century it was common practice to rent out real estate in _sors_ , that is, in patches of 7.1 to 7.6 hectares.[48] By the mid-16[th] century, this appeared to have changed as the territory size is referred to as _sors_ in only 72 instances – or slightly less than a third – of all rental contracts. In 30 other contracts (about 13% of the total), the property size is given in morgen. Since only seven out of 226 notarial instruments lack precise information about the duration of the tenure, it is more useful to enquire about the length of the rental contracts. On average, real estate was leased for 89 months during the 1540s. This decreased to 49 months during the 1550s and increased again to 63 months during the decade leading up to the Cyprus War. The three-decade average was 67 months. 

Moreover, every document details the date of remittance. If the rent was not paid in advance the payments were due on previously-fixed days. Typically, feast days were chosen as paydays, with the following appearing most often in the rental contracts: St George (23 April), James, son of Zebedee (25 July) and Michaelmas, the feast of St Michael the Archangel (29 September). Less often, payments were set for Christmas, Easter or around New Year. In addition to rental dues, some contracts also specified dates for special gifts ( _honorantiae_ ) that were to be consigned to the landlord, for instance, prior to Lent ( _carnis privum_ ), Easter and Christmas. As in the concessions, these typically involved small livestock or poultry. 

Due to the landowner’s desire to ensure the integrity of his or her possession, most documents included provisions against fire clearing or other forms of forest clearance[49] and exemptions from duties linked to weather damages or incursions 

> 48. During the 15[th] century, the typical property on the mainland was one sors or ždrijeb, equalling 30 32 morgens (7.1 7.6 hectares), of which about an eighth was unavailable for farming as it was occupied by various houses and farm buildings. Another small part was usually used for growing grapes. The rest was under the plough. Several of such compounds made up the hamlets and small villages of the hinterlands of Zadar, as described in detail by Raukar, _Zadar u XV. stoljeću_ , pp. 71-196. 

> 49. E.g., in spring 1540, the Benedictine noble nunnery of St Mary, represented by “venerabila domina Donata de Nassis benemerita Abbatissa” and two other nuns, leased a

<!-- page 158 -->

Zadar’s Property Markets 

157 

from across the Ottoman-Venetian border. Examples include a case from autumn of 1543 when “the Reverend Matheus de Marchettis, canon of Zadar, albeit in his capacity as provost [ _comendatarius_ ] of the Abbey of Saint Michael on the Mountain on the island [of Ugljan] across the channel of the diocese of Zadar [ultra barchaneum Jadrensis diocesis]” leased the abbey’s entire property to “Gregorio Gerdouich son of the late Laurentius of the same island [Ugljan]” and the latter’s absent brother. In exchange for the annual rent of 50 ducats, the tenants received usufruct rights for a period of three years, including 118 small livestock ( _animalia minuta_ ). This particular contract included the following clause: 

Likewise, if the said tenants suffer any damage, God forbid, during said tenancy of three years, by way of hailstorm or weather damages, then the Abbot himself shall make restitution to the tenants according to municipal law [Zadar’s statutes].[50] 

A second example involved cross-border raids that originated from Ottoman territory. In May 1545 “Johannes de Pechiaro son of the late Franciscus, nobleman of Zadar” leased four _sors_ (between 28.4 and 30.3 hectares) near the village of Polišane to “the valiant Petrus Clada, captain of the stratiotti”. For the duration of three years, the tenant agreed to pay an annual rent of 10 ducats, a much lower sum. This was most likely due to two factors: first, no livestock was involved, and second, the Ottoman-Venetian border was much closer, which prompted the contracting parties to include the following clause: “In the event that the said Petrus is unable to receive the benefit from the said four _sors_ caused by war or Turkish incursion, which God shall prevent, he should not be held liable for payment for that year”.[51] 

In some documents the landlord, who usually paid for damage to his or her property, capped his liabilities to a third of the sum.[52] This had to be agreed

Slightly fewer were leased on ecclesiastical property, and the remaining quarter was stipulated in the houses of the involved individuals.[55] 

In summing up the main characteristics of the rental market, it is critical to acknowledge the dominance of the two upper strata, the nobility and clergy. On average, about three-quarters of all leased land belonged to them. While the distribution between these two groups varied over time, their combined share remained constant. Most ecclesiastical property belonged to institutions, such as Zadar’s archbishopric, the various congregations, parish churches and hospitals. Including both landlords and tenants, the nobility and clergy made up almost a third of all leaseholders. While neither merchants nor members of the intellectual elites owned significant amounts of real estate, in combination they made up about a quarter of all tenants. 

Rental market turnover totalled 20,997 ducats during the entire period under survey, of which roughly three-quarters went to nobility or clergy. Price

developments mirrored those in the sales market. While rents on the islands or other areas of Zadar’s jurisdiction did not change much, dues in the territory – and hence within sight of the city walls – skyrocketed and increased almost fivefold. In most other aspects, such as date of remittance, the delivery of special gifts and other additional obligations, the rental contracts contained provisions comparable to the concessions.

<!-- page 162 -->

## 5. Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

[U]nder the Old Régime, a high proportion of crimes did not come before the courts but were dealt on an infrajudicial level. But, although interest in infrajustice constitutes a crucial step for research, the fear that it may turn out to be yet another “historiographical illusion” is altogether legitimate: there is a tendency to exaggerate the frequency of the phenomenon and to make it a sort of panacea, thus forgetting that a great amount of criminal behaviour not only is not dealt with by the judicial system, but is missed by the infrajudicial system as well.[1] 

Benoît Garnot 

Much like the detailed case study about property transactions in the preceding pages, good, evidence-based and quantitative enquiries into crime history in early modern Dalmatia are hard to come by. While Garnot referred to Old Regime France and provided at least a modicum of references in combination with the above-mentioned caveat about the many (more?) known unknowns, one of the core weaknesses of the (social) history of everyday life in the Venetian Adriatic are crime and punishment. While there are some indications about it focusing on Venice proper, it is hard to find studies on its overseas possessions that go beyond the anecdotal reconstruction of one or the other example.[2] 

Beyond the shallow waters of the Venetian lagoon, with perhaps Dubrovnik serving once more as the proverbial exception proving that rule, generalities abound. Hence, we learn that procedures have been interpreted as a kind of “continuous litigation” (Edward Muir), see blanket commentary like “the nature of Venetian justice itself was complex” while also the prime “venue for mediation between governors and governed” (Monique O’Connell) or simply attempted manifestations of “the idea of a regime offering justice to all its subjects” (Benjamin Arbel).[3] Beyond what is known as detailed in Chapter 1, we note that even the most recent publications discussing legal or crime history in Renaissance 

> 1. Garnot, “Justice, infrajustice, parajustice et extra justice”, p. 103. 

> 2. The classic study is by Ruggiero, _Boundaries of Eros_ . See also his “Wayfarers in Wonderland”. Note, too, the exemplary account by Davis, _War of the Fists_ . 

> 3. Muir, “Was there Republicanism?”, pp. 137-167; “Sources of Civil Society”, pp. 392-400; O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ , pp. 75-97: 75-76; Arbel, “Venice’s Maritime Empire”, pp. 156-164: 156.

<!-- page 163 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

162 

Dalmatia typically focus on a very specific subset compartmentalised along neat, self-contained, if anachronistic, categories of civil or criminal law. 

In Dalmatia, once the Serenissima had taken over a city or town, compacts were drawn up that outlined the conditions of Venetian rule: when speaking of civil law, the new governors were assisted by councillors drawn from the local nobility, but the former were in no ways bound to any advice offered by the latter; in criminal cases or those involving Venetian patricians and citizens, the rulers of Venice enjoyed exclusive privileges to determine the outcome.[4] In civil cases and those involving local affairs, what is today referred to as the principle of subsidiarity typically applied, thus creating a kind of hierarchy of jurisprudence: in most Venetian possessions overseas, the various communal statutes continued to be in use, albeit amended to vouchsafe the Serenissima’s domination.[5] Beyond the city walls, in part due to the Venetians’ lack of interest and capabilities, life – and discord – was governed much like before 1409/1420: village communities continued to enjoy considerable autonomy in these matters.[6] 

Conflict, widely understood, at these local levels often carried a very personal note. In this, life on the Adriatic coast mirrored both what transpired in Venice proper as well as in the wider context of Mediterranean culture. In addition to social strife in the various coastal communities along class and social status group lines, confrontations between related noble families – akin to the conflict between the houses of Capulet and Montagu immortalised by Shakespeare in his _Romeo and Juliet_ (an almost exact contemporaneous 16[th] -century story) – arose ever so often, if the report by Giovanni Battista Giustiniano is any indication. Writing in 1553, he noted that Zadar’s “nobles live together harmoniously” and that “there is no hatred between them, except for the Tetrici and Civallelli families who are currently at odds after the recent murder of one of Battista Tetrico’s sons by Francesco Civallello”. This triggered a reprisal killing “by a servant of Nicolò Tetrici”, who later escaped.[7] 

Then as now, everyday life in the Renaissance Adriatic (and elsewhere across Europe) offered a never-ending stream of possibilities for conflict, ranging from not infrequent inheritance quarrels between next-of-kin to violent crimes, such as armed assault and battery, (attempted) murder or (grand) theft, to very human disputes among neighbours over noise or construction projects.[8] Viewed through the lenses of anthropology and ethnography, “conflict” as a meta-category of human interactions is both an inherently social and at the same time also a highly 

4. Cozzi, “Politica, società, istituzionie”, pp. 195-201. 

> 5. Krekić, “Developed Autonomy”, pp. 196-197; Malz, “Die Dalmatinische Städtewelt”, pp. 114-115; Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , p. 369; and Schmitt, “Südosteuropa als Kommunikationsraum”, pp. 90-100.

8. The classic study is by Muir, _Mad Blood Stirring_ , esp. the introduction on xix-xxviii. See also his updated survey, “Anthropology of Venice”. Further, see Pust, “Borderlands, CrossCultural Exchange and Revenge”, esp. points 2 and 3.

<!-- page 164 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

163 

“normalised” phenomenon.[9] “Conflict” may therefore be studied as a multifaceted “embedded social action”, defined as an “ensemble of moral values, proper norms, and institutional arrangements” that are characterised by relatively high degrees – although decreasing the further one ventures into the rural hinterlands – of institutionalisation via, for instance, law codes, procedural arrangements, including courts, lawyers and the possibility of extrajudicial resolution. These notions, of course, presuppose a relatively stable and normalised range of expectations among both rulers and ruled, as well as the adherence of all parties to codified (and unwritten, customary) societal norms.[10] 

One of the chief limitations of studying such past conflicts are the availability and nature of the source materials. The following examples and analyses depart from the premise that the studied conflicts are manifestations of normative, if not normalised, occurrences characterised by at least two metaaspects: norm transgression followed by sanctioning and resolution, both of which included consequences for the involved parties’ social standing, personal wealth etc.[11] The examples discussed below were all found in the protocol books of Zadar’s notaries, yet the _Statuta Iadertina_ played a correspondingly big role as its clauses provided a durable framework for the successful resolution of any quarrel, irrespective of the various distinctions setting the parties apart, such as confessional affiliation, geographical provenance or social descent. Not to put too fine a point on this, but there is some truth to Venice’s role as an arbiter (as seen, for example, on the façade of the Ducal Palace, which features a _Justitia_ without blindfolds[12] ) or, to cite another example, in the Senate’s reply to a petition by the quarrelling nobles and citizens of Hvar noting that “all are equal under the law [omnes in iustitia sint equales]”.[13] 

In what follows these introductory considerations, I shall first consider several brief individual examples of intrapersonal conflicts scattered throughout the notarial protocol books. This is done to provide an overview of what kind of problems arose and how they were dealt with outside the (criminal) jurisdiction overseen by the count or captain, and reserved for Venetians. In a second step, I undertake a quantitative analysis – truly the first of its kind – of all existing 294 individual notarial deeds stipulated between 1540-1569 relating extrajudicial settlements to shed new light on the intersections of justice, infrajustice, parajustice and extra justice, to paraphrase Benoît Garnot once more. The final section of this chapter explores a very detailed and long-running individual case involving the related-by-marriage Tetricus and (vs.) Grisogonus families of Zadar whose protagonists furiously fought over a patch of land with some livestock in 1561. Documented extensively by notary Simon Mazzarellus in two protocol

> 10. On “embedding”, see Elvert, “Markets of Violence”. For the quotes above, see his “Conflict”, pp. 2543-2545: 2543. 11. Cf. Elvert, “Conflict”, p. 2545. 

> 12. Her image adorns the cover of Rösch, _Venedig_ . 

> 13. Ljubić, _Listine_ , vol. IX, p. 247. See also Schmitt, “Venezianisches Südosteuropa”, pp. 99-100.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

164 

books containing a wealth of sources, ranging from depositions to transcripts of lawyers interviewing local residents to claims (and counterarguments), as well as copies of all relevant notarised deeds going all the way back to the late 1480s. What unites these examples is also what renders this (new) chapter so valuable: there is hardly any systematic research on these matters dealing with the lagoon metropolis or Dubrovnik, with perhaps the extensively documented case study of the _Salamankezi vs. Sorbonezi_ by Stjepan Ćosić and Nenad Vekarić serving as the proverbial exception that proves this particular rule.[14] 

## 1. _Settling out of court in Dalmatia: exemplary cases_ 

Speaking very broadly, as these documents are found among the other notarised deeds, they also mirror the source materials discussed so far in terms of structure, wording and, perhaps even more crucially, language use. All surviving extrajudicial settlements were composed in Latin to ensure their longevity as well as their usefulness across jurisdictional boundaries, if needed. A first example of such a contract pitted “the heirs of the recently deceased Simon de Meldulla and his sister Magdalena” against “Georgius a Lacu”; the parties struggled over usufruct rights concerning a vineyard outside Zadar and a house in the city’s St Marcella parish. If there is a crucial difference between these kinds of deeds and most other notarised documents, it is perhaps the comparatively extensive listing of all relevant information that reaches as far back in time as required by the circumstances of the case. Simon and his sister Magdalena – who, incidentally, was Georgius a Lacu’s mother – had inherited these possessions in equal parts, with one crucial addition: upon Simon’s death, possession of all property was to devolve on Magdalena and her heirs. As this clause would negatively affect Simon’s children, there are references to earlier suits going back as far as the mid1520s, which were resolved, at least for the time being, by the validation of the paternal testament by Zaccaria Vallaresso on 17 February 1526. Yet, despite this official ruling confirming the validity of Georgius’ claims to the entire inheritance, it took from September to mid-December 1560 for the opposing closely related parties – Simon being Georgius’ uncle – to resolve the matter. In autumn of that year, Simon appointed his son, the “master-painter Marcus Antonius”, his representative – again highlighting the importance of the analysis of the _procurae_ carried out in Chapters 2 and 3 – “specifically to negotiate with Georgius a Lacu in his capacity as heir of the late Magdalena, sister of said constituent Simon”.[15] After several months of presumably arduous dealings, a document found among notary Daniel Cavalca’s protocol books reveals that Simon had died in the meantime and the resolution came about in a relatively simple manner: in exchange for Georgius covering the incurred legal fees worth 24 libras 4 soldi and “paying the outstanding salary of the late Simon’s maid Matthea worth 34 libras”, Marcus 

14. Ćosić, Vekarić, _Dubrovačka vlastela_ . 

15. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 5, cc. 267r-267v, 26 September 1560.

<!-- page 166 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

165 

Antonius reaffirmed his grandfather’s testament that had given rise to the conflict in the first place.[16] 

More often than such peaceful resolutions, the notarised settlements speak of everyday violence. Due to territorial losses incurred at the end of the 15371540 war against the Ottomans, the security situation beyond Zadar’s territory ( _Astareja_ , _ager publicus_ ) was rather precarious. Many outlying villages and hamlets had been abandoned, and those who continued to work in the fields were subject to frequent cross-border incursions or fell victim to armed marauders roaming the countryside.[17] Against this background of perennial, if low-intensity, _relazioni_ and conflict outside the city walls documented consistently in the reports by Venice’s governors, the notarial deeds shed light on comparable, if not necessarily less violent, occurrences in Zadar. In summer 1561, for instance, “ser Johannes, son of the late Simon Cichia”, a mercenary tasked with protecting the city’s castle, and “Johannes Stipacinouich”, a local fisherman, got into a fight that saw the latter “gravely, and life-threateningly wounding” the soldier, which led to a criminal investigation by the communal magistrate. In the end, the criminal chamber sentenced the offender to “pay for all care, medication and all other expenses” for the injured Johannes’ recovery.[18] 

To cite a third example to illustrate the wide range of incidents settled out of court, mention shall be made of a murder case. In the mid-1540s, “Michael, son of the valiant knight Georgius Renessi” killed “Petrus Luchissa”, who was survived by his brother “Andreas”. Invoking God’s grace and Christ crucified, Andreas now showed mercy and came to an extrajudicial conflict resolution with the murderer’s father. The terms and conditions are explained quickly: Michael was to remain banished for life from Zadar and its jurisdiction; his father Georgius Renessi obliged himself as guarantor and pledged “all his present and future movable and immovable property” as surety that his son would remain in the neighbouring jurisdiction of Šibenik. Humbly, Georgius accepted these terms that at least kept his son alive as Andreas in exchange promised to cease all pending civil or criminal proceedings against his brother’s killer.[19] 

While these three examples reveal the wide range of cases settled out of court – which call into question the conventional, if questionable, analytical distinction between civil and criminal cases – there are many more examples that relate more mundane reasons for conflict. Take, for instance, the case of “ser Michael of Koroni”, another soldier guarding Zadar’s castle, who had increased his house’s wall, located in the city’s St Catherine’s parish, by six-and-a-half feet (or slightly more than 2.1 metres).[20] As Michael _shared_ this wall with his neighbour “Catherina, wife of the late Simon Sestichiuich”, and had apparently 

> 16. If not noted otherwise, information in this paragraph derives from HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 2C, cc. 37v-38r, 14 December 1560. 

> 17. As detailed, e.g., by Giacomo Pisani, count of Zadar, upon his return to Venice in 1566, via _Commissiones_ , vol. III, pp. 164-167: 165-166. See also Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 13-14. 

> 18. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 1, 6, cc. 16r-16v, 9 July 1561. 

> 19. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 1, f. 12v, 15 August 1545. 

> 20. 1 foot = c. 0.34m, thus 6.5 feet = c. 2.145m, via _Statuta Iadertina_ , p. 759.

<!-- page 167 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

166 

not obtained his neighbour’s prior consent, a settlement was drawn up arranging to equally share the construction costs of 10 libras per foot height. This resulted in Catherina paying Michael a total of 32 libras 10 soldi, with the latter agreeing to discuss further changes to the shared parts of their properties.[21] 

Finally, mention shall be made of a rather delicate dispute involving the Franciscan convent of the Third Order Regulars and their neighbour, the artisan “Franciscus Tubicina of Zadar”. The former’s abode was located in the westernmost corner of the city known as “ye olde parish [in contrata veteris]”, in the vicinity of St Jacob’s Hospital.[22] As the hospital was located across from the cathedral of St Anastasia, it is possible to locate approximately where this conflict arose:[23] as mentioned in the settlement, the Third Order Regulars’ convent and Franciscus Tubicina shared the north-west and south-west walls, which is also the reason the conflict arose in the first place.[24] Earlier in 1564, the Third Order Regulars had “commenced construction of a sewer, or latrine [ _necessarium_ ] in the very same place of their convent that touches on the afore-mentioned master Franciscus’ property in the north-west and his house in the south-west”. Olfactory consequences aside, Franciscus “claimed that a latrine thus constructed would cause damage to the house”, yet the Venetian count Andreas Bondumier (in office 1563-1564) green-lighted the construction. In addition, Bondumier held that “the friars may continue to construct, or build, said latrine [even] before Franciscus’ appeal was heard before the competent authorities”. While the notarial deeds are, sadly, silent on the particulars of these proceedings, the settlement, arrived at on 29 July 1564, contained the following clauses: first, Franciscus “ceased the appeals procedure” and promised not to cause further problems with new suits or a petition to the Venice-based Quarantia. In exchange, the Third Order Regulars “obliged themselves to extend the shared wall along the entire length” of the shared boundary, extending “all the way from the north-west corner to the public road in the south-east” of the property. Furthermore, the shared wall was to be raised “to reach the height of the second floor”, while the proximate origin of the quarrel, the latrine, was to be constructed “not in the north-east corner, with the discharge of said latrine to be along the north-east wall of Franciscus’ house”. A subsidiary clause of the settlement held that the friars might use the shared wall for their latrine, but that if they did, that shared wall would have to be raised to the level of the second floor. If the Third Order Regulars would do so, and perhaps ironically so, the discharge of their latrine would flow onto the street across from Zadar’s cathedral of St Anastasia. 

21. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Augustinus Martius, I, 1, 3, s.p. 26 October 1548. 

22. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Franciscus Thomaseus, I, 1, 2, f. 3v, 24 September 1550. 

23. Cf. the maps in Klaić, Petricioli, _Zadar_ , p. 285; and Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , p. 135. Note that the present-day structures look very different due to massive damage incurred by Allied (mainly US-UK) bombing in the Second World War.

Orig. “in quoddam loco dicti monasterij Contiguo domui dicti magistri Franciscu a parte quirinali, et boreali domus dicti magistri Francisci”, via HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, 1, 2, 2, ff. 42v-43r, 29 July 1564. <!-- page 168 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

167 

Attached to the settlement, the deed contains several additional clauses that speak to the diachronic qualities of such extrajudicial arrangements. In all, four points are considered. First, the Third Order Regulars affirmed not to charge, nor sue, Franciscus or his children for costs incurred so far; instead, an accounting was envisioned for the third generation, specifically “after the death of Franciscus’ sons or daughters and not before his grand-children will have come of age [ad etatem legitimam]” based on a jointly arrived-at estimate. Second, in the case that Franciscus sold his abode, the above-detailed mutual obligations would be transferred to the new owner. The convent, third, obliged itself to cover Franciscus’ legal expenses and, finally, affirmed that all stipulated clauses would remain valid even in the case of any property transfer other than a sales contract.[25] 

## 2. _Settling out of court in Zadar: a quantitative analysis, 1540-1569_ 

These four examples provide a good first impression of the wide range of legal and social problems that were settled out of court in Zadar. The first take-away is that the modern(ising) distinction between criminal and (vs.) civil law is hard to apply in the Renaissance Adriatic. In fact, and with explicit reference to Garnot’s pioneering work on Old Regime France, “a high proportion of crimes did not come before the courts but were dealt with on an infrajudicial level”.[26] Whereas Venetian Dalmatia-Albania was not (too) structurally different from early modern France, and despite the relative abundance of such extrajudicial settlements in the protocol books of Zadar’s notaries, these instances of “infrajudicial” conflict resolution continue their existence as the proverbial poor relation of crime history in general (whose main efforts revolve around the intersections of the proverbial postmodern trinity of sex/gender, age and social status).[27] This particular situation is arguably (even) worse with respect to Venetian studies, whose two most recent collective volumes – _Reconsidering Venice_ , co-edited by John Martin and Dennis Romano (2000), and the _Companion_ , edited by Eric Dursteler (2013) – do not mention these aspects, even at the most superficial level.[28] There are, however,

26. Garnot, “Justice, infrajustice, parajustice et extra justice”, p. 103. See the many more examples from the Middle Ages to the present in his _L’infrajudiciaire_ . For a rare example of application into Adriatic contexts, see Lonza, “L’accusatoire et l’infrajudiciaire”. 

27. Offered here as _pars pro toto_ , note that the textbook by Schwerhoff, _Historische Kriminalitätsforschung_ , discusses these three topics at length on pp. 15-30 (esp. pp. 15-22, 27-30) while affording “infrajudicial” considerations a mere couple of paragraphs on pp. 81-82. 

28. Virtually the best – as well as most telling – indication is offered by Povolo, “Liturgies of Violence”, p. 538, who speaks of “the transformations of the seventeenth century”, specifically “the last two decades, when…the Council of Ten more firmly took control over certain violent crimes…that had for centuries been under the jurisdiction of local courts”. These changes did away with a series of decrees by the Council of Ten dating from the 1570s, which

<!-- page 169 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

168 

a few references hinting at these infrajudicial solutions – albeit without citing Garnot’s work or mentioning these considerations – found in Fabian Kümmeler’s study of rural Korčula in the 15[th] century.[29] Essentially identical considerations characterise less-recent works by Oliver Schmitt (among other things), while Ersie Burke’s insightful consideration of the quite distinct Greek immigrant population of 16[th] -century Venice provides a contrast here.[30] 

This chapter, however, seeks to move beyond the state of research by offering both a qualitative discussion and a quantitative analysis of infrajudicial conflict resolution pioneered by Garnot. In so doing, I am furthermore informed by consideration of the multi-dimensional, overlapping communicative, cultural, economic, linguistic, political, religious and social contexts alluded to by the sociologist Martin Löw.[31] Therefore, we may identify three distinct areas of interest: the protagonists’ “milieux”; the arbiters’ contribution, or agency; and the written rules – as well as the unwritten conventions and expectations of all involved parties. Together, these aspects informed the decision to settle any given conflict under whatever set of circumstances accompanied them. Garnot used the term “parajustice” to indicate situation-dependent adaptations of existing rules and “extrajustice” to indicate the occasional toleration of established deviant behaviour and/or norms if doing so contributed to the successful settlement of a dispute.[32] 

The evidence discussed in this segment argues that these low-visibility, low-intensity aspects of conflict resolution were both relatively common and widespread across all socio-functional fault lines. Moreover, consideration of the documentation these cases left in the notarial records permits the reconstruction of a corresponding variety of entanglements between individual micro, intermediate and macro contexts that reveal the complexity of – some might wish to label it “irrationality” – of what many historians categorise based on clear-cut distinctions, such as criminal vs. civil law (which hold some merit 

held that “anyone in any part of the state who was caught in the act of committing a serious crime – such as murder, kidnapping, rape, arson or roadside robbery – could be killed with impunity”.
