Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

169 

only if the mostly high-profile cases are considered), which, as the above handful of examples and the following quantitative analysis show, become increasing tenuous once the available information is aggregated. By including these previously overlooked sources preserved in the notarial protocol books, the historian arrives at a more comprehensive understanding and reconstruction of the past, and having so far looked a several examples of _individual_ realities, the subsequent quantitative analysis lends itself to more general considerations.[33] 

Of course, not all cases were as delicate – rather: stank to high heaven – as the dispute between Franciscus Tubicina and the Third Order Regulars of St Francis indicates. Yet, the aforementioned cases are very much drawn from the proverbial middle of the road of everyday life in the Renaissance Adriatic. As far as the region’s communal statutes from Zadar to Korčula to Split suggest, the various legal frameworks ordering social relations accepted, as well as widely diffused, ways and means to settle conflicts out of court.[34] For the subsequent quantitative analysis, I have identified a total of 294 individual notarial deeds stipulated between 1 January 1540 and 31 December 1549, all of them found throughout the records of Zadar’s fifteen notaries. These 294 deeds constitute about a sixth of the 1,772 property transactions that form the evidentiary basis of Chapter 4, although when broken down further, these extrajudicial settlements exceeded the number of rental and leasehold arrangements by about 30% (see Table 5.1). 

Many of these extrajudicial arrangements simply resolved controversial issues in a practical, straightforward and swift manner. In the absence of otherwise established channels or procedures, regaining money owed or settling accounts, debt repayment, follow-up arrangements of property transactions and dowryrelated settlements along these lines were quite common courses of action. Of course, some of these cases stand out for their particulars, such as the provision for child-support, or alimony, payments between “Franciscus de Martinis, citizen of Zadar, on the one hand, and Catherina Corculanona, his former maid, on the other hand, with whom said Franciscus had fathered a daughter by the name of Margarita” some unspecified time ago. While at the time of stipulation “Margarita is supporting her mother Catherina [quidem Margarita presente alimenta pro dicta Catherina eius matrem]”, Franciscus and his former main now agreed on him paying “four and half ducats a year…in alimony [pro alimento] over the next

Put differently, these contributions stay largely within that well-worn, if increasingly anachronistic, divide.

<!-- page 172 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

171 

common occurrences.[37] There is a wealth of evidence related to more “mundane” settlements, such as the dispute between a landlord (the nunnery of St Mary) and a tenant (Dominicus Spirondelli, a citizen and merchant) that ties together virtually all the previously-discussed aspects of everyday life in 16[th] -century Zadar. The Benedictine nuns were represented by their procurator, the nobleman Donatus Crissana (Chapters 2 and 3), while the conflict arose over a modification to a rented house without prior consent of the landlord; Dominicus had “obturated with a wall the already grilled window of a storage facility” owned by St Mary’s, hence the parties arranged for the following solution: Dominicus “promised, and solemnly obliged himself to…open up, or have someone else remove the obstacle, and return the aforementioned window to its prior state”.[38] 

While many of these examples would quite readily fall into the category of “civil, or private, law”, 16[th] -century Dalmatians also, and quite readily so, settled offences that today would undoubtedly be classified as pertainng to “criminal law”: assault and battery, property damage and bodily injury, as well as homicide and manslaughter are mentioned in these deeds. I have consciously used present-day terminology here, and am doing so not to score some cheap, if anachronistic, points but to call attention to the limitations of using our contemporary categories of civil and criminal law to relate pre-modern experiences. It was decidedly not the case that these notarial deeds never related to the _Statuta Iadertina_ in general, or Zadar’s criminal chamber in particular. We note the challenges deriving from using our present understanding of these matters to infer that comparable considerations at least partially apply to past societies.[39] One of these occurrences is conveyed in the case of “Simon Blasich of Punta Ljubač” in Zadar’s jurisdiction in the settlement of a case of – in today’s language – assault and battery carried out by “Johannes and Thomas Percinouichj, siblings, and Peter Marcelich”, who all hailed from the same place. In a deed notarised by Petrus de Bassano in the mid-1540s, the parties agreed to “amicably make…peace” as Simon Blasich had been injured by the three assailants. “Because of the bodily harm, there was an investigation by the criminal chamber”, but since the settlement resulted in Simon dropping all charges (provided his opponents did the same), peace and harmony between these four inhabitants of Punta Ljubač was restored.[40] 

late priest’s house, as stipulated in HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 4, ff. 152v-153r, 8 August 1557. 

37. Ex-post redistribution quarrels related to testamentary bequests were more common the higher the social and/or economic standing of the deceased, which quite often induced the designated heirs to struggle for bigger shares of the testator’s or testatrix’s legacy. Since such a case is discussed in detail in the final segment of this chapter, it suffices to add a few references to relevant deeds, incl. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 9, ff. 22r-22v, 17 July 1543; Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 3, ff. 13v-14r, 26 October 1564; or Simon Budineus, I, 1, 6, cc. 309r-309v, 12 May 1561. 

38. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 3, ff. 15v-16r, 8 November 1564. For a transcription, see my “To Avoid the Costs of Litigation”, pp. 141-142, n. 38. 

39. For the legal framework, see the _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 180-190. 

40. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 10, f. 7r, 8 May 1544. For a transcription, see my “To Avoid the Costs of Litigation”, pp. 142-143, n. 40.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

172 

It goes without saying that even several comparable examples are too little to ascertain the tenuous nature of the conventional distinction between civil, private, and criminal law, hence these episodes beg consideration of the various elements of crime that were settled out of court.[41] Before proceeding, a few words of caution are in order: as far as the available documentation permits (Table 5.1), I have conducted quantitative analyses of the various underlying deviant actions. Compiled in Table 5.2, these provide clues about these elements of crime, which are indicative of the precarious, if not at least partially questionable, nature of our contemporary understanding of their categorisation along contemporary distinctions, such as “criminal” and (vs.) “civil” law (the former conventionally relates to offences against the public order, interest and welfare, which is why it is typically state officials who administer justice; by contrast, the latter, while governing intra-personal conflict, is also governed by statutes, but instead of state officials, it is typically the injured or offended party who files charges with the judiciary). Do note, however, that I am using modern, English legal terms for these elements of crime in the below compilation, with significant additional information provided in the caption.

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

173 

both bodily injury and property damage as these entailed similar legal consequences, such as banishment from – in this case, Zadar’s – jurisdiction; (6) while most deeds falling into this category relate to homicides, there is also one case that, using today’s terminology, would be considered a negligent homicide (HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, ff. 73r-73v, 13 June 1555), as well as a perpetrator awaiting execution “for plundering [causa predationis]” (HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 1, E, s.p., 16 May 1543); (7) two of these three cases are discussed below. 

The problems of using present-day categories, such as “civil” and “criminal law”, come immediately to the fore in light of the findings summarised in Table 5.2. Even with considerable taxonomic leeway, any such undertaking quickly founders on the realities of 16[th] -century Zadar, where – and this must not be overlooked or downplayed – approximately 13% of extrajudicial settlements pertained to violence against another person or persons, including homicide and manslaughter (that is, cases that in the present would never result in such settlements).[43] 

As regards the juxtaposition of the elements of crime and (versus) class/ status group affiliation, mention shall be made that the urban nobility – widely understood (meaning that at least one constituent was of noble descent) – engaged in these arbitration efforts more often with respect to property or money dealings as opposed to episodes of physical violence (assault and battery, homicide or manslaughter). While this is not to say that members of the nobility did not conduct themselves in such manner, if the four (out of sixteen) examples of “unnatural deaths” that concerned individuals of privileged descent are any indication, the nobility’s share is roughly in line with their share among stipulating parties.[44] 

Another surprising finding of this foray into infrajudicial settlements is summarised in Table 5.3. Once these deeds are subjected to an analysis of the socio-functional status of the involved parties akin to that carried out in the preceding chapters (Tables 2.6 and 2.7; 4.6, 4.10 and 4.11), the following contours of who settled out of court emerge. 

The findings are indicative of four characteristics, two quite specific and the other two more general. First, the nobility’s shares among those who sought to settle out of court, as well as those who acceded to such settlements, remained relatively constant during these three decades. This finding aligns quite well with their activities in Zadar’s property markets where those of privileged descent made up about a third of all participants (based on a total of 1,771 individual transaction as discussed in Chapter 4). With respect to extrajudicial settlements, we note the nobility’s slightly lower shares at 29% of the sample. Second, while it could be expected that the clergy – comprising both natural and legal persons – would appear less frequently in these arbitration documents, especially relative to their relative weight in property transactions, their share of around 10% was not zero.

(a) lists the various class, status (corporate, or _ständisch_ ) and/or functional grouping; (b) these columns provide details about the totals (no.), with the numbers of absent constituent parties represented by procurators in parentheses, as well as their relative share over the entire threedecade period; (c) corresponding details about recipient parties, like in the aforementioned category; (d) for particulars on this grouping, see Table 2.6. 

Speaking once more rather generally, third, we note that almost three out of ten parties – 81 constituent and 78 recipient parties (about 29% each) – did not appear in person in front of the notary who recorded the settlement. Hence, once more, the over-arching importance of the _procurae_ analyses (which, incidentally, also played a considerable role in property transactions discussed in Chapter 4), even though the explanation in the present contexts is perhaps a tad more straightforward: if one was wanted for a grave transgression, or even if one had already been sentenced to, say, banishment from Zadar’s jurisdiction, to settle the matter meant to employ a representative with commensurate legal powers to remedy the situation. It is certainly not a surprise that _procurae_ appointments constituted approximately a sixth of all surviving notarised deeds in the period under survey;[45] what _is_ surprising, though, is that this source category, despite its obvious importance for the functioning of the economic, legal and social and economic fabric of urban societies in the Catholic-Italianate sphere of cultural diffusion, has yet to attract commensurate scholarly attention. 

Finally, mention must be made of the sex differences in the sample, which reveal a certain imbalance between women initiating versus entering such extrajudicial settlements. There were sixty women among the constituent (or about 20%) versus thirty-seven (about 12-13%) among the recipient parties. In this respect, the deeds in this sample differ markedly from the other sets of notarised arrangement discussed so far (and those discussed in subsequent chapters). On average, women initiated about a quarter of all procuratorial appointments, while their share among appointees stood at a measly 2%. Speaking of property transactions, women made up about a sixth of all sellers versus less than a tenth 

45. Apart from the information in Chapters 2 and 3, see also my “Procuratorial Networks”.

<!-- page 176 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

175 

of buyers. Their relative shares drop further in those segments of the real estate markets that deal with planting concessions and land grants, as well as rental and leasehold arrangements: in the former, women were noted as property owners in merely 7% of cases (35 deeds out of 478 contracts) versus a mere seven recipients (or between 1-2%). As regards the latter, the data point to female landowners in about 13% of cases (30 deeds out of 226 contracts) versus less than a third of that value among the tenants (eight contracts). It is clear, therefore, that women were more actively participating in this particular segment than in the other parts of daily life, as documented in the notarial records. 

## 3. _Kinship ties and legal disputes: Grisogono vs. Tetrico_ 

One of the best-documented cases of such an extrajudicial settlement concern the dispute between two of Zadar’s prominent noble families. Their fight exemplarily displays virtually all the tools employed in 16[th] -century legal proceedings and is instructional with respect to the eventual resolution of virtually any such feud. The concluding part of this chapter is based on two entire notary books I found among the records of Simon Mazzarellus, who was active between 1555 and 1567; he was furthermore the son of Johannes Michael Mazzarellus, a fellow notary, nobleman of Trogir and citizen of Zadar. Both worked as communal chancellors ( _cancellarius communitatis_ ) in their adopted hometown. Reference is made specifically to Simon’s books three and four, entitled “processo arbitrale (1561)” and “ad processum”, respectively, with the former containing copies of the various filings and the latter a variety of relevant documents, ranging from copies of the (yet unprinted) titles from the _Statuta Iadertina_ to petitions, affidavits, the settlement, etc. The former contains a total of 19 folio pages and the latter 46 folio pages, all of which are filled with information on both their recto and verso sides.[46] 

The documentation revolves around a legal suit between the Grisogono and Tetrico families that took place from spring through autumn 1561. At the root of this conflict stood the last will and testament of the “late meser Piero Detrico, son of the late Gregorio”, specifically the clauses pertaining to the named residual, or universal, heirs, which read as follows: “As universal heirs of all other movable and immovable possessions…are instituted meser Zuane [Johannes], Gerolamo [Hieronymus], and Zoilo [Zoylus], his legitimate and natural sons, in equal parts”.[47] The rich documentation among Simon Mazzarellus’ protocols permits a “thick” reconstruction of what transpired in 1561, and it sheds considerable light into the procedural considerations underwriting such – admittedly rather high-profile – 

46. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3 and 4, with the main settlement taking place in 1561.

<!-- page 177 -->

legal matters.[48] Yet to understand the drawn out dispute between the involved Grisogono and Tetrico families, it is necessary to consider their kinship ties. 

Table 5.4: Grisogono-Tetrico kinship ties

(a) Lists the different generations (from top to bottom); (b) these three men – Petrus, Franciscus and Hieroymus, sons of the Antonius Grisogono and Magdalena Tetrica – are half the protagonists, and they are the _defendants_ in this lawsuit; (c) they were opposed by their distant relatives Nicolo and Cornelia Tetrico who acted as _tutors_ of (d) the otherwise unnamed children of their brother Alvise Tetrico and his wife Maria, and they are _plaintiffs_ in this lawsuit; (†) points to the root cause of these matters, specifically the testamentary clauses pertaining to the universal heir of the late Piero Tetrico. 

Piero Tetrico’s testament constitutes the proximal origin of the conflict, which comes to the fore immediately in the surviving documentation: he had designated his three sons Zuane (Johannes), Gerolamo (Hieronymus) and Zoilo (Zoylus), as well as their _male_ descendants, as his universal heirs. This is perfectly in line with established practices for the intergenerational preservation of familial property along patrilineal considerations.[49] Given the absence of legitimate male 

48. Geertz, “Thick Description”, esp. pp. 5-10. On his influence, see Lichterman, “Clifford Geertz”, pp. 78-80; Clark, “Pragmatic Reading”, pp. 108-112. 

49. Grbavac, “Testamentary Bequests”, p. 69; “Patrizierinnen”, pp. 50-72. On Dubrovnik, see Janoković-Römer, _Rod i grad_ , pp. 77-93, 130-137; Prešecki-Turk, “Christliche und jüdische Frauen”, pp. 98-104.

<!-- page 178 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

177 

descendants, Piero amended his testament on 3 September 1504 and determined that his property should devolve onto his daughters’ male offspring. As it happened, neither of his daughters had sons, “except Magdalena”, hence Piero made the latter’s three sons – the Reverend Franciscus, as well as his two brothers Petrus and Hieronymus – his universal heirs. In so doing, Piero sought to preserve the family patrimony in male hands, even if he had to both go over his children’s generation and employ his daughter Magdalena as a “stopgap” measure to do so. There was but one testamentary exception to this clause, and it related to a house in Zadar’s St Vitus or St Michael’s parish, for which “the captain Manolj Paleologo” had been granted usufruct rights on 21 September 1523.[50] 

In doing so, Piero creatively used the conventional sex difference of testamentary practices in the area of Italianate cultural diffusion to preserve his family’s wealth (this is why women’s testaments – with testatrixes leaving bequests on their own without a male relatives’ input – are a wonderful and oftensought source to study female agency, which this study does in Chapter 6).[51] This crucial, sex-determined cultural practice caused this dispute as fathers (and other male relatives) conventionally set aside a part of the family patrimony as dowry for one or more daughters; this was typically done via a so-called dowry contract ( _instrumentum dotis_ ) that specified the amount of money, its composition (money, goods, property) and the ways and means the sum would be transferred. In exchange, the bride-to-be ratified the dowry contract and legally confirmed the receipt of her share of the paternal patrimony, thereby preventing her father, brother(s) or other male siblings from future lawsuits.[52] At this intersection of both customary arrangements and legal culture of the Renaissance Adriatic, we can identify what lay at the heart of the matter: Piero’s inheritance was disputed because of the lack of male heirs by his sons Zuane, Gerolamo and Zoilo, as well as by their four sisters Laura, Catherina, Helena and Coliza. Instead, part of Piero’s legacy was claimed by Nicolo and Cornelia Tetrico, who acted as tutors, or guardians, of the unnamed children of their brother Aloysius Tetrico and his wife Maria. What tied all of these 16[th] -century inhabitants of Zadar together was Gregorius Tetrico, Piero’s father, grandfather of Aloysius, Cornelia and Nicolo Tetrico, and great-grandfather of Petrus, Franciscus and Hieronymus Grisogono (Table 5.4; note that I am using the Dalmatian-Venetian names on purpose as some of the documents are written in that vernacular and to facilitate differentiation in the following reconstruction). 

Basically, the lawsuit that took place in spring and summer 1561 was about a particular part of the late Piero’s legacy – specifically, “a patch of land on the island of Iž (Eso) with 66 heads of small livestock [animali minuti]”, most likely goats or sheep – in a very specific context. As a statement by the Tetrico family dated 20 June 1561 intimates, they sought to reclaim this possession and all livestock “because, as they hold, these animals were mistreated…by the late donna 

50. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3, cc. 2r-3v, 4 June 1561, at cc. 2v-3r. 

51. Guzzetti, _Venezianische Vermächtnisse_ , pp. 25-26; Sperling, Wray, “Introduction”, pp. 12-13. 

> 52. See the appendix for a transcription of such a dowry instrument.

<!-- page 179 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

178 

Nicolotta, wife of the aforementioned late meser Piero, who also abused many possessions of her late husband”. This patch of land had been acquired by the late Antonius Grisogono, the statement further held, although the plaintiffs note that this purchase may not have included the livestock: “at the time of acquisition, no mention was made of any animals”, Nicolo and Cornelia held, and “as this is the truth, it is neither right nor honest [come è la verità, non è giusto ne honesto] that the animals are included in the restitution” of Nicolotta’s dowry.[53] Conventionally, once a woman married, her dowry was administered by her husband, with the marriage contracts specifically noting the latter’s obligation “not to misuse” the spouse’s dowry and provide for the complete restitution upon his death.[54] 

The reply by the Grisogono brothers was filed on 1 July 1561, and it contains information that permits the reconstruction of the following timeline and underscore the diachronic qualities of these proceedings. Piero Tetrico had “acquired the patch of land with his animals” on 21 February 1483, “and in over X, XX, XXX, XXXX, and L years, and in everybody’s memory as well, there always had been circa 66 heads of small livestock on that property”.[55] This was followed by another interpellation by the Grisogono brothers on 9 July 1561, in which they disputed their opponents’ claims – by asserting that the latter had “submitted false claims” –, noted that Magdalena was the only surviving child of Piero and asserted that they were legitimate children of Magdalena. To support their claims, Franciscus, Petrus and Hieronymus submitted as evidence both the late Piero’s testament (originally composed on 3 September 1504) and the amendments made, via a codicil, on 21 September 1523 (these changes also provided for Manoli Paleologo renting that house).[56] 

It took several weeks until mid-August 1561 for the Tetrico family to come up with a response, which contained several additional charges: according to Cornelia and Nicolo, the late Piero Tetrico had changed his testament one more time before he died, designating Magdalena’s dowry worth 600 ducats. This clause stood in contrast to Piero’s will stipulated in 1522. That was when his daughter married Antonius Grisogono and, “as a counter-dowry”, Zuane Tetrico had promised Magdalena 110 ducats, handed over “in the name of Lodovico Tetrico” and “in tante bollette”. Pointing to the deed dated 19 March 1522, it was claimed that these transactions had never been accounted for properly.[57] This was a point conceded rather swiftly by the Grisogono brothers, who resolved this issue the next day, as a short note written in the margins of the same folio indicates.[58] Resolution of this minor and separate issue, however, did not solve the dispute over the patch

<!-- page 180 -->

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements 

179 

of land on Iž, let alone improve the sentiments between the adversaries. Hence, Cornelia and Nicolo Tetrico maintained that they considered their opponents’ claims “impertinent” and reaffirmed their commitment to pursuing this case “for justice to be served [per giustizia esser giusto]”.[59] 

The next phase of this legal case then involved the lawyers venturing to the island of Iž to learn about the state of the property, the number of livestock and to interrogate the locals. There exists in these files a quite detailed questionnaire produced at the request of the Tetrico plaintiffs to counter the Grisogono brothers’ seemingly better claims to at least the livestock (ownership of the property was, at this time, no longer really disputed: it belonged to Magdalena’s heirs). Containing a total of twelve numbered items, questions 1 and 2 investigated whether there were, in fact, 66 animals on the property during the _entirety_ of the past 60 years or so and whether Antonius Grisogono ever sold the land to the Tetrico family. Questions 3 through 7 dealt with Antonius selling the land on 9 May 1522 and revolved around the central issue: did he sell it with or without the livestock? As a follow-up, the questionnaire also addressed the issue of ownership of these 66 animals, to the parcel of land in question, and if Antonius sold them together with the land. Question 8 sought to clarify if, when the Tetrico family gave the parcel of land in question to Antonius as part of his wife’s dowry in July 1515, these 66 animals were there and to whom they belonged.[60] 

Another folio documents the replies by two witnesses. The first of them, “Zuane Oporouich of the late Thomas of Iž”, then residing in Zadar, swore that there had always been livestock on the property for the past 64 years as far as he was concerned, although he expressed uncertainty about their exact number: “as regards the quantity, I don’t know…I think there were about 50 animals…but I don’t remember exactly [la quantità io non so…penso che siano stati animali 50 in circa…ma io non lo so particolarmente]”. As regards the key eighth question, Zuane testified that “the livestock always went with the property”, indicating that the property transfer via Magdalena’s dowry transaction signified that the animals came into the possession of Antonius Grisogono.[61] The second witness, “Zuane Oporouich of the late Marin of Iž”, corroborated the information provided by his namesake, with one crucial difference with respect to the pertinent question of livestock ownership, noting that “as far as I remember, the animals had always belong to the Tetrici, and not to the Bartholazio [li animali Sono Stati delli Tetrici et non Bartolazio]”.[62] 

At that point in time, the entire inheritance feud had essentially devolved into a complex set of interrelated claims and counterclaims that were subject to interpretation by the attorneys employed by both sides. In recognition of this situation, both parties agreed to an extrajudicial settlement determined by a _single_ arbiter, “Gregorius Civallellus”, a fellow nobleman, with the listing of the case’s intricacies covering the entirety of two folio pages in Simon Mazzarellus’ protocol 

59. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3, c. 17r, 26 August 1561. 60. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3, cc. 19r-19v, 8 September 1561. 61. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3, cc. 20r-20v, 14 September 1561. 62. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 3, c. 20v, 17 September 1561.

<!-- page 181 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

180 

book. The ruling determined, first and foremost, the acquittal of the Grisogono brothers, meaning that they were to keep the animals. Second, as regarded the outstanding sum of 100 ducats, the Grisogono brothers were permitted to “offset [it] with all income” generated by the leasehold of the property on Iž until 5 February 1555. The outstanding sum – given as 436 libras 10 soldi, which corresponds to 36[3] /8 ducats – was to be paid by the Grisogono brothers in annual instalments of 20 ducats. Thus, the dispute between the Tetrico and Grisogono families ended, and it did so with the following clause: the arbiter Gregorius Civallellus listed his price, a fee of five ducats, to be borne in equal parts by the opposing parties.[63] 

At the end of this richly-documented case, I delimit myself to but a few lines: the most important aspect to consider is the comprehensive documentation in protocol books three and four by notary Simon Mazzarellus. In them, there are copies of most, if not all, of the documents pertaining to this case, including the testament and codicil by Piero Tetrico,[64] the sales contract concerning the property on Iž,[65] the dowry instrument for his daughter Magdalena, composed on the eve of her marriage to Antonius Grisogono,[66] and several other documents pertaining to this case, including extracts from the _Statuta Iadertina_ ’s third book governing the rules for testamentary clauses and inventories.[67] One of the most fascinating aspects of this richly documented case of an extrajudicial settlement is its long history that extends from 1561 all the way to more than eighty years earlier. As regards what transpired after the settlement, there is one thing that can – and must – be said with certainty: there were not many, if any, hard feelings between the litigants, that is, if the appointment in 1564 of “Petrus, son of the late Antonius Grisogonus” as testamentary executor by none other than “Nicolaus Tetricus, son of the late Aloysius [Alvise]” is any indication. It appears that while the aboverelated conflict between next-of-kin was acrimonious, blood, in many ways, ran much thicker than water.[68]

An analysis of the sample of 294 individual notarised deeds relating extrajudicial settlements from Zadar show the essential qualities of the Venetian _commonwealth_ : transcended borders, jurisdictional boundaries and language

Crime, Punishment and Arbitration Settlements

barriers, and shared cultural, economic, historical, legal and social characteristics formed the chief, if not only, points of reference.[69] The above-discussed cases are indicative of the flexible nature of social and legal norms that allowed for both _synchronous_ (across spatial and normative borders) and _diachronous_ conflict resolution, with the latter coming powerfully to the fore in the Grisogono-Tetrico dispute. While the examples discussed here and elsewhere are indicative of the relative widespread use of such settlements, my sample of 294 individual cases is significantly larger than, say, the number of rental or leasehold contracts (226 deeds, see Chapter 4) over the entire three-decade period from 1540 through 1569 across the Renaissance Adriatic.[70]

Moreover, as the sample of 294 deeds relating to arbitration settlements indicates, our contemporary understanding of criminal and (vs.) civil, or private, law is quite problematic, including the terms themselves. The above-discussed cases demonstrate that the study of these “infrajudicial” aspects is both fruitful and promises to pose significant questions to crime historians and legal scholars alike. The evidence before us indicates that Dalmatians in the 16[th] century settled a wide variety of disputes outside the public court system, including capital crimes, such as assault and battery, manslaughter and homicide, as well as virtually everything 

> 69. Apart from the introduction, see Obolensky, _Byzantine Commonwealth_ , pp. 2, 9. See also Fowden, _Empire to Commonwealth_ , pp. 3-11, 100-137. 70. See my “To Avoid the Costs of Litigation”.

<!-- page 183 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

182 

else. Given the sheer quantity of individual cases – a number that easily exceeds the number of properties sold in the city of Zadar by 30% (Table 8.4) – a good case can be made that such extrajudicial settlements were both widespread and perfectly normal. There is, furthermore, little reason to consider that these practices were not widely diffused in earlier or later centuries, in particular before the end of the Venetian Republic (1797) at the hands of Napoleon. 

While this has yet to be determined empirically, these pages here reflect this author’s hopes that more crime historians will follow Benoît Garnot’s path and pay more attention to the ambiguities and ambivalences of the late mediaeval and early modern administration of justice to complement the already existing emphases on class, status group affiliation and sex.[71] As the above data make clear, there may even be a small class-and-status group bias towards the preference of urban elites to settle all kinds of matters outside of the court system (Table 5.3). In the final analysis, and that small distinction notwithstanding, settling disputes in these “infrajudicial” ways was part and parcel of everyday life in 16[th] -century Venetian Dalmatia, and we shall turn our attention to a different, if more frequently studied, aspect in the next chapter. 

71. Cf. Kesselring, “Bodies of Evidence”, p. 246.

<!-- page 184 -->

## 6. Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

Notaries scribbling away in towns up and down the eastern Adriatic coast, from Trieste to Ragusa, were part of a rich culture of municipal record keeping. Like their Italian counter parts on the opposite shore of the sea, the archives of the eastern Adriatic world bear witness to this flurry of writing and record keeping.[1] 

Erin Maglaque 

Venice’s expansion into the eastern Mediterranean in the Middle Ages resulted in a Latin minority in charge of what was politically feasible and culturally conceivable.[2] Although this general characterisation pertains to the _stato da mar_ as a whole, the situation was a little bit different in the Adriatic where class and status group distinctions were perhaps a tad more relevant than confessional diversity. Although individual Venetians were widely spread out within the territories, the _respublica_ ’s influences were clearly visible across economic, legal and social structures. The written legacy – in particular notarial protocols – allows for the reconstruction of a lively picture of 16[th] -century routine, more in touch with the intricacies of Mediterranean daily life than is possible in other periods. Chapters 6 and 7 bring together this original archival documentation – the “moving image”, in Sally McKee’s words – and existing published research – the “soundtrack of the city’s bustle” – to illuminate the personal and quotidian lives of the inhabitants of Dalmatia’s noble and non-noble population.[3] 

Before we proceed, a few words of caution are in order. For this chapter, I have compiled information from all marriage-related contracts I found in the notarial protocols from 1 January 1540 through 31 December 1569. The sample consists of a total of 656 individual deeds, of which about a fifth are marriage contracts ( _contractus matrimonium_ ) and the remainder are dowry accords of quitclaims ( _quietatio dotis_ ), as detailed in Table 6.1. Apart from quantitative indicators about the geographical origins of the contracting parties, the following pages also contain a wealth of qualitative information as I have sought to illustrate these general considerations with examples that fall into three distinct analytical categories: namely, Zadar’s urban nobility, the admittedly few Venetian patricians

> 2. Cf. McKee, “Women”, p. 34.

<!-- page 185 -->

184 

appearing in these records, and the equally small number of nobles from the Croatian hinterlands. For this second edition, I have added a handful of examples drawn from the rich documentation of Zadar’s non-noble population to serve as a kind of “control group”.

## 1. _Geographical and social mobilities_ 

Though usually perceived to be a relatively static society, Venice in reality offered “a certain degree of social mobility up and down the status hierarchies… characterized by remarkable geographical mobility”.[4] These movements of allegiances, money and people took place within the “nexus of state power and personal influence” that characterised the _stato da mar_ .[5] Analysis of the economic, geographical and social dimensions of the Dalmatian nobility as a body politic must depart from the given legal framework. This chapter begins with a survey of Zadar’s body of statutory law to provide a foundation for the subsequent analysis of notarial acts – marriage contracts, dowry quitclaims and other related documents as indicated above (Table 6.1). 

Many Dalmatian noble families in the mid-16[th] century made marriage alliances with their peers in cities along the eastern coast of the Adriatic. Doing so necessitated the social and legal integration of individuals in new, if very similar, urban environments. This chapter contributes to an understanding of this phenomenon by analysing the notarial acts with respect to relationships between social status and personal wealth. From this analysis emerges a more nuanced “picture of the self-identity of the Dalmatian urban nobility through 

> 4. Martin, Romano, “Reconsidering Venice”, p. 21. 

> 5. O’Connell, _Men of Empire_ , p. 12.

<!-- page 186 -->

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

185 

their willingness or unwillingness to mix their own blood with that of members of other élites”.[6] 

Zadar’s communal law contains several clauses, subsumed under the title “On marriages”, or _De nuptiis_ , which contains four dedicated titles outlining the legal framework of marriage; these applied to all social classes.[7] First, it was not permitted to marry without parental consent without forsaking one’s title to a dowry or a share of the inheritance.[8] The only exception to this rule was the clause which detailed the procedure for cases in which the legal guardianship of the offspring was held by non-consanguineous kin.[9] If, for whatever reasons, daughters of at least twenty years of age were not married, they were permitted to choose a partner without parental consent.[10] Analogous laws existed for widows: without the consent of her children, a woman was not allowed to remarry after her husband’s death.[11] 

A separate title of the _Statuta Iadertina_ dealt with dowries. This section of the communal law contains twice the number of clauses than the one discussed above.[12] It is followed by legal definitions of natural and legitimate offspring, which was the only part of the marital legislation subject to amendments by Venice after 1409.[13] In late 1563, the city’s four councillors introduced a motion to increase the commune’s control over the birthrights of their fellow noblemen in Zadar by mandating registration with the public administration within eight days after birth.[14] 

As indicated by Table 6.1, the following analysis is based on a total of 656 individual notarial deeds relating to marriage and dowries. As shown, the ratio of marriage contracts and dowry instruments differed, and there were, on average, four times as many of the latter than of the former. If we only consider the instruments in which one or both parties was of privileged descent, the numbers change: the nobility’s share was around 13% (88 of 656 instances), that is, slightly

Given these numbers, the most important aspect is the geographical distribution of the contracting parties. Despite the variations over the three decades, a certain tendency is visible. Between a quarter and a third of both contracting parties originated outside of Zadar’s jurisdiction. The endogamous marriage practices of the Dalmatian nobility were not confined to individual cities along the eastern seaboard of the Adriatic, as the following examples illustrate.[16] 

In early 1540, “Clara, legitimate and natural daughter of the honourable lord Johannes [Michael] Mazzarellus, a nobleman of Trogir and chancellor of the honourable commune of Zadar”, married a fellow nobleman. Her husband, “Georgius de Grisanis [or Crissana], son of the late Franciscus, a nobleman of

<!-- page 188 -->

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

187 

Šibenik”, came from an area located in-between Trogir and Zadar. In addition to the sizeable dowry worth 800 ducats, their marriage contract reveals two pieces of information. First, the bride and her father were nobles of the small coastal town of Trogir, situated approximately 16 kilometres west of Split. Second, Johannes Mazzarellus’s move from his native town to Zadar (a distance of some 104 kilometres) signified a large geographical distance and, given his occupation as communal chancellor and notary, a qualitative rise within his status group.[17] Clara’s fortunes, however, did not last too long as Georgius died in the mid-1540s. In 1548, the widowed Clara married a second time, this time to “the renowned doctor of both laws Hieronymus de Hermolais, a nobleman of Rab”, with the latter becoming Johannes Mazzarellus’ new son-in-law. Consequently, Mazzarellus transferred Clara’s original dowry to her second husband and augmented it by promising 200 additional ducats.[18] 

A comparable event took place in December 1541 when “the magnificent Franciscus Paladinus, acclaimed sopracomitus [commander] of the trireme from Hvar [pharensis], and the equally acclaimed and excellent doctor [of both laws] and knight Johannes Rosa, nobleman of Zadar” came together. Franciscus’s daughter, “the honest lady Cornelia, his legitimate and natural daughter”, was promised to the renowned nobleman from Zadar. Both contracting parties came from the upper echelons of the nobilities of either place. According to contemporary reports, the Paladinus family disposed of an annual income of 500 ducats,[19] and captainship of the trireme indicates that Franciscus had the financial, military and social means to provide for the warship, which probably elevated him further among his fellow noblemen from Hvar.[20] Johannes Rosa (Ivan Rosa) and his family were explicitly referred to as being among the most prominent and wealthy of Zadar’s nobles.[21] The listing of his education ( _leges utriusque doctor_ ) and knighthood ( _eques_ ) suggests that his social status, too, was elevated in relation to his fellow noblemen. It comes as no surprise that Franciscus promised Johannes a stately dowry of 1,200 ducats, of which a third was to be paid in specie and another 200 ducats “in silken clothes, gold, and silver [in pannis sericis, auro, et argento]”. The second half of the dowry was to be paid in annual instalments of 50 ducats. The marriage contract was drawn up in Zadar “in the house of the respectable Petrus Civallellus”, which was located in St Michael’s parish.[22] It is not clear who benefitted more from this marriage alliance, but the next example is very explicit about this issue.

<!-- page 189 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

188 

In spring of 1553 “Coliza, wife of the late reputable Georgius Dragoeuich, a nobleman of Šibenik”, promised the hand of her daughter to “Petrus Antonius of the late Hieronymus de Ferra, a nobleman of Zadar”. After Coliza had secured the consent of her two sons, one obstacle remained in the way of “the noble and honest lady Philippa, daughter of said the Georgius” and her equally noble dowry of 1,100 ducats: the bride’s sister, “Margarita, wife…of Bernardinus [de Carnarutis], and daughter of the aforementioned Coliza”. Their marriage contract specifically stipulated that Philippa’s dowry payment was to be deferred until Bernardinus had received the rest of his wife’s dowry.[23] Despite the fact that the Ferra family disposed of only 100-300 ducats in annual income and was considered a relatively poor noble family by Venice’s legates,[24] there may have been something other than precedence at play. Bernardinus Carnarutus (Brne Karnarutić) was a renowned soldier and accomplished writer; when he was younger, Bernardinus had fought under Nikola Šubić Zrinski (Zrínyi Miklós, 1508-1566), the Ban of Croatia, against the Ottomans in Hungary. While the former gave up soldiering in the mid-1540s, the latter died defending the city of Szigetvár (Siget) against the troops of Suleiman I in 1566. More prosaically, Carnarutus is mainly known for his literary oeuvre, which consists of poems and prose lauding the gallantry of his former commander. His book, _Vazetje Sigeta grada_ or “The Fall of Szigetvár”, was the first Slavic epic, and it was published in Venice in 1584. Thanks to his literary skills, Bernardinus Carnarutus also enjoyed close ties with his peers in Dubrovnik, the foremost centre of Slavic-Croatian writing in the 16[th] century. It is likely that, by tying this knot, the Ferra family increased their social standing among their fellow Dalmatian noblemen (see also Chapter 2).[25] 

As indicated by these examples, Dalmatian nobles engaged in endogamous marriage practices. Geographical differences among the various coastal communities played only a minor role. Still, the majority of families chose to marry locally, that is, within Zadar’s jurisdiction, although absent detailed comparative studies of especially numerically smaller coastal communities like Pag, Trogir or Rab, this might be a function of the relative size and availability of spouses. In the summer of 1546 “young nobleman Laurentius de Nassis, son of the late Johannes”, gave the hand of his sister, “Catherina” to his fellow nobleman, “Vito de Cedolinis, son of the late Simon”. Since the bride’s father was already dead, Laurentius promised his brother-in-law a dowry worth “four-hundred fifty-one ducats”. Of this sum, 120 ducats were still in the possession of a third party, and Laurentius contractually agreed to redeem two pieces of property “in the name of Vito and his brother from the hands of ser Simon de Grisogonis, son of the late Peter”. One of these parcels of land was located “near Zadar in the area called Celopech, or Orisaz”, and the other was on the island of Lukoran across the Canal 

23. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 3, f. 8r, 29 March 1553. 

24. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 197. 

25. Fine, _When Ethnicity Did Not Matter in the Balkans_ , p. 197. In addition to the literary connection, Carnarutus also had a personal interest: his illegitimate daughter “Judita” was married to “magister Nicolaus de Andreis de Ragusa”, a master-painter, who received a dowry worth 250 ducats. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes a Morea, I, 1, 6, f. 25v, 12 November 1566.

<!-- page 190 -->

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

189 

of Zadar. The remaining 331 ducats were to be disbursed in movable goods after Catherina had moved in with her husband. The contract was written “in the house of Simon de Matafaris alias Chrina, son of the late Peter” located in Zadar’s St Michael’s parish.[26] 

On another occasion, in summer 1557, “Bernardinus Galellus…and Simon Civalellus, son of the late Gregorius”, came together. In the name of the Holy Spirit, Bernardinus promised his daughter, the “honest lady Catherina”, to Simon, accompanied by a dowry of 800 ducats. One half of this was to be handed over once the bride moved in with her husband, while the second half was to be paid in annual rates of 25 ducats.[27] A couple of months later, in January 1558, the two parties reconvened “in the storehouse [ _apotheca_ ] of Baptista de Nassis…in the communal main square” to draw up a formal dowry quitclaim issued at the request of Simon, who stated “he has had, and truly so, received…four hundred and one ducats…in part in denarii and specie, in part in gold, silver, pearls and silken garments, and partly in woollen and linen cloth”.[28] 

In these and many other comparable cases, no differences between marriages of individuals from within with their spouses from beyond Zadar’s jurisdiction are evident. Irrespective of geographical provenance, the urban nobilities of the Venetian Adriatic viewed themselves as belonging to the same social group and acted upon these sentiments. 

As for the Venetian nobility in Zadar, their numbers were small. In only 120 instances are individuals referred to as “Venetian”, and almost all of them were serving the republic in Dalmatia. This suggests that there were few incentives besides public service to move to the Adriatic. Some names appear in the records more than once, and on many occasions these Venetians acted as procurators for Zadar-based constituent parties.[29] Most of them were affiliated with the military of the Republic of St Mark, hence caution is warranted to infer more as the same individual was often 

26. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Marcus Aurelius Sonzonius, I, 1, 1, ff. 11v-12v, 24 August 1546. Fortunately, Catherina’s (nuncupative) testament is available, too. Both her brother Laurentius and her husband Vitus are named as her executors, who should bury her “in ecclesia Sancte Mariae monialium”, hinting at her preference for the Benedictines over the Dominicans and Franciscans. Another, more delicate factor is also known. Apparently, Catherina was pregnant _before_ the marriage contract was written. Her testament, written three months prior, makes this clear: “Instituit, ac voluit filium, auf filiam, Sique essent aut forent”, as her residual heir. Only after the eventual death of her unborn child did she designate Vitus as her heir, on the condition that he pay her beloved kinsman “Donatus de Ciuallello” the sum of 50 ducats out of her dowry. 190 

appointed multiple times. Given their marginal appearance in the notarial deeds, it comes as no surprise that Venetians appear on only three occasions in marriage contracts. The most important and prominent case was the Zadar-based branch of the Venier family.[30] In late 1542 “ser Georgius de Venerio, son of the late ser Georgius citizen of Zadar”, confirmed receipt of the dowry of his wife, “Nicolota, the legitimate and natural daughter of the late Simon Coreuich, once a notary and citizen of Zadar”. As regards the clauses and, perhaps most importantly, the value of the dowry, there are no essential difference between this contract and those of the Dalmatian locals discussed so far. The quitclaim mentions that Nicolota’s dowry amounted to 3,029 libras in movable and immovable goods, a sum roughly equivalent to some 490 ducats, and specified the particulars of its disbursement.[31] 

Other Venetian patricians who appear in the marriage-related deeds include “Marcus Antonius Lauretanus [Loredan], son of the late magnificent Jacobus, patrician of Venice [patritij Veneti]” and “Hieronymus de Mosto, son of the late magnificent Johannes Franciscus”. The former issued a quitclaim for a dowry worth 641 libras and 14 soldi (or some 103 ducats) that he had received with the hand of his wife, “Helysabeth…from ser Johannes Anzolerio, citizen and resident of Zadar”.[32] The latter, Hieronymus da Mosto, or Gerolamo da Mosto, a resident of Novigrad, also issued a dowry quitclaim. His wife, “Sancta, daughter of Vendramin de Brissia [Brixen], dwells, as he said, in Padua”, and brought with her a dowry worth 150 ducats.[33] 

A related, albeit minor, aspect in the notarial records of the period under survey concerns the connections of Dalmatian nobles with the CroatianSlavonian interior. One procuratorial appointment, for instance, tells the story of the posthumous voyage of a Zadar-born nobleman, Theodosius de Begna, who had died “somewhere in Hungary and Croatia” (see also Chapter 2).[34] In a second instrument, the “young nobleman of Šibenik, Melchior Cossirich, son of Jacobus’s [union with] the late lady Magdalena, daughter of the late count Georgius Bencouich, a Croatian nobleman from Plauno” (Plavno) appointed a procuratrix. With his father’s consent, Melchior tasked “lady Helena Bencouichia, daughter of the aforementioned late count Georgius”, to acquire the rightful share of his mother’s inheritance. The appointee was to journey “into the borders of the Kingdom of Hungary [infra fines Regni Hungariae]”, meet with their relatives and retrieve all money and movable and immovable goods. At the time of writing, Helena “was the wife of count Stephanus Crouat, and, as it is said, already living in Zagreb county [in comitatu Zagrabiensis commorantem]”.[35] 

> 30. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 172; Anzulović, “O opstojnosti hrvatskog pučanstva”, p. 271; Knapton, “Stato da Mar”, pp. 332-335; Mayhew, _Dalmatia_ , pp. 102-103. 31. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Johannes Michael Mazzarellus, I, 2, 2, s.p., 22 December 1542. 32. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 1, 3, c. 32r, 11 January 1556.

HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 1, cc. 13r-14r, 8 January 1556. 35. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 3, c. 189r, 2 July 1559.

<!-- page 192 -->

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

191 

The paucity of documents relating to the Croatian-Hungarian realm, and marital connections in particular, suggests that there were not many such ties between the Venetian dominions along the Adriatic coast and the hinterlands of the western Balkans. In the case of 16[th] -century Zadar, only two dowry instruments reveal clues about the existence of such ties. Gabriel Cernotta, himself a nobleman from Rab and one of Zadar’s notaries, had married the daughter of one of the rural nobles from Posedarje, a small fortified village within Zadar’s jurisdiction. In the summer of 1553, Gabriel, “as procurator and in the name of his wife lady Margarita, daughter of the late count Nicolaus de Possedaria”, formally confirmed the receipt of his wife’s dowry worth 250 ducats.[36] “Vido Posedaria”, who was among those named explicitly in a report to the Venetian Senate, stipulated on behalf of his late brother.[37] 

In the second contract, “count Gregorius Paladinich, son of the late count Gasparus, a nobleman of Croatia”, similarly confirmed the receipt of 620 libras (about 100 ducats) in both specie and movable goods. His wife, “lady Marieta”, was the daughter of Marcus Antonius de Bassano, father of notary Petrus de Bassano. While the sources do not mention that the de Bassano family was of privileged descent, its members evidently commanded a social status high enough to enable Marieta to marry a nobleman.[38] 

A trend of particular interest that emerges in the marriage-related contracts is that dowries regularly appear to have exceeded the annual income of noble families, which ranged between 100 and 300 ducats per year.[39] These families were probably able to pay for such large dowries because dowry payments could be deferred, were paid out over several years and the families owned real estate, which provided a secure source of future income (see also Chapter 4). 

As for the geographical origins of the contracting parties, other conclusions are worthy of note. According to the contracts, most individuals who married into Zadar’s nobility were of equally privileged descent and were referred to as noblemen, typically in combination with additional information about their place of origin. While the sample does not allow for the assessment of the desirability of marital ties between, for instance, families from a coastal centre and the wider Croatian-Hungarian hinterlands, other aspects can be surmised. A quarter of the cases mention family ties to places ranging from the Kvarner Gulf to the Bay of Kotor. And while it is pointless to muse about upward social mobility among the Dalmatian nobility, consideration of incremental status increases within that 

Satisfactione et per solution…bonorum paternorum, maternorum, Avitorum et aliorum quorumcumque dicta dominae Margaritae”. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 2B, cc. 35r-36r, 6 May 1560. Almost all other dowry instruments, quitclaims, marriage contracts and testaments of Zadar’s nobility omit this specific wording, which explicitly refers to, in Chojnacki’s words, the bride’s “rightful share of the patrimony [her] indisputable right to a dowry”. Chojnacki, “Dowries and Kinsmen”, p. 575.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

192 

and writer Bernardinus Carnarutus to a noblewoman from Šibenik, and by the two members of the Mazzarellus family of Trogir who moved from a small town to the provincial capital and became Zadar’s communal chancellors.[40] 

Then – as now – the urban elites set the tone, so to speak, for the rest of society, and this certainly also applied to 16[th] -century Zadar, as the following three examples of marriage-related deeds pertaining to the commoners illustrate. My first example here concerns “Johannes Phylllipcich, son of the late Sion of Racice in Zadar’s jurisdiction”, who married “Lucia, the daughter of Johannes Paulichieuich of Tkon”, in January 1544. Johannes’ bride was the domestic servant ( _ancilla_ ) of “ser Johannes de Venetijs, a fruit vendor and resident of Zadar”, who furnished her with a dowry worth 211 libras paid out in cash (125 libras) and otherwise unspecified movable goods.[41] Essentially the same conditions can be found across the other 568 marriage-related deeds that deal with commoners, such as “Antonius Cosulich, son of the late Marinus from island of Sali”, whose sister “Julia”, in January 1559, had married “Johannes Iuancich, also known as Parolizza”, from the same village. Although payment had commenced “many years earlier”, her dowry was worth 98 libras 16 soldi, and it had now been fully transferred.[42] Finally, mention shall be made of the case of “Catherina, wife of the late ser Marcus Rachouich, once a sailor, citizen and resident of Zadar”, whose daughter “Clara” married “Salichio, son of the master-caulker [ _calafatus_ ] Mathaeus Chreglich”. Catherina was accompanied by, and had received the consent of, her brother, “the Reverend Father Johannes Rachouich” to do so, thereby once more connecting the example to issues related to representation and female agency. Clara’s dowry amounted to 150 ducats, of which two-thirds were to be transferred “in movable goods” and the remainder in cash.[43] 

In combination with the procuratorial analysis of Chapters 2 and 3, these findings suggest that the kinship ties of Dalmatian nobles extended over a wide area, circumscribed by, but not confined to, the Venetian possessions in the Adriatic. Future studies are likely to confirm that the “remarkable geographical mobility” ascribed to Venice proper by John Martin and Dennis Romano was, _mutatis mutandis_ , also a key feature within the upper social milieux in the Venetian Adriatic and, very likely but varying geographically, comparable in the other possessions of the Republic of St Mark in the eastern Mediterranean.[44] 

> 40. Population estimates for the 16[th] century are hard to come by; however, it is the proportion of population size that is of interest here. In 1553, Zadar’s inhabitants numbered c. 6,500. Among them were c. 1,400 able-bodied men, while Trogir proper was home to c. 300 able-bodied men. If the same proportion of able-bodied men to the general populace is assumed, Trogir proper was home to c. 1,500 inhabitants, or about a fifth of Zadar. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, pp. 198, 208.

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture

## 2. _Material culture: testaments and inventories_ 

In addition to being devout and loyal to the Most Serene Republic, Zadar’s nobility was described by Venice’s legates as relatively poor, especially in comparison to Venice proper. Despite their limited wealth, Dalmatian noble families lived and clothed themselves _all’Italiana_ . This was attributed by Venice’s officials to their regular contact with individuals from the Apennine peninsula.[45] By now, scholarly research into the self-representation of Venice’s body politic has established a reliable framework for further study of her dominions.[46] Yet, with the exceptions of the Republic of Dubrovnik and the island communities of Rab and Korčula, and Trogir, this area of inquiry has been largely neglected in the other Adriatic dominions of the _respublica_ .[47] The main obstacle is the lack of illustrative sources. Nevertheless, this problem may be resolved by the “abundance of written sources” – more specifically, wills and inventories.[48] 

45. Giovanni Battista Giustiniano described the nobles in 1553 as follows: “La richezza di questi nobili non è molta, perchè la maggior intrada, che sia fra loro, è di ducati quattrocento, cinquecento et fino settecento, come li Tetrici, Rosa, Civallelli, et altri, ch’hanno ducati cento d’entrada, ducento e fino trecento. E sono di questi nobili molti poverissimi, i costumi dei quali sono quasi italiani, perchè la maggior parte de nobili vive, favella et veste all’usana d’Italia, il che forse avviene per la frequenza de forestieri, nobili veneziani, generali, proveditori, capitanii, sopracomiti et altri, che vi praticano continuamente”. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 197. Comparable statements can be found about other urban centres in Venetian Dalmatia, such as Koper, Šibenik, Trogir and Split. See _ibid_ ., pp. 191, 204-205, 208, 215. As to the Albanian towns Ulcinj and Bar, by contrast, Venice’s legate wrote of “costumi barbari, parlano lingua albanese tutta differente dalla Dalmatia”. _Ibid_ ., pp. 227, 231.

On Rab, see Mlacović, “Družbeni” (both unavailable to the author). See also Mlacović, _Građani plemići_ , esp. pp. 202-288. The named individuals were the sons of Federicus Grisogonus (Federik Grisogono), the renowned medical doctor and professor at the University of Padua. In autumn 1555, “Reverenda domina sor Marchetta [Grisogona] Monialis professa in monasterio Sanctae Mariae Jadrensis ordinis Sancti Benedicti” donated 200 ducats to her late brother’s sons, “dominis Hieronimo et Julio, ad praesens in Patavino [Padua] Gimnasio existentibus”. The two beneficiaries were absent but their brother Pompeius was present and accepted the donation on their behalf. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 1, 1, s.p., 11 November 1555. In the late 1550s, “dominus Pompeius Chrysogonus quondam excellentis domini Federici artium et medicinae doctoris nobilis Jadrensis agens nomine proprio ac nomine et vice dominorum Hieronymi et Julij fratrium

<!-- page 195 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

194 

For the discussion of material culture in 16[th] -century Zadar below, I have surveyed all existing testaments and codicils preserved in the protocol books of the city’s notaries; in all, they amount to 1,107 individual documents that were written and authenticated by fourteen different notaries. Of these, the men and women who belonged to Zadar’s nobility required a total of 111 documents (78 testaments, 33 codicils), and their share of approximately 10% corresponds to the nobility’s share among the city’s population. Moreover, 49 documents in the sample were requested by women.[49] The distribution of these documents among the fourteen notaries, though, is very uneven, with Johannes Michael Mazzarellus, Zadar’s communal chancellor and a nobleman of Trogir, firmly leading the pack in this regard: in his protocol books, there are no less than 443 testaments and codicils (which correspond to some 40% of the total).[50] As regards the sub-set of documents by noble men and women discussed in the following pages, almost two-thirds of these (72 out of 111) were written down by aristocratic notaries Gabriel Cernotta (3 individual documents), Johannes Michael Mazzarellus (66) and the latter’s son Simon Mazzarellus (3). With these fundamentals clarified, let us now turn to the contents of these documents. 

Structurally, an enduring mediaeval feature of the testaments and codicils from 16[th] -century Zadar is the persistence of charitable bequests undertaken “for the soul” ( _pro anima_ ) of the testator or testatrix.[51] Conventionally, these clauses relate to the determination of the gravesite, charitable donations to the lazaretto, the Chest of St Simeon and several (partially optional) bequests to other ecclesiastical institutions.[52] One of the testaments’ core provisions was the nomination of executor(s) who were nearly always close relative(s); in my sample of noble testaments, three out of four (this happened in 33 instances out of the 49 testaments and codicils written at the request of women) executors were close relatives whose primary task was to arrange for the clauses to be carried out faithfully.[53] These conditions were followed by a listing (if relevant) of the so-called paraphernalia – those female possessions that the testatrix could dispose 

suorum modo in Ghymnasio patavino studentium” leased all jointly owned salt pans “in valle Pagi in confinio Sancti Joannis de Cangerich” to “Reverendo domino Joanni Ifcich canonico pagensis”. In exchange for the annual payment of 230 libras, the canon leased the entire income of the salt pans for the duration of five years. Let us begin by examining testaments of female members from the three wealthiest families of Zadar’s nobility: the Tetrico, Rosa and Civallello families.[55] The first example is the testament of “the noble lady Felicita, wife of the renowned Franciscus Tetricus, nobleman of Zadar, and daughter of the late domini Nicolaus Buchia, a nobleman of Kotor”.[56] Among the first clauses is the provision that she wanted to be buried by the Franciscans. This was followed by donations to their church.[57] For additional ornaments, Felicita ordered that the following movable goods be given:

from Felicita’s dowry and transferred to the Franciscans who, in return, were obliged to read a mass every year in her memory for the equivalent of 30 ducats until the total sum was paid (that is, over the five years after her death). Perhaps to hedge against ill tidings, Felicita furthermore determined several additional requiems to be celebrated in the churches dedicated to St Catherine (Benedictines) and St Donatus, while leaving small amounts of money to the reliquary chapel of St Simeon and Our Lady of Peace in Zadar’s suburbs. All these bequests were tied to masses in the testatrix’s memory.[60] Her husband, Franciscus, was to receive “one garment of black cloth and one garment ex Sarzia rouana lined with fox fur”; her godmother, “the lady Magdalena, wife of the brave constable Johannes a Lacu”, was to receive “one golden ring with the arms of the House of Tetrico”.[61] Magdalena’s daughters were to receive “one vestem a dorso of coarse wool…and another chemise made of Milanese cloth”. Finally, Felicita’s former maid, “Catherine daughter of Matheus Sagoraz”, received “all chemises and any other items of everyday clothing”.[62] 

Another case is the testament of “the noble and honest lady Marchetta, daughter of the late Philippus de Ferra and wife, in her second matrimony, of the late Petrus Ciuallelli, nobleman of Zadar”. She, too, destined the Franciscan church as her gravesite, instructing her heirs to “have the friars of St. Francis celebrate one hundred _missas parvas_ for her soul…upon the death of the testatrix…with befitting donations [cum helemosino consueta]”. She obliged her two children, “Thomasina and Hieronymus”, with the correct execution of her last will. Among the many clauses in which money was destined to kinsmen, there is a hint at movable goods: her other son, “the Reverend Canon Zoylo”, was to receive “a silver chalice, gilded, with a paten”, worth up to 200 libras.[63]

A third example is the testament of “the noble lady Euphemia, daughter of the late Johannes de Grisogonis and wife of the renowned doctor of both laws Simon Rosa”. In contrast to the two above-related cases, Euphemia preferred the Benedictine nunnery of St Mary as her final resting place. In addition to the clauses providing for the testatrix’s immovable property – a plot of land near the villages of Banj, a warehouse in the vicinity of Zadar’s main square and possessions elsewhere[64] – her testament contains remarks about movable goods: “Thus she willed and destined Magdalena, natural daughter of her son Michael… each and every good wearable a dorso…and another chemise made of black Milanese [cloth], and two gowns from Serbia [ex rassie] and ten ducats”. While it is impossible to ascertain whether these movable goods came out of Euphemia’s dowry or her paraphernalia, she could bequeath them at her own discretion.[65] 

There are also, of course, testaments from among less wealthy families, and the last will of “the noble and honest lady Lucretia, daughter of the late Cresius de Gallellis and wife of the late Carolus de Cedulinis” serves as an example.[66] She ordered her grave to be constructed “in St Mary’s”, which was run by the Benedictines. After Lucretia handled her customary charitable bequests,[67] she destined “one of her chemises of black wool to Lucia, mother of the priest Johannes Francouich”. The priest was to receive “the below-listed goods as related in the common tongue [vulgari sermone describenda], that is, un letto de piuma col Suo cavazzal, un paro de lineaoli, uno paro de intimele et un cussin de piuma”. Lucretia’s sister received “do camise uno paro de lineoli, et uno paro de intimelle” and 200 ducats, which were to be paid out of the testatrix’s income deriving from her immovable property. The testament contains several additional clauses concerning monetary matters[68] and the designation of the residual heir: Lucretia bequeathed her entire real estate and the corresponding income to all “the impoverished and old maidens [unmarried women]”. The testament was to be preserved in the nunnery of St Mary and contained the following provision: 

Boyci” would inherit their mother’s goods. In addition, the testatrix had the notary insert the provision “utriusque sexus” between the lines. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Simon Mazzarellus, I, 3, no. 4, 11 March 1557. 

64. Her immovable property was transferable only to legitimate male offspring: “Cum condictione per dictum testamentum Testamentaliter expresse apposita ex filij dictorum filiorum Suorum legitimariorum”.

67. All testaments typically included small bequests to the “fabrica cappelle Sancti Simeonis Justi”, the “lazaretto pestiferorum”, one or another school or hospital or a combination thereof. 

68. In an additional dowry instrument, Lucretia was promised “a Bernardino et Joanne Petri fratribus de Carnaruto ducatorum centum quinquaginta”, out of which only 50 ducats had been paid accordingly. Hence, she went on to bequeath the total sum of 100 ducats (of which 50 ducats were still outstanding) to “domina Marine olim uxor quondam domini Donati de Carnarutis sorori dicti testatrices”.

<!-- page 199 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

198 

Once two hundred ducats have been accumulated, the aforesaid executors should give and disburse these 200 ducats as an increase of the dowry of one of the aforementioned noble maidens, poorer or older…if she wished to marry, lady Baldissara, daughter of the late Federicus Grisogono, son of the late Francis.[69] 

These 200 ducats were to be _accumulated_ out of the income of Lucretia’s real estate, the alienation of which was explicitly prohibited. The rest of the testament contains a list of the testatrix’s properties, which suggests that she was comparatively wealthy, despite not being specifically indicated as such by Venice’s legates. Thus, the wealth of Zadar’s nobles does not seem to have been limited to the Tetrico, Rosa and Civallello families.[70] 

Two other examples support this conclusion: first, there is the testament of “the lady Gelenta, daughter of the late Simon Ciprianj of Zadar”. She preferred her family grave “in St Mary’s of the Dominicans, or St Simeon’s”. The testatrix then left “Lucretia and Raphaela, daughters of her late brother Antonius”, 300 libras (or about 48 ducats) each in the case that her nieces wished to marry. Her maid, “Agneti”, received “a long gown from Serbia [ex rassia] and a kerchief [faciolum a capite]”.[71] 

Finally, we have the testament of “lady Catherina, daughter of the late Hieronymus de Nassis, nobleman of Zadar, and wife of the late Franciscus Gallelli”. Again, the testatrix preferred the Franciscan church[72] and left some of her movable goods to her relatives. Two of Catherina’s nieces, “Helisabeth and Magdalene”, were in the noble nunnery of St Mary, and each received one ducat and “a kerchief”. In addition, “Cathussa, formerly [Catherina’s] maid, received a pair of gloves…and a cord to keep the kerchief in place, with a joint value of three libras”. Similarly, the testatrix “left to Franiza of Ugljan, a lay nun [pizochara] serving in the hospital of St Bernard of Zadar, one of the two leather garments

Zadar’s Urban Elites and Their Material Culture 

199 

made of lambskin and a kerchief [faciolum a capite]”. As would be expected, Catherina’s maid received goods as well.[73] 

This cursory overview illustrates how bequeathed goods, testaments and inventories contribute significantly to the study of the material culture of Dalmatia’s elites. As has been demonstrated, money, jewellery and expensive garments were predominantly bequeathed to other nobles and members of the clergy. In addition, these sources offer insights into the self-representation of Dalmatia’s urban nobility. The possessions and status symbols range from the explicit mention of a signet ring or piece of furniture to a burial “in the habit of the order” (in whose church the grave was to be located).[74] Right of abode, small amounts of money, pieces of furniture or clothing of lesser quality and value were typically willed to former domestic servants or otherwise acquainted individuals. The rich archives of Dalmatia’s cities offer ample possibilities for future research into the material culture and self-representation of the urban elites as a whole. One example is the “Inventory of the goods and inheritance of the late Nicolaus Fanphoneo, nobleman of Zadar”, composed by notary Petrus de Bassano. Twelve folii long, it meticulously documents movable and immovable goods “as required by the nobleman Gregorius de Fanphogna, his brother and testamentary heir”.[75] Among other items, the inventory includes a list of books the deceased Nicolaus owned, alluding to the potential of inventories in assessing the level of education among Dalmatian nobles, another aspect that is (still) a major lacuna of the field:[76] 

Then, there are several different books, totalling 17, that is, one by Horace, one by Cicero, one by Quintilian, one by Juvenal, one by the lexicographer Lorenzo Valla, one with the Epistles of Ovid, [one with] the Rules of St Benedict, one by Petrarch, one by Vergil, Ovid’s Metamorphoses, one by Gaius Matius, the Epistles of Cicero, the Meditations of Saint-Bonaventure, one by Livy with a red leather jacket, another small book by Juvenal, a small Grammar book and an old missal of parchment.[77]

Urban Elites of Zadar 

200 

lives (if applicable) before the property would ultimately devolve onto the next generation.[78] Still, the reasons for composing a testament in 16[th] -century Zadar were essentially similar to those 200 years earlier, which we know about thanks to Branka Grbavac’s work.[79] The one structural difference appears to be the absence of explicit mentions of the fear of death, even though references to imminent birth, old age or disease continue to appear here and there. Unlike their peers in the 14[th] century, Renaissance Zadar’s noble women were no longer exclusively bequeathing their worldly goods to their daughters (which was done more consistently during the late mediaeval era, especially in Venice).[80] If the aim was to keep the patrimony within the woman’s family, analysis of these clauses shows that about half of all residual heirs instituted by testatrices in Zadar were one or more sons (meaning that their mothers’ dowries would, in large parts, devolve onto the male side of the family).[81] 

Finally, mention shall be made of the many objects listed in the testaments. While their study continues to be an essential factor for the material possessions of the urban elites (due to the absence of many pictorial sources), another difference compared to the 14[th] century emerges as there were fewer liturgical objects circulating in the period under survey here.[82] Comparable aspects certainly informed the testamentary practices of the commoners, whose detailed study, however, will hopefully follow in the not-too-distant future.[83]

In general, there appears to be no structural, or fundamental, difference in the qualities and clauses governing noble and nonnoble last wills, with the chief difference being individual wealth.

## 7. Zadar’s Elite Residents and Citizens 

In displaying titles with some regularity honored citizens looked upward, not toward any assimilation with the true aristocracy but toward establishing symbolic bonds with their superiors, and downward, to the mass of the populace from which they hoped to distance themselves. Already possessed of wealth and office, they saw honorific descriptives as one more element that set them off from the truly plebeian.[1] 

James S. Grubb 

Over the course of centuries, enduring myths based on the writings of Marcantonio Sabellico, Gasparo Contarini’s treatise _De magistratibus et republica Venetorum_ and others’ texts[2] shrouded the realities of the Republic of Venice in written accounts (as they still do today in modern scholarship).[3] Typically, contemporaries characterised the society of early modern Venice as tripartite, made up of patricians, citizens and foreigners.[4] The ruling patricians were defined elaborately, but the non-noble elites remained obscure and undifferentiated. From the 15[th] century, however, this distinct “ordine cittadinesco” became increasingly diversified in written records.[5] Like their patrician peers who ruled the Republic of Venice, members of this particular group of original citizens were eventually required to provide information about the legitimacy of their birth if The result they aspired to certain public offices like the Procurator of St Mark.[6]

3. Crouzet-Pavan, _Venice Triumphant_ , pp. 84-96; Grubb, “When Myths Lose Power”, pp. 50-60; Muir, _Civic Ritual_ , pp. 13-61; Povolo, “Creation of Venetian Historiography”, pp. 491-519; Queller, _Venetian Patriciate_ , pp. 3-28; Martin, Romano, “Reconsidering Venice”, pp. 2-9. See also Finlay, _Politics in Renaissance Venice_ , Gullino, “Il Patriziato”, pp. 379-413. 

4. The bipartite model fell out of use from the 18[th] century since it was recognised that educated and skilled original citizens ( _cittadini originari_ ) were increasingly tied to the ruling patricians via honours, public offices and other rewards. In exchange, these elite commoners provided essential services for the continuity of government, marked by the rotation of “amateur patricians” in and out of office, a notion introduced by Ranke.
