Urban Elites of Zadar 

202 

was a “distinctively subaltern class” of citizens of Venetian origin tied to, but distinctively separated from, the ruling patriciate.[7] 

Once the splendour of Renaissance Venice and its lagoon is left behind, however, the picture becomes much less clear. Unfortunately, the historiography of the _stato da mar_ is uneven in this regard, too. Tendencies to focus on the larger (sub)regions – Crete, Cyprus and Constantinople – have resulted in less scholarly interest in, and documentation of, other geographical areas.[8] The most recent contributions have focused heavily on the administrators of Venice’s maritime state and investigated the patricians sent to administer and govern the Doge’s subjects, while issues of citizenship and residency rights have hardly been addressed.[9] To address these issues at least for central Dalmatia, the following pages examine a broader spectrum of social groups in Zadar, namely Venetians, Croats, Jews and the non-noble elites. 

## 1. _Venetians_ 

The number of Venetian patricians and commoners who lived in the _stato da mar_ , and in Zadar in particular, is hard to assess, especially since the military presence distorts the picture considerably. In the period surveyed, I found 145 individual notarial deeds (out of more than 6,000) that refer to individuals with geographical, political or social origins in the Venetian lagoon. And even this number is misleading because sometimes the same individual appears on multiple occasions. Hence, while the following discussion of examples seeks to offer a picture of the activities of these groups, it must be acknowledged that the data is incomplete and biased toward those individuals who appear more than once in the records. 

As regards the presence of Venetian patricians, we note the appearance of names such as Balbi, Bembo, Boldù, Bragader, Canal, Ciconia, Contarini, da Mosto, Dandolo, Dolfin, Falier, Foscari, Gritti, Loredan, Malipiero, Marcello, Memmo, Michiel, Mocenigo, Molin, Nani, Navager, Pisani, Priuli, Querini, Renier, Salamon, Savorgnan, Trevisan, Tron, Vallaresso, Venier and Zen. Further

Zadar’s Elite Residents and Citizens 

203 

mention is made of a member of the Lippomano family (although it is noted that they hailed from Bergamo).[10] The most striking aspect – and one that is consistent in the notarial records – is that these Venetians are conventionally mentioned as holding both citizenship ( _civis_ ) and/or residency rights ( _habitator_ ) in Zadar, and these very often include Venetian military officials like “Aloysius Memo [Alvise Memmo]”, then in charge of the castle, who appointed the “Bernardinus Contarenus, son of the late Carolus”, his procurator to regain whatever outstanding payments from the competent offices in Venice.[11] It was more typical for Venetian officers to participate in social arrangements, which happened when “Michael Salomon, son of the late Nicolaus”, then serving “as captain of Zadar”, lent a hand to one of the soldiers under his command: as documented by Johannes Michael Mazzarellus, Michael paid out the dowry – worth 200 ducats – of “Dorothea, daughter of ser Paulus Spinelli, a citizen of Padua”, to her husband, “ser Franciscus Gallo, cannoneer [ _bombarderius_ ] in Zadar’s main fort”.[12] 

The by far most numerous references in the notarial deeds, however, were made with respect to the Zadar-based Venier family. Well-established by the mid-16[th] century, they operated out of their residence in Zadar’s “St Stephen’s parish”.[13] We also know from contemporary accounts by Venice’s legates that members of this family were tasked with the military security of the immediate environs around Zadar. The village of Zemunik, just ten kilometres away from the city walls (today the location of Zadar’s airport), boasted a “castello di meser Thomaso Venier et fratelli”, as was noted in the _relazioni_ of Venice’s syndic Antonio Diedo, written in 1553.[14] 

Apart from these military endeavours, no distinction can be discerned in activities or habits between the Venetian patrician family of the Venier and their Dalmatian peers. They leased their real estate to acquaintances, like their fellow soldier “Johannes Rimanich”,[15] and conceded other parts to labourers.[16] And like

Urban Elites of Zadar 

204 

Zadar’s nobles, Thomaso and his siblings carried out procuratorial duties. For instance, in June 1559 “the lady Nicolota, wife of the late Georgius Venerij, once a citizen and resident of Zadar, acting in her own right [mulier Sui Juris]” appointed “the magnificent and generous Sanctus Venerio, a patrician of Venice”, to settle her legal troubles. The instrument cites a sentence issued on 12 June 1555 “by the acclaimed Natale Donato, then serving as Provveditore generale in Dalmatia”. Sanctus was to recover the sum of 79 libras from Nicolota’s adversary, “Demetrij Lascari”, who evidently did not pay his rent on time.[17] 

When Thomaso and his brother Stephanus were not carrying out procuratorial duties,[18] they were galley commanders in the service of the Republic of St Mark. In December 1542, Thomaso paid the 44 oarsmen of his galley for their military service. In the presence of the two witnesses, “ser Christopher Apolonius Scriuanello and Martin Bolicich of Zadar, the bireme’s pilot”, Thomsaso paid out a total of about 2661/2 ducats to his crew. Four out of ten oarsmen hailed from within Zadar’s jurisdiction (17 of 44) while another sixth (7 out of 44) originated on the large southern Dalmatian island of Korčula; yet there were also oarsman from other parts within and outside of Venice’s _stato da mar_ , e.g., “Petrus Radimouich of Šibenik”, “Elias of Dubrovnik, son of John”, “Lio of Kythira” in the Ionian Sea or “Alexander of Koroni”. All 42 sailors had served on other warships before, which were commanded by Venetian patricians hailing from 15 different families. As far as the social composition of the crew is concerned, the quitclaim also lists an artisan among the rowers, one “Athanasius of Korčula, son of Vasili”, a ship’s carpenter specialised in oars-making aboard the trireme of “the magnificent Giovanni Battista Carebli” before enlisting as an oarsman with Tommaso’s bireme.[19]

## _Sources_ : see note 19. 

(a) gives the names of the 44 sailors; (b) provides the present-day toponyms (which facilitates locating these places on contemporary maps) – note that the asterisk (*) indicates places within Zadar’s jurisdiction, on which see also the below map; (c) indicates earlier service on warships commanded by the patricians listed; (d) gives the amounts paid in libras and soldi; (e) Helia most likely served with Francesco Pasqualigo, referred to as “proveditor with several galleys” by Francesco Longo, via _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 117; (f) Radus’ service began when Gaspar Querini commanded the trireme, which was subsequently taken over by Grimani; (g) Demetrio was not present, and in his stead his brother Nicolaus received the payment; (h) Georgius was not present, and in his stead his brother Damianus received the payment. 

- While this is a singular document among the notarised deeds from 16[th] century Zadar I surveyed for this study, there is, of course, much more than meets the eye. First, as regards the Serenissima’s navy, for the period from 1540 through 1569, the extant notarial records mention the ship type to have been a trireme 162 times while a bireme was recorded 62 times. I compiled these data from 6,425 individual notarial deeds recorded during this period with the identifier _sopracomes_ , or galley commander, appearing as either constituent or recipient party in the deeds.[20] 

Second, as regards the crews, Venice’s galleys were typically manned by sailors and oarsmen, so-called _galeotti_ , of Albanian, Dalmatian and Greek origins; and while there was a tradition of paying freemen to row, the 16[th] century witnessed the rise of warships whose crews consisted of either convicts and/or slaves, which introduces the notion of involuntary (military) migration on top of the confounders mentioned above. At the same time, the conscription of able-bodied freemen ( _uomini da fatto_ ), prone to desertion upon partial advance payment for their service, became less and less important from around 1550 onwards. We note, moreover, that Zadarbased notaries also composed 31 substitution contracts between an already-serving oarsman and a third party who, in exchange for a small extra payment ( _zonta_ ), replaced the former.[21] During the next major Ottoman war over Cyprus (15701573), Venice contributed the most warships at Lepanto, but the Serenissima’s manpower was insufficient to man all these vessels. This problem was resolved by taking aboard troops in the service of the Spanish monarchy, which introduces yet

another (confounding) variable into the already confusing discussion of foreigners and (involuntary) military migrations. Even so, most Venetian galleys would not _stato da_ have been operable during the conflict if not for Venice’s subjects from the _mar_ : freemen, convicts and slaves alike.[22] 

Third, consideration thereof points at another hitherto overlooked aspect: the frequent use of third parties to conduct business in the name of someone else. As regards Tommaso Venier’s bireme, this occurred in the cases of “Demetrio Persalondi” and “Georgius de Lustiza, son of the late Vuchichi”, who were both absent. In their stead, their brothers “Nicolaus” and “Damianus”, respectively, appeared in front of Tommaso Venier and received payment “in the name of [their] brother[s]”, once more pointing to the importance of the procura-related analyses undertaken in Chapters 2 and 3.[23] 

Returning once again to the Venier brothers, we learn a bit more about the composition of the crews of Venetian warships through their roles as commander of a galley about a decade later. In this capacity, Stephanus issued a quitclaim to this ship’s wainwright in late November 1552: “master carpenter Iseppus, son of the late Bernardinus de Venetijs”, confirmed the receipt of 500 libras, not for services rendered on board the ship but because he had married Stephanus’s maid, “Catherina”. Her dowry was composed of movable goods worth 407 libras. The rest was paid in cash. In addition, the instrument also mentions “ser Fantinus, son of Johannes de Venetijs, cannoneer [ _bombarderius_ ] aboard the same trireme”, the galley’s artillerist.[24]

The latter’s importance grew over the course of the 16[th] and 17[th] centuries as a consequence of the increasing militarisation of the frontier areas of the _stato da mar_ .[25] The procuratorial appointments involving military personnel reveal the problematic payment practices of Venice.[26] For instance, in early 1560 Zadar’s castellan, “the magnificent Aloysius

Memo [Memmo]”, appointed “the acclaimed Bernardinus Contareno… provisor generalis [ _provveditore generale_ ] in Dalmatia” in absentia to represent him in Venice. The task was simple, as well as a testament to Venice’s rather widespread payment behaviour: Bernardinus was to obtain “from the magnificent office of the chamberlain of the Republic of Venice…all and whatever quantity of money that the [castellan] claimed he was supposed to have in regards to an increase of his salary”.[27] As several comparable examples I found among the notarised deeds show, Aloysius’ case was by no means a singular matter; in fact, such appointments, while once more underscoring the importance of the analysis undertaken in Chapters 2 and 3, occurred frequently enough to warrant consideration, as the example of “Xacman Gleglieuaz and Petrus Naycinouich” indicate: between them, they sent the same procurator to Venice on at least three different occasions to obtain all outstanding payment between 1543 and 1552 – and in all three cases, that man was “the acclaimed patrician Petrus Vallaresso”, son of the former count of Zadar, Zaccaria Vallaresso.[28] And while Petrus’ exact role within the fabric of the Venetian administration at that time remains unclear, he was appointed to carry out exactly the same task for two other soldiers, “Petrus Clada, captain of the stradioti deputised to guard Trogir”, and “the brave Hector Renessi, captain of the stradioti stationed in Zadar”. In both cases, Vallaresso was tasked with obtaining outstanding payments from their previous posting “from the chamber of Kefalonia”.[29]

Soon after his arrival in Dalmatia, he found that rising costs were not just caused by the increasing military presence and the upkeep and expansion of fortifications.[30] Much to his dismay, he discovered that large expenditures went toward Ottoman subjects who visited and negotiated with the Venetian administration in Zadar. They ate and drank away a small fortune without ever returning the favour.[31] To increase

of months after his arrival in Dalmatia. He convened a meeting in his audience chamber and laid out his plan. Two prominent Zadar residents, “the nobleman meser Gieronimo Grisogono and the citizen meser Zanantonio Rossetto”, were tasked with a specific and potentially dangerous assignment. Because the city’s provisions of corn ( _biave_ )[32] had dropped considerably, Pisani gave them “four hundred and thirty ducats…in specie” and sent them forth to resupply the dwindling stocks. Because war was raging elsewhere – the emperor’s requisitions are cited explicitly – supplies were in high demand. Pisani came up with a rather ingenious plan: he gave Gieronimo and Zanantonio an additional 290 ducats “to obtain these in grains and other foodstuffs in Turkish territory so as to benefit this city and its jurisdiction [per investire quelli in formenti et altre biave nelle parti di Turchia al benefficio si di questa città come del Suo territorio]”, specifically earmarked to acquire foodstuffs from subjects of Venice’s potential future adversary.[33] Despite renewed Ottoman attacks on the Knights Hospitaller in 1565, Gieronimo and Zanantonio, “desputised to provide foodstuff for 250 of this city”, were at least partially successful: they came back with supplies and even returned 465 libras and 9 soldi to the communal treasury, as evidenced by a quitclaim notarised in summer 1565.[34] 

## 2. _Zadar’s elite citizens_ 

Referred to as “fedelissimo” by Paulo Giustiniano, the most renowned individuals of non-noble descent were “un Simon Bertonichio, il capitanio Peregrin de Marco, Francesco de Ventura, Zuan Rimondin, Hierolimo di Lorenzi et altri simili”.[35] We have already encountered them, albeit under their Latin(ised) names, throughout the previous pages of this study: Simon Bertonich, Peregrinus de Marco, Franciscus Venturinus, Johannes Raimundinus, Hieronymus de Laurentijs and a few others. The following discussion, based on information gleaned from procuratorial appointments and property transactions, examines this social class and its activities in more detail.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

210 

While the notarial instruments do not yield information about Simon Bertonichio, the situation is markedly different for “the captain Peregrin de Marco”, who commanded a small cavalry detachment of five mounted men, which earned him the designation as “captain of the Croats”.[36] Except for two sisters, Francischina and Ursia,[37] who lived behind the walls of the Benedictine nunnery of St Catherine’s, the base of the de Marco family was the fortified village of Turanj, located about 21.5 kilometres southeast of Zadar. Around the mid-16[th] century, two families bearing the de Marco surname can be identified. One of these was Peregrin’s family, which centred around Peregrin as soldier, citizen and resident of Zadar. Like many other soldiers in the service of the Republic of Venice, he had been on duty elsewhere before he made his home in central Dalmatia, and he had to deal with the unreliable payment practices of his employer. In a procura from the early 1540s, Peregrin was “intervening in his own name, and in the name of his mother, the lady Anzola, and of all his other absent brothers and sisters”. On behalf of himself and his relatives, he appointed a fellow soldier, “ser Dominicus de Tervisio [Tarvisio], a mercenary soldier [stipendiatum] guarding the public square”, to collect the outstanding payments “from the chamber of Krk”.[38] More than a decade later, Peregrin appeared in another procura, which tied him to “ser Simeon Britanicus, citizen and public interpreter [ _interpres publicus_ ]”. Simeon had lost a legal feud against Peregrin and, believing in his chances in an appeal, formally appointed “the Reverend Petrus, his son, the primicerius of Zadar”, to travel to Venice and present the case in front of the Quarantia. The core of the issue was that Peregrin had successfully appealed against a previous sentence, hence Simeon sent his son to reverse the outcome.[39] 

Both de Marco families owned considerable amounts of real estate within Zadar’s jurisdiction. Peregrin and his brothers appear in the records seven times conceding parts of their property to colonists. They owned around 18.5 hectares near the villages of Nin, Turanj, Sv Filip i Jakov, Kožino and Gaženica (some of the land may have been rented by them and contracted to third parties).[40]

The other de Marco family, led by “ser Martinus de Marco alias Mestrouich”, a merchant and citizen of Zadar, possessed real estate within Zadar’s jurisdiction too (1.5 sors or 48 morgen near Nin).[41] Martinus also possessed property closer his hometown, but the instruments are silent on the exact nature of the ties between these two families who bore the same surname.[42] In fact, in one contract both Peregrin and Martinus appear as constituents, but without reference to familial relationship.[43] 

By virtue of marital alliances, the de Marco family was related to at least two other non-noble families of comparable social status. In autumn 1559, “Franciscus de Ventura, son of the late Petrus, a citizen of Zadar, and Simon de Marco”, Peregrin’s brother, met “in the residence of outstanding doctor of both laws Pasinus de Pasinis”, located in the parish of St Simeon. The reason was that Franciscus or “Francesco de Ventura” (as he is called in Antonio Diedo’s _relazione_ )[44] promised to Simon the hand of his daughter, “the honest lady Gasparina, his legitimate and natural daughter”. Together with Gasparina’s hand, Simon was to receive a stately dowry of 700 ducats, of which 550 ducats came to the bride directly from her father. The remaining 150 ducats were “a bequest to Franciscus as per the testament of the late Laurentius de Ventura alias a Putheo, son of the late Georgius, once a citizen of Venice”.[45] Of the total sum of 700 ducats, 250 ducats were to be paid in cash, with the remainder to be transferred in annual instalments of 25 ducats and movable goods.[46] A couple of years after

the marriage, Simon issued a formal quitclaim and exonerated his father-in-law of the entire debt.[47] 

In addition to their ties with other families of non-noble yet elevated status,[48] the de Marcos were possessed of sufficient prestige to enable Simon’s sister, “honest lady Laura”, to marry upwards: Peregrin, who stipulated on behalf of his siblings, managed to breach the social boundaries separating Zadar’s noblemen from the city’s commoners. Laura’s husband, “Antonius de Begna alias Grascich, son of the late Damyanus”, was a nobleman from Zadar. She, too, received a comparably large dowry worth 600 ducats from Peregrin, as well as 50 additional ducats “from the late lady Matthea, Pellegrino’s first wife”. We note, in passing, that Laura’s dowry compared quite favourably to the dowries promised – and paid out – by the local noble families discussed above.[49] 

These non-noble families were instrumental in the functioning of the Venetian administration. Zadar’s “elite citizens” (James Grubb) and their families participated in local defence, trade, public administration and other essential services. For instance, “Paulus de Pasinis” was a citizen and merchant of Zadar, and his relative, “Pasinus de Pasinis”, was a doctor of both laws, canon and civil ( _leges utriusque doctoris_ ), hence a member of the city’s intellectual elite.[50] Another prominent elite non-noble, “Hieronymus de Laurentijs” – known to Venice’s legates as “Hierolimo di Lorenzi”[51] – belonged to this particular socio-occupational group too (see also Tables 2.8 and 2.9).[52] He was related by marriage to another non-noble family of considerable social status: in the mid-1540s, he married the daughter of “the renowned Simon Britanicus”, a citizen and “interpres publicus” of Zadar (whom we met before discussing the de Marco family).[53] Together with the hand of

Zadar’s Elite Residents and Citizens 

213 

“Francischina, daughter of said Simon”, Hieronymus received a dowry worth 450 ducats, which was fully paid by his father-in-law in late autumn 1547.[54] Hieronymus, it shall be noted, had at least one daughter, “Helysabeth”, who married “Georgius de Aymila, a nobleman of Brač [Abrachiae]”. Again, an important and comparatively wealthy commoner managed to have his daughter marry upward, transcending the social boundaries (although it is likely that Helysabeth’s comparatively high dowry of 500 ducats played a role, too).[55] 

Zadar’s “elite citizens” further included individuals like Franciscus Justus or de Justis, the fiscal chamber’s scribe ( _scriba camerae fiscalis_ ),[56] and the two attorneys Franciscus Petrouich and Hieronymus de Bassano (see also Chapter 2). Their activities and ties to other families of both noble and non-noble descent mirror the patterns described above. For instance, notary Petrus de Bassano, and brother of attorney Hieronymus, paid his niece’s dowry of 200 ducats: “lady Maria, his niece from his late brother Michael” (another brother of both Petrus and Hieronymus), was the legitimate wife of “Johannes Segotich, a nobleman of Nin”.[57] Speaking of Zadar’s notaries, a second such example is that of Marcus Aurelius Sonzonius, a notary and barrister of Zadar, whose daughter was married to “Andreas Postner, a merchant from Ljubljana”. According to the marriage contract written in Crete, Postner received a dowry of 500 ducats.[58] 

In the end, it must be left to future scholars to elaborate on questions such as whether cities in the _stato da mar_ enacted legislation similar to that of Venice proper that formalised the existence of and regulated this particular group of non-noble elites.[59] In discussing the ties and activities of Zadar’s “elite citizens”, though, we can observe that the social and spatial boundaries may have been less rigid in the _stato da mar_ compared to Venice, if Erin Maglaque’s recent work is any indication: a mobility cutting, apparently, both ways. But while her study focused on the downward trajectories of two “marginal” patricians, these pages about Zadar’s “elite citizens” speak of the opposite direction.[60]

## 3. _Croats and Jews_ 

As shown in the procuratorial analysis (Chapters 2 and 3), exchange between the coastal communities and the wider hinterland of the western Balkans was limited. This is reflected in the absence of reports or directives of Venice’s civil and military servants. Nevertheless, every now and then Croats appear in the protocols of Zadar’s notaries. 

One example involves, again, Franciscus de Ventura, who appears in an instrument from the mid-1560s. Earlier, he had partnered with “Bernardus Michaglieuich from Croatia [de partibus Croatiae]…acting as procurator for the lady Helena, daughter of the late count Georgius Bencouich de Plauno [Plavno]”. Together they surveyed a document drawn up by the “Reverend chapter of Zagreb, written and ratified according to the mores of that place or chapter”. Prior to the event, Franciscus had leased the usufruct rights “to the forested area near the village of Bahelizze” to Helena in exchange for the payment of 297 libras and 12 soldi. Now that Bernardus had paid the landlord, Franciscus issued a quitclaim and exonerated the tenant of her debt.[61] 

Contacts like these, while relatively rare, were not completely unknown and occurred in a variety of circumstances. This is exemplified by the presence of the members of the Mogorichia (Mogorić) family in Zadar’s St Catherine nunnery (see also Chapter 3, specifically Table 3.2). As for the relatives of sister Helena, in the spring of 1565 three more names appear in the notarial records: “count Georgius Mogorich, son of the late count Martin, count Thomas Mogorich, son of the late count Johannes, and count Nicolaus Mogorich, son of the late count Franciscus, uncle [ _patruus_ ] and nephews, residents of Croatia from a place by the name of Bosiglieuo [Bosiljevo]”.[62] The three men, possibly involved with the Venetian military, owned property in Zadar’s jurisdiction, which they leased to “Simon Mazzarello, nobleman of Trogir and chancellor of the magnificent commune”. In all, they transferred three sors, or approximately 7-7.5 hectares, to the communal chancellor.[63] The properties were located near the villages of “Radohouo [and] Reiane [Režane]”, as well as in an otherwise unidentified place by the name of “Franulschina”. In exchange for an annual rent of 26 ducats, Simon was granted usufruct rights. In addition, the contract included a reference to the location of the properties. Režane and probably also the other two plots were located close to the Ottoman-Venetian border, hence the following clause was included: “if…within said term of three years any damage should occurs, whether due to plague, war or Turkish incursions, hailstorm or weather damage, which God shall avert”, then the landlords were liable for damages assessed by jointly appointed evaluators.[64] 

61. HR DAZD 31 BZ, Daniel Cavalca, I, 2, 6, s.p., 18 September 1564. 

62. There are two towns bearing this name in Croatia, one in Karlovac County (Karlovačka županija) and the other in Bjelovar-Bilogora county (Bjelovarsko-bilogorska županija), making identification difficult. Since Bosiljevo in Karlovac County is much closer to the Dalmatian coast, it may be the likelier place of origin of the Mogorichia family.

Zadar’s Elite Residents and Citizens 

215 

soldiers who served in the various military units originated from the hinterlands. Only rarely, though, do these soldiers appear in the notarial instruments, usually appointing procurators to retrieve outstanding payment for their service (see also Chapter 2). Occasionally, individuals acted on their own behalf. In summer 1540, two soldiers, “Matheus Liuaza and Bilulus Sbizich, members of the company of the valiant Nicolaus Tetricj, nobleman of Zadar and captain of the Croat horse”, sold two slaves to “Georgio, son of the late Paulus Marizieuich, a resident of Castro Ariolo in the jurisdiction of Lanzani [Lanciano]”. The two individuals for sale, referred to as “Turkish captives [Captivos turchas]”, were boys “by the name of Balia, aged about ten, and the other’s true name is Schenderbeg, aged about six”. They had been captured near the fortified village of Ražanac and were sold for the combined price of 30 ducats, which the buyer paid in specie.[65] 

Most of the military units appearing in the notarial records were composed of Croats, and most of their members remain unnamed. Individual soldiers are identified only with explicit reference to their unit commander, who might be a nobleman of Zadar like Nicolaus Tetricus, a member of the Mogorichia family or an individual from the hinterlands of the western Balkans like “Johannes Rimanich, captain of the Croats from Sliuniza [Slivnica]”. In spring 1553, “Marcus Jelacich de Varicassane and Jacobus Fogusich de Sliuniza, acting as guardians of the young, underage sons of Paulus”, sold three-quarters of a morgen (about 1,770.5 square metres) to “the valiant Johannes Rimanich”. The plot of land was located near the village of Slivnica, “adjacent to the property of the magnificent lords of the House of Venier [de cha Venerio]”, and the captain paid 31 libras and 4 soldi for the property.[66] 

As for those of the Jewish faith, their numbers were very small, as indicated by their rare appearances in the notarial documents.[67] Only 18 individuals appear in the books of Zadar’s notaries during the period under survey.[68] Their small _Statuta_ numbers are also reflected in the absence of references to Jews in the _Iadertina_ and the _Commissiones et Relationes Venetae_ . It is not known whether the Jews of Zadar were expelled in 1568[69] or what their situation was during the Cyprus War, but the post-war efforts of Daniel Rodriga may have proved favourable for Jews in Zadar, as they were in Split.[70] While the Portuguese (Ponentine) Jew Rodriga is perhaps by far the most well-known protagonist –

mainly due to his role in the creation of the free harbour ( _scala_ ) of Split after the Cyprus War – most of the available literature simply “recycles” his information, including Benjamin Ravid’s recent synthesis that posits that his lobbying of the Venetian government “commenc[ed] in 1573”.[71] Rodriga’s presence in Dalmatia is typically dated to after the Cyprus War, but I found a notarial deed from 1568 that refers to him as “console dilla Nation hebrea in Narenta”.[72] Renzo Paci, author of the essential study _La “Scala” di Spalato_ , which appeared over fifty years ago, dated Rodriga’s first documented appearance in the Adriatic to the year 1573 (this is the date reproduced ever since), stating that Rodriga himself had “perhaps suggested creation of a free harbour to the sanjakbey”, and the notarial deed preserved in the Croatian State Archive in Zadar provides prima facie evidence for Paci’s consideration: Rodriga was already established in Dalmatia before the outbreak of the Cyprus War, albeit not on the Venetian side of the border.[73] 

The following pages, by contrast, examine the Jewish population of Zadar and, more specifically, the importance of kinship relations. This analysis is based on pioneering scholarship on the Jews of Venice and recent comparative studies.[74] One case involves two Jewish families of ambiguous ties. In winter 1567 “the honest lady Laura, legitimate and natural daughter of ser Melis Zizo, a Hebrew and resident of Zadar”, formally renounced any future claims on parts of her father’s patrimony ( _refutatio bonorum_ ) in exchange for a dowry.[75] About two weeks later, the marriage contract was drawn up “between the excellent medical doctor, the Hebrew Salvator Alfari and lady Laura, daughter of the Hebrew ser Mellis Zizo” (unlike the above-discussed marriage contracts between Zadar’s Christians – which typically involved the groom and a male relative of the bride – this contract is different insofar as the bride and groom are named the constituent parties). It also contains clauses pertaining to the dowry, written in Hebrew (“written in Hebrew letters by Jacob Sassi, a resident of Venice and attested to by two witnesses”). In line with expected practices, Laura’s husband formally issued a quitclaim confirming the receipt of 150 ducats in cash and 200 ducats in

movable goods to exonerate his father-in-law of all debts. In addition, Salvator Alfari promised his wife a counter-dowry of 175 ducats.[76] 

A quarter-century earlier, Mele Zizo, Laura’s father, had married “the lady Preciosa, daughter of the lady Stella Marcilia, wife of the late ser Rafael Belinfante”, in the Apulian coastal town of Monopoli “in the Hebrew tradition [ad costume Ebraico]” as early as 1532 or 1533.[77] By the time of their daughter’s marriage, the couple lived in a house in Zadar’s parish of St Simeon that belonged to the Crissana family.[78] Their son “Salamon” had married half a decade earlier, and the surviving dowry quitclaims indicate that despite their relocation across the Adriatic, the ties to their former hometown of Ancona persisted. Written “nella città d’Ancona, uno in Hebreo et l’altro in lingua Latina”, further details about the kinship ties emerge: in spring 1562, Salamon had married “the lady Perla, the daughter of the late Hebrew Mele Belinfante”, with the bride bringing with her a dowry of 220 ducats in both “denari et robbe”. She received a counter-dowry worth 50 ducats. Their fortune did not last long. Salamon died within a couple of years, and his father acted as Perla’s counterpart and “gubernator di detti pupillj et bonatenente del detto quondam Salamon suo figlio”.[79] Another instrument, written two decades earlier, revealed something else: Perla’s father, Mele Belinfante, was the brother of Preciosa, wife of Mele Zizo and mother of Salamon. Mele Zizo and Mele Belinfante were brothers-in-law whose children married each other, testifying to high levels of endogamy practiced by comparatively small Jewish communities (bordering on incestuous relations).[80] 

The few resident Jews were involved in several business deals with other Zadar inhabitants. Mele Zizo, for instance, was a banker and moneylender.[81] Two other Jews, “ser Abramus Vigleta, son of the late Isach de Alexandria, and Juda, or Leonus Gomiel, son of the late Joseph of the same place”, had deposited 400 ducats, which Mele returned in his town of residence, Zadar, “partially in gold, and in part coins”, in the mid-1540s.[82] A decade later, the banking endeavours of

Urban Elites of Zadar 

218 

Mele Zizo attracted more attention. Based on a sentence issued by “the doctor of both laws Petro Fanfoneo”, Mele Zizo was required to make restitution to the sum of 6,504 libras and 14 soldi – about 1,050 ducats – to “the lady Regina, wife of the late Hebrew ser Gabrielis Bellinfante, acting as mother, tutor and legitimate guardian of the young children of the late ser Gabrielis, with ser Melle de Ariano and ser Mahir Cohen, both Hebrews, acting as co-guardians”. Upon receipt of the money, the guardians issued a joint quitclaim and formally acknowledged the end of the (otherwise unexplained) feud and absolved Mele Zizo from all further liabilities “pertaining to either his banco in Zadar, or his business facility [ _apotheca_ ] he owns in Apulia”.[83] 

Among the Jews of Zadar, however, one other individual stood out: “ser Mayr Choen, a Spanish Hebrew and now a resident of Zadar”. He is first mentioned in the second half of the 1540s. Not much is known about him prior to his relocation to Dalmatia, other than his Sephardic origins. Mayr Choen (or Mahir Cohen) appeared in two other instruments: in the first instance, he lent the sum of 105 scudi (about 115 ducats) to “ser Jacobus Bono, captain of a marciliana, citizen and resident of Zadar”.[84] In the second instrument, written on the same day after the credit payment had been agreed upon, the same two individuals formed a “societas”[85] in which each party contributed 105 scudi. Jacobus also obliged himself “to practice the art of commerce, both in buying the merchandise of any kind and condition, at his own pleasure, and according to [his] own best judgement”. If all went well, Jacobus was to receive two-thirds of the profits, and in case the mercantile endeavours failed, the losses were to be divided equally. Mayr Choen stood to profit anyway as he had provided the entire capital. Jacobus was already in his partner’s debt for the former’s share of the capital and, if things went well, stood to gain only 16% of the potential profits. Conversely, if things were to go awry, equal division of the capital ensured that Mayr was to lose only what he would have lost anyway. Jacobus, on the other hand, bore not only the risk to his life but also his ship and all tangible assets and would still have had to compensate Mayr.[86] 

in the early modern Mediterranean is documented in an instrument from early 1562. [Falier], son of the acclaimed Lucas, patrician of Venice”, their procurator. To be on the safe side, the notary added that the constituent party acted “in the presence and with the consent of Curtius, son of the valiant Johannes de Suave, captain of the soldiers guarding the fort of Novigradi [Novigrad] in the jurisdiction of Zadar, [Margarita’s] husband”. Marcus Falier was to obtain “her share and part of the goods of the late lady Milia, her sister”, as well as “all and any amount of money that is hers deposited in the Venetian Ghetto in the bank of the sons of the Hebrew Council”.[87] While this deed did not contain information about the sum of money, I found a second instrument in the books of Simon Mazzarellus two years later in which Johannes de Suave confirmed the receipt of 50 ducats and 12 grossi from Marcus Falier.[88] 

These examples illustrate the interconnectedness and spectrum of activities of late mediaeval and early modern Mediterranean cities and their inhabitants. Zadar, located in central Dalmatia, and its inhabitants were connected to Venice, Padua and Alexandria, and with Ancona, Novigrad and the hinterlands of the western Balkans. Married Venetian civil or military officials may have brought their wives and, possibly, children with them to their assigned posts, which have thus far received little scholarly attention.[89] In the final pages of this study, we shall enquire not so much about these extra-Dalmatian ties but investigate the spatial distribution of select members of Zadar’s urban elites within the built environment of the city.

<!-- page 222 -->

## 8. Topographies of Everyday Life and Zadar’s Cityscape 

Mediterranean history is in large part a history of diasporas, of floating, mobile populations, often from “outside”, in which it is perhaps more difficult (though not impossible) to establish a microhistorical viewpoint than when dealing with the social landscape of an _ancien régime_ village or a nineteenth-century working-class community.[1] 

Colin Heywood 

The pages of this study are teeming with 16[th] -century people, men and women alike, and one of the chief challenges for the historian of any age is to render any number of individual case studies into something approximating a coherent analysis. Over the past half-century, one of the core challenges was (is) how to combine the study of _structures_ (status groups, classes) with any of the individuals cited as examples. Both aspects have, of course, been intensively studied by scholars focusing on diaspora groups and minorities, religious or otherwise, urban societies, and those historians interested in trade and epistolary networks. There can be little, if any, doubt that their study in the Mediterranean – and, arguably, also well beyond – is influenced in one or the other way by the legacy of Fernand Braudel.[2] 

Several decades have come and gone since, and the Braudelian challenges have kept at least one or two generations of scholars across many disciplines quite busy. In the past generation alone, the advent of cultural and perceptual turns has further contributed to the diversification of themes and approaches, and not all of them bear much relation to each other.[3] We note, in passing, that “few if any of the field’s practitioners have made use of microhistory as a technique”, as Colin Heywood noted little over a decade ago, and this state of affairs is as good a point of departure as any for the final segment of this study.[4] Taking recourse to several such episodes drawn from the rich evidence preserved by Zadar’s notaries, I shall seek to outline, in as much detail as possible, the cityscape as it appeared to several protagonists on both an individual level as well as in the aggregate. 

> 1. Heywood, “Microhistory/Maritime History”, p. 84 (emphasis in the original). 

> 2. Purcell, “The Boundless Sea”; Heyberger, “La frontière méditerranéenne”; Lee, “Lessons”. 

> 3. Follow the trajectory of these trends via Horden, Purcell, “The Mediterranean”; the essays in _Braudel Revisited_ ; Fusaro, “After Braudel”. 

> 4. Heywood, “Microhistory/Maritime History”, p. 84.

<!-- page 223 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

222 

My foray into the spatial disposition of actors, places and their relationships is twofold: on a primary plane, such enquiries relate to the main themes of the Braudelian _Méditerranée_ , in particular to the tendency of contemporaries – and many later scholars – to consider the early modern period in terms of virtually fixed and comparatively unchanging categories. These (mis)conceptions, however, stand in stark contrast to “the underlying realit[ies] of economic, social, and geographical fluidity”, as John Martin and Dennis Romano noted.[5] On a secondary plane, the incredible information density arising from more than fifteen years of research into the protocol books of Zadar’s notaries – which have resulted in the creation of a still-growing body of entries in spreadsheets that contain a wealth of data points – is highly promising as it permits the reconstruction of many points of connectivity between individuals, their relatives, places and events that would otherwise escape the historian’s notice. In other words, the cross-referencing of a select number of individuals leads well “beyond the individual” and might, so I hope, inspire other researchers to attempt comparable reconstructions.[6] 

Anyone seeking to conduct such an undertaking must confront the sizeable literature about one or the other diasporic community and their similarly large, and still growing, literature on travelling objects.[7] Certainly, microhistorical accounts of any individual and/or artefact come with their own limitations, yet these may, to a certain extent, be dealt with by taking recourse to approaches associated with the “history from below”, in particular if such findings may be aggregated. What Alf Lüdtke, Lutz Niethammer and others call _Alltagsgeschichte_ , or the history of everyday life, does hold one of the more promising solutions to overcome these legitimate limitations. Thus, the following pages seek to put individuals, their activities and connections on a “map”, and thus permit the historian to explore several otherwise hidden opportunities that come to the fore “by attempting to classify the life-history of individuals within a particular social and historical context”.[8] In these following pages, we shall add a spatial component by collating the available archival evidence centred on one or the other individual. Hence it becomes possible to reconstruct what I conceive of as “topographies of everyday life”, by which is meant a “cartographic” reconstruction uniting spatial and temporal dimensions, the former relating to synchronic aspects and the latter alluding to diachronic characteristics. Their combination enables a more comprehensive reconstruction than either set of circumstances would do on their own.[9] Thus emerges the possibility to survey, analyse and map, across time and space, the ties that bound our forebears together. 

> 5. Martin, Romano, “Reconsidering Venice”, p. 21 (my modification). 

> 6. I have begun to explore these issues in my “Alltagstopographien”, pp. 62-69, and “Beyond the Individual”, pp. 136-142. 

> 7. On the former, now see the essays in Christ, Morche, Zaugg, Kaiser, Burkhardt, Beihammer, _Union in Separation_ . On the “material culture of connections”, see the essays in Gerritsen, Riello, _Writing Material Culture History_ and their _The Global Lives of Things_ . All three titles are understood as _pars pro toto_ . 

> 8. Niethammer, _Posthistoire_ , p. 149. 

9. See my “Alltagstopographien”, pp. 62-65. For the metaphor, cf. also McKee, “Women”, p. 35.

<!-- page 224 -->

Topographies of Everyday Life 

223 

## 1. _Microhistory,_ Alltagsgeschichte _and topographies of everyday life_ 

Among the many wonderful microhistorical accounts detailing the lives and times of early modern Europeans, few compare to either Carlo Ginzburg’s _The Cheese and the Worms_ and Fernand Braudel’s _Mediterranean_ , in particular as regards their impact beyond the field of history.[10] Despite the wealth of documentation, we note that the former’s approach especially constituted “essentially a historiographic practice” without an “established body of orthodoxy to draw on”, as Giovanni Levi maintained over thirty years ago, though much has changed since then.[11] Surveying the historiographic landscape of the past generation, microhistory has certainly come of age, yet this assessment does not signify that one should simply stop to try out new ideas; in fact, if the trajectories of its primary practitioners are any indication, it is a call to build, expand upon and seek to move beyond established considerations introduced in the past decades.[12] 

Most studies of the early modern Mediterranean focus on its most prominent aspects and distinct inhabitants across a select number of bigger or more important cities; of these, the focus has been especially on merchant communities and confessional diaspora communities. A second strand of research focuses on history’s “bigger men”, and some similar women. What unites most of these studies is that they have been written from the perspective of the centre and/or ascribe certain qualities to one or the other location or group.[13] I maintain that more “qualitative” work needs to be conducted, in particular in places that are more remote from the focus of these studies; there is a need to look for “ _ordinary_ people”, their seemingly mundane activities, their comparatively unimportant (family) relations and business associates, as well as their movable and immovable goods.[14] All of these in a way “anchor” these past lives in time and space, as well as render them intelligible to the historian.[15] The evidentiary basis of the present work is very appropriate to study neither the structures governing Venice’s overseas possessions nor its most prominent protagonists; rather, the seemingly random and scattered evidence of the resident population takes the centre-stage. 

How does one analyse local, if not localistic, events like the same small patch of land in 1542, the temporary concession of usufruct rights to a local merchant in 1549 or the appointment of a cleric as a family’s tutor? To move beyond the antiquarian- 

10. Amelang, “The Cheese and the Worms”, pp. 31-32. 

11. Levi, “On Microhistory”, p. 93. 

12. E.g., Jacobson Schutte, “Carlo Ginzburg”; Molho, “Carlo Ginzburg”; Muir, “Observing Trifles”; and the comments by the participants of the roundtable on the 25[th] anniversary of _The Cheese and the Worms_ , published as “Il formaggio e i vermi venticinque anni dopo”. 

13. Within the context of the present study, see e.g. the essays in Ducellier, _Les chemins de l’exile_ ; Imhaus, _Le minoranze orientali_ ; the essays in Balard, _Migrations et diasporas_ , in particular the contributions by Bernard Doumerc and Thierry Ganchou; Petta, _Despoti_ . Of more recent vintage are, e.g., Gelder, _Trading Places_ ; Burke, _The Greeks of Venice_ ; and the survey by Ravid, “Venice and its Minorities”. 

14. Cf. Lewis, _Weight of Glory_ , p. 46 (emphasis in the original): “there are no _ordinary_ people”, which rhymes well with the original impetus behind the key practitioners of microhistory. 15. Cf. Heywood, “Microhistory/Maritime History”, p. 85.

<!-- page 225 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

224 

positivistic impetus of simply collecting and listing such snippets of information, the combination of microhistory and the history of everyday life ( _Alltagsgeschichte_ ) offers a fruitful avenue. In fact, doing so permits the historian to study with sense and purpose what often appears “from above” like a bewilderingly heterogeneous and somewhat eclectic collection of mundane, if not trivial, activities. Having collated information gleaned from over 6,000 individual notarised deeds and several hundred testaments, I am able to “zoom in” and “out” nearly at will, which permits the investigation of individual cases and their aggregation – and this points squarely beyond simple, if not simplistic, top-down, one-point and normative approaches. Cognisant of the often merely implicit linguistic dimensions – most of early modern Dalmatia’s residents spoke “Slavic” and “Venetian”, and while the latter was used as vehicular language (and thus more prevalent among the elites) – we note that the everyday life of “ _ordinary_ people” was characterised, first and foremost, by situational multilingualism and a correspondingly wide range of cultural practices; neither the normative nor the shifting territorial boundaries reflected clear-cut distinctions, and neither did social status and/or class affiliation between locals (nobles, citizens and residents) and “foreigners” (technically everyone who did not enjoy citizenship rights), or between Christians and their non-Christian neighbours.[16] 

To tackle the analytical and epistemological challenges, I am using the term “topographies of everyday life” to adequately reflect on the embeddedness of individuals and institutions in their respective communicative, cultural, economic, linguistic, political, religious and social contexts. As these change over time, the concept is used to more fully understand an individual’s roots within, and ties to, his or her social environment by first contextualising the various episodes, aggregating and finally connecting these situational instances.[17] Doing so goes a long way toward revealing many links and shared experiences with differing overlaps that flesh out – and, ideally, point toward hitherto under-explored connections – the nexus of person, spatial and temporal dimensions of _individual_ realities. Moreover, while such an approach relates to current debates on microhistory, the following pages add a strong geographical dimension – a perspective from South-Eastern Europe – to these discussions that has been virtually been excluded from the considerations by Jürgen Schlumbohm, John Brewer and Filippo de Vivo.[18] 

16. The most recent addition to these considerations is by Sadovski, “Die gesellschaftlichen Strukturen”, pp. 333-375.

Topographies of Everyday Life 

225 

## 2. _Topographies of everyday life in 16[th] -century Zadar_ 

In the following discussion, my point of departure was Zadar’s more prominent scions, if only because I was aware of, say, the likes of Bernardinus Carnarutus (Brne Karnarutić) or Federicus Grisogonus (Federik Grisogon) when I began my archival research. Once I compiled my spreadsheets with data from all surviving notarised deeds, it became possible to correlate and cross-reference also other, less prominent individuals. The following pages are thus a systematic foray into the lives and experiences of 16[th] -century individuals. Speaking generally, the Adriatic’s history lends credence to the notion of a specific way of live – dubbed “ _Homo adriaticus_ ” by Sante Graciotti over thirty years ago, to which one of, if not the, leading scholars of our day and age, Egidio Ivetić, pointing also to Sergio Anselmi’s contribution, considered a “state of mind” or “ _forma mentis_ ”.[19] It is in this spirit that the following pages explore the _shared_ and _linked_ histories of a select number of individuals who happened to be in Zadar in the mid-16[th] century and whose range of mind and activities clearly transcended any one place. 

Take, for instance, a _procura_ written at the request of the Zadar-based notary Franciscus Thomaseus (whose protocol books form part of the evidentiary foundation of this study). Of noble descent, albeit from the island of Rab in the Kvarner Gulf, he lived and worked in Zadar leaving three books filled with 258 individual deeds stipulated between 2 October 1548 and 17 January 1561. Let us go back to the appointment, though, which was written in early 1558 and which tells a particular story. Franciscus had been invited to attend a religious ceremony – a patrician’s baptism – in Venice. While this fact alone suggests close ties to certain elite circles, he found himself unable to attend in person. Hence, he turned to two individuals to represent him “at the holy Baptismal font on the occasion of the yet-unborn son or daughter of the much-acclaimed Marcus Antonius Cornelius [Marc’Antonio Corner], a patrician of Venice, and his equally muchacclaimed wife”. Needless to say, the two appointees had to be of social standing commensurate to the occasion, hence the choice fell on “the magnificent Michael Fuscareno [Michele Foscari], son of the glorious Hieronumus [Gerolamo]” and “Christophorus de Nassis”. While the former was a Venetian patrician, the latter was a scion of Zadar’s most prestigious noble families with a long and distinguished record of serving the Most Serene Republic.[20] 

In the preceding pages, I have repeatedly pointed to these _procura_ contracts to gain a more comprehensive understanding of everyday life in the Renaissance Adriatic. As elaborated in Chapters 2 and 3, these deeds often involve the available, educated elites of noble and non-noble descent, and in the following pages, I wish to illustrate this point in yet another way that points well beyond any individual. Among the most important functions of the _procura_ was that it solved the problem of institutional ownership. This is perhaps best illustrated by briefly revisiting the 

in particular. Doing so means the perpetuation of a traditional but increasingly anachronistic divide in the mould of “slavica non leguntur”.

Urban Elites of Zadar 

226 

land grants ( _concessiones_ , _pastinationes_ ) explored in detail in Chapter 4: of the 478 individual deeds conferring usufruct rights to tenants notarised between 1540 and 1569, a total of 134 (about 28% of the total) noted a member of the clergy as property owner; of these, three out of four were _legal_ persons that required representation to act (there were but 33 natural persons, as per Table 4.6). Virtually the same issue appears in the rental arrangements ( _locationes_ ), of which 226 individual deeds were stipulated during the same period: of these, 61 deeds list an ecclesiastical property owner (circa 27% of the total), yet only eight of these instruments were written at the request of a natural person (Table 4.10). 

These structural factors – _conjoncture_ s, as Fernand Braudel would have it[21] – shaped economic and social life in post-mediaeval, preindustrial Europe, and their implications with respect to the connections of individuals and such institutions is very well worth exploring in detail. While Zadar’s statutes, compiled and printed in 1563/1564, offer a detailed framework about such appointments – procurators had to be at least twenty years old, and male offspring could represent anyone with fatherly consent – it is noteworthy that the _Statuta Iadertina_ is silent on what today is called “conflict of interest”.[22] Even the briefest glance at the available evidence (930 individual _procura_ appointments, as detailed in Tables 2.6 and 2.7) immediately reveals that individuals acting on behalf of Zadar’s ecclesiastical institutions frequently conducted their business in front of notaries with whom they were related. Take, for example, the protocol books of Franciscus Thomaseus, which twice note the metropolitan chapter as the constituent party. On one occasion, the chapter was represented by one “Antonius Mirchouich” and on the other occasion by the notary’s brother, Johannes Thomaseus.[23] In a document from the second half of the 1550s, a full listing of the metropolitan chapter’s members is mentioned: “Franciscus de Grisogonis, archpriest, Petrus Britanicus, _primicerius_ [the head of the city’s canons], Johannes Donatus Begna, canon and vicar, Johannes Sichirich, Matthaeus de Marchettis, Antonius Mirchouich, Sanctus de Sanctis and Vincentius de Ventura”. The five individuals listed at the end of this quote were all canons.[24] The noble descent of the scions of the Grisogonus

Topographies of Everyday Life 

227 

and Begna families is clearly visible, while the other listed individuals clearly fell into the category of “elite citizens” (James Grubb) and relate to the relevant pages in Chapter 7: Johannes Thomaseus, Sanctus the Sanctis and Matthaeus de Marchettis were kinsmen of notaries Franciscus Thomaseus, Paulus de Sanctis and Horatius de Marchettis; both the Britanicus and Ventura families were part and parcel of the most important non-noble urban elite of Zadar as listed in the report by Venetian patrician and captain of Zadar Paolo Giustinian in 1553.[25] 

Using the metropolitan chapter as an example, the first aspect to observe is that this legal person acted much like any natural person-as-property owner would. This corporate body administered the archbishopric’s real estate, appointed representatives to deal with issues elsewhere and engaged in a variety of other transactions. In all these dealings, the boundaries between what we today consider conflicts of interest and interpersonal relations were repeatedly crossed. In the mid1540s, for instance, Johannes Mazzarellus, a nobleman of Trogir and chancellor of Zadar, and chapter member Johannes Thomaseus (brother of notary Franciscus Thomaseus) went to notary Paulus de Sanctis (kinsman of chapter member Sanctus de Sanctis) and leased the right to collect “all fruits, revenues and income” deriving from the archbishopric’s properties throughout the city’s jurisdiction. In addition, in exchange for 620 ducats per year, they also acquired, for the duration of three years, the right to collect the Church’s tithes in Zadar’s and neighbouring Pag’s jurisdiction.[26] A month later, the two Johns made the “Rev. Johannes Barbiricich, a priest of Zadar, their partner” and invited him to join their tax-farming enterprise. While both contracts have identical clauses, the price to join this arrangement was a surcharge of 15 ducats per annum, which raised the annual rent to 635 ducats.[27] 

A comparable transaction took place a few years later in spring 1551 when “archdeacon Hieronymus de Cortesijs, Johannes Thomaseus and Paulus de Sanctis junior, all canons of the metropolitan cathedral of St Anastasia”, leased the archdiocese’s property near the hamlet of Miljačka to “Petrus Sterpacnich” and his absent brother, “Marcus”. In so doing, the three canons invoked a “special mandate to act on behalf of the other members of the chapter” and transferred usufruct and cultivation rights for four years to the two tenants. Part of the property was undeveloped, and therefore Petrus and Marcus Sterpacnich were not required to consign special gifts ( _honorantiae_ ).[28] As happened on several other occasions, chapter members conducted their business with a familiar notary, in this case Franciscus Thomaseus (brother of chapter member Johannes).[29] 

The protocol books of canon, humanist and notary Simon Budineus (Šime Budinić) contain another similar transaction from the late 1550s. In it, “Marcus

Loredan, bishop of Nin and procurator generalis” of Zadar’s archbishop Muzio Calino, leases “all and every income of the archbishopric…for three years” – once again to metropolitan chapter member and cleric Johannes Thomaseus and his brother, the notary Franciscus Thomaseus. Like in the above-related rental arrangement, the leasehold contract includes the right to collect tithes in both Zadar’s and neighbouring Pag’s jurisdictions. The clauses of both arrangements were basically identical, with the main difference being the price tag: instead of 620 or 635 ducats per annum, the Thomaseus brothers agreed to pay 800 ducats per year, payable in cash in two instalments, one due on 1 April and the other at the end of July. In exchange, the two tenants were to receive “nine barrels of wine belonging to the archbishopric”, to be returned at the end of the rental period (the reason for this additional clause, however, was not specified).[30] 

The overarching importance of these transactions is that ecclesiastical institutions were _legal_ persons and thus had to conduct their business via empowered representatives. Zadar-based institutions frequently did so, resorting to the services of notaries who were related to their agents, as the above-related examples of the Thomaseus brothers or the de Marchettis[31] and de Sanctis families illustrate.[32] The notary who was most favoured by his fellow clergymen, however, was Simon Budineus: members of the metropolitan chapter approached him on ten separate occasions, and his interlocutors were Franciscus and Johannes Thomaseus,[33] Sanctus de Sanctis,[34] Vincentius de Ventura[35] and the chapter _in toto_ , albeit represented by other members, such as “archdeacon Johannes Barbiricich”.[36] Moreover, Zadar’s archbishop(ric) approached Simon Budineus another six times, with archbishop Muzio Calino appearing three times in person.[37] These were not all very important persons in 16[th] -century Dalmatia who counted among

## 3. _Two case studies: Simon Budineus and Hieronymus Cortesius_ 

The notary most favoured by men and women of the cloth, ecclesiastical leaders like Zadar’s archbishop Muzio Calino and legal institutions alike, was Simon Budineus, born between 1530 and 1535 and known to posterity as Šime Budinić. According to the available biographical sketch by Franjo Švelec, he was the son of Michael Budinich and his wife Clara, daughter of the canon Sanctus de Sanctis (who we met earlier in these pages as a member of the metropolitan chapter). Per the same synthesis, Michael and Clara had two more sons, Stephanus and Antonius, as well as one daughter, Hieronyma. Simon Budineus first appears in the notarial protocols in 1556 when, in October of that year, he signed the first deed he stipulated as “cleric”.[40] In subsequent years, Simon had an impressive number of functions and sinecures (benefices without pastoral duties), as detailed by historians Šime Urlić, Stjepan Antoljak and Ante Strgačić: in 1558, he became rector of the church of Saints Sylvester and Thomas in Zadar; in 1560, he was ordained and celebrated his first mass before receiving the prebendary of Saint Peter of Iž from canon Sanctus de Sanctis. By 1563, Simon had acquired the chaplaincy of Saint Mark in the Suburbs and had been appointed archiepiscopal chancellor. Later that year, as reported by Švelec, Simon engaged in questionable conduct at a funeral and found himself threatened with excommunication but was quickly rehabilitated. Towards the end of his first sojourn in Zadar, in 1565, he went to the synod of Trogir, where the resolutions of the Council of Trent were proclaimed in Dalmatia.[41] 

Thereafter, Budineus went to Rome and began translating CounterReformation doctrine into South-Slavic, and while that much is known (to certain extents and only in very general terms), precious little is known about his formative years. It is generally presumed that he began his education in Zadar before attending university elsewhere, “perhaps in Padua”, noted Švelec in the _Croatian Biographical Lexicon_ , adding that “nothing is known about his early years”.[42] We may, however, derive some facts from Budineus’ later work as a theologian, humanist and translator: it is virtually certain that he was fluent in the _lingua franca_ , as the Venetian vernacular was the administrative, commercial and military vehicular language in the Adriatic.[43] He also certainly knew South-Slavic

> 43. In his 827 notarised deeds, there are four instruments written in Venetian: HR DAZD 31, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 1, cc. 24r-24v, 15 January 1557 m.v. [1558]; I, 1, 2, cc. 72v-73r, 25

<!-- page 232 -->

and, of course, Latin, which was the language of the Catholic Church, humanistic enquiries across the republic of letters and the primary language used by notaries in the Italianate sphere of cultural diffusion.[44] 

Given the absence of archival source materials, circumstantial evidence gathered from the notarial records shows that Simon Budineus’ (alleged) parents, Michael Budinich and his wife Clara, lived in a “house befitting their standing” – Michael was a goldsmith – located “in the Arsenatus parish near Santa Marcella” in the northern corner of Zadar. Theirs was a “casa da statio” with a grand salon on the first floor and two large rooms on the ground floor “che vardano verso a Strata sirocale”, today Zadar’s main boulevard, Široka ulica (Calle Larga). Beyond the portego and a staircase made of stone, there were also a covered courtyard with a well, a kitchen and magazines. We know about these details due to the division of the maternal inheritance between Antonius and Stephanus – and there is no mention of Simon (hence the addition of the term “alleged” above).[45] 

As far as the Budinich family’s activities are concerned, the city’s notaries recorded the following events: in early 1540, Clara and her fellow legal guardian, the lawyer Hieronumus de Cortesius of Rab (“more” about him is found below), appear in the records of Petrus de Bassano. On behalf of her two son Antonius and Stephanus – note again the absence of Simon Budineus – Clara and Hieronymus conceded eight morgens (or close to two hectares) of vineyards with olive trees located “underneath Monte Ferro” near present-day Zerodo (Cerodolo) to “Laurentius and Milichus, known by the name Despot, of Zadar, and Barichius Thoglianouich”, a day-labourer from the nearby village of Kamenjani. In exchange for their labour and consignation of a third of all olives and a quarter of the grape harvest, the three tenants received usufruct rights for three years. Interestingly – and contradicting the available literature by Antoljak and Strgačić (who date Michael Budinich’s appearance in Zadar to 1517, his death to the mid-1530s and refer to him as goldsmith) – Clara’s former husband is referred to as “once a citizen and merchant of Zadar”.[46] In addition to these contractual obligations, the deed notes Antonius as “one of the late ser Michael’s children already of legitimate age”. As per the _Statuta Iadertina_ , boys under the age of 14 were legally required to have at least one legal guardian, and even if we allow for the slight dating discrepancies deriving

from the use of the Venetian calendar,[47] Antonius was likely born in the first half of the 1520s at the earliest, but possibly (even) a few years later; his brother Stephanus was born later (for otherwise there would have been no need for guardians),[48] perhaps in the late 1520s. This reading is supported by another deed notarised in summer 1542 in which both Clara and Hieronymus were again acting “as tutors, they maintained”, selling three morgens of vineyards near the lazaretto to “Franciscus of Rab, a master-cobbler”, for the price of 8 ducats.[49] With the guardianship still active in that year, the birth of Stephanus, son of Michael Budinich and Clara, could have occurred as late as 1528. 

What about Simon Budineus, or Budinich? Here, the notarial records contradict the arguments advanced by the available literature by Stjepan Antoljak, who dated Michael Budinich’s establishment in Zadar to 1517 and held that Stephanus was his older son.[50] In a sales contract from February 1543 (if the date is _more veneto_ , that would be 1544), we learn that Michael Budinich’s wife Clara was also known as “Dobrula sive Clariza” and that she married Michael in 1521, “according to the dowry instrument written by Petrus Franciscus Parisij of Milan, And _that_ instrument – a certified notary of Ragusa, dated 15 February 1521”.[51] which I obtained via the good offices of my friend Nicolò Villanti – corroborates the information related above: “Michael Antonius Budinich of Zadar” – who is listed without any trade or occupation – married “Dobrula, daughter of the late Johannes Nicolaus de Besantis of Ragusio” in Dubrovnik sometime between 4 December 1520 and 15 February 1521.[52] Both Antonius and Stephanus were _not_ conceived out of wedlock, which is confirmed by a donation recorded in 1565 While it cannot in which Clara called both her sons as “soi figlioli carnali”.[53] be ruled out that Simon Budineus was somehow related to this family, there is not a shred of evidence to support this consideration, and the dowry instrument from Dubrovnik – which was unavailable to me when I composed my pertinent

article – also calls into question Clara/Clariza/Dobrula’s family. Based on the above-cited literature, I noted her as a member of the de Sanctis family, a fact that is contradicted by the dowry instrument from Dubrovnik: her late father was “Johannes Nicolaus de Besantis”, and there is similarly no shred of evidence tying Clara/Clariza/Dobrula to the de Sanctis family.[54] 

There is, however, scattered evidence of other connections: in 1550, Antonius and Stephanus Budinich sold twelve morgens “partially with vines and in part uncultivated land” in the vicinity of Gaženica (Gasenica) to the metropolitan chapter for 90 ducats. The transaction was facilitated by the executors of the late Johannes Rosa, former bishop of Krk, and there was considerable overlap between his executors on the one hand and the metropolitan chapter on the other hand.[55] Among these individuals were cleric Sanctus de Sanctis, the master-goldsmith Matthaeus Boriceuich, the Reverend Gregorius Clauario and, once again, friend of the family and former tutor of the Budinich brothers, Hieronymus Cortesius of Rab.[56] 

There are two main, if intimately inter-related, issues debated by Šime Urlić, Stjepan Antoljak and Ante Strgačić across the 20[th] century, one being the chronology of the main events of Budineus’ career and the other being his provenance. It is fairly certain that Simon Budineus was away from Zadar in the first half of the 1550s, as evidenced by the first notarised deed he stipulated on 3 October 1556, which pinpoints his arrival to summer or early autumn of that year. His first instrument is accompanied by a carefully worded and illustrated signet that reads as follow: “I, Simon Budineus, cleric of Zadar, publicly sworn notary under Holy Imperial and Jadertine authority…”.[57] Speaking very broadly, disagreements between these three accounts focus on the key appointment as sacristan ( _mansionarius_ ) of Zadar’s cathedral church, which occurred around Budineus’ ordination: both Urlić and Strgačić assert that this happened by 1559/1560, which is confirmed by a notarial deed from January 1560. According to Urlić, Budineus was ordained on 4 April 1560, which is disputed by Strgačić, who suggested 23 April 1560, but who agreed that Budineus read his first mass on the occasion of Margarita Mirinka’s funeral.[58] As regards the uncertainties in

the literature about Simon’s benefices and sinecures, the notarial records provide clarification beyond the available accounts: in January 1563, Budineus is referred to as “assistant [or deputy] deacon and sacristan of Zadar’s cathedral church, parishioner of the church of Saints Sylvester and Thomas, chaplain of the chapel of Saint George within and of the chapels of Saints Mark and Ursula outside the city walls, and of a house opposite the church of Saint Elias”. While Urlić mentions these facts without citing a source, Strgačić cites a source but omits the date, which is offered – 18 January 1560 (perhaps _more veneto_ ) – by notary Daniel Cavalca.[59] It was not until Sanctus de Sanctis’ death in 1565 that Budineus acquired the former’s – his uncle’s – sinecures and had become chancellor of the archbishopric of Zadar.[60] 

Shifting focus, Simon Budineus left six protocol books that contain a total of 827 individual deeds. Their analysis shows that he was the notary of choice among Zadar’s ecclesiastical estate. Individuals (natural persons) and institutions (legal persons) alike were his single-biggest customer base: 346 of the 827 deeds – or about 42% – in his protocol books were requested by, or written at the behest of, members of the clergy.[61] As Budineus rose comparatively quickly through the ranks, he counted among his customers very important individuals, such as archbishop Muzio Calino and the bishop of Nin, Marco Loredan;[62] five of Zadar’s eight convents, that is, the nobles-only nunnery of St Mary (Benedictines), the convents of St Nicholas (Franciscans), St Demetrius (Dominicans) and St Marcella (Poor Clares);[63] and a large number of other, smaller institutions, such as parish churches, chapels and hospitals, as well as many individual from Zadar and elsewhere in the Venetian Adriatic, including the parishes of St Elias and St Simeon of Zadar, as well as the brotherhoods of St Jacob, the Blessed Virgin and Mary the Merciful.[64] 

Apart from his own protocol books, Budineus also appeared frequently in the deeds of his fellow notaries from 1557 through 1568/1569, which is to say that he was present in Zadar a few years longer than he was active as a notary.[65] Setting aside his ecclesiastical duties and humanistic endeavours, Simon’s activities are

comparable to those of his fellow “elite citizens”: Budineus engaged in property transfers, acted as procurator and notarised testaments.[66] These activities mirror the class-and/or-status group behaviour of elements of Venice’s population that James Grubb identified: Budineus was not of privileged descent, but his expertise and service were indispensable for public and ecclesiastical administration.[67] 

Essentially the same can be said about “the lawyer Hieronymus Cortesius”, who we encountered before; he was one of the guardians of the Budinich brothers (not related to Simon). Hieronymus was originally from Rab, related to his uncle, “the Reverend Stephanus de Cortesijs”, and he had become an archdeacon in his adopted home.[68] Hieryomus’ connections are documented across 59 individual deeds and they mirror, virtually exactly, the activities and ties of Simon Budineus, ranging from property transactions to procuratorial appointments.[69] They also include high-profile ties all over the Adriatic, ranging from “Albertus Duymius, bishop of Krk” to taking care of business for “the doctor of both laws Johannes Jovinus”, who was en route to Venice.[70] In closing, I shall cite but one more example to drive home, once more, the issue of legal persons requiring representation as the following case shows. Under the leadership of the Poor Clares’ abbes, “sister Magdalena de Nassis”, twelve nuns jointly appointed Hieronymus their legal representative. We note, in passing, the dual twin characteristics of such contracts: an individual (abbess Magdalena), representing a _legal_ person (the Poor Clares of Zadar), conferred powers of attorney on a _natural_ person (Hieronymus Cortesius), who was empowered to act in the name of the entire convent, that is, a _legal_ person.[71] In

Topographies of Everyday Life 

237 

What these exemplary case studies do perfectly as they are, though, is serve as vivid and dense illustrations of the interconnectedness of that _shared_ body of water known as the Adriatic. While the material discussed in this chapter so far has highlighted the embeddedness of Zadar, located in central Dalmatia, across the entire region and transcending, albeit to varying degrees, the boundaries of Venice’s possessions, the notarised deeds also include details about their specific place of stipulation within Zadar, and it is to these hitherto overlooked particulars that we now turn. 

## 4. _Zadar’s cityscape(s)_ 

for elites and non-elites alike, focusing on the distinction between public and private spheres. In so doing, I am following up on the preceding pages as well as on considerations by the urban sociologist Martina Löw, who identified this dichotomy as one of the three chief directions for future research.[72] I shall do so in two steps: first, I provide an overview of the locations of the stipulation of the more than 2,000 real estate transactions concerning the areas outside the city walls. The second set of data analyses the 255 contracts that transferred property within the city walls over the same period. A brief section on the implications of these findings concludes this segment.

Zadar proper (by which is meant the built-up area within the city walls, excluding the suburbs) was organised into about 20 ecclesiastical parishes, plus a few neighbourhoods with secular designations that do not refer to a church.[73] Table 8.3 summarises the locations of stipulation of the 2,026 individual contracts that transferred property, organised in supraordinate categories. Most contracts were drawn up in decidedly public places: a little over a third were written in one of the city’s squares or adjacent structures, such as the communal loggia or the jurists’ bench; slightly more than a quarter of the instruments were written in a private residence of one of the city’s inhabitants. In 330 out of the 529 occurrences noted above, the contracting parties met in the house of a nobleman of Zadar. On many occasions these notarial acts were drawn up in the presence of the communal official – who was typically also a nobleman – whose signature was required to validate any instrument.[74] These 330 instances made up only a sixth of the total number of property transactions. Conversely, the houses of the nobility accounted for almost two-thirds of all locations of stipulation in this sub-category. 

This analysis reveals two other relevant categories of urban space: business or storage facilities ( _apotheca_ , 254 deeds) and administrative chancelleries ( _cancellaria_ , 301 deeds). As regards these spaces, there exists no clear distinction between public and private as commercial premises served similar purposes to communal and administrative premises. While the business facilities were often in the hands of non-nobles who engaged in a wide variety of economic activities, they also served as locations in which contracting parties met. Likewise, the role of the public chancelleries was not clear-cut because three of the fifteen notaries active in Zadar worked as communal officials: Johannes a Morea served in the criminal chancellery ( _cancellarius ad criminalium_ ); and Johannes Mazzarellus and his son Simon spent at least a part of their public life as communal chancellors

examinatores se non subscribant instrumenti continentibus maiorem poenam quarti”; Ref. 118, “Quod iudices examinatores non se subscribant instrumentis aut testamentis in quibus relinquatur aliquid ecclesiis, religiosis aut ecclesiasticis personis”; Ref. 119, “Quod iudices examinatores non se absentent a civitate, nisi unus cum licentia domini comitis”; Ref. 156, “Quod iudices examinatores subscribere acta notariorum. De examinatione notariorum. Quod notarii absentes extra civitatem per duos menses debeant relinquere in cancellaria sua acta et prothocolla notarilia”. _Statuta Iadertina_ , pp. 598-602, 670.
