( _cancellarius communitatis_ ). These three alone were responsible for slightly more than a third of all contracts written in their workplaces, which highlights a large degree of overlap between their profession (notary) and public function (chancellor). Frequently, both retail facilities and public buildings were used to conduct business transactions or activities otherwise unrelated to the purpose of these public spaces. Combined, these three subcategories ( _apotheca_ , _cancellaria_ , _domus_ ) account for the location of stipulation of 1,084 notarial acts, or slightly more than half of the sample summarised in Table 8.3. Their combined share easily exceeds the fully “public” sphere ( _platea_ ). 

Moving to the subject of property transferred in Zadar proper, Table 8.4 shows that the main difference between the real estate transactions within versus without the city walls is the number of _female_ contracting parties. In all three previously analysed categories, women never amounted to more than about 12% among constituent parties and some 4% among recipient parties (Chapter 4).

Property transactions within the city walls mainly transferred a house ( _domus_ ), a small house ( _domuncula_ ) or parts thereof.[75] As for the contracting parties, the combined number of vendors who belonged to the nobility or clergy was 62 (about 24%). The number among the buyers was 34 (about 13%). Thus, urban real estate transactions were firmly in the hands of Zadar’s non-noble inhabitants.[76] Like other segments of the real estate market, the city proper, its

Table 8.5 provides further select particulars for four of the city’s most important parishes in terms of prominence and frequency of real estate transactions. The total amount of money transferred by the 255 contracts amounted to about 12,671 ducats. While the numbers vary somewhat among the four parishes, taken as a whole, they reveal a considerable increase in the number of individual contracts and amount of land turnover (in ducats) inside the city walls in the 1560s, which is consistent with the data for the city’s jurisdiction (Chapter 4). This is also consistent with trends in other segments of Zadar’s property market. Also interesting is the regularity of the named property locations within the administrative, ecclesiastical and social subcategories of the urban space. Most parishes were mentioned between ten and twenty times as the approximate location of the transferred property.[80] But there were exceptions to this. Some parishes, like St John or the Blacksmiths’ parish, were mentioned as many as 40 times, while others appeared only very infrequently or a few times only; examples of the latter category include the parishes centred around the noble nunnery of St Mary’s and St Donatus’ (mentioned twice each), the 

(16.5%) for the artisans, 16 (6%) for the soldiers, 12 (5%) for members of the intellectual elite (of whom three were of noble birth) and 20 (or 8%) for the merchants. As before, the largest number of contracting parties – 131 (51%) – belonged to neither category. 

77. The three-decade totals for the vendors are 192 (or 75%) for the city proper; 5 (2%) for the suburbs; and nine (3.5%) for Zadar’s territory ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ). If the location of noble residences is considered, further particulars emerge. The city inside the walls was subdivided into 20 parishes (or 25 churches).[83] However, in the 529 contracts that refer to noble houses as the location of stipulation, only sixteen parishes are named. This suggests that the nobility preferred to live in certain parishes. In the mid-16[th] century these parishes were St Anastasia, St Demetrius, St Chrysogonus, Sts Thomas and Silvester, the Holy Forty Martyrs Church, St Vitus, St Catherine, St Salvator, St Lawrence, the noble nunnery of St Mary, St Mary of the Priests,[84] Sts Stephen/Simeon/Rochus,[85] St Dominic, St Michael and St John

Urban Elites of Zadar

or the Blacksmiths’ parish. In addition to these parishes, several neighbourhoods with secular (rather than ecclesiastical) designations appear in the records as preferred places of residence: the old Arsenal ( _arsenatus_ ) in the city’s eastern corner, the communal main square and the localities “hospitum”, “porta macella” and “beccaria” or the Butchers’ Gate (in Croatian, Klaonica vrata).[86] 

The parishes not associated with residing nobles were St Nicholas, St Francis, St George, St Elijah, St Donatus’, St Bernard, St Peter the Old, St Nicolas,[87] Sts Cosmas and Damian and the church of St Mary “de bon gaudio”, located next to the captain’s palace.[88] The nobles tended to avoid the southern and western areas of Zadar. The exception was the parish of St Donatus’, located next to St Anastasia Cathedral, the archiepiscopal palace and near the churches of the Holy Forty Martyrs, St Peter, Sts Cosmas and Damian and St Mary “de bon gaudio”. 

## *** 

This chapter has offered tentative insights into Zadar’s changing cityscape and several of its more prominent “users” in the mid-16[th] century. In so doing, the epistemological path pursued in these pages, labelled “topographies of everyday life”, suggests a fruitful avenue of research that allows the historian to flesh out multiple connections, aggregate individual sources and seeks to place the various findings on a map. If combined with the available literature, several new insights can be gained. Of these, perhaps the shifting linguistic boundaries across the 20[th] century become visible. Take, for instance, the way Urlić in his 1916 referred to Simon Budineus’s provenance, quoting the Latin phrase “avus maternus” – meant as Sanctus de Sanctis – and explained that “its meaning is generally understood to have been maternal uncle”. Antoljak, by contrast, writing forty years later, suggested that the same term meant “grandfather”, thus significantly re-imagining the relationship between Simon Budineus, Sanctus de Sanctis and, by extension, Clara/Clariza/Dobrula, wife of Michael Budinich.[89] The systematic study 

was that a document named the patron saints Simeon and Rochus as equals: “ecclesie divi Simeonis seu Roci”, as per HR DAZD 31 BZ, Cornelius Constantius, I, 1, 3, cc. 9v-10r, 27 April 1569. 

Macelli”, “contrata porte Civitatis vocate della becharia” or “prope portam Civitatis vocatam la porta della becharia In contrata hospitum”. For the location, see Petricioli, Raukar, Švelec, Peričić, _Zadar_ , p. 135. <!-- page 244 -->

Topographies of Everyday Life 

243 

of the existing evidence reveals not a single instance in which Simon Budineus is mentioned as related to Stephanus or Antonius Budinich; moreover, Michael Budinich’s receipt of his wife’s dowry in Dubrovnik in 1521 offers further evidence that Clara/Clariza/Dobrula was not related to Sanctus de Sanctis. Apparently, Urlić’s reading was closer to reality than Antoljak’s re-interpretation, which informs Švelec pertinent entry in the _Croatian Biographic Lexicon_ .[90] Hence, future research may enquire further about Clara/Clariza/Dobrula, the marital ties of her sons Antonius and Stephanus (who married into the Saghich and Mathapharis families of Zadar, respectively) and, last but not least, the rest of Simon Budineus’s career, including his education and, perhaps more importantly, his sojourn in Rome. 

Finally, surveying the locations of stipulation of more than 2,000 individual deeds raises an additional set of questions about the functions and locations of public spaces (chancelleries, the loggia), business facilities, ecclesiastical spaces (churches, monasteries) and noble residences. An L-shaped area in the north and east of the city was the zone preferred by Zadar’s nobility. This area extended from the castle in the northern corner to the main communal square in the eastern corner to the citadel in the southern corner. It covered roughly two-thirds of the city. The nobles avoided the parishes in the southwestern and westernmost parts of Zadar. This provides a starting point for future studies that may seek to assess in more depth the social distribution of the city’s housing.

## Conclusion 

The _Homo adriatius_ is a _forma mentis_ and a paradigm in the principle of cultural openness, and the Adriatic lends itself to precisely this approach. The Mediterranean is a sea of diversity. The Adriatic, despite its borders and conflicts, is the sea of convergence.[1] 

Egidio Ivetić 

The Republic of St Mark embarked upon the 16[th] century as a state whose power had declined significantly relative to the contemporaneous ascent of its main allies and rivals. Two universal monarchies, the Spanish and Ottoman Empires, were engaged in a decade-long struggle for pre-eminence in the Mediterranean. The events surrounding the ascent of these powers form the backdrop for this book as it examines contemporary developments in Venice’s _stato da mar_ , specifically during the three decades circumscribed by the naval battles of Preveza (1538) and Lepanto (1571). Analysing the business activities, social relations, material culture and spatial characteristics of the urban elites in the _stato da mar_ , this study sheds new light on developments in Venetian Dalmatia during the nadir of Christian naval power and Muslim dominance, using as a central case study the city of Zadar, then the capital of Venice’s Adriatic province. 

The decades prior to the Cyprus War were a particularly harsh period for the inhabitants of Dalmatia. The enemy stood at the gates, the Serenissima was almost exclusively interested in the security of her vital shipping routes along the eastern Adriatic coast and the population of the urban communities found itself in serious difficulties. After the end of the Old Regime, Dalmatia’s past was exploited by (Yugo)Slavic and Italian historiographers as a means of furthering their respective nationalistic ends. These two groups of historians usually treated Venice and her dominion over parts of the western Balkans from opposing viewpoints. As the 20[th] century progressed, these perspectives slowly retreated. But apart from a few scholars, renewed academic interest in the middle decades of the 16[th] century has yet to materialise. The present study offers a contribution to recent debates about the increased Venetian resistance to change from the 16[th] century onward found in the literature. Hence, this book re-examines the perceived absence of “events 

1. Ivetić, _History of the Adriatic_ , p. vi.

<!-- page 247 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

246 

worthy of the attention of posterity”, as Pierre Daru (1767-1829) infamously dismissed this era, thus setting the parametres for two centuries of scholarship.[2] 

Analysis of the notarised deeds and testaments written between 1540 and the outbreak of the Cyprus War reveal considerable change beneath the surface of Zadar’s society. In the present work, these developments have been examined in the following way: at the beginning, I offered an in-depth analysis of one particular, if woefully neglected, source type: the _procura_ . I had not planned to do so when I set out to study this topic in my underlying graduate dissertation; their importance only became apparent after I had had collated information from all notarised deeds in spreadsheets and noted that their sheer numbers exceeded almost all other contract types studied in these pages (except property sales outside the city walls). Given the realities of premodern, pre-industrial life, however, their importance for the proper functioning of Mediterranean urban communities is immediately revealed once the distinction between natural and legal personhood, the requirements of absentee contracting – especially when settling outside the judicial system – and a myriad of other aspects of everyday interactions are considered in combination, such as legal guardianship for underage children, property transactions of ecclesiastical institutions or the dubious payment behaviour of Venetian authorities for deployed soldiers. In short: their in-depth analysis permits the reconstruction of the directions, networks, intensity and motivations of communication across the Adriatic, including several case studies detailing both individual and institutional experiences. 

Shifting gears, second, this book then analysed in great detail the property transactions in Zadar and its jurisdiction, breaking down the city’s _contado_ (the area controlled by the city) and assessing, for the first time, economic developments on the mainland and the islands, including a first-of-itskind comparison of price differentials. Scholarship on Dalmatian economic development during the second Venetian dominion (1409-1797) maintains that stagnation and decline ensued. My book shows that such generalising assertions need additional investigation and, possibly, revision. 

Third, most studies on Venetian Dalmatia investigate everyday life and interactions within urban communities by focusing virtually exclusively on the highest social stratum, the local nobility. By investigating their activities and comparing them with the non-noble “elite citizens” (James Grubb) who were essential to the administrative, economic and social dynamics of Venetian society, this study offers a thorough analysis of 16[th] -century Dalmatia. This is accomplished by collating and assessing marriage-related instruments, extrajudicial settlements and other notarised deeds to assess and compare the range of activities of select non-noble individuals with those of their more privileged peers. In doing so, this study joins comparable scholarly efforts focusing on other regions and periods of Venice’s _stato da mar_ and offers a distinct contribution to the study of Renaissance Dalmatia. 

2. Orig. “l’histoire des Vénetiens s’écoule sans être marquée par des évènements dignes d’occuper la postérité”, wrote Daru, _Histoire de la république de Venise_ , vol. IV, p. 118. Translation via Norwich, _History of Venice_ , pp. 459-460.

<!-- page 248 -->

Conclusion 

247 

It is precisely the combination of these three interrelated themes that enables the reconstruction of Zadar and its inhabitants around the middle of the 16[th] century. The nadir of Christian naval power and corresponding Ottoman control of the eastern Mediterranean were felt on land and sea alike. Venetian Dalmatia was no exception, and Ottoman raiding parties reached the environs of Zadar as early as the 1430s, resulting in incessant and increasing pressure from beyond the borders for at least the subsequent 250 years. The consequences of these events led to frequently changing borders, heightened insecurity in the hinterlands and seriously impacted the lives of Dalmatia’s residents under the control of the Most Serene Republic. Most importantly, improvements in military technology made defence of the vast expanse of Venice’s maritime state increasingly expensive due to the need for extensive construction and recruitment; while these factors rendered many mediaeval fortifications obsolete, the early modern era was characterised by the construction of vast new and improved fortifications. While these projects brought employment, they constituted a drain on local resources, necessitated transfer payments from other areas of the Venetian republic and brought with them frictions between civil and military populations living in close quarters. On an economic plane, these conditions often led to discontinued agriculture (on the mainland), cross border raids, and changing Venetian economic policies contributed to an increasing dependency on the Serenissima, in particular as foodstuffs and other goods that once were produced locally had to be imported. 

As the Republic of St Mark increased her military commitments to defend her maritime state in the aftermath of the battle of Preveza (1538), changes in the density, directions and flows of communication and migration ensued. By analysing the _procurae_ in the books of Zadar’s notaries, this study documents shifts in the origins and destinations of individuals living in central Dalmatia. The data are clear that the communication frequency with Venice proper increased notably in the three interwar decades. This was mirrored by a corresponding decline in the importance of the other destinations along the oriental littoral of the Adriatic. 

Economic changes are more challenging to assess. The combination of internal and external factors had a significant impact on the development of Dalmatian cities after the advent of the second Venetian dominion in 1409. By the turn of the 16[th] century, the economic life of the coastal cities under Venetian rule was reduced to “little or nothing”.[3] Developments in the first two decades after the conclusion of the Ottoman-Venetian war from 1537-1540 compounded these trends. The decade prior to the Cyprus War was different, however. In contrast to the preceding long economic decline, Zadar witnessed a marked upturn in economic activities in the 1560s as both transferred acreage and turnover doubled. Unfortunately, we lack comparable analyses for the periods prior to the Ottoman-Venetian War and for the decades after the Cyprus War (1570-1573). Thus, it cannot be stated conclusively whether these ten years were an economic 

3. Budak, “Urban élites”, p. 186.

<!-- page 249 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

248 

exception, although if the experiences of Kotor (Cattaro) explored by Michele Santoro are any indication, the reduction of Venetian influence and control over economic activities – and the growth of importance of both the Ottoman and Habsburg Empires – reduced the export-orientation of the various Venetian cities; in its stead, commerce, production and trade become more local and selfsufficient, with few, if any, outstanding activities.[4] These findings corroborate the considerations of Arié Malz voiced almost twenty years ago. Hence, we may conclude that economic dynamics became a function of local population As numbers, possibilities of production and ease versus difficulty of transport.[5] regards Zadar – whose experiences did not differ significantly from those of Kotor – border revisions and the accompanying shrinking of its jurisdiction in the 1570s characterised the situation considerably; economic recovery, certainly compounded by the eventual creation of a free harbour in Split in 1592, and thus the re-routing of trade flows, soon stalled.[6] 

Venice’s increased commitment to defending the _stato da mar_ had another, less visible, consequence: as the Republic of St Mark poured increasing quantities of supplies, men and money into the defence of her overseas possessions, the relationship between the Venetian newcomers and the resident populations changed. The past two or three decades have witnessed an increase in studies of how Venice’s representatives integrated into host societies in the wider eastern Mediterranean. This book contributes to these efforts by broadening the discussion to include a wider spectrum of urban elites, rather than “just” the nobility. In doing so, it paints a more inclusive image of the Adriatic coastal cities around the middle of the 16[th] century. Despite obvious differences in scale and institutional complexity,[7] Venice’s Adriatic dominions strongly resembled the lagoon metropolis in terms of their social diversity and fluidity. In existing scholarly contributions, however, the “underlying reality of economic, social, and geographical mobilit[ies]” so characteristic of the Venetian ruling class have rarely been the focus of interest for the peripheral areas of the _stato da mar_ , especially in the Adriatic.[8] 

the city’s rich archives provides “moving image[s]” _and_ an accompanying “soundtrack”, to borrow from Sally McKee’s work.[9] What emerges is a vivid reconstruction of urban daily life and a better understanding of the relations of 

   4. Santoro, “Governare la frontiera”, pp. 139-193. 

5. Malz, “Die dalmatinische Städtewelt”, pp. 104-116, albeit _pace_ his teleological 

modernisation paradigm. 

6. As recently as 2008, the late mediaeval and early modern eras were called a “[p]eriod of stagnation” by Raukar, “Croatia within Europe”, p. 26. On Split, see Paci, _La “Scala” di Spalato_ . 

7. Around 1550, Zadar was home to some 6,500 inhabitants while the population of Venice proper is estimated at c. 170,000 inhabitants. Mocellin, “Città fortificata”, pp. 43-4, 60-61; Malz, “Die dalmatinische Städtewelt”, p. 106. Population data for Venice via Sardella, _Venise_ , p. 10. 

8. Martin, Romano, “Reconsidering Venice”, p. 21 (my modification). 

9. McKee, “Women”, p. 35 (my modification).

<!-- page 250 -->

Conclusion 

249 

Dalmatian coastal communities with each other and the wider Mediterranean world around them. Through the combination of quantitative approaches with those sources that can be analysed thus – from _procurae_ to property transactions to extrajudicial settlements – with a strong focus on microhistorical episodes, a diverse and more comprehensive reconstruction of everyday life on the streets of Zadar emerges. 

Dalmatia’s archival wealth and the exemplary scholarship on the Republic of Dubrovnik[10] provide ample possibilities to expand the analytical picture of this region in the early modern period. Future research may examine such subjects as the relationship between the mainland coastal areas and the islands, the intricate connections between the cities and the rural parts under their jurisdictions, links between marriage patterns and social mobility and the material culture and selfrepresentation of ecclesiastical and secular elites. In addition, if the pioneering combination of microhistory and the history of everyday life, or _Alltagsgeschichte_ , added to this second edition (Chapters 7 and 8) is any indication, Venice’s overseas dominions lend themselves rather well to such a kind of “mapping”. Particularly desirable are studies focusing on the Venetian communities in the Kvarner Gulf (Cres, Pag, Osor and Rab), in southern Dalmatia around the large islands of Brač and Hvar, and Korčula (although the publication of Fabian Kümmeler’s study is duly noted), and the communities of Split, Šibenik and Trogir on the mainland, although Lena Sadovski’s new book on Split, Klis (Clissa), Omiš (Almissa) and Poljica (Poglizza) is both timely and highly welcome.[11] 

For too long, the early modern history of Adriatic communities, inextricably linked by the sea, has been the subject of interpretations intended to serve nationalistic aims or territorial claims. In the early 21[st] century, the history and historiography of the Adriatic are no longer constrained by political borders, yet barriers of language and perception linger. This book has sought to further our understanding of Dalmatia’s rich heritage, characterised by Italian, Venetian and (Yugo)Slavic cultural contributions, as well as, in part, by its Ottoman-Turkish heritage, and, hopefully, this study contributes to the overcoming of centuries of separate historiographies.

> 11. Kümmeler, _Korčula_ ; Sadovski, _Venice and the Dalmatian Hinterland_ ; and Santoro, “Governare la frontiera”, while going a long way toward filling these gaps, leave ample opportunities to address the lament voiced in 2011 by Schmitt, “L’apport des archives de Zadar”, p. 54.

_Sources_ : see Table 4.1; 2,026 contracts were analysed. _Disclaimer_ : This is not a complete list of toponyms in all 2,026 contracts since in some contracts no names or specifics are given. The listing provides an indicative overview of the inhabited villages. 

_Nota bene_ transliteration, and the third the Italian name (if known); the fourth column categorises the toponyms in Zadar’s territory ( _ager publicus_ , _Astareja_ ), jurisdiction (excluding the territory), the minor districts of Ljubač, Nin, Novigrad and Vrana, and the villages on the islands. I have ordered these places alphabetically; *indicates existence in 1527, † indicates appearance on the Venetian side of the border after the demarcation in 1576, both according to Mayhew, “Behind Zara”, pp. 310-315, which is based on the report by Zacharias Vallaresso from 1527, via _Commissiones_ , vol. I, pp. 219-222; and Anzulović, “Razgraničenj”, pp. 102-108. Note, however, that Mayhew contrasts the 83 villages listed by Vallaresso with the 54 villages appearing in 1576 after the redrawing of the borders, adding her own disclaimer stating that this “is not the final number of villages […but] to give the an idea about the large number of villages”, in Mayhew, “Behind Zara”, p. 310. 

My listing above notes 85 villages for C. 1550. However, it must be stressed that the numbers for the 1527 and 1576 lists represent only one year while the data above covers thirty years. The main changes occurred during the Cyprus War and in its wake.

**Map 1.** Zadar’s jurisdiction in the 16[th] century (scale: 1:200,000, map design by the author), showing the names and approximate locations of towns and villages outside the city walls. Under Zadar’s jurisdiction but not on the map are the islands of Olib, Premuda and Silba. For the Italian toponyms, see list above. 

Towns, villages and islands are indicated by their Croatian name and marked with a dot. Field names are in italics.

## Sample Transcripts 

The transcripts offer examples of the sources used, which are quoted extensively and analysed quantitatively throughout the book. I have reproduced as accurately as possible the notarial texts as they were originally written down. Hence, the spelling differs from today’s standardised Italian and Latin. I have provided transcriptions and summaries and have written out the abbreviations. All homonyms and toponyms below are spelled as they appear in the notarial manuscripts. I have, however, added in brackets the standardised spelling to Venetian family names. For toponyms, footnotes are provided that include present-day names. 

**==> picture [16 x 5] intentionally omitted <==**

_Procura_ : Catherina, daughter of the late Nicolaus de Comulis,[2] nobleman of Split and first wife of Johannes Michael Mazzarello, nobleman of Trogir, appoints her son, Simon Mazzarellus, doctor of both laws, to recuperate her dowry from her brother Petrus de Comulis. 

_Source_ : HR DAZD 31, BZ, Simon Budineus, I, 1, 1, cc. 28v-29r, 31 January 1557 (m.v.). 

Die penultima mensis Januarii 1557. Temporibus et Preturæ etc. Coram viro Nobile Jadrensis domino Donato Ciualello honorando Judice examinatore Curia Jadra. Personaliter constitute Nobilis domina Catherina uxor quondam spectabili domini Johannis Mazzarello Traguriensis [Trogir], et filia quondam domini Nicolai Comulouich, Nobilis spalatensis [Split], omnibus omni meliori modo via Jure causa ac forma, quibus Melius et efficatius de Jure potuit, et debuit fecit, constituit, creauit et solemniter ordinauit suum uerum certum legitimum et indubitatum procuratorem absentem, factorem et negotiorum suoum infrascriptorum gestorem et commissum spetialem et generalem ita tamen tamquam spetialis generalitati non derroget nec e contra, videlicet, excellentem Leges utriusque Doctorem dominum Simonem Mazzarellum filium suprascripti quondam spectabilis domninus Johannes eius viri in primo matrimonio presentem, et onus procurationis huius modi acceptantem spetialiter et expresse ad nomine dicta constituentis et pro ea a quibuscumque personis Tam in ciuitate Spalati, quam alibi, eidem domina constituent dare debentibus quacumque causa et occasione, et spetialiter occasione sui crediti Dotalis eleuandum et exigendum quascumque pecuniarum quantitates, ac quecumque alia bona eidem domina constitutenti sub quouis titulo spectantia, et pertinentia, et que ad eam quovismodo 

2. Noted in the 1553 travelogue by Gian Battista Giustinian (and considered a listing of nobles by Grga Novak), the Comulouich, or de Comulis, likely refer to the “Canuli” family mentioned in this report. _Commissiones_ , vol. II, p. 215; Novak, _Povijest Splita_ , vol. II, pp. 68, 221. Sadovski, “Die gesellschaftlichen Strukturen”, pp. 380-381, notes that the Comuli family may not have split completely from the Petrarchis family by the 1530s.

<!-- page 263 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

262 

spectare et pertinere possint, et id spetialieter ab heredibus quondam domini Petri de Comulis fratris eiusdem domina constituenti, Item ad recuperadnum et rehabendum a quibuscumque personis tam in dicta civitate Spalati quam alibi ubilibet. Quecumque bona tam mobilia quam stabilia pro quemquam forsam detenta et usurpata, dicta domina constituenti pertinentia quomodocumque nihil penitus exceptis aut excluso, et de exactis elleuatis recuperates rehabitis quietandum, et quietationes faciendum in forma iuris solita, et consueta, iuxta morem ibidemsuarum, cum necessarijs et opportunis, Bonaque praefata quecumque locandum et deslocandum precio et causa, prout eidem ex.d. Procuratori placuent et videbitur pro tempore quo sibi libuerit, super que praemissis et quod eorum rogandum et rogari faciendum quecumcumque Infrascripta, et publica documenta; Item ad nomine eiusdem domina constituentis quibuscumque collonis tam Jure pastinationis quam aliter ad laborandum quaternus opus erit comparendum coram celeberrimo Magistratu ubilibet, Eiusdem Ciuitatis spalati, ac aleo quovis Jusdicente uti oportuerit, et expedierit agendum, petendum, respondendum in animam dictae domina constituentis Jurandum, et quidcumque honestum, et licitum iuramentum prestandum, et prestari uidendum Testes acta has, scripturas ac quecumque Jura producendum, et ex aduerso produci uidendum causa pro dicta dicendum, et excipiendum litem contestandum, et contestari uidendum In causa concludendum, et conclude petendum, et instantiam sententiam et sententias feri petendum et audiendum eam, et eas executoini debita demandandum, et demandari petendis, ac ab ea seu eius quatenus opus erit appellandum, et appelari proseguendi, et Generaliter omna alia et singular faciendum, dicendum, gerendum et exercendum quæ in premissis et causa et neccesaria fuerint seu quomodolibet oppportuna et quæ ipsamet constituens faceret et facere posset si paresens personaliter addesset etiam si Talia forent quemandatum exigerent magis spetialiter quàm presentibus expressum est, cum plena libera, et omnimoda facultate, acutoritate et mandato in omnibus Promitens etc. sub obligatione etc. Actum Jadræ domi habitationis dicti excellentis d. Simonis [Mazzarello], Presentibus Ibidem magistro Hieronymo Mircouich et magistro Antonio filio magistri Martini Remerij buttarijs Jadrensis etc.

_Sales contract_ : master-cobbler Franciscus de Pesaro, a soldier in Zadar’s main fort, sells a house in Pag to his nephew, the Rev. Johannes Bucifalich, for the price of 36 libras. 

_Source_ : HR DAZD 31, BZ, Petrus de Bassano, I, 1, 10, f. 2v, 2 April 1544. 

Die mercurij Secunda mensis aprilis 1544, Indictione 2[da] , Temporibus et anno, Ibique, coram viro Nobilis Jadrensis domini Michele de Rosa honorando Judice Examinatorre Curia Jadre, personaliter constitutus magister Franciscus de Pesaro cerdo stipendiatus in castro magno Jadrensis, ac libere, per se suosque heredes, vendidit, ac alienauit d. presbytro Johanni Bucifalich, Nepoti suo ex fratre, de Pago ibidem presenti ac pro se suisque heredibus Ementi, ac aquirenti Tertia partem vnius domunculam de lignamine, Ex Tribus Lateribus, ac ex quarta Latere de muro, Posita Pagi super proprio Terreno, proindiviso com ipso d. presbytro Johanne Emptore, cum vno alio Tertio, ac cum Franciscso Tubicina Comitem Strenuij d. Nicolai Tetrici, uxor nomine, pro vno alio Tertio, cum vno Loco vacuo, Posito penes dictam rem venditam versus viam publicam, hos Infra, ut dixerunt Confines, videlicet, de quirina, magister

<!-- page 264 -->

Appendix 

263 

Vitus Marturelich, sutor, de borea, Sive prouennza, Ideo dominus presbyter Johannes Emptor, de Trauersa, sive Borea, sor Stana Regoichca monacha, ac de siroco via publica, saluis semper alijs suis verioribus confinibus si qui sunt etc ad habendum, Tenendum, possidendum, gaudendum, usufructandum, ac alienandum, ac quicquid dicto domino presbytro Johanni, Suisque heredibus deinceps proprio placuerit fatiendum, tamquam de re sua propria, cum omnibus suis Juribus ac Jurisdictionibus habendum, ac pertinens, quovis modo spectantibus, ac pertinentibus, tam de Jure quam de consuetudine, ac hoc pro pretio, ac nomine veri pretij librarum Triginta Sex parvorum de quibus £ 36, ipse venditor dixit, ac confessus fuit, habuisse ac recepisse, a dicto domino Emptore libras quinqua, Exceptioni sibi non datarum, ac non Solutarum dictarom £ 5, ac Spei futura habituris omnio renuntians, residuum vero, videlicet, £ xxxj [added is “31” above the Roman numerals] ipse dominus presbyter Johannes Emptor promisit dare dicto venditori in duobus Terminis hic Jadre, videlicet, medietatem subsequentem de anno 1545, Sub obligatione omnium bonorum Suorum, quas quidem res, Sic ut suora venditas dictus magister Franciscus promisit pro se, ac heredes suos ipsi domino presbytro Johanni Emptor ibidem presenti, ac acceptanti, Semper, ac quomodocumque in Jure Legittime, deffendere, ac Excalumnare ab omni homine communi collegio, ac uniuersitate, sub pena qualibet in Statutis Jadre contenta, ac obligatione omnium Suorum bonorum presentium et futurorum.

_Planting concession, or land grant_ : the Reverend Johannes Donatus Begna, acting on behalf of the benefice of the altar of St John the Evangelist in St Simeon’s church, grants two gonjaj near Punta Pašman to Gregorius, Simon, Martinus and Andreas Pifcinouich, all sons of Matthaeus from the same place, for the duration of nine years in exchange for a quarter of the harvest. 

_Source_ : HR DAZD 31, BZ, Horatius de Marchettis, I, 1, 5, cc. 18v-19r, 11 December 1569. 

Die undecimo mensis Decembris, 1569, Indictio xij 

Temporibus ert praetura ut saupra. Coram viro Nobile Jadrensis Domino Gregorio Calina honrando Judice examinatore curiae Jadrae, personaliter constitutes Reverendus dominus Joannes Donatus Begnius Vicarius ecclesia Metrpolitica Jadrensis, et plebanus ac cappellanus ut dixit beneficiatus altaris Sancti Joannis Euangelistae in Eccelisa Sancti Simonis Justi Jadrae, quo es de Iure patronatus Nobilium de Fanfoneis, et dicto plebani nomine cupiens preseruare et augere ac meliorare bona dicti altatris, per se et successores suos Dedit, Tradidit et ad pastinandum, et plandandum Concessit Gregorio, et Simonem Pifcinouich filijs Matthei de Puncta Pasmani [Punta Pašman] Jurisdictionis Jadrensis, ibidem presentibus stipulantibus et recipientibus ac acceptantibus nominibus eorum proprijs et eorum heredum, ac nominibus Martini et Andree fratrium suorum absentium pro quibus promiserung de rato in eorum proprijs bonis, Gognalia duo in circa Terreni positj in pertinentijs dictam Punctam Pasmani, Cum pactis modis condictionibus et obligati sint pastinare et plantare dicta duo gognalia Terreni in annis nonem proxime futuris Incepti a die presentis stipulationis, bonis, et uitibus et [unreadable] plantandam,

<!-- page 265 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

264 

et pastinandam bene fideliter et diligenter laborare et cultuuare, Semel putaree bor[rea] in anno Zappare, seu ligonizare more bonorum Sozalim suis debitis, et congruis temporibus iuxta dispositionem ordinum et statutorum Jadrae, sub penis in dictis Statutis contentis respondendo in anno decimo proxime uenturo quartum rectum, et Integrum de musto et uinis dicto Reverendi domini et successoris conducendum et defferedum ibidem ad marinam in barca patroni sumptibus et expensis omnibus dictorum Sozalium, Et Idem Sozales in dicto Termino non pastinauerint Integraliter praedictas duo Gognalia terreni tam sint obligat respondere quartum dicto domino de fundo non plantato sicut de eo tam esset vel erit plantatum ad estimationem pro bonum uirorum ac pastibus tunt elligendum, Item tam in executione partis Ilustrissimu ducis domini Venetiarum dicti Sozales Teneant plantare pedes duodecim olivariorum pro quodlibet gognali, et de oliuis tam primum fructa receperint respondere quartum de siculi de musto expressum est, Qua omina et singula Eodem partes prefatis nominibus ad inuicem promiserunt attendere et obseruare, firma et rata habere tenere et non contrafacere dicere uel nomine per se uel alios sub pena quarti in Statutis Jadre contenta, et obligatione uicissim omnium suorum dictij nominibus boborum mobilium et stabilium presentium et futurorum, Actum Jadra in Domo habitationis suprascripti Reuerendi domini Vacarij in contrata Sancta Anastasia uel Sanctorum 40 martirum, presentibus Clericis Mattheo Nouacouich et Simone Ture habitatoribus Jadram Testibus habitis etc.

_Rental or leasehold contract_ : Marcus Mathaceus, acting on behalf of the Rev. Camillus de Rubeis, procurator of the convent of St Chrysogonus of Zadar, grants the convent’s property near Neviđane Pašman, to Michaeli de Radinis, citizen and merchant of Zadar, for the duration of six years 

_Source_ : HR DAZD 31, BZ, Horatius de Marchettis, I, 1, 5, cc. 18v-19r, 11 December 1569. 

## Die 22 mensis Decembris 1557 

Temporibus et Praeturæ etc., Coram viro Nobile Jadrensis domino Donato Ciuallello honorando Judice Examinatore curia, Personaliter constitutes dominus Marcus Mathaceus procurator et commissus ut dixit Reverendi domini Camilli de Rubeis perpetuu comendatarij Abbatiaæ Sancti Chrysogonis de Jadre dicto procuration nomine per se et successors suos titulo libera menta, ac simplices locationis duratura ut infra dedit, locauit et ad affictus concessit ser Michaeli de Radinis ciui et mercatori Jadræ presenti et pro se ac heredibus suis conducenti et accipienti totum Villam siue Insulam Neuiane [Neviđane] de nomine Abbatia predictæ com omnibus et quibuscumque Juribus, actionibus, Jurisdictionibus ac alijs pertinentijs quibuscumque ad dictam villa mut supra locatam seu uerius ex ea Abbatia predict quoquomodo spectantibus, et pertinentibus tam de iure quam de conseutudine et com omnibus regalijs et honorantijs eidem Reverendo domino comendatario spectantibus ut dictum est, et com molendino ab aleo et ceteris quibuscumque nil penitas except aut resseruato, Ad Introitum durante triennio habendum, tenendum, gaudendum, usufructandum, fructusque quoscumque ac utilitates quascumque ex dicta re locata percipiendum, meliorandum et non deteriorandum, et hoc pro annis tribus proxime venturis incepturis a xxiij mensis Instantis, quod erit crastino die et ut sequitur finituris ita nihil quod conductor predictus percipiat et procipere habet

<!-- page 266 -->

Appendix 

265 

tres rectos introitus ex suprascripta villa seu Insula Neuiane ut supra conducta, et hoc precio, et nomine precij locati et conducti inter partes conuento, ducatorum septuaginta quinque ad rationem librarum 6 4 parvorum pro ducato singular anno dicti triennij soluendum per dictum conductam praefato domini procuratori locator sive successors suo legittimo seu uerius praefato Reverendo domino Comendatario modo et terminis infrascriptis, videlicet, de sex mensibus in sex menses, dimidium Seu dicit annui pretij in capite primorum sex uienium anni aliud uero dimidium in capita anni et sic de anno in annim duranti huiusmodi locatione absque aliqua contradiction obstaculo seu alia quouis cauillatione aut strepitu Judicij prout et quemadmodum praefatus conductor se per se ac heredes suos precisi locationis huiusmodi modo praefato Soluturum obligavit, cum hac per partes expresse condictiones et declaration quod durante ista locatione si conductor antedictus in molendino ab oleo suprascripto aliqua fecerit melioramenta ad ea soluenda seu satisfacienda locator praefatus minime teneat nisi dum taxat si pro conductor confici fecerit aliqua uasa ab oleo dicta uullgo finaccia tam conductor ipse ea in fine huius locationis leuare possit libere absque alicuius contradictionem hoc etiam inter ipsas partes pacto apposite, et solemni stipulation uallato, quam si interim durante hac ipse locatione, locator antedictus ulla terrena I uilla praefata contendere et dare voluerit pastinandum quibuis sozalibus ad libere facere possit. Declarantes attenderer quod terrena quæ sunt sub uilla dicta Podvornice ea minime dare possit pastinanda verum terrena que sunt a uilla superius montem uersus ea ad plantandum libere concedere possit. Pro quibus omnibus et singulis in praesenti instrument expressis firmitter attendendis et obseruanis partes predicta quibus supra nominibus nec non locator ipse quo supra nomine non mannisse venendo et indemni conservando conductore praefato in huiusmodi locatione et eo tenendo ab die hodie et persona et persona in re ut supra locata, In Judicio et extra proque locata huiusmodo habenda firma, rata et grata, attendenda et obseruanda et eo in aliquo non contravenendo obligarung et hipothecarint omnia et quecumque eorum dictis nominibus bona mobilia et stabilia presential et futuras. Actum Jadra in claustro ecclesiae Sancti Grisogoni praefati, Presentibus Ibidem ser Pasino Cerono mercatore et magistro Johannes Dusmanouich Butario, habitore Jadra, Testibus habitis, uocatis, rogatis, etc. 

Ego Donatus Ciualellus suprascripto sub[scripsit] 

**==> picture [16 x 6] intentionally omitted <==**

_Arbitration settlement_ : Zadar’s Third Order Regulars, represented by the Rev. Simon Glauich, provost, Father Felix, Father Simon Margitich and Father Blasius Baichinich, and Franciscus Tubicina resolve their quarrels over the construction of a latrine along a shared wall (Chapter 5). 

_Source_ : HR DAZD 31 BZ, Nicolaus Drasmileus, I, 2, 2, ff. 42v-43r, 29 July 1564. 

1564 Indictione septima, die uero Sabbati uigesimo nono mensis Julij Temporibus et regiminis etc. Coram uiro nobile Jadrensis spectabili doctore Hieronymo Grisogono Honorando Consiliario Celeberrimi domini Comitis praefati. Cum uersa fuerit lis, et controuersia Inter Reuerendos patres fratres Sancti Joannis tertij ordinis Sancti Francisci de Jadra ex una, et magistrum Franciscum Tubicinam ex alia super eo Sit quod dicti Reuerendi fratres Intendebant facere unum Conductum, siue necessarium In quoddam loco dicti monasterij Contiguo domui dicti magistri Francisci a parte quirinali, et boreali

<!-- page 267 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

266 

domus dicti magistri Francisci qui magister Franciscus Contradicebat Ex quo asserbat quod tale necessarium obfetorem damnum daret domui ipsius magistri Francisci et pluribus alijs rationibus super qua differentia declaratum fuit superioribus diebus per celeberrimos domines rectores quod dicti Reuerendi fratres possent procedere ad constructionem, siue edificationem dicti necessarij ante terminatione dictus magister Franciscus appellauerat propria partes praefate, uidelicet, Infrascripti Reuerendi frartres dicti monasterij capitulariter congregati ad Sonum campanulem In loco eorum monasterij uti Guardianus, Reuerendus frater Simon Glauich, frater Felix de Jadra frater Simon Margitich de Jadra, et frater Blasius Baichinich omnes fratres dicti monasterij habentes nomine ut dixerunt In capitulo ex parte una, et dictus magister Franciscus Tubicem ex alia sponte, et ultro medio et interpositione comunem amicorum ad hec pacta, concordium, et transactionem Insimul deuenerunt Imprimis dictus magister Franciscus se remouit ab appellatione praefata ita quod dicti partes ea non obstante possint, et ualeant proseque opus ceptum In fieri faciendo dictum necessarium cum hoc pacto quod dicti fratres teneant et debeant pro ut promiserunt, et solemniter Se obligauerunt facere murum per medium fouea Jam facta pro faciendo dictum necessarium Incipiendo a muro boreali Sit a uia publica usque ad murum Sirocalem qui murus sit altus usque ad plenum Solarium, et quod In parte quirinali fiat necessarium ipsum, et non In parte transuersali, et quod in alia parte dicta fouea confinantis cum muro transuersali cum dicto magistro Francisco fieri debeat locus ante purgandum publicibus Hoc declaratum quod quandocumque dicta fouea necessarij sic ut supra diuisa a parte quirinali Impleta fuerit In altum prout alta est uia, siue strata quod tunc liceat dictis fratribus facere necessarium etiam In dicta parte confinanti cum ipso magistro Francisco cum muro trauersali, et quem dicti fratres elleuarunt murum suprascriptum trauersalem ab proprio sollario supra ad Cuius muri elleuati solutionem pro dimidia tenebatur dictus magister Franciscus Ideo dicti uenerabiles fratres sponte promiserunt dicto magistro Francisco quod ab eo, nec ab filijs suis non exigent pecunias expenditas in ipso muro pro medietate spectanti ad ipsum magistrum Franciscum Sed liceat eis exigere ipsas pecunias post mortem filiorum siue filiarum dicti magistri Francisci ab eius nepotibus postquam prouenerint ad etatem legitimam Juxta extimationem fiendum de ipsa medietate muri, et pro ut si ipse magister Franciscus Uendiderit hoc habet in ipsam domum liceat eis exigere dictum Creditum ab emptoribus eiusdem domus, et Insuper dicti fratres remiserint dicto magistro Francisco Expensis In lite secutos, Hoc pacto Inter partes apposito quod si dictus magister Franciscus, heredes, et successores suis uellent uendere dictam domum suam aut permutare quod eodem pretio, et modo praeferant dicti fratres Item quod per hoc Instrumentum non derogetur clausuli Contenti in Sententia suprascripta si quidem damnum reciperet ipse magister Franciscus suum eius domus prope necessarium praefatum, qua omnia etc. 

Actum Jadra ubi supra praesentibus domino Johanne Donato Grisogono et magistro Johanne quondam Rade Sutore stipendiato Celeberrimi domini capitanei testibus habitis, uocatis, et rogatis etc.

## Die 22 mensis octobris 1562. 

Temporibus et pretura ut supra. Coram viro nobile Jadrensis spectabili domino Zoilo de Ferra honorando consiliario praefati celeberrimi domini Comitis. Ibique quum sit iuxta dictum et publicam affirmationem partium infrascriptarum, videlicet, Reverendi domini Mathei de Marchettis canonici Jarensis et una et magistri Luca filij posthumi quondam magistri Luca Marislauich de Spalato [Split] habitatoris Jadra partibus ex altera, quo idem Reverendus dominus Matheus Bernardinam nepotem suam filiam quondam Magdalena de Aurana [Vrana] eidem magistro Luca legitimo iunxerit ad ritum ad ritum [ _sic_ ] sacrosancta Mater Romanae Ecclesiae matrimonis, et pro tempore huius contra promiserit sibi in dotem et pro dote eiusdem Bernardina uxoris dicti magistri Luca dare eidem ita tunc contentanti et ad praesens affirmanti ducatos ducentos ad rationam librarum 6 parvorum 4 pro ducato hoc modo, videlicet, ducatos centum in una domo structa Jadra ad campum Sancti Lucae in curia dicta Raunich et una uinea gognaliorum octo in circa Cum eius proprio terreno plantata partim per Sozales et partim per ipsum Reverendum dominum Matheum, qua item olinata est posita subtus montem Belueder agriJadrensis quamdomum cum ipso Terreno uineato et oliuato ut dictum est idem magister Lucas accipit pro fundo dotali de communi concordio estimato dictis ducatis centum alios autem ducatos centum promiserit eidem dare partim in pecunia numerate et partin in uestimentis a dorso dictae Uxoris suae et alijs bonis mobilibus aestimandis pro comunis amicos per partes eligendum, et super hac dotis promissione nullum inter eadem partes fuerit huiusquem confectum Instrumentum, Ideo prius et ante omni nunc ad presentam praefati spectabili domini consiliarij mei notarij et testium idem Reverendus Matheus promissionem ipsam dotis ut prefertur tunc factam ipse verio magister Lucas stipulationem et acceptantionem eiusdem ex nunc prout per Se et heredes eorum illas approbant et huius instrumenti tenore ratificant, Inde uerio praefatus magister Lucas non vi dolo aut mentu, sine ulla machinatione ductus sed sponte et ex ueritate rei per se et heredes suos contentus et confessurs est ae habuisse, et cum effectu recepisse a dicto Reverendo domino Matheo preaesente et hanc confessionem pro Se et heredibus suis stipulante et acceptante in partem et pro parte dotis et supra promissa ducatorum ducentorum Easdem domum et uineam per dum dotale ut supra apprehendum pro dictis ducatis centum, Item pro parte et ad computum aliorum ducatorum Centum huius dotis confessus est partier, et de lato subu iuramento per praefarum spectabiliem consiliarium iurauit habuisse ante presetem stipulationem libras centum et triginta, et solidos septem paruorum in pecunia numerate Renuntians exception Earundem ad praesens non habitarum, et non receptarum omnique alij et statum auxilium, Item habuisse et recepisse ad nomen dotis predicta prout re uera ad praesentiam praefati spectabili domini Consilisarij mei Notarij et testium infrascriptorum habuit et receipt a dicto Reuerendi Domini Matheo dante et numerante in pecunia numerate ducatos triginta ad [rationem] librarum 6 paruorum 4 pro ducato quae omnes pecuniae reducta in ducatos faciunt ducatos quinquaginta unum ad [rationem] librarum 6 paruorum 4 pro ducato et solidis treis. De quibus docatis centum et quinquaginta uno habitis et receptis t supra rati0one dotis anteniminatae computatis, domo et uinea Cum terreno supradictis, Ideo magister Lucas per se et heredes suos fecit finem et quietationem perpetuam ac generalem eidem Reverendo domini Matteo presenti et pro se ac heredibus suis stipulanti et acceptanti Cum pacto de ulterius non petendo Sauo semper ressiduo dotis ut suprascripti promissa, Quam partem dotis ut supra habitam idem magister Lucas

<!-- page 269 -->

Urban Elites of Zadar 

268 

promisit conseruare, manutenere, non distrahere nec aliqualiter malignare quinimo eam illi, aut illis cui uel quibus Jus aut casus dederit restituere et reddere in omnem euentum dotis restituendam, et premissa omnia attendere, et inuiolabiliter obseruare, ac in nullo contrafacere dicere, vel uenire per se vel alios aliqua ratione, vel causa de Jure vel de facto sub poena quarti in statutis Jadra contenta, et obligatione omnium suorum bonorum mobilium et stabilium presentium et futurorum. 

Actum Jadra sub lodia Communis Presentibus domino Paladino Ciualello, et domino Hieronymo Cortesio Jadrensis etc. 

Ego Zoilus Ferra consiliarius celeberrimi domini comitis Jadr subscripsi

## _Primary Sources from the Croatian State Archive in Zadar_ 

All the below-listed primary sources that constitute the foundation of this study are found in the fond HR DAZD 31 Bilježnici Zadra (Notarii civitatis et districtus Iadrae)-Zadar (XII-1797); 1279-1797, and I used the records of the following notaries:

|---|---|---|---|
|Augustinus Martius|1540-1552|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 2)|
|Cornelius Constantius|1567-1569|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 4)|
|Daniel Cavalca|1551-1566|I-III|deeds (fasc. 1-2), wills (fasc. 3)|
|Franciscus Thomaseus|1548-1561|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 3-4)|
|Gabriel Cernotta|1562-1564|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 2)|
|Horatius de Marchettis|1567-1569|I|deeds and wills (fasc. 1)|
|Johannes a Morea|1545-1569|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 3)|
|Johannes Michael Mazzarellus|1540-1554|I-IV|deeds (fasc. 2), wills (fasc. 6-8)|
|Marcus Aurelius Sonzonius|1544-1548|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 6)|
|Nicolaus Canali|1548-1567|I|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 4)|
|Nicolaus Drasmileus|1540-1566|I, IV|deeds (fasc. 1-2), wills (fasc. 4)|
|Paulus de Sanctis|1540-1569|I-II|deeds (fasc. 1), wills (fasc. 2)|
|Petrus de Bassano|1545-1551|I|deeds (fasc. 1-5), wills (fasc. 6)|
|Simon Budineus|1556-1566|I|deeds (fasc. 1)|
|Simon Mazzarellus|1555-1569|Ie|deeds(fasc. 1),wills(fasc. 3)|



_Notes_ : I have surveyed all 6,436 deeds notarised between 1 January 1540 through 31 December 1569, of which 6,108 (or 95%) were stipulated in Latin. 

(a) lists the notaries in question; (b) gives the period when the named individual was active and/or from when deeds are included in the subsequent analyses; (c) provides details about the boxes (kutija, busta) where these deeds are found; (d) gives further particulars about the various fascicles, many of which contain several protocol books; (e) there is but one exception to the listing; for reasons of space, fasc. 2, which contains five books detailing arbitration settlements, is omitted in the above listing. 

In addition, I have taken recourse to one particular primary source collection in Dubrovnik: HR Državni arhiv u Dubrovniku, _Liber dotium notariae_ , vol. XII (with special thanks to Nicolò Villanti).

<!-- page 300 -->

## Index 

This is a conceptual index, incorporating key concepts, major themes and important terminology to illustrate, to a limited extent, the main arguments discussed in this study. Proper names and toponyms appear as they do in the book, except for those that are generally familiar. With respect to places bearing different names today, I have added their modern-day equivalents in parentheses. Note that the index “only” covers the main text, thus excluding the captions of tables, figures and footnotes. The reader is cordially invited to peruse the open access version of this book to sift through these for (much) “more” in terms of anecdotes, names, dates and places, extended and original quotations, commentary on the literature and supporting evidence for the claims advanced throughout the book. Likewise, I have refrained from reproducing references to every single toponym and place in Zadar’s jurisdiction; if one wishes to look up these, the reader is similarly invited to refer to the open access version of this book and to make good use of the “list of toponyms” in the Appendix. 

To facilitate orientation, I have grouped some of the entries in the following index into supra-ordinate categories, such as family surnames, placenames and battles. The same applies to social status groups, such as artisans, merchants or clergy, as well as to supra-ordinate groupings, such as “Venetian instituions”. Some idiosyncrasies could not be avoided, however, such as my qualitative, case-by-case judgment of the various groupings, most prominently the separation of members of the nobility from clergy. Moreover, I have refrained from listing all instances of any one term or idea, instead providing references to specifc institutions, names or places in their relevant context. One note: “q.”, or quondam, indicates “son or daughter of the late”, to indicate parental ties.

Finito di stampare nel mese di settembre 2025 da The Factory s.r.l. Roma

<!-- page 310 -->

viella historical research 

Stephan Karl Sander-Faes 

## Urban Elites of Zadar 

Dalmatia and the Venetian Commonwealth (1540-1569) 

This book examines the economic, geographical and social mobility, as well as everyday life – including crime history and material culture – of the inhabitants of the city of Zadar between the battles of Preveza (1538) and Lepanto (1571). The city, then known as Zara, was the capital of Venice’s dual province of Dalmatia and Albania, a major hub for commerce, communication and exchange and a vibrant port city connected to the wider Mediterranean world. 

Considered a period “not marked by any events worthy of the attention of posterity” by the French historian Pierre Daru two centuries ago, a view mirrored by most scholars since, this book offers the first in-depth foray into everyday life, widely understood, along the frontiers of Latin Christendom during the apogee of Ottoman dominance in the 16[th] century. In a first step, quantitative and qualitative analysis of the humble _procura_ contract details the quintessential qualities for the functioning of Renaissance communication and its foundational role in the economic, legal and social affairs in and across the Adriatic. In a second step, the book examines Zadar’s property markets, with particular attention to price changes in the city and its jurisdiction, including real estate transactions on the islands off the coast. The third and fourth parts focus on life in the bustling streets of Zadar, as well as the diverse interaction of its inhabitants – nobles, citizens, residents and foreigners alike. Relaying information about extrajudicial settlements, and drawing on testaments and codicils, a more vivid and socially inclusive reconstruction of urban life in the Renaissance Adriatic emerges. 

**Stephan Karl Sander-Faes** is Associate Professor in History at the Dept. of Archaeology, History, Cultural Studies and Religion at the University of Bergen, Norway, as well as Privatdozent in Early Modern and Modern History at the University of Zurich, Switzerland. His research focuses on Central and Eastern Europe after the Middle Ages.
